% Arabic font's ascenders, descenders, and diacritics increase Latex's line
% spacing irregularly for some lines more than others to result in a visually
% unappealing output.
%
% I tried setting \lineskiplimit=-\maxdimen as some had suggested this but it
% completely destroys linespacing in tables and around tikzpictures.
%
% Then I found that \smash forces a 0 depth and 0 height for it's argument. So
% the Arabic text is ignored by Latex for line spacing purposes. Call \smash on
% the Arabic text input to babel's \foreignlanguage as below.
%
% I also tried calling smash on the output of \foreignlanguage but that seemed
% to cause problems on chapter headings.
%
% However, with this solution, some itemized lists have Arabic text directly on
% top of each other and the diacritics collide. For these lists insert a
% \vphantom{\huge J} for each item to locally increase line spacing. See
% examples in script.Rmd and inna_and_its_sisters.Rmd.
%
% The other unsolved issue with the solution is that it messes up the bidi dir
% in the sidebar contents in my PDF reader
\AddBabelHook[arabic]{smash}{foreign}{\def\BabelText##1{\smash{##1}}}

\chapter*{Preface}\label{preface}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Preface}

\markboth {\textsc{\MakeLowercase{Preface}}}{\textsc{\MakeLowercase{Preface}}}

\begin{center}
\tradarab{
ุงูุฑุญูู
ุงูุฑุญูู
ุงููู
ุจุณู
}
\end{center}

The primary texts of Islฤm (the Qurสพฤn and the แธคadฤซtอกh) are in Arabic. So too is much of its scholarly literature. However, there is a multitude of Muslims for whom Arabic is not a native language, yet who are familiar enough with English to study textbooks written in this language. The goal of this book is to help them learn Arabic at a beginner's level so that, together with a study of the appropriate expositional texts, they are one step closer to understanding the primary texts in their original language. We hope that this will, if Allฤh wills, make them feel more connected to the primary texts and their teachings. Furthermore, they can be empowered to study the vast body of Arabic Islฤmic literature.

This book is a teaching grammar and not a reference grammar. So, in the initial chapters, topics are presented sequentially at only a basic level, without treating them exhaustively, before moving on to the next topic. Furthermore, since this is a beginner's textbook, only the more common usages are explained.

We have also aimed to make this a self-instruction textbook so that a diligent student should, if Allฤh wills, be able to study it without an instructor. The target learner is someone who has not been exposed to grammatical terminology like \emph{inflection}, \emph{case}, \emph{mood}, etc. While terminology is necessary for a rigorous non-immersive learning of language, we have tried to steer away from Latin-based terms like \emph{accusative} and \emph{jussive}. Such terms, when first encountered by an uninitiated learner, may deter from proceeding further. (Learning a language can be hard enough without getting the feeling that your grammar book is accusing you of something!) So we have in some places translated the meaning of Arabic grammar terms to English. In other places, we have used established English grammar terms where the terms are basic enough. We have even, in places, invented terms where we deemed appropriate. The drawback to this non-standard approach, however, is that the student may not be able to immediately relate the terminology he has learned in this book to established terminology in other grammar textbooks. To remedy this to some extent, we provide a glossary in the appendix which maps the grammatical terminology used in this book to other, established, Latin-based and Arabic-based counterparts.

It may also be appropriate to inform the reader that we chose to present a simplified version of Arabic grammar. As such, the grammar presented here may not be entirely consistent with the comprehensive and harmonious framework developed by the Arab grammarians. We chose this approach because we felt that exposing the beginner to complex grammatical details at this stage would be more of a hindrance than a help in learning the language.

This book is produced with the R bookdown package. The code and text are open-sourced and developed at
\href{https://github.com/adamiturabi/arabic-tutorial-book}{github.com/adamiturabi/arabic-tutorial-book}.
The typeset output is published at
\href{https://adamiturabi.github.io/arabic-tutorial-book/}{adamiturabi.github.io/arabic-tutorial-book/}.

\textsc{The Authors}

\chapter{Introduction}\label{introduction}

All praises are due to Allฤh. We praise Him, seek His help, and ask for His forgiveness. We seek refuge in Allฤh from the evil in our souls and from our sinful deeds. Whomever Allฤh guides, no one can mislead. Whomever Allฤh leads astray, no one can guide. I bear witness that there is no one worthy of worship except Allฤh. I also bear witness that Muแธฅammad is His servant and messenger.

May the peace and blessings of Allฤh be upon the Prophet Muแธฅammad, his family, his companions, and those who followed them with good conduct.

\section{History of Arabic}\label{history-of-arabic}

Allฤh, may He be glorified and exalted, revealed the Qurสพฤn 1400 years ago to the Prophet Muแธฅammad, may Allฤh grant peace and confer blessing upon him. The language of the Qurสพฤn is the Arabic language, as it was understood by the Arabs at that time.
The sayings and actions of the Prophet, may Allฤh grant peace and confer blessing upon him, were recorded by his companions also in this Arabic language.
We will call the Arabic of this pre-Islฤmic and early Islฤmic era as Classical Arabic.
The Classical Arabic language consisted of multiple dialects that were spoken by the different tribes and in the different regions of the Arabian peninsula.

All languages change naturally over time. For example, English has changed to such a degree that the Old English language spoken 1400 years ago would be unintelligible to us today. So too did the Classical Arabic dialects begin to change. But as part of preserving His religion, Allฤh preserved the Arabic language as well. This was by means of the efforts of scholars who recorded the Classical Arabic language of the time of the revelation.

In the process of preserving Arabic, one particular variety became standardized and gained prevalence as a literary language over the other dialects of the Arabic of the early-Islฤmic period. This \emph{Standard Arabic}, in its early period after standardization, is called Classical Standard Arabic.
The pre-Islฤmic and early Islฤmic Arabic dialects (of which Classical Standard Arabic is but a standardized variety) are then referred to, collectively, as pre-standard Classical Arabic. Classical Standard Arabic was used as the language of religious scholarship, science, and literature in the Islฤmic world. As scholars developed new branches of religious and secular sciences, new terms and meanings were added to it that are termed post-classical. A few words were also borrowed from foreign languages and Arabicized, as needed by the different scientific disciplines. (Pre-Standard Classical Arabic itself had a few Arabicized foreign borrowings from neighboring languages.) These additions were, by and large, deliberate, done by scholars who were experts in their fields and also well versed in Classical Standard Arabic, and validated by subsequent generations of scholarly discourse. Besides these needed additions, the grammar and core language remained remarkably unchanged.

While Standard Arabic was thus preserved from major change and was used for literary purposes, the language that was spoken by Arabs in their day-to-day lives continued to change over time from the pre-standard Classical Arabic dialects into the modern colloquial dialects. And so today, there exist two very distinct types of Arabic: the preserved Standard Arabic which is taught at schools and is primarily a written language, and the modern colloquial Arabic dialects which Arabs learn as their mother tongue and which are primarily only spoken and not written.

\begin{figure}
\centering
\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-3-1.pdf}
\caption{\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-3}Timeline of the development of Standard Arabic.}
\end{figure}

In modern times, many new words and meanings have been added to Standard Arabic, often via translation from Western languages, to keep up with technological advancements and modern media.
This modern development of Standard Arabic is called Modern Standard Arabic.
There are also a small amount of words, meanings, and grammatical usages, which existed in Classical Arabic, but which are deemed archaic, and are therefore largely unused, in Modern Standard Arabic.

Figure~1.1 (above) depicts this historical development of Standard Arabic.

\section{Scope of this book}\label{scope-of-this-book}

In this book, we will study Standard Arabic. We will focus on the pre-modern language. If Allฤh wills, this will help you to begin to understand the language of the Qurสพฤn, the แธคadฤซtอกh, and Islฤmic literature.

If your goal is to learn Modern Standard Arabic, then this book may still be of help because the core language and the grammar are essentially the same. However, you may prefer to study from a resource that focuses on the modern language.

This book does not touch at all upon the modern colloquial dialects that are spoken in the Arab world today.

\section{How to study from this book}\label{how-to-study-from-this-book}

We will start with the Arabic script and present in each chapter a new concept of Arabic grammar, together with examples. We will also give vocabulary for you to memorize and have chapter exercises. Unfortunately, some of the sentences we present, both as examples and as chapter exercises, because of their construction and subject matter, may seem of dubious usefulness to a learner wanting to learn practical usage. We ask that you overlook this and bear with us as we try to reinforce grammatical concepts. In answering the exercises, we strongly recommend that you memorize the vocabulary in full and write down the answers on paper with a pen.

We strongly recommend that you \textbf{not}:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  answer the exercises verbally without writing them down,
\item
  look up the answers before attempting to write the answer yourself,
\item
  look up words in the vocabulary list without memorizing them,
\item
  proceed to the next chapter before memorizing the vocabulary and going through the exercises.
\end{itemize}

Be aware that while Arabic grammar requires effort to master to a proficient degree, the real barrier to reading and understanding Arabic texts by oneself is vocabulary. Arabic is a very rich language and knowledge of a few thousand words is needed before the student can begin to read texts independently.

You may also find yourself having to go back a few chapters every once in a while and revising the concepts therein. This is very normal and not a cause for any alarm. It may also prove beneficial to re-do the exercises of that chapter when this occurs.

\chapter{The Arabic script}\label{the-arabic-script}

\section{The Arabic alphabet}\label{the-arabic-alphabet}

The alphabet consists of both consonants and vowels. In the English word \enquote{banana}, \enquote{a} is a vowel, and \enquote{b} and \enquote{n} are called consonants.
The Arabic alphabet traditionally has 28 letters, shown in the table below.

  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.0465}}

  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.0698}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1628}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.6512}}@{}}

1 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} & \emph{ฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} สพalif & A vowel like in English \enquote{man}. But after these letters (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎุุฑุุตุุถุุบุู}) it sounds like \enquote{awe} in English \enquote{awesome}. \\
2 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจ} & \emph{b} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุงุก} bฤสพ & Equivalent to English \enquote{b} in \enquote{boy}. \\
3 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} & \emph{t} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุงุก} tฤสพ & Similar to English \enquote{t} in \enquote{tall} but softer. Touch the tongue against the back of the top front teeth instead of just the gum. \\
4 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซ} & \emph{tอกh} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูุงุก} tอกhฤสพ & Similar to to English \enquote{th} in \enquote{think} but softer. Have your lips and cheek in a wide grin. Loosely bite the tip of your tongue between your front teeth and then force air out trying to hiss \enquote{ssss}. Keep your tongue touching the top and bottom teeth and the hiss should come out like a \enquote{th} sound. \\
5 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌ} & \emph{j} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููู} jฤซm & Equivalent to English \enquote{j} in \enquote{just}. \\
6 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญ} & \emph{แธฅ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุงุก} แธฅฤสพ & Similar to English \enquote{h} in \enquote{hat} but pronounced from the bottom of the throat. Take care there is no scraping as with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎ}. \\
7 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎ} & \emph{kอกh} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุงุก} kอกhฤสพ & Similar to \enquote{ch} in Scottish \enquote{loch}. Try saying \enquote{kh} but with a scraping sound. \\
8 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏ} & \emph{d} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุงู} dฤl & Similar to to English \enquote{d} in \enquote{dog} but softer. Just like with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช}, touch the tongue against the back of the top front teeth instead of just the gum. \\
9 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐ} & \emph{dอกh} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุงู} dอกhฤl & Place your tongue as in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซ} and force air out. But this time instead of trying to hiss \enquote{ssss} try to buzz \enquote{zzzz} and again keep your tongue touching the top and bottom teeth. \\
10 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑ} & \emph{r} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุงุก} rฤสพ & Equivalent to English \enquote{r} in \enquote{rat}. \\
11 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒ} & \emph{z} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูุงุก} zฤสพ & Equivalent to English \enquote{z} in \enquote{zoo}. \\
12 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณ} & \emph{s} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููู} sฤซn & Equivalent to English \enquote{s} in \enquote{see}. \\
13 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุด} & \emph{sอกh} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดููู} sอกhฤซn & Equivalent to English \enquote{sh} in \enquote{show}. \\
14 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุต} & \emph{แนฃ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุงุฏ} แนฃฤd & An emphatic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณ} that will be described later. \\
15 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถ} & \emph{แธ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุงุฏ} แธฤd & An sound unique to Arabic that will be described later. \\
16 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุท} & \emph{แนญ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูุงุก} แนญฤสพ & An emphatic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} that will be described later. \\
17 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธ} & \emph{dฬฃอกh} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธูุงุก} dฬฃอกhฤสพ & An emphatic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐ} that will be described later. \\
18 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุน} & \emph{ษ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููู} ษayn & A sound similar to strangulation or gagging. Try to sound \enquote{a} from the bottom of the throat. \\
19 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบ} & \emph{gอกh} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูููู} gอกhayn & Somewhat like a \enquote{gh} sound but much softer. Try pronouncing \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎ} but without any scraping. \\
20 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \emph{f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุก} fฤสพ & Equivalent to English \enquote{f} in \enquote{fox}. \\
21 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \emph{q} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงู} qฤf & Similar to English \enquote{k} in \enquote{kite} but further back in the throat. \\
22 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉ} & \emph{k} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงู} kฤf & Equivalent to English \enquote{k} in \enquote{kite}. \\
23 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \emph{l} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงู} lฤm & Equivalent to English \enquote{l} in \enquote{light}. \\
24 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \emph{m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} mฤซm & Equivalent to English \enquote{m} in \enquote{man}. \\
25 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \emph{n} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} nลซn & Equivalent to English \enquote{n} in \enquote{nut}. \\
26 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \emph{h} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุก} hฤสพ & Equivalent to English \enquote{h} in \enquote{hat}. Much softer than \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญ} \\
27 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \emph{w}/\emph{ลซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงู} wฤw & As a consonant it is equivalent to English \enquote{w} in \enquote{water}. It is also a vowel equivalent to English \enquote{oo} in \enquote{moon}. \\
28 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \emph{y}/\emph{ฤซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุก} yฤสพ & As a consonant it is equivalent to English \enquote{y} in \enquote{yellow}. It is also a vowel equivalent to English \enquote{ee} in \enquote{meek}. \\
\end{longtable}

Note that the letters \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}~wฤw and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}~yฤสพ are both vowels and consonants. But that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} สพalif is only a vowel. The consonant corresponding to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง}~สพalif is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก}~hamzah. Although \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก}~hamzah ought to be considered a letter in its own right, it was historically only pronounced and not written. So it is written as a pronunciation mark and is traditionally not considered part of the 28-letter alphabet.

\subsection{Pronunciation notes}\label{pronunciation-notes}

Some of the sounds are similar to sounds in English but others are very different. Here we will attempt to describe the sounds but we recommend that you learn the correct pronunciation from an experienced Arabic or Qurสพฤn teacher. Online videos may also help in practicing the sounds.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุต} แนฃฤd, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุท} แนญฤสพ, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธ} dฬฃอกhฤสพ}{ุต แนฃฤd, ุท แนญฤสพ, and ุธ dฬฃอกhฤสพ}}\label{ux635-sad-ux637-tae-and-ux638-pae}

The letters \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณ} sฤซn, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} tฤสพ, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐ} dอกhฤl are pronounced with the mouth and lips in a wide grin. Now try pronouncing them, in turn, with the lips round forming a small circle. The sounds will be emphatic and will be \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุต} แนฃฤd, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุท} แนญฤสพ, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธ} dฬฃอกhฤสพ respectively.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถ} แธฤd}{ุถ แธฤd}}\label{ux636-dad}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถ} แธฤd is thought to be unique to Arabic. There are two ways to pronounce it. The first is similar to an emphatic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏ}. The second is almost similar to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธ}. We reiterate that it is best to use audio training to help with pronouncing these sounds.

\section{Writing Arabic words}\label{writing-arabic-words}

\subsection{Letters in different positions}\label{letters-in-different-positions}

Arabic is written right-to-left, unlike English and most other languages which are written left-to-right. When writing, the letters in a word are generally joined to each other, except for six out of the 28 letters, which join only to the letter preceding them but not to the letter following them. These six partially-joining letters are \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุ ุฏุ ุฐุ ุฑุ ุฒุ ู}.

When joining the letters, letters are modified in order to join to the preceding and following letter. The fully-joining letters can be in four positions:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  by itself (isolated),
\item
  in the beginning of a group of joined letters,
\item
  in the middle of a group of joined letters,
\item
  in the end of a group of joined letters.
\end{enumerate}

As we just mentioned, six of the letters (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุ ุฏุ ุฐุ ุฑุ ุฒุ ู}) don't join to the following letter. So these letters can only occur only in the end of a group of joined letters, or isolated by themselves.

In this book we will show a \enquote{Simplified Arabic} writing style where, in each of the four positions, the letter maintains its basic shape and is usually only slightly modified to join to the previous and following letter with horizontal lines.

To explain the method of modifying the letters when joining them, we will take \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจ} as an example and start with the isolated form:

Isolated form: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจ}

To modify this into the end form, we simply join a horizontal line to the right of the letter:

End form: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุจ}.

To get the middle form, we take the end form \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุจ} and cut off its tail which is at its left, and replace it with a horizontal line. We also move the dot slightly to get:

Middle form: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุจู}

And finally, to get the beginning form, we take the middle form \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุจู} and remove the horizontal line at the right:

Beginning form: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจู}

Now most of the letters follow this common technique but a few of them are modified a little further in each form. These, more complicated, letters are \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนุ ุบุ ฺฉุ ูุ ู} and you can study them and the rest of the letters in the table below:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lllll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
No. & Isolated & End & Middle & Beginning \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
1 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุง} & none & none \\
2 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุจ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุจู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจู} \\
3 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุช} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุชู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชู} \\

13 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุด} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุด} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุดู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดู} \\
14 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุต} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุต} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุตู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตู} \\
15 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุถ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุถู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถู} \\
16 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุท} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุท} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุทู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทู} \\

19 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุบ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุบู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบู} \\
20 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \\

24 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \\
25 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \\
26 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \\
27 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & none & none \\
28 & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \\
\end{longtable}

You can see that each letter maintains a basic shape and is modified for each of the four positions.

\subsection{Joining the different forms to make a word}\label{joining-the-different-forms-to-make-a-word}

Notice that when we modified the isolated form to get to the beginning, middle, and end forms, we added a horizontal line to each or both sides. It is this horizontal line which joines to the horizontal line of the neighboring letter.

As an example, we would like to join the following letters (starting from the right): \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู-ุน-ุด-ุฑ} into one word. The first letter is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} so we modify it to its beginning form \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}. The next two letters are converted to their middle forms \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุนูุ ูุดู}. And the last letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑ} is converted to its end form \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุฑ}. Then we join the horizontal lines together and get \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููุนููุดููุฑ}. Usually, when we join letters like this we shorten the horizontal lines so you will generally see the word like this \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุนุดุฑ}.

In this example, we needed the beginning, middle, and end forms of the letters. Isolated forms are used in a word when there is a partially-joining letter present that won't join to the following letter. The letter after a partially-joining letter will be in its beginning form even though it is in the middle of a word. But if it too is a partially-joining letter, or it is the last letter in the word then it will take its isolated form.

Let's take a look at some examples where a group of disjoint letters are joined to form a word:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Disjoint & Joined \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐ-ู-ฺฉ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง-ุญ-ู-ุฏ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุญูุฏ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑ-ุณ-ู-ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑุณูู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู-ุฒ-ู-ุฑ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุฒูุฑ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑ-ุง-ุฒ-ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑุงุฒู} \\
\end{longtable}

Notice that in the last example, all the letters were in the isolated form.

\subsubsection{Simplified and Traditional writing styles}\label{simplified-and-traditional-writing-styles}

We have just shown how letters join to each other with a horizontal line in the Simplified Arabic writing style. Traditional Arabic writing styles are a little more complex than Simplified Arabic: some letters join almost vertically instead of horizontally. But when you get familiar with the Simplified Arabic writing style, if Allah wills, it will not be too difficult for you to read the Traditional Arabic writing style as well.

Here are some comparisions of letters joining to each other in the Simplified Arabic and Traditional Arabic writing styles.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Disjoint & Joined (simplified) & Joined (traditional) \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช-ู-ุฑ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุฑ} & {\tradarab{ุชูุฑ}} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง-ู-ุญ-ุฌ-ุฌ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงูุญุฌุฌ} & {\tradarab{ุงูุญุฌุฌ}} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง-ู-ู-ุง-ุณ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงููุงุณ} & {\tradarab{ุงููุงุณ}} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู-ู-ุญ-ุฉ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญุฉ} & {\tradarab{ููุญุฉ}} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจ-ุญ-ุฑ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจุญุฑ} & {\tradarab{ุจุญุฑ}} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณ-ุญ-ุฑ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณุญุฑ} & {\tradarab{ุณุญุฑ}} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู-ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & {\tradarab{ูู}} \\
\end{longtable}

\subsection{Looped tฤสพ}\label{looped-tae}

Looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} is a special letter which is merged from two letters of the alphabet. It is a tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} but it is written as a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} hฤสพ with two dots above it. Looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} is pronounced exactly as a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} tฤสพ, except when it is at the end of a sentence in which case it is pronounced as a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} hฤสพ as we'll explain later, if Allฤh wills. Looped tฤสพ occurs only at the end of a word so it has only an end form and an isolated form (used when the letter before it is a partially-joining letter).

Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุงุทูุฉ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดุฌุฑุฉ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุชุงุฉ}
\end{itemize}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} is called \enquote{open tฤสพ} when needed, to differentiate it from looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}.

\subsection{Writing hamzah}\label{writing-hamzah}

We have mentioned that hamzah was a later addition to the Arabic alphabet and originally it was only sounded and not written. Hamzah can be written in a number of different ways:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \enquote{Seated} above (or below) a vowel letter: Hamzah can be written above the vowel letters thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃ ุค ุฆ}. When written over \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} will not have any dots, thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฆูุ ูุฆูุ ูุฆ}. It may also be written under an สพalif thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅ}. Examples: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนุงู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณุคูฺฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุฆุฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅู}.
\item
  \enquote{Unseated} after a letter. This has two sub cases:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\alph{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Standalone, after a partially-joining letter or at the end of a word. Examples: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชุณุงุกู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุกู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนุจุก}.
  \item
    Inline, in the middle of a word after a fully-joining letter. In this case hamzah is written above the horizontal line that joins the letters. Examples: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎุทูููุฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุง}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจุฑููููู}.
  \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

In all cases it is pronounced the same. There are actually a set of fairly complicated rules that determine which of the above ways to choose when writing hamzah. We present these rules in Appendix \ref{hamzarules}. We recommend that for now, you memorize the spelling of each word that we present that contains a hamzah. When you are sufficiently advanced, and curious enough, you may refer to Appendix \ref{hamzarules} to learn the full set of rules.

\subsection{Disambiguating letters that look similar}\label{disambiguating-letters-that-look-similar}

Some letters are very similar to each other and only differ in their dots or other slight differences. You should take care to distinguish between these letters. We will describe their similarities and differences here.

The letters \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซ} differ only in their dots and are otherwise identical in all positions. \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} are similar in initial and middle positions to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซ} but differ from them and from each other in isolated and final positions. Compare all five in the table below:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}llll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Isolated & End & Middle & Beginnning \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุจ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุจู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุช} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุชู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุซู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \\
\end{longtable}

These groups of letters differ too, only in their dots:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญ}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุด}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุต} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุท} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุน} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบ}
\end{itemize}

The letters \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} are similar in the initial and middle positions except for the dots. But in the isolated and final positions, the tail of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} goes lower than that of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}llll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Isolated & End & Middle & Beginnning \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \\
\end{longtable}

Be careful also not to confuse \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} in their middle forms. The loop for \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} is round where it is triangular and flat-topped for \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบ} (as it is for \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุน}). Compare their middle forms in the table below:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Isolated & Middle \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุบู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \\
\end{longtable}

The letters สพalif \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} and lฤm \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} could also be confused for each other. Their forms are shown here again for easy comparison:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}llll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Isolated & End & Middle & Beginnning \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุง} & none & none \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \\
\end{longtable}

\subsection{Joining lฤm and สพalif}\label{joining-lam-and-ealif}

When the letter สพalif follows lฤm we would expect them to be joined like this \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู+ุง} โ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง}. But actually, they are joined in a special way

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู+ุง} โ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุง}

When the combination occurs at the end of a group of joined letters, it will appear thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง}

Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุง}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงูุฅููุงู}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงูุตูุงุฉ}
\end{itemize}

\section{Vowels and pronunciation marks.}\label{vowels-and-pronunciation-marks.}

\subsection{Short Vowels}\label{short-vowels}

Arabic has six vowels. There are three short vowels which don't have letters in the alphabet. Instead they are shown with pronunciation marks:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{a} as the first vowel in English \enquote{manipulate}, written with an \emph{a}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} which is a small diagonal line above the letter like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{ma}.
\item
  \emph{i} as in English \enquote{bit}, written with an \emph{i}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} which is a small diagonal line under the letter like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูู} \emph{bi}.
\item
  \emph{u} as in English \enquote{put}, written with an \emph{u}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} which is like a tiny \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} wฤw above the letter like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{fu}.
\end{enumerate}

Examples of words with short vowels:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุญู} \emph{fataแธฅa}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู} \emph{ษamila}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชููู} \emph{qutila}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Long Vowels}\label{long-vowels}

There are also three long vowels which are part of the alphabet:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{ฤ} generally written with an unmarked \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} สพalif and with the preceding letter having an \emph{a}-mark. Example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{mฤ}. This vowel is mostly pronounced like the vowel in English \enquote{man}. If however, it comes after these letters \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎุุฑุุตุุถุุทุุธุุบุู} it is pronounced like English \enquote{awe}.
\item
  \emph{ฤซ} like in English \enquote{meek} written with an unmarked \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} yฤสพ with the preceding letter having an \emph{i}-mark. Example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{fฤซ}.
\item
  \emph{ลซ} like in English \enquote{moon} written with an unmarked \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} wฤw with the preceding letter having an \emph{u}-mark. Example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู} \emph{dอกhลซ}.
\end{enumerate}

Examples of words with long and short vowels:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุฑูููู} \emph{hฤrลซnu}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฐูุง} \emph{kadอกhฤ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฑูุงุฏููุจู} \emph{sarฤdฤซba}
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\emph{ฤ} vowel written with a small สพalif}{ฤ vowel written with a small สพalif}}\label{a-vowel-written-with-a-small-ealif}

Sometimes the \emph{ฤ} vowel is written as a small สพalif \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูฐ}, called a \enquote{dagger สพalif}, instead of a regular สพalif \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง}. This is done only for a few commonly used words. Here are some examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง} \emph{hฤdอกhฤ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐู~ูฐููฺฉู} \emph{dอกhฤlika}
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\emph{ฤ} vowel written with a yฤสพ}{ฤ vowel written with a yฤสพ}}\label{a-vowel-written-with-a-ya}

In some other words, the \emph{ฤ} vowel is written with a yฤสพ instead of an สพalif \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง}. When this happens, we will write the yฤสพ without its dots and write a dagger สพalif \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูฐ} above it, like this \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}. Here are some examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููููฐ} \emph{ษalฤ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูููููฐ} \emph{ramฤ}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Zero-vowel written with a รธ-mark}\label{zero-vowel-written-with-a-0-mark}

As we have seen above if an Arabic letter has a vowel after it it will take one of the three pronunciation marks: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. If, however, there is no vowel after the letter we will put a zero-vowel รธ-mark on it \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. This mark can generally only occur if there is a short vowel before the letter. Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} \emph{kam}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุฐู} \emph{mundอกhu}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููููู} \emph{minhum}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููููุง} \emph{minhฤ}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Semi-vowels}\label{semi-vowels}

Arabic has two short semi-vowels:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{aw} like in English \enquote{show}. This is written with a wฤw with a รธ-mark on it and a \emph{ฤ} vowel before it. Example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{law}.
\item
  \emph{ay} like in English \enquote{bait}. This is written with a yฤสพ with a รธ-mark on it and a \emph{ฤ} vowel before it. Example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} \emph{kay}.
  Examples with short semi-vowels:
\end{enumerate}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุญูฺฉู} \emph{wayแธฅaka}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูููุฑููู} \emph{gอกhayruhu}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููููู} \emph{qawluhu}
\end{itemize}

It also has two long semi-vowels:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{ฤw} like in English \enquote{cow}. This is written with a wฤw with a รธ-mark on it and a \emph{ฤ} vowel before it. Example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงูู} \emph{wฤw}.
\item
  \emph{ฤy} like in English \enquote{bye}. This is written with a yฤสพ with a รธ-mark on it and a \emph{ฤ} vowel before it. Example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุงูู} \emph{sอกhฤy}.
\end{enumerate}

These long semi-vowels are rare and may only occur at the end of a sentence.

\subsection{Doubled letters}\label{doubled-letters}

A word may contain \enquote{doubled} letters. This is when the same letter occurs, one after the other; the first letter has a รธ-mark, and the second letter has a vowel. For example, in the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุชููู} \emph{qattala}, the letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} is doubled. When this occurs, we actually only write the letter once and put a \enquote{doubling mark} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} on it, like so: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูููู} \emph{qattala}. When pronouncing this word, stop at and stress the doubled letter \emph{qattala} and make sure it does not sound like the undoubled letter in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชููู} \emph{qatala}. Examples with doubled letters:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑู} \emph{kabbara}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฏูููู} \emph{แธฅadduhu}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููุงูู} \emph{faษษฤla}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูฺฉููููู} \emph{sikkฤซnu}. Note that the \emph{i}-mark is below the doubling mark but above the letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉ}. This is the most common way to write this, although having the \emph{i}-mark below the letter is also sometimes done as well. (In this case, the doubling mark will still be above the letter.)
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููููุฏู} \emph{saffลซdu}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุงูููููู} \emph{แธฤllฤซna}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฒููููููู} \emph{muzzammilu}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\emph{n}-marks}{n-marks}}\label{n-marks}

Arabic also has three distinctive pronunciation marks, collectively called \emph{n}-marks.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{an}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}
\item
  \emph{in}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}
\item
  \emph{un}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}
\end{enumerate}

These \emph{n}-marks may only occur on a letter at the end of a word. They are pronounced as a short vowel (\emph{a}, \emph{i}, or \emph{u}) followed by an \emph{n}. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงูููู} \emph{sฤlimun}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงูููู} \emph{sฤlimin}.

As a spelling rule, if a word ends with an \emph{an} mark, we will generally add a silent สพalif after it, for example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงููู} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงููููุง} \emph{sฤliman}. This is done for all words except:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  If the word ends with a looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}. In this case we don't add the silent สพalif. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุงุถูุจูุฉ} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุงุถูุจูุฉู} \emph{gอกhฤแธibatan}, not \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุงุถูุจูุฉูุง} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุงุถูุจูุชูุง}.
\item
  If the word ends with a \emph{ฤ} vowel, whether written with an สพalif \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} or as a yฤสพ with dagger สพalif \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}. In this case, the \emph{an} mark is put on the letter before the สพalif \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} or yฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} and the final vowel letter becomes silent and is not pronounced. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุตูุทูููููฐ} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุตูุทูููู} \emph{muแนฃแนญafan}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุตูุง} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุตูุง} \emph{ษaแนฃan}.
\item
  If the word ends with a hamzah. In this case, we might or might not write a silent สพalif, depending on the following rules:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\alph{enumii}.}
  \item
    If there is an สพalif before an unseated hamzah \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, then we don't add a silent สพalif. For example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุงุก} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุงุกู} \emph{dฤสพan}, not \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุงุกูุง}.
  \item
    Otherwise, we add a silent สพalif after the hamzah. However, this may affect the writing of the hamzah, for example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูุชูุฏูุฃ} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูุชูุฏูุกูุง} \emph{mubtadaสพan}. This is discussed further in Appendix \ref{hamzarules}.
  \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

Here are some examples of words with \emph{n}-marks:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุนูุฏู} \emph{saษdun} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจูุง} \emph{แธarban} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุถู} \emph{qฤแธin} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุนูุฉู} \emph{saษatan} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุนูุงุกู} \emph{duษฤสพan} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุกูุง} \emph{imraสพan} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดููููููุง} \emph{sอกhayสพan} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููุกูุง} \emph{sลซสพan} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุจููู} \emph{gอกhabanun} \vphantom{\huge J}
\end{itemize}

\section{Connecting hamzah}\label{connecting-hamzah}

Some words in arabic begin with a รธ-mark. When this occurs a connecting hamzah \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑ} (written as a tiny \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตู} on an สพalif) is put before it. If this word comes in the beginning of the sentence the connecting alif is pronounced as a hamzah. Otherwise this connecting hamzah is not pronounced and the word is connected to the final vowel of the previous word in pronunciation. In this tutorial we will transcribe the connecting hamzah with a hyphen \enquote{-}. Examples of connecting hamzah:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุชูุญู ูฑููุจูุงุจู}\\
\emph{สพiftaแธฅi -lbฤba}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุธูุฑู}\\
\emph{สพundฬฃอกhur}

If the previous word does not end with a vowel, then a helper vowel is added. The most common helper vowel is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู ูฑููฺฉูุฑูููู}\\
\emph{zayduni -lkarฤซmu}

When one word ends in a long vowel and the next word begins with a connecting hamzah, the long vowel becomes a short vowel in pronunciation, but in writing the long vowel's letter is retained. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐู ูููููุง ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู}\\
\emph{สพakอกhadอกha minna -lkitฤba}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู ูฑููููุฑููููููู}\\
\emph{dอกhu -lqarnayni}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู ูฑููุจูููุชู}\\
\emph{fi -lbayti}

\section{Pronouncing the end of a sentence}\label{pronouncing-the-end-of-a-sentence}

When a word is at the end of a sentence and it ends with a long vowel, then the final long vowel is pronounced normally. However, when a word at the end of a sentence does not end with a long vowel, then the final letter's pronunciation mark is pronounced as a รธ-mark when vocalizing the sentence. If the final letter is a looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} then it is pronounced as a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} hฤสพ with a รธ-mark.

This change in pronunciation is only vocal, it does not affect how we write the pronunciation mark. Here we give some examples of words pronounced if they were at the end of a sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุญู}\\
\emph{fatยทแธฅ}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุจูุฉู}\\
\emph{ษuqbah}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงููุฏูุงูู}\\
\emph{wฤlidฤy}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงููุฏูููู}\\
\emph{wฤlidayy}

If however, the final letter's pronunciation mark is a \emph{an} mark then it is pronounced as a long-\emph{ฤ} vowel. The only exception is if the final letter were looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉู}, in which case it is then pronounced as a hฤสพ with a รธ-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}. Here are examples of words with \emph{an} marks pronounced as if they were at the end of a sentence.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููุง}\\
\emph{mafษลซlฤ}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงุฌูุฏูุง}\\
\emph{sฤjidฤ}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑููููุนูุฉู}\\
\emph{marfลซษah}

Note that the above exception is only for looped tฤสพ. If a hamzah with an \emph{an} mark occurs at the end of a word, then it too will be pronounced as if it had a long-\emph{ฤ} vowel after it. Such is the case, whether or not a silent สพalif is written after the hamzah. Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูุชูุฏูุกูุง} is pronounced \emph{mubtadaสพฤ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุนูุงุกู} is pronounced \emph{duษฤสพฤ}
\end{itemize}

Similarly, if the word has a final yฤสพ that represents the long-\emph{ฤ} vowel, and the letter before has an \emph{an} mark, it is pronounced with the long-\emph{ฤ} vowel at the end of the sentence. For example:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุตูุทูููู} is pronounced \emph{muแนฃแนญafฤ}
\end{itemize}

Except in this section, we will usually transcribe Arabic into English letters without modifiying the transcription for the last word in the sentence. This is because the last vowel mark is helpful for us to learn the grammatical function of the word. But when saying the sentence out aloud you should pronounce the ending of the final word as we have just described.

For example, the sentence:\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ุฅูููู ูฑููุจูููุชู}

will be transcribed, in the remainder of this book, as:\\
\emph{dอกhahaba สพila -lbayti}

but should be pronounced as\\
\emph{dอกhahaba สพila -lbayt}

\section{Qurสพฤnic script}\label{qureanic-script}

In printed volumes of the Qurสพฤn, the spelling words is a little different from non-Qurสพฤnic Standard Arabic. The reasons for this are beyond the scope of this book. Here we'll just give a few examples and note that these differences are typically only found in printed volumes of the Qurสพฤn.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Standard Arabic & Qurสพฤnic Arabic \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุตููููุงุฉู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุตููููููฐุฉู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุณููููุงููุงุชู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุณููููููฐูู~ูฐุชู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ูฑุจููู ุฃูููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูููุคูููู} \\
\end{longtable}

\chapter{Nouns}\label{nouns}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-1}

A noun is a kind of word that is the name of something or someone.

Here are some examples of common nouns in Arabic:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Arabic word & Transcription & Definition \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูู} & \emph{rajul} & man \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} & \emph{kitฤb} & book \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุช} & \emph{bayt} & house \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฌูุฑูุฉ} & \emph{sอกhajarah} & tree \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุจูุฑ} & \emph{แนฃabr} & patience \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุช} & \emph{waqt} & time \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูุนูุงู} & \emph{แนญaษฤm} & food \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} & \emph{สพibn} & son \\
\end{longtable}

Note that the final letter in each word, above, does not have a vowel mark. This is because, the final vowel mark is actually variable, as we shall see later in this chapter.

When we discuss nouns outside of sentences we shall pronounce the looped \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} as a \emph{h}. Therefore,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฌูุฑูุฉ} \enquote{tree}, in isolation, is pronounced \emph{sอกhajarah}, not \emph{sอกhajarat}.

Some nouns begin with a connecting hamzah, for example: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} \emph{สพibn} \enquote{son}. When in the beginning of a sentence, the connecting hamzah will be pronounced with an \emph{i}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.

\section{Definiteness}\label{definiteness}

When talking about nouns it is necessary to introduce a topic called \emph{definiteness}.

A noun is \emph{definite} when the person or thing it refers to is known. For example, if you say, \enquote{The man arrived.} then the usage of the word \enquote{the} before \enquote{man} tells us that the man is known to us. Therefore the noun \enquote{man} is definite in this sentence.

Conversely, if we had said \enquote{A man arrived.} then the use of \enquote{a} before \enquote{man} tells us that the man is unknown to us. Therefore \enquote{man} is indefinite in this sentence.

\enquote{The} is called the \emph{definite article} and \enquote{a} is called the \emph{indefinite article}.

\subsection{Definite nouns in Arabic}\label{definite-nouns-in-arabic}

The definite article in Arabic is
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal}. It corresponds to the English definite article \enquote{the}.
In order to make a noun definite, we attach
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal}
to its beginning.

For example, the definite noun \enquote{the book} in Arabic is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจ} \emph{สพalkitฤb}.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal}
begins with a connecting hamzah; the hamzah will be pronounced only in the beginning of a sentence. And when it occurs in the beginning of a sentence, the hamzah is pronounced with a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} a-mark.

\subsubsection{Sun letters and moon letters}\label{sun-letters-and-moon-letters}

The noun \enquote{man} in Arabic is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูู} \emph{rajul}. To make this noun definite, we add \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal} to the beginning of the word. But instead of becoming \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฌูู} \emph{สพalrajul} the word becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู} \emph{สพarrajul}. The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} becomes silent and the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑ} gets doubled. This happens because the first letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑ} in the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูู} \emph{rajul} is from a group of letters called \enquote{sun letters}. For all nouns beginning with sun letters, when \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal} is put in the beginning, the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} becomes silent and the sun letter becomes doubled.

The rest of the letters in the alphabet are called \enquote{moon letters} and for words that begin with moon letters, the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} does not become silent and the moon letter does not become doubled. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉ} is a moon letter and we have already seen that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} \emph{kitฤb} \enquote{book} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจ} \emph{สพalkitฤb} \enquote{the book}.

The sun letters are \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช ุซ ุฏ ุฐ ุฑ ุฒ ุณ ุด ุต ุถ ุท ุธ ู ู}.\\
The moon letters are \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก ุจ ุฌ ุญ ุฎ ุน ุบ ู ู ฺฉ ู ู ู ู}.

The names \enquote{sun letters} and \enquote{moon letters} were given because of the Arabic words for \enquote{sun} and \enquote{moon} respectively. \enquote{The sun} in Arabic is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููููุณ} \emph{สพasอกhsอกhams} which begins with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุด} which causes the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} to be silent. \enquote{The moon} is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููููุฑ} \emph{สพalqamar} which begins with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} which does not cause the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} to be silent. Thus \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุด} represents the sun letters and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} represents the moon letters.

Here are some examples of words that begin with sun letters:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Definite noun
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูู} \emph{rajul} \enquote{man} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู} \emph{สพarrajul} \enquote{the man} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุงุฌูุฑ} \emph{tฤjir} \enquote{trader} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุชููุงุฌูุฑ} \emph{สพattฤjir} \enquote{the trader} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจูุฉ} \emph{luษbah} \enquote{toy} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุนูุจูุฉ} \emph{สพalluษbah} \enquote{the toy} \\
\end{longtable}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{The definite article \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal} with nouns with an initial connecting hamzah}{The definite article ูฑููู สพal with nouns with an initial connecting hamzah}}\label{the-definite-article-with-nouns-with-an-initial-connecting-hamzah}

If the definite article \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal} is with prefixed to nouns that have an initial connecting hamzah, then the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} shall no longer have an รธ-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. Instead it shall have an \emph{i}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููููฑุจูู}\\
\emph{สพali-bn}\\
\enquote{the son}

\subsection{Indefinite nouns in Arabic}\label{indefinite-nouns-in-arabic}

Arabic has no indefinite article corresponding to the English indefinite article \enquote{a}. In order to make a noun indefinite in Arabic, it is simply written or pronounced without the definite article
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal}.
For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} \emph{kitฤb} \enquote{a book}.

\subsection{Differences in definiteness between Arabic and English}\label{differences-in-definiteness-between-arabic-and-english}

The articles \enquote{a} and \enquote{the} are types of words called \emph{determiners}. Besides \enquote{a} and \enquote{the}, English has other determiners like \enquote{some}, \enquote{this}, \enquote{that}, etc. that can make a noun definite or indefinite.
For example:

\enquote{This man gave that boy some food.}

In the above sentence \enquote{man} and \enquote{boy} are definite, and \enquote{food} is indefinite.

English can also have definite or indefinite nouns without determiners. The definiteness of the noun is then determined by the meaning of the sentence. Consider, for example, the sentence:

\enquote{Time is valuable.}

Here, we are not talking about some indefinite amount of time, but rather the general concept of time, which is known to us. Therefore, the noun \enquote{time} here is definite.

Consider now the sentence:

\enquote{We don't have to leave just yet; we have time.}

Here, \enquote{time} has an indefinite meaning \enquote{{[}some{]} time}.

As opposed to this complicated situation in English, Arabic uses only the definite article
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal}
to make common nouns definite.
So when translating sentences from English to Arabic, you must first determine whether the noun is definite or not in English, and then use
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal}
when the noun is definite.

Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \enquote{This man gave that boy some food.}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    man: definite; Arabic: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู} \emph{สพarrujul}
  \item
    boy: definite; Arabic: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุบูููุงู} \emph{สพalgอกhulฤm}
  \item
    water: indefinite; Arabic: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูุนูุงู} \emph{แนญaษฤm}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \enquote{Time is valuable.}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    time: definite; Arabic: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููููุช} \emph{สพalwaqt}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \enquote{We don't have to leave just yet; we have time.}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    time: indefinite; Arabic: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุช} \emph{waqt}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\section{State}\label{state}

Nouns in Arabic can be in one of three \emph{states}. You may think of the grammatical states of nouns like the physical states of matter: solid, liquid, and gas. The same water can be in a solid ice state, or a liquid water state, or a gaseous water vapour state. Similarly, the same noun, in Arabic, may be in one of the three grammatical states:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  u-state: indicated by a \emph{u}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} (for definite nouns) and an \emph{un}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} (for indefinite nouns) on the final letter of the word.
\item
  a-state: indicated by an \emph{a}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} (for definite nouns) and an \emph{an}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} (for indefinite nouns) on the final letter of the word.
\item
  i-state: indicated by an \emph{i}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} (for definite nouns) and an \emph{in}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} (for indefinite nouns) on the final letter of the word.
\end{enumerate}

Here are the nouns \enquote{a book} and \enquote{the book} in their three states:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2979}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3830}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3191}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
State
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Indefinite \enquote{a book}
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Definite \enquote{the book}
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{kitฤbun} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{สพalkitฤbu} \\
a-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง} \emph{kitฤban} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{สพalkitฤba} \\
i-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{kitฤbin} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{สพalkitฤbi} \\
\end{longtable}

The choice of which state a noun is in depends on its function in a sentence. For example, if the noun is a subject of a sentence, it will usually be in the u-state. And if it is used adverbially, it will often be in the a-state. And if it occurs after a preposition, it will be in the i-state. We will learn more about putting nouns in their different states throughout this book.
Generally speaking, the u-state is the normal state. And there needs to be a reason to take the noun out of the u-state and into one of the other states.

\section{Grammatical gender}\label{grammatical-gender}

Some nouns designate animate beings like \enquote{man}, \enquote{woman}, \enquote{boy}, \enquote{girl}, \enquote{dog}, \enquote{cow}, etc.
Other nouns designate inanimate objects like \enquote{book}, \enquote{house}, \enquote{hand}, \enquote{tree}, \enquote{city}, \enquote{food}.

There are three grammatical genders in English:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  The masculine gender. This is used for nouns that designate male human beings and also some male animals. The pronouns used for the masculine gender are \enquote{he}, \enquote{him}, and \enquote{his}.
\item
  The feminine gender. This is used for nouns that designate female human beings, and also some female animals. The pronouns used for the feminine gender are \enquote{she} and \enquote{her}.
\item
  The neutral gender. This is used for nouns that designate inanimate objects and animals in general. The pronoun used for the neutral gender is \enquote{it}.
\end{enumerate}

In Arabic, there are only two grammatical genders: the masculine gender and the feminine gender.
All nouns in Arabic are either masculine or feminine in gender.
Nouns that designate male human beings are assigned the masculine grammatical gender. And
nouns that designate female human beings are assigned the feminine grammatical gender.
As for nouns that designate inanimate objects and animals, these, too, are assigned either a masculine or a feminine gender. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} \emph{kitฤb} \enquote{book} in Arabic is masculine. And \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฌูุฑูุฉ} \emph{sอกhajarah} \enquote{tree} in Arabic is feminine.
We shall discuss this in more detail below.

\subsection{Nouns that designate animate beings.}\label{nouns-that-designate-animate-beings.}

In Arabic, in terms of their form, nouns that designate animate beings are in three categories:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  There are separate nouns for the male and female animate being and the nouns match to each other.
\item
  There are separate nouns for the male and female animate being but the nouns are unrelated.
\item
  The same noun is used for both sexes.
\end{enumerate}

We will discuss each of these categories below.

\subsubsection{Matching nouns for male and female animate beings}\label{matching-nouns-for-male-and-female-animate-beings}

In Arabic for some nouns that designate animate beings, the nouns for both sexes match each other. Here are some examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Arabic word & Gender & Definition \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} \emph{สพibn} & masc. & son \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูููุฉ} \emph{สพibnah} & fem. & daughter \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููู} \emph{แนญifl} & masc. & child \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููููุฉ} \emph{แนญiflah} & fem. & (female) child \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููุณูุงู} \emph{สพinsฤn} & masc. & human being \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููุณูุงููุฉ} \emph{สพinsฤnah} & fem. & (female) human being \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฑู} \emph{แธฅurr} & masc. & free man \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฑููุฉ} \emph{แธฅurrah} & fem. & free woman \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูููุจ} \emph{kalb} & masc. & (male) dog \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูููุจูุฉ} \emph{kalbah} & fem. & (female) dog \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑู} \emph{hirr} & masc. & (male) cat \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑููุฉ} \emph{hirrah} & fem. & (female) cat \\
-- & -- & -- \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู} \emph{muษallim} & masc. & (male) teacher \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููุฉ} \emph{muษallimah} & fem. & (female) teacher \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูุงููุจ} \emph{แนญฤlib} & masc. & (male) student \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูุงููุจูุฉ} \emph{แนญฤlibah} & fem. & (female) student \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุงุญูุจ} \emph{แนฃฤแธฅib} & masc. & (male) companion \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุงุญูุจูุฉ} \emph{แนฃฤแธฅibah} & fem. & (female) companion \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุฏููู} \emph{แนฃadฤซq} & masc. & (male) friend \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุฏููููุฉ} \emph{แนฃadฤซqah} & fem. & (female) friend \\
\end{longtable}

In each of the words in the table above, the feminine noun is basically the same as the masculine noun but with the addition of a looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} at the end. For example,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููู} \emph{แนญifl} (masc.) is a child, and its feminine is
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููููุฉ} \emph{แนญiflah} (fem.).

As a matter of fact, the looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} is called a feminine marker for singular nouns. There are a couple of other, less common, feminine markers besides looped tฤสพ that we will learn them later, if Allฤh wills.

Note that the vowel-mark before the looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} is always an \emph{a}-mark.

Note also that we have divided the table above into two groups. The first group contains nouns that have a primitive meaning, without a primarily adjectival or verbal quality in the meaning, for example \enquote{human} \enquote{cat}, etc.
The second group contains nouns that have an adjectival or verbal quality. For example, a \enquote{teacher} is someone who teaches. A \enquote{friend} is someone who is friendly. And so on.

This grouping will become important when, if Allฤh wills, you study morphology, and the classification of nouns into primitive and derived nouns. But we can give a short preview here: Basically, for the second group (the one that has adjectival or verbal meanings), the formation of the feminine noun by adding a feminine marker (like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}) to the masculine noun is normal and expected. Whereas, for the first group (the one that refers to primitive nouns without a verbal or adjectival meaning), the fact that the feminine and masuline nouns match each other and differ only by the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} is something that, although somewhat common, is more of a coincidence.

Another noteworthy point is that, for many primitive nouns (the first group), only one of the masculine/feminine pair may be used to refer to beings of either sex. What we mean by this is that, for example,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูููุจ} \emph{kalb}, while remaining a masculine noun, can be used to refer to both \enquote{a (male) dog} and \enquote{a (female) dog}, especially if the animal's physical gender is not particularly important to what is being said.
And \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูููุจูุฉ} \emph{kalbah} (fem.) \enquote{a female dog} is typically only used when it is needed to specify the gender of the animal.
Conversely, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑููุฉ} \emph{hirrah} \enquote{a (female) cat} may be used to refer to cat of either physical gender, especially if it is not obvious whether it is a male or female cat.

This preference of the noun of one gender to refer to beings of either physical gender is arbitrary and case-by-case. For example,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููู} \emph{แนญifl} (masc.) is commonly used to say \enquote{a child}, regardless of whether the child is a boy or a girl. But \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููููุฉ} \emph{แนญiflah} is fairly common too specifically for \enquote{a female child}.

As another example, the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููุณูุงููุฉ} \emph{สพinsฤnah} (fem.) \enquote{a female human being} is rarely used at all. Instead, the word
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููุณูุงู} \emph{สพinsฤn}, while remaining a masculine noun, is almost always used to refer to \enquote{a human being} in general, regardless of actual gender.

On the other hand,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} \emph{สพibn} \enquote{son} and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูููุฉ} \emph{สพibnah} \enquote{daughter}
are only ever used for their respective gender. So
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} \emph{สพibn} (masc.) \enquote{a son} is never used to mean \enquote{a daughter}.
And \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูููุฉ} \emph{สพibnah} (fem.) \enquote{a daughter} is never used to mean \enquote{a son}.

There aren't very many of such nouns. And we have covered a few of the common ones above. A good dictionary will also provide guidance in this regard.

As for the second group of words (the one that has adjectival or verbal meanings), they are typically only ever used for their respective gender. So, for example,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู} \emph{muษallim} (masc.) is only used for \enquote{a (male) teacher}. And
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููุฉ} \emph{muษallimah} (fem.) is only used for \enquote{a (female) teacher}.

\subsubsection{Unrelated nouns for male and female animate beings}\label{unrelated-nouns-for-male-and-female-animate-beings}

For other nouns that designate animate beings, the nouns for the male and female sexes are completely unrelated. Here are some examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Arabic word & Gender & Definition \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจ} \emph{สพab} & masc. & father \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} \emph{สพumm} & fem. & mother \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูููุงู} \emph{gอกhulฤm} & masc. & boy \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉ} \emph{jฤriyah} & fem. & girl \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏ} \emph{ษabd} & masc. & male slave \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฉ} \emph{สพamah} & fem. & female slave \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูุฏ} \emph{สพasad} & masc. & lion \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูููุฉ} \emph{labwah} & fem. & lioness \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูููุฑ} \emph{tอกhawr} & masc. & bull \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุฑูุฉ} \emph{baqarah} & fem. & cow \\
\end{longtable}

Even in these nouns you can see that the feminine noun usually ends with a looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} feminine marker. There are only a few commonly used feminine nouns that don't end with a feminine marker like looped tฤสพ. \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} \emph{สพummun} \enquote{mother} is one of these exceptions.

\subsubsection{Using the same noun for both sexes}\label{using-the-same-noun-for-both-sexes}

There are other nouns for animate beings where the same word is used for both sexes. The word itself will still be either grammatically masculine or feminine. Here are some examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Arabic word & Gender & Definition \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฎูุต} \emph{sอกhakอกhแนฃ} & masc. & person \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุณ} \emph{nafs} & fem. & self \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุฏููู} \emph{ษaduww} & masc. & enemy \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููููุงู} \emph{แธฅayawฤn} & masc. & animal \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูุงุฆูุฑ} \emph{แนญฤสพir} & masc. & bird \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฏ} \emph{qird} & masc. & monkey \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุงููุฉ} \emph{แธฅamฤmah} & fem. & dove \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููููุฉ} \emph{namlah} & fem. & ant \\
\end{longtable}

So for example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฏ} \emph{qirdun} \enquote{monkey} is grammatically masculine but it will be used for both a male and a female monkey.
Similarly, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฎูุต} \emph{sอกhakอกhแนฃ} is a masculine noun meaning \enquote{person}. While remaining grammatically masculine, it can be used to refer to persons of male or female persons.

Note also that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุณ} \emph{nafsun} \enquote{self} is a feminine noun but it does not end in a looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}. It is one of the small number of feminine nouns that don't have a female marker, like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} \emph{สพummun} (fem.) \enquote{mother}.

\subsection{Nouns that designate inanimate objects}\label{nouns-that-designate-inanimate-objects}

As mentioned earlier, nouns that designate inanimate objects are assigned a fixed grammatical gender. There is usually no discernable reason why some are assigned a masculine gender while others are assigned a feminine gender.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Arabic word & Gender & Definition \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} \emph{kitฤb} & masc. & book \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุช} \emph{bayt} & masc. & house \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู} \emph{qalam} & masc. & pen \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูุนูุงู} \emph{แนญaษฤm} & masc. & food \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุก} \emph{mฤสพ} & masc. & water \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉ} \emph{madrasah} & fem. & school \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏููููุฉ} \emph{madฤซnah} & fem. & city \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุฑูููุฉ} \emph{gอกhurfah} & fem. & room \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฌูุฑูุฉ} \emph{sอกhajarah} & fem. & tree \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุณ} \emph{sอกhams} & fem. & sun \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุฑ} \emph{qamar} & masc. & moon \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููู} \emph{ษilm} & masc. & knowledge \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููุฉ} \emph{quwwah} & fem. & strength \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุงุฉ} \emph{แธฅayฤh} & fem. & life \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุช} \emph{mawt} & masc. & death \\
\end{longtable}

In these nouns as well, we note that feminine nouns usually end with the feminine marker looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}.
But here too, we find another exception:
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุณู} \emph{sอกhamsun} \enquote{sun} which is feminine but does not end with a feminine marker.
These exceptions are not very many and, if Allฤh wills, we will not find it hard to memorize them.

There is a sub-group of nouns that designate inanimate objects, but can also be used to refer to animate beings. Here are a couple of examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Arabic word & Gender & Definition \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑููููููุฉ} \emph{rahฤซnah} & fem. & pledge \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุถูู} \emph{ษuแธw} & masc. & member \\
\end{longtable}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑููููููุฉ} \emph{rahฤซnah} is a feminine noun meaning \enquote{pledge}. For inanimate objects it refers to something that is held as a security or a collateral. With its animate meaning, it is used to refer to a human hostage.

Similarly,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุถูู} \emph{ษuแธw} is a masculine noun meaning \enquote{member}. For inanimate objects it refers to a limb which is the member of a body. With its animate meaning it refers to a person who is a member of a professional organization.
