That is why we used the variant \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โููู} \emph{-ya} when it was followed by a connecting hamzah (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููููููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}), and the variant \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูู} \emph{-ฤซ} when it was not followed by a connecting hamzah (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููููู ุฑูุฌููู.}).

  This preference is not mandatory. So it is allowed for \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูู} \emph{-ฤซ} to be used when followed by a connecting hamzah. When this happens, the long vowel \emph{-ฤซ} will be shortened to \emph{-i} in connecting it to the next word, although the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูู} is retained in writing. For example,

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
  \emph{saสพalani -rrajul.}\\
  \enquote{The man asked me.}
\end{itemize}

\section{Multiple verbs for one doer}\label{multiple-verbs-for-one-doer}

In this section we will use the verbs:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Root & Completed-action form 1 verb & Meaning \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฏุฎูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุฎููู} \emph{dakอกhala} & \enquote{entered} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฎุฑุฌยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุฑูุฌู} \emph{kอกharaja} & \enquote{exited} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุกฺฉูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู} \emph{สพakala} & \enquote{ate} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุดุฑุจยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฑูุจู} \emph{sอกhariba} & \enquote{drank} \\
\end{longtable}

Consider, now, the sentence:

\enquote{I entered the room, ate, drank, and exited.}

The doer in this sentence is the pronoun \enquote{I}. This same doer is doing the action of multiple verbs:
\enquote{entered}, \enquote{ate}, \enquote{drank}, and \enquote{exited}.
When we try to express this sentence in Arabic we must remember that every verb shall have its own doer, and that the doer shall occur after it in sentence word order. So we will say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุฎูููุชู ูฑููุบูุฑูููุฉู ููุฃูฺฉูููุชู ููุดูุฑูุจูุชู ููุฎูุฑูุฌูุชู.}\\
\emph{dakอกhaltu -lgอกhurfata faสพakaltu fasอกharibtu fakอกharajt.}\\
\enquote{I entered the room and then I ate and then I drank and then I exited.}

Note also, that we need to replace the commas by connecting particles like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{wa-} \enquote{and}, or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{fa-} \enquote{so}/\enquote{and then}, etc. We chose
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{fa-} which implies consequence or subsequence between the individual events.

Let's now try this sentence with a doer noun instead of a doer pronoun:

\enquote{The girl entered the room, ate, drank, and exited.}

Here is our translation:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุฎูููุชู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ูฑููุบูุฑูููุฉู ููุฃูฺฉูููุชู ููุดูุฑูุจูุชู ููุฎูุฑูุฌูุชู.}\\
\emph{dakอกhalati -ljฤriyatu -lgอกhurfata faสพakalat fasอกharibat fakอกharajat.}\\
\enquote{The girl entered the room and then she ate and then she drank and then she exited.}

Each verb again has its own doer, which is coming after the verb in sentence word order. The doer of the first verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุฎููู} \emph{dakอกhala} \enquote{entered} is the noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู} \emph{สพaljฤriyatu} \enquote{the girl}.
The subsequent verbs all have doers too but they are the invisible doer pronouns for the singular feminine absentee participant. That is why we don't write them. Note also that every verb has the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชู} of femininity attached to it to indicate its singular feminine absentee doer.

\section{Order of words in a sentence}\label{order-of-words-in-a-sentence}

\subsection{Changing the order of words for emphasis}\label{past-verbs-order-of-words}

In Arabic, the doer always follows the verb. So the normal order of a sentence is verb-doer-doee. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง.}\\
\emph{kataba -rrajulu kitฤbฤ.}\\
\enquote{The man wrote a book.}

However, we will often come across sentences like:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู ฺฉูุชูุจู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง.}\\
\emph{สพarrajulu kataba kitฤbฤ.}

It may appear as if \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู} \emph{arrajulu} is the doer and it is coming before the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู} \emph{kataba}. But actually, this is not the case. As a matter of fact, this sentence is basically a subject-information sentence.

Here \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู} \emph{arrajulu} \enquote{the man} is the subject of the sentence, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง} \emph{kataba kitฤban} \enquote{he wrote a book}, itself a verbal sentence with an invisible doer pronoun, is the information about the subject. So the translation of the sentence is technically:

\enquote{The man, he wrote a book.}

However, this is an awkward translation so we will usually translate it as \enquote{The man wrote a book.}

The question arises: if both sentences above have the same translation, then would we say \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู ฺฉูุชูุจู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง.} \emph{สพarrajulu kataba kitฤban.} instead of the more normal \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง.} \emph{kataba -rrajulu kitฤban.}? The answer is that this change in the sentence's word order is done in order to give more emphasis to the doer, as if to say:

\enquote{\emph{The man} wrote a book.}

So in Arabic, the order of words is generally more flexible than in English and this is often used to give emphasis to certain words.

\subsection{Verbs pull definite nouns towards them}\label{verbs-pull-definite-nouns-towards-them}

When a verb has a doer noun and a doee noun, the normal order of words in a sentence is: verb, doer noun, doee noun. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู.}\\
\emph{kataba -rrajulu -lkitฤba.}\\
\enquote{The man wrote the book.}

There is a tendency, in Arabic, for verbs to \emph{pull} definite nouns towards them. This means that if there are any indefinite nouns, they have a tendency to get pushed father away. So, for example,
if a verb's doer is an indefinite noun and the doee is a definite noun, the doee will often (but not always) precede the doer. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ุฑูุฌููู.}\\
\emph{kataba -lkitฤba rajul.}\\
\enquote{A man wrote the book.}

The vowel-marks at the end of the nouns, and context, will tell us which is the doer and which is the doee. In this particular example, it was optional, and not mandatory to make the definite doee precede the doer in sentence word order. So we could have also said, instead:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ุฑูุฌููู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู.}\\
\emph{kataba rajuluni -lkitฤb.}\\
\enquote{A man wrote the book.}

Now let's take a look at sentences with pronouns. Remember that pronouns are a category of nouns, and also (from section~\ref{definiteness-of-pronouns}) that they are definite nouns. In fact they are stronger in definiteness than words that are made definite using \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}. This because if when we say \enquote{The man wrote the book.} instead of \enquote{A man wrote the book.}, we assume that everyone knows which man we are referring to. Now if we replace \enquote{the man} with the pronoun \enquote{he}: \enquote{He wrote the book.}, then this assumption becomes stronger. \enquote{He} is, in a sense, more definite than \enquote{the man.}.

So now, when the direct doee noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{สพalkitฤba} \enquote{the book} is replaced with the pronoun \enquote{it}, the doee pronoun must be attached to the verb, and then the doer noun follows the doee pronoun:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\emph{katabahu -rrajulu.}\\
\enquote{The man wrote it.}

This can be seen as a mandatory case of the verb pulling the definite noun toward it.

Now, consider a sentence with an indirect doee. Again, the normal order of words in a sentence is verb, doer noun, preposition, doee noun. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุฅูููู ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -lgอกhulฤmu สพila -lmadrasah.}\\
\enquote{The boy went to the school.}

Now, if we replace the indirect doee noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู.} \emph{สพalmadrasati} \enquote{the school} with the pronoun \enquote{it}, the indirect doee pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{-hฤ} \enquote{it} is attached, not to the verb, but to the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููฐ} \emph{สพilฤ} thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููููุง} \emph{สพilayhฤ} \enquote{to it}. So it possible to preserve the original order of words in the sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุฅูููููููุง.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -lgอกhulฤmu สพilayhฤ.}\\
\enquote{The boy went to it.}

While the above sentence is correct, it is in fact more common to place the preposition and doee pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููููุง} \emph{สพilayhฤ} \enquote{to it} right after the verb, and before the doer noun, thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ุฅูููููููุง ูฑููุบูููุงูู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba สพilayha -lgอกhulฤmu.}\\
\enquote{The boy went to it.}

This is because the pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{-hฤ} \enquote{it} is stronger in definiteness than \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุบูููุงู} \emph{สพalgอกhulฤm} \enquote{the boy}. So the verb has a stronger pull towards it.

This ordering of words due to the attractive pull of the verb is largely learned by experience. The more you read Arabic, the better feel you will get for it, if Allฤh wills.

\section{Negating completed-action verbs}\label{negating-completed-action-verbs}

In order to negate a completed-action verb, the particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{mฤ} is placed before it. This gives the meaning of the action of the verb did not get, or has not got, done. So for example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\emph{mฤ dอกhahaba -rrajulu.}\\
\enquote{The man did not go.} or,\\
\enquote{The man has not gone.}

\section{\texorpdfstring{The particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏู} \emph{qad}}{The particle ููุฏู qad}}\label{the-particle-ux642ux62f-qad}

The particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏู} \emph{qad}, when placed before a completed-action verb emphasizes that the action of the verb has already or definitely occured.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏู ุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\emph{qad dอกhahaba -rrajulu.}\\
\enquote{The man has already gone.} or,\\
\enquote{The man did go.}

\section{Separating doee pronouns from the verb}\label{separating-doee-pronouns-from-the-verb}

FIXME: move to imperfect verb chapter

We have mentioned that doee pronouns are attached to the verb. Sometimes there is a need to separate the doee pronoun from the verb. When separating the doee pronoun from the verb, it is instead attached to the prefix \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุง} \emph{สพiyyฤ}. So then we get the following doee pronouns:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.7000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Person
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Doee pronoun
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Absent person (masc.) \enquote{him} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุงูู} \emph{สพiyyฤhu} \\
Absent person (fem.) \enquote{her} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุงููุง} \emph{สพiyyฤhฤ} \\
Addressed person (masc.) \enquote{you\textsubscript{masc.}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุงฺฉู} \emph{สพiyyฤka} \\
Addressed person (fem.) \enquote{you\textsubscript{fem.}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุงฺฉู} \emph{สพiyyฤki} \\
Speaking person (masc. and fem.) \enquote{me} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุงูู} \emph{สพiyyฤya} \\
\end{longtable}

Note that for the speaking person \enquote{me}, there is no intervening \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} between the prefix \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุง} \emph{สพiyyฤ} and the doee pronoun. Note also that only \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{-ya} is allowed to be attached to the prefix \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุง} \emph{สพiyyฤ}. This is because \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูู} \emph{-ฤซ} is not permitted to be used with words that end in a long vowel (\emph{-ฤ}, \emph{-ฤซ}, or \emph{-ลซ}) or a semi-vowel (\emph{-ay} or \emph{-aw}). And the prefix \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุง} \emph{สพiyyฤ} ends with the long-vowel \emph{ฤ}.

But we may ask why is there a need to separate the doee pronoun from the verb? This can occur for a couple of reasons:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\item
  If there are multiple doee pronouns, only one of them can be attached to the verb. Example,

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจูุชูููู ููุฅููููุงูู.}\\
  \emph{แธarabatnฤซ wa สพiyyฤhu.}\\
  \enquote{She hit me and him.}
\item
  If the doee is placed before the verb for emphasis. Example,

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุงูู ุถูุฑูุจูุชู.}\\
  \emph{สพiyyฤya แธarabat.}\\
  \enquote{She hit \emph{me}.}
\end{enumerate}

\section{TODO}\label{todo}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Multiple verb doers: Copy over from sound plurals and rework.
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฒ ุชุฃููุซ ุงููุนู ููุฌูุจู}
\end{enumerate}

\chapter{Adjectival nouns and descriptive noun phrases}\label{adjectival-nouns-and-descriptive-noun-phrases}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-5}

So far we have studied common nouns like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูู} \emph{rajul} \enquote{a man} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุช} \emph{bayt} \enquote{a house}.

In this chapter we will study \emph{adjectival nouns}. Adjectival nouns are a class of nouns that don't denote objects. Rather they describe some quality of an object.

\section{Adjectives in English}\label{adjectives-in-english}

In English we usually use adjectives to describe nouns. For example, the word \enquote{big} is an adjective. It can be used in a couple of different ways:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  It can be used to describe a noun in an descriptive noun-phrase. For example:

  \enquote{a big car}
\item
  The adjective \enquote{big} can also be used as the information of a sentence, describing the subject noun. For example:

  \enquote{The car is big.}
\end{enumerate}

But the adjective \enquote{big} cannot be used by itself as a noun, for example, as the subject of a sentence. So we can't say:

\(\times\) \enquote{The big is fast.}

We would have to say something like:

\enquote{The big car is fast.}

instead.

\section{\texorpdfstring{Terminology: the \emph{describer} and the \emph{describee}}{Terminology: the describer and the describee}}\label{terminology-the-describer-and-the-describee}

We take this opportunity to introduce some grammatical terminology. The descriptive noun-phrase \enquote{a big car} consists of two parts:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  The adjective \enquote{big}. It is describing the car. We will call it the \emph{describer} in the noun-phrase.
\item
  The common noun \enquote{a car}: It is being described by the describer. We will call it the \emph{describee}.
\end{enumerate}

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-7-1.pdf}

We will reserve this terminology of \emph{describer} and \emph{describee} only for the noun and adjective in an descriptive noun-phrase. So we won't use this terminology for the sentence: \enquote{The car is big.}

Instead, here we will continue to use the existing terminology of \emph{subject} and \emph{information}. The definite noun \enquote{the car} is the subject of this sentence, and the adjective \enquote{big} is the information.

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-8-1.pdf}

\section{Adjectival nouns in English}\label{adjectival-nouns-in-english}

Consider the English word \enquote{antique}. It is what we will call a \emph{adjectival noun}.

It can be used just like an adjective to describe a noun as part of a noun-phrase. For example:

\enquote{The antique table is expensive.}

In the above sentence the adjective \enquote{antique} is a describer and is describing the noun \enquote{table}.

It can also be used as the information of a sentence, just like an adjective. For example:

\enquote{The table is antique.}

But what makes it different from an normal adjective is that it can also be used by itself as a noun. For example:

\enquote{The antique is expensive.}

Here \enquote{the antique} could refer to any entity that can be described by the quality of being old and valuable. The adjectival noun does not require any other noun in this sentence and can stand on its own as the subject of the sentence.

Adjectival nouns are rare in English. Instead, adjectives are usually used when we want to describe a noun.

\section{Adjectival nouns in Arabic and genderizability}\label{adjectival-nouns-in-arabic-and-genderizability}

Arabic does not have adjectives. It only has adjectival nouns.

The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซr} is an example of an indefinite adjectival noun in Arabic. It describes the quality of being \enquote{small} or \enquote{little}. It can be used to denote any person, animal, or things that can be described as being small. Technically we could translate it as \enquote{a little one\textsubscript{m}} or \enquote{a small one\textsubscript{m}}.

Being a noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซr}, like all other nouns in Arabic, will have a grammatical gender. Since it does not end with a feminine marker like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, we can state that
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซr} is a masculine noun.

Adjectival nouns, typically, are genderizable. This means that we can feminize
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซr} (masc.) to get the feminine noun.
We will feminize
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซr} (masc.) with the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to get the feminine adjectival noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑูุฉ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซrah} (fem.) \enquote{a little one\textsubscript{f}}.

Generally, the dictionary will typically only supply the masculine adjectival noun.
And we are expected to know how to feminize it to get the feminine adjectival noun.

As opposed to adjectival nouns, common nouns are not genderizable. So, for example, if we know that the noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูููุงู} \emph{gอกhulฤm} \enquote{a boy} exists, we cannot assume that we can feminize it, by using the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, for example, getting: \(\times\) \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูููุงููุฉ} \emph{gอกhulฤmah}. This would be a misguided attempt to obtain the meaning for \enquote{a girl} in Standard Arabic. Instead, we have to look up the Arabic word for \enquote{a girl} in the dictionary separately, and we find that it is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉ} \emph{jฤriyah}.

Many times times, a masculine/feminine common noun pair will exist, that differ only by the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}. For example:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} \emph{สพibn} \enquote{a son} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูููุฉ} \emph{สพibnah} \enquote{a daughter}.
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู} \emph{muษallim} \enquote{a teacher\textsubscript{m}} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููุฉ} \emph{muษallimah} \enquote{a teacher\textsubscript{f}}
\end{itemize}

This does not indicate that the common noun is genderizable. Rather, when the common noun masc./fem. pair has a meaning that is derived from a verb or an adjective (like
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููุฉ}),
then the masculine/feminine pair are co-derived as separate non-genderizable words. We will discuss this in more detail in later chapters, if Allฤh wills.

And when the common noun masc./fem. pair has a primitive (non-verbal and non-adjectival) meaning, (like
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูููุฉ}),
then this is only a coincidence. We alluded to this in section~\ref{related-nouns-for-male-and-female-animate-beings}.

\subsection{Examples of Arabic adjectival nouns}\label{examples-of-arabic-adjectival-nouns}

Here are some examples of Arabic adjectival nouns that we will use in this chapter.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Arabic adjectival noun & Meaning \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑ} \emph{kabฤซr} & a big one \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซr} & a small one \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทููููุจ} \emph{แนญayyib} & a good one \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏููู} \emph{qadฤซm} & an old one \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุฏููุฏ} \emph{jadฤซd} & a new one \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทููููู} \emph{แนญawฤซl} & a long/tall one \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุณูุน} \emph{wฤsiษ} & a wide one \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุฑูุจููู} \emph{ษarabiyy} & an Arab \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุดููููุฑ} \emph{masอกhยทhลซr} & a famous one \\
\end{longtable}

\section{The describer and the describee in descriptive noun-phrases}\label{the-describer-and-the-describee-in-descriptive-noun-phrases}

Let's learn how descriptive noun-phrases are formed in Arabic.

We learned in section~\ref{terminology-the-describer-and-the-describee}
above that descriptive noun-phrases consist of a describer and a describee.

In English descriptive noun-phrases, like \enquote{the small house}, the adjective describer (\enquote{small}) comes before the describee (\enquote{house}). Also, only one definite article (\enquote{the}) is used before the entire noun-phrase.

Here is the equivalent Arabic descriptive noun-phrase:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-9-1.pdf}

Note the following:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The adjectival noun describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุบููุฑ} \emph{สพaแนฃแนฃagอกhฤซr} \enquote{the small one\textsubscript{m}} comes after the describee \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุช} \emph{สพalbayt} \enquote{the house}.
\item
  Both the adjectival noun describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุบููุฑ} \emph{สพaแนฃแนฃagอกhฤซr} \enquote{the small one\textsubscript{m}} and the describee \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุช} \emph{สพalbayt} \enquote{the house} get the definite article \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \enquote{the}.
\item
  The adjectival noun describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุบููุฑ} \emph{สพaแนฃแนฃagอกhฤซr} \enquote{the small one\textsubscript{m}} is genderized to match the describee \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุช} \emph{สพalbayt} \enquote{the house} in gender.
\item
  The adjectival noun describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุบููุฑ} \emph{สพaแนฃแนฃagอกhฤซr} \enquote{the small one\textsubscript{m}} matches the describee \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุช} \emph{สพalbayt} \enquote{the house} in state. In this example, they were both in the u-state but we will see examples in the other states as well.
\item
  The word-for-word equivalence of the above descriptive noun-phrase is \enquote{the small-one house} but we will usually give the more natural translation: \enquote{the small house}
\end{itemize}

Let's try another example: let's try to translate the sentence: \enquote{The little girl took a new book from the good mother.}

Here is the sentence in Arabic:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐูุชู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ูฑูุตููุบููุฑูุฉู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง ุฌูุฏููุฏูุง ูููู ูฑููุฃูููู ูฑูุทูููููุจูุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพakอกhadอกhati -ljฤriyatu -แนฃแนฃagอกhฤซratu kitaban jadฤซdan mina -lสพummi -แนญแนญayyibah.}\\
\enquote{The little girl took a new book from the good mother.}

This sentence has three descriptive noun-phrases. We will analyze each one individually:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ูฑูุตููุบููุฑูุฉู}\\
  \emph{สพaljฤriyatu -แนฃแนฃagอกhฤซratu}\\
  \enquote{the little girl}

  In this phrase the definite feminine noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู} \emph{สพaljฤriyatu} is the doer of the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐู} \emph{สพakอกhadอกha} \enquote{took}. Therefore it is in the u-state. It is also the describee in the descriptive noun-phrase. Its describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุบููุฑูุฉู} \emph{สพaแนฃแนฃagอกhฤซratu} follows the describee and is made to match the describee in state (u-state), gender (feminine), and definiteness (definite).
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง ุฌูุฏููุฏูุง}\\
  \emph{kitฤban jadฤซdan}\\
  \enquote{a new book}

  In this phrase the indefinite masculine noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง} \emph{kitฤban} is the doee of the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐู} \emph{สพakอกhadอกha} \enquote{took}. Therefore it is in the a-state. It is also the describee in the descriptive noun-phrase. Its describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุฏููุฏูุง} \emph{jadฤซdan} follows the describee and is made to match the describee in state (a-state), gender (masculine), and definiteness (indefinite).
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃูููู ูฑูุทูููููุจูุฉู}\\
  \emph{สพalสพummi -แนญแนญayyibati}\\
  \enquote{the good mother}

  In this phrase the definite feminine noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฃูููู} \emph{สพalสพummi} is following the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{min} \enquote{from}. Therefore it is in the i-state. It is also the describee in the descriptive noun-phrase. Its describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุทููููุจูุฉู} \emph{สพaแนญแนญayyibati} follows the describee and is made to match the describee in state (i-state), gender (feminine), and definiteness (definite).

  Note carefully that the describer matches the describee in gender, not necessarily in having the same \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} ending. The feminine adjectival noun describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุทููููุจูุฉ} \emph{สพaแนญแนญayyibah} is still formed using the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, despite the feminine describee \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃููู} not having the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} feminine marker.
\end{enumerate}

Sometimes, a common noun of one gender is used to refer to persons of either gender. For example:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  the noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฎูุต} \emph{sอกhakอกhแนฃ} is itself a masculine noun but it may be used to refer to both male and female persons.
\end{itemize}

If such a noun is a describee, then we will prefer to match the describer to the grammatical gender of the noun, not the physical gender of the person it is referring to. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ุดูุฎูุตู ุทููููุจู.}\\
\emph{สพaljฤriyatu sอกhakอกhแนฃun แนญayyib.}\\
\enquote{The girl is a good person.}

See how we preferred to use the masculine adjectival noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทููููุจ} \emph{แนญayyib} instead of using the feminine \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทููููุจูุฉ} \emph{แนญayyibah}.

\section{Adjectival nouns as the information of a sentence}\label{adjectival-nouns-as-the-information-of-a-sentence}

\subsection{Indefinite adjectival noun}\label{indefinite-adjectival-noun}

Let's see how to use Arabic adjectival nouns as the information of a sentence.

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-10-1.pdf}

In the above sentence, the indefinite adjectival noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซr} \enquote{a small one}
is used as the information of a sentence. Its indefiniteness and u-state is indicated by the \emph{un}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} on its end.

When an adjectival noun is the information of a sentence, then it shall be genderized to match the gender of the subject noun. The subject noun in this case (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุช}) is masculine. Therefore, the masculine adjectival noun (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ}) is chosen.

Technically, the translation of this sentence is \enquote{The house is a small one.}
However, because Arabic has only adjectival nouns and not adjectives, it is how we can express the English sentence \enquote{The house is small.} Therefore we can also translate it into English as such.

Now let's try a sentence with a feminine subject:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ุตูุบููุฑูุฉ.}\\
\emph{สพaljฤriyatu แนฃagอกhฤซrah}\\
\enquote{The girl is a little one\textsubscript{f}.} = \enquote{The girl is little.}

In the above example the subject (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉ} \enquote{the girl}) was feminine. Therefore, we feminized the masculine adjectival noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซr} with the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to get the feminine adjectival noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑูุฉ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซrah} \enquote{a little one\textsubscript{f}}
and used the feminine adjectival noun in the sentence.

\subsection{Definite adjectival noun}\label{definite-adjectival-noun}

Let's see if a definite adjectival noun can be used in the information. For example, we would like to say \enquote{The old tree is the big one.}

The subject of the sentence is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุฌูุฑูุฉู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู} \emph{สพasอกhsอกhajaratu -lqadฤซmuiatu} \enquote{the old tree}.
And the information is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููฺฉูุจููุฑูุฉู} \emph{สพalkabฤซratu} \enquote{the big one}. When we put the two together we get:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุฌูุฑูุฉู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู ูฑููฺฉูุจููุฑูุฉู}\\
\emph{สพasอกhsอกhajaratu -lqadฤซmatu -lkabฤซratu}

The problem is that the above could also be interpreted as one phrase \enquote{the big old tree}, and not as the complete sentence \enquote{The old tree is the big one.} This is the same problem that we highlighted in section~\ref{chap-smp-sent-sec-def-info}.

The solution, too, is the same. We insert a detached pronoun, that matches the gender of the subject, between the subject and the information. So in order to get our intended meaning, we will say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุฌูุฑูุฉู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู ูููู ูฑููฺฉูุจููุฑูุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพasอกhsอกhajaratu -lqadฤซmatu hiya -lkabฤซratu.}\\
\enquote{The old tree is the big one.}

\section{Adjectival nouns used without a described noun}\label{adjectival-nouns-used-without-a-described-noun}

We have mentioned that adjectival nouns are just like other nouns that we have learned so far, in that they have gender, state, and definiteness. Can we then use
an adjectival noun by itself and not when it is describing another noun?

The answer is yes, we can. So for example, you can say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฑูุจู ูฑูุตููุบููุฑู ุญููููุจูุง.}\\
\emph{sอกhariba -แนฃแนฃagอกhฤซru แธฅalฤซbฤ.}\\
\enquote{The little one drank some milk.}

The above is a correct sentence. But, by itself, it is not very clear. What do we mean by \enquote{the little one}? Is it a little boy, or a little cat, or something else? So, context would be needed to know what exactly is being denoted by the adjectival noun when it is used by itself independently.

Here is the same sentence again, but this time with some clarifying context.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููููุชู ูฑููุฃูููู ูฑูุตููุบููุฑู. ููุดูุฑูุจู ูฑูุตููุบููุฑู ุญููููุจูุง.}\\
\emph{แธฅamalati -lสพummu -แนฃแนฃagอกhฤซra. wasอกhariba -แนฃแนฃagอกhฤซru แธฅalฤซbฤ.}\\
\enquote{The mother carried the little one. And the little one drank some milk.}

So now we can tell that what is meant by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุตููุบููุฑ} \emph{สพaแนฃแนฃagอกhฤซr} \enquote{the little one} here is \enquote{the baby}.

\section{Adjectival nouns re-used as common nouns}\label{adjectival-nouns-re-used-as-common-nouns}

Sometimes, an adjectival noun, through much usage, acquires the meaning of a common noun. It then gets listed with this meaning in the dictionary. We actually just saw an example above. The adjectival noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซr} \enquote{a little one} is commonly used to mean \enquote{a baby}. Of course, context would be needed to know whether, in a particular sentence, it has its common noun meaning: \enquote{a baby}, or its general adjectival noun meaning: \enquote{a little one}.

The opposite of
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซr} \enquote{a little one}
is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑ} \emph{kabฤซr} \enquote{a big one}. It too has acquired the common noun meaning of \enquote{an elder person}. Here is an example of its usage:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏููู ูฑููฺฉูุจููุฑู ููููุนูุธู ูฑููุบูููุงูู.}\\
\emph{qadima -lkabฤซru wawaษadฬฃอกha -lgอกhulฤma.}\\
\enquote{The elder arrived and admonished the boy.}

When an adjectival noun gets re-used as a common noun, it loses its genderizability.
For example, the feminine adjectival noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูููุฉ} \emph{แธฅasanah} (fem.) \enquote{a good one} is re-used as a common noun meaning \enquote{a good deed}. So we can use it in a sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุตููููุงูู ุญูุณูููุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพaแนฃแนฃiyฤmu แธฅasanah.}\\
\enquote{Fasting is a good deed.}

The subject in this sentence is the masculine noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููููุงู} \emph{สพaแนฃแนฃiyฤm} \enquote{fasting}.
And the information is the feminine noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูููุฉ} \emph{แธฅasanah} \enquote{a good deed}.
Note that the information does not match the subject in gender. This is because it lost its genderizability since it is no longer acting as an adjectival noun \enquote{a good one\textsubscript{f}}, but rather as the common noun \enquote{a good deed}.

What if we have the sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุฏูููุฉู ุญูุณูููุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพaแนฃแนฃadaqatu แธฅasanah.}

The feminine gender of the subject
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุฏูููุฉ.}
\emph{สพaแนฃแนฃadaqah} \enquote{charity} now matches the gender of the information
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูููุฉ} \emph{แธฅasanah}.
So now, technically, the information could be the adjectival noun, meaning \enquote{a good one\textsubscript{f}}. So the sentence could mean:

\enquote{Charity is good.}

Or the information could be the common noun, meaning \enquote{a good deed}. Then the sentence would mean:

\enquote{Charity is a good deed.}

Context would be needed to tell us which meaning is intended.

\section{Common-nouns used as describers in a noun-phrase}\label{common-nouns-used-as-describers-in-a-noun-phrase}

Usually, adjectival nouns are used as the describer in an descriptive noun-phrase. However, we also often find a common noun used as a describer. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุฑูุฌููู ููุนูููููู.}\\
\emph{huwa rajulun muษallim.}\\
\enquote{He is a teacher\textsubscript{m} man.}\\
= \enquote{He is a man who is a teacher\textsubscript{m}.}

\section{Multiple adjectival nouns describing the same noun}\label{multiple-adjectival-nouns-describing-the-same-noun}

In English we can have a noun described by multiple adjectives separated by commas and the word \enquote{and}. For example, \enquote{The building is big, tall, and wide.} In Arabic we will separate the multiple adjectival nouns with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{wa-} \enquote{and}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุงุกู ฺฉูุจููุฑู ููุทูููููู ููููุงุณูุนู.}\\
\emph{สพalbinฤสพu kabฤซrun waแนญawฤซlun wawฤsiสพun}\\
\enquote{The building is big and tall and wide.}

In an English descriptive noun-phrase, multiple describers may describe the same describee, without being separated by the word \enquote{and}. For example, \enquote{The man is a famous Arab writer.} In Arabic, we can do the same, except the describees will be in the reverse order:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู ฺฉูุงุชูุจู ุนูุฑูุจูููู ููุดููููุฑู.}\\
\emph{สพarrujulu kฤtibun ษarabiyyun masอกhยทhลซr.}\\
\enquote{The man is a famous Arab writer.}

\section{A prepositional phrase separating the describer from the describee}\label{a-prepositional-phrase-separating-the-describer-from-the-describee}

Consider the phrase:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูููู ูฑููููฺฉูุชูุจูุฉู}\\
\emph{kitฤbun mina -lmaktabati}\\
\enquote{a book from the library}

If we want to add a adjectival noun as to describe \enquote{the book}, we may add it either before or after the prepositional phrase describer. Here are both examples as complete sentences:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง ุตูุบููุฑูุง ูููู ูฑููููฺฉูุชูุจูุฉู.}\\
\emph{qaraสพa kitฤban แนฃagอกhฤซran mina -lmaktabati.}\\
AND\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง ูููู ูฑููููฺฉูุชูุจูุฉู ุตูุบููุฑูุง.}\\
\emph{qaraสพa kitฤban mina -lmaktabati แนฃagอกhฤซran.}\\
\enquote{a small book from the library}

The first option is usually chosen as a matter of preference but the second option is legitimate too.

\chapter{Semi-flexible nouns}\label{semi-flexible-nouns}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-6}

Nouns are of two main categories of nouns, with regard to their endings in the different noun states:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Rigid nouns.
\item
  Flexible nouns. These are further sub-divided into:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\roman{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Fully-flexible nouns.
  \item
    Semi-flexible nouns.
  \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

So far we have been mostly studying fully-flexible nouns. In this chapter we will learn about semi-flexible nouns.

Here is an example of the kind of nouns we have learned so far:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
State & Indefinite & Definite \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู} \\
a-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูููุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู} \\
i-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู} \\
\end{longtable}

As you can see, the noun has \emph{n}-marks when it is indefinite, and also, the vowel mark on the last letter changes for each state that the noun is in. These kinds of nouns are called \emph{fully-flexible} nouns. They are by far the most common type of noun.

There are some nouns, however, that are \emph{semi-flexible}.
Here is an example of a semi-flexible noun, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ} \enquote{a desert}:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
State & Indefinite & Definite \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุญูุฑูุงุกู} \\
a-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุญูุฑูุงุกู} \\
i-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุญูุฑูุงุกู} \\
\end{longtable}

As you can see, when \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ} is indefinite, it does not have an \emph{n}-mark. Also, when it is indefinite and in the i-state, the vowel mark on its final letter is not \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, as you might expect but \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} . And so the noun looks identical in the a-state and i-state when it is indefinite.

When it is definite, however, it looks just like fully-flexible nouns.

So there are two differences between fully-flexible and semi-flexible nouns:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  When indefinite, a semi-flexible noun does not have an \emph{n}-mark.
\item
  When indefinite and in the i-state, a semi-flexible noun's final letter does not have an \emph{i}-mark. Instead it shall have an \emph{a}-mark, just like when it is in the a-state.
\end{enumerate}

The other category of nouns are \emph{rigid} nouns. Rigid nouns don't change their endings due to their state. They are much fewer in number compared to flexible nouns. Pronouns are an example of rigid nouns.

\section{Feminine markers}\label{feminine-markers}

Before we discuss semi-flexible nouns in more detail, we will discuss feminine markers. We already know of one feminine marker: the looped-tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}. When a singular noun ends with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, then that is an indication, with very few exceptions, that it is a feminine noun. Examples are:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1111}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4444}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4444}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Root
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Feminine noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Masculine noun from same root (if any)
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฌุฑูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉ} \enquote{a girl\textsubscript{f}} & -- \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุนููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููููุฉ} \enquote{a scholar\textsubscript{f}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููู} \enquote{a scholar\textsubscript{m}} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซฺฉูุจยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูููุจูุฉ} \enquote{a dog\textsubscript{f}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูููุจ} \enquote{a dog\textsubscript{m}} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุดุฌุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฌูุฑูุฉ} \enquote{a tree} & -- \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุตุบุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑูุฉ} \emph{adj.} \enquote{small\textsubscript{f}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \emph{adj.} \enquote{small\textsubscript{m}} \\
\end{longtable}

As you can see, the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} is never part of the noun's root. It is thus considered \emph{extrinsic} to the root. Also, sometimes, but not always, the feminine noun is formed by adding the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to the end of a masculine noun.

It is also important to note that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} is only a feminine marker for singular nouns. When we learn plurals, if Allฤh wills, we will see that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} is used frequently with masculine plurals.

Now we will learn of two more feminine markers: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}.

Here are some examples of nouns that end with these two feminine markers:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1111}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4444}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4444}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Root
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Feminine noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Masculine noun (if any)
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุตุญุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \enquote{a desert} & -- \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญูุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงุก} \emph{adj.} \enquote{red\textsubscript{f}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูููุฑ} \emph{adj.} \enquote{red\textsubscript{m}} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฐฺฉุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููฐ} \enquote{a remembrance} & -- \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุบุถุจยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูููฐ} \emph{adj.} \enquote{very angry\textsubscript{f}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูุงู} \emph{adj.} \enquote{very angry\textsubscript{m}} \\
\end{longtable}

When extrinsic to the word's root, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} are feminine markers, just like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}. However, one important difference from \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} is that sometimes
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} may not be extrinsic to the word's root. In this case, they will not be feminine markers, and the noun will regularly be a masculine noun. Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1579}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5789}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2632}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Root
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Pattern using paradigm \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุนูยป}
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุฏูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููููุฏูููฐ} (masc.) \enquote{the guidance} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุนูู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฎุจุกยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุจูุงุก} (masc.) \enquote{a tent} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงู} \\
\end{longtable}

These cases will become more clear, if Allฤh wills, when we study weak roots (roots that contain a weak letter like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุกุ ูุ ู}).

Otherwise,
when extrinsic to the word's root, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} are consistently feminine markers, just like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}.

Also, just like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} are only feminine markers for singular nouns. We will see, if Allฤh wills, that they are used frequently with masculine plurals.

By the way, another difference from \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} is that when
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} are feminine markers, and a masculine counterpart exists, then the feminine noun is not formed by simply adding the feminine marker to the end of the masculine noun. The masculine and feminine nouns are different internally as well. For example, the feminine noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงุก} \emph{adj.} \enquote{red\textsubscript{f}}
is not formed simply by adding the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} to the end of the masculine noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูููุฑ} \emph{adj.} \enquote{red\textsubscript{m}}.

We will discuss this in more detail below.

\section{Categories of semi-flexible nouns}\label{categories-of-semi-flexible-nouns}

We now return to our discussion of semi-flexible nouns. Semi-flexible nouns, in terms of their formation, fall under different categories. We will discuss them below.

When discussing semi-flexible nouns in isolation we will add the numeral~2 as a superscript to their ending, thus:
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ}\textsuperscript{2}. This is to indicate their semi-flexibility.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Nouns that end with an extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}}{Nouns that end with an extrinsic ุงุก}}\label{nouns-that-end-with-an-extrinsic-ux627ux621}

If a noun ends with an \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, which is extrinsic to the word's root, then it shall be a semi-flexible noun.

We have already seen an example of such a noun above: \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ}\textsuperscript{2} \enquote{a desert}. The root of this noun is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุตุญุฑยป}. You can see that the ending \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} is not part of the root. Therefore it is a semi-flexible noun.

Furthermore, we have also learned that this \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, which is extrinsic to the word's root, is a feminine marker for singular nouns, just like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, except that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} does not generally make a noun semi-flexible.

Here is an example sentence with this noun:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ุฅูููููฐ ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู ููุงุณูุนูุฉู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -rrajulu สพilฤ แนฃaแธฅrฤสพa wฤsiษah.}\\
\enquote{The man went to a wide desert.}

Note that the vowel mark on the final letter of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพa} is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, not \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, even though it is indefinite and in the i-state (because it is preceded by the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููฐ} \emph{สพilฤ} \enquote{to}). This is because it is a semi-flexible noun.

\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ}\textsuperscript{2} in this sentence
is also a describee, whose describer is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุณูุนูุฉู} \emph{wฤsiษatin} \enquote{wide}. The final vowel mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} on the describee \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพa} has no effect on the final vowel mark on the describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุณูุนูุฉู} \emph{wฤsiษatin} \enquote{wide}. All that matters in this regard is the state of the describee.

Note, also, that the describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุณูุนูุฉ} is feminine to match the gender of the describee
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ}\textsuperscript{2}.

Note, as well, that the describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุณูุนูุฉู} has an \emph{n}-mark as it is indefinite and fully-flexible. The inability of its describee
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ}\textsuperscript{2}
to have an \emph{n}-mark (because of its semi-flexibility) does not affect the describer.

Also, beware, as we've already mentioned, that there are some words where the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} ending may be part of the word's root, for example
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุจูุงุก} \emph{kอกhibฤสพ} \enquote{a tent} from the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฎุจุกยป} on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุจูุงุก}. Such words will be fully flexible.
Also, for the same reason, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} in this word is not a feminine marker, and the word is masculine.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Nouns that end with an extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}}{Nouns that end with an extrinsic ููฐ}}\label{nouns-that-end-with-an-extrinsic-ux649}

If a noun ends with an \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} which is extrinsic to the word's root, then it shall be a semi-flexible noun.

We've already seen an example of such a word: \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููฐ} \emph{dอกhikrฤ}\textsuperscript{2} \enquote{a remembrance}. The root of this word is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฐฺฉุฑยป} and it is on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููฐ}.

We've also learned that, similar to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, this \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}, which is extrinsic to the word's root, is a feminine marker for singular nouns.

Since \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููฐ} \emph{dอกhikrฤ}\textsuperscript{2} already ends with the vowel-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูฐ}, the last letter won't have any additional vowel markers and therefore the word will appear the same in all states:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
State & Indefinite & Definite \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููฐ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฐููฺฉูุฑูููฐ} \\
a-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููฐ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฐููฺฉูุฑูููฐ} \\
i-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููฐ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฐููฺฉูุฑูููฐ} \\
\end{longtable}

Therefore, the state of such nouns cannot be determined by the vowel mark on their final letter, and has to be deduced otherwise by their function in the sentence.
Nevertheless, these nouns are still included in the category of semi-flexible nouns, and not rigid nouns. This is because rigid nouns are closed set consisting only of pronouns and other similar words.

Here is an example of this word in a sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููฐ ุฌูููููุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพalkitฤbu dอกhikrฤ jamฤซlah.}\\
\enquote{The book is a beautiful remembrance.}

Note, again how the describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููููููุฉ} \emph{jamฤซlah} is feminine and in the u-state, in order to match the gender and state of the describee \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููฐ} \emph{dอกhikrฤ}\textsuperscript{2}.

Beware also that, just like in the case of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, there are some words where \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} may be part of the word's root, e.g.~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฏูููฐ} \emph{สพalhudฤ} \enquote{the guidance} whose root is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุฏูยป}. Because here the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููููุฏูููฐ} is part of the word's root, therefore it shall not be a semi-flexible noun. So, when it is indefinite, it will have an \emph{n}-mark when it is indefinite: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏูู} \emph{hudan} \enquote{a guidance}. Also, for the same reason, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} in this word is not a feminine marker, and the word is masculine.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Nouns on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูู}}{Nouns on the pattern ุฃูููุนูู}}\label{nouns-on-the-pattern-ux623ux641ux639ux644}

If a noun is on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูู} \emph{สพafษal} then it shall be a semi-flexible noun. By the way, there is no feminine marker on such words, so they will be masculine by default.

Most colors and many physical characteristics fall into this pattern. Colors and physical characteristics are adjectival nouns. The masculine noun for such adjectival-nouns is on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูู} \emph{สพafษal}. And the feminine adjectival noun is on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงุก} \emph{faษlฤสพ} (which is itself a semi-flexible noun pattern because of the extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} ending). Here are some examples of such adjectival nouns:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1136}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3182}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2955}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Root
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Masc. Noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Fem. noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
