\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญูุฑยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูููุฑ} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงุก} & red \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุณูุฏยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูููุฏ} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุฏูุงุก} & black \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุจูุถยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูููุถ} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุถูุงุก} & white \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุนุฑุฌยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนูุฑูุฌ} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุฑูุฌูุงุก} & lame \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญูุฑยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูููุฑ} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงุก} & beautiful eyed \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุจฺฉูยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูฺฉูู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูฺฉูููุงุก} & mute \\
\end{longtable}

Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูุณู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ูููููุตูุง ุฃูุจูููุถู.}\\
\emph{labisa -rrajulu qamฤซแนฃan สพabyaแธ.}\\
\enquote{The man wore a white shirt.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Adjectival nouns that end with an extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงู}}{Adjectival nouns that end with an extrinsic ุงู}}\label{adjectival-noun-an-diptote}

The letters \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงู} may be an extrinsic ending for nouns. This ending is not a feminine marker so the noun would typically be masculine. This ending may cause the noun to be semi-flexible.

This category is more complicated than the previous ones. The following conditions must be satisfied for a word that ends with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงู} to be a semi-flexible noun:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  The noun must be a adjectival-noun on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงู}. So the common noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูุนูุจูุงู} \emph{tอกhuษbฤn} \enquote{a serpent} of the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุซุนูยป} is a common noun and therefore, not a semi-flexible noun.
\item
  The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงู} must be extrinsic to the word's root. So \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุจูุงู} \emph{jabฤnun} \enquote{cowardly}, an adjectival noun of the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฌุจูยป}, is not a semi-flexible noun.
\item
  The feminine of the adjectival noun shall not be formed by adding \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to the masculine noun. So \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏูููุงู} \emph{nadmฤn} \enquote{regretful}, an adjectival-noun from the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุฏูยป}, is not a semi-flexible noun, because its feminine is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏูููุงููุฉ} \emph{nadmฤnah}.
\end{enumerate}

It is rare that this last condition fails. Most adjectival nouns that end with an extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงู} are of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงู} \emph{faษlฤn} and their feminine is of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููฐ} \emph{faษlฤ} (which is itself a semi-flexible noun pattern). These adjectival-nouns typically have an emphatic meaning. The following are examples of semi-flexible adjectival-nouns that fall into this category:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1136}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3182}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2955}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Root
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Masc. Noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Fem. noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Meaning
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุบุถุจยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูุงู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูููฐ} & very angry \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุนุทุดยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุทูุดูุงู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุทูุดูููฐ} & very thirsty \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฌูุนยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุนูุงู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุนูููฐ} & very hungry \\
\end{longtable}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Nouns of the patterns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงููู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงูููู}}{Nouns of the patterns ููููุงููู and ููููุงูููู}}\label{fafafif-diptote}

Nouns that are of the patterns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงููู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงูููู} are also semi-flexible nouns. Here each letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} could be any letter of the alphabet.

Here are some examples of these nouns:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุงุฌูุฏ} \emph{masฤjid}\textsuperscript{2} \enquote{mosques}
\item
  \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุชููุญ} \emph{mafฤtฤซแธฅ}\textsuperscript{2} \enquote{keys}
\end{itemize}

These patterns are only used for plurals and we will study them in more detail in chapter~\ref{broken-plurals}
, if Allฤh wills.

\chapter{Duals}\label{duals}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-7}

For any number greater than one, English uses the plural. For example, the plural of \enquote{house} is \enquote{houses}. So in English we will say:

\enquote{two houses}

Arabic, on the other hand, uses the plural only for nouns in number three and higher. For nouns that are two in number Arabic uses the \emph{dual}.

Since English does not have a dual, we will sometimes indicate it using the the subscript 2, thus: \enquote{houses\textsubscript{2}}, to mean \enquote{two houses}.

\section{Forming the dual}\label{forming-the-dual}

The dual is formed by appending the dual suffix \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูุงูู} \emph{-ฤni} when the noun is in the u-state and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โููููู} \emph{-ayni} when the noun is in the a-state or i-state. Definite nouns, which have \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} in their beginning are dualized the same way.

For example, when we dualize \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุช} \emph{bayt} \enquote{a house} in order to say \enquote{houses\textsubscript{2}}, we get:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2381}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3810}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3810}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
States
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Indefinite
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Definite
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชูุงูู} \emph{baytฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุชูุงูู} \emph{สพalbaytฤni} \\
a- and i-states & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชููููู} \emph{baytayni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุชููููู} \emph{สพalbaytayni} \\
\end{longtable}

Note that indefinite duals don't have \emph{n} marks. The only difference between definite and indefinite duals is the definite article \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \enquote{the}.

Here are examples of duals in sentences:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  u-state:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจูุงูู ููู ูฑููุญููููุจูุฉู.}\\
  \emph{สพalkitฤbฤni fi -lแธฅaqฤซbah.}\\
  \enquote{The books\textsubscript{2} are in the bag.}
\item
  a-state:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ฺฉูุชูุงุจููููู.}\\
  \emph{qaraสพa -lgอกhulฤmu kitฤbayn.}\\
  \enquote{The boy read two books.}
\item
  i-state:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูุชู ูฑููุฃูููู ุนูููู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุชููููู.}\\
  \emph{gอกhaแธibati -lสพummu ษala -ljฤriyatayn.}\\
  \enquote{The mother became angry at the girls\textsubscript{2}.}
  \#\#\# Nouns ending in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}
\end{itemize}

If a noun ends with a looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, then it is converted to an open tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} before appending the dual suffix. For example, dualizing \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฌูุฑูุฉ} \emph{sอกhajarah} \enquote{a tree}, we get \enquote{trees\textsubscript{2}}:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2381}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3810}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3810}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
States
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Indefinite
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Definite
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฌูุฑูุชูุงูู} \emph{sอกhajaratฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุฌูุฑูุชูุงูู} \emph{สพasอกhsอกhajaratฤni} \\
a- and i-states & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฌูุฑูุชููููู} \emph{sอกhajaratayni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุฌูุฑูุชููููู} \emph{สพasอกhsอกhajaratayni} \\
\end{longtable}

Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุฌูุฑูุชูุงูู ููู ูฑููุญูุฏููููุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพasอกhsอกharatฤni fi -lแธฅadฤซqah.}\\
\enquote{The trees\textsubscript{2} are in the garden.}

If a feminine noun does not have a looped tฤสพ then it will simply be appended with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูุงูู} \emph{-ฤni} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โููููู} \emph{-ayni}. For example, dualizing \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} \emph{สพumm} \enquote{a mother} in order to get \enquote{mothers\textsubscript{2}}, we get:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  u-state: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุงูู} \emph{สพummฤni}
\item
  a-state and i-state: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููููู} \emph{สพummayni}
\end{itemize}

There are some nouns that end with an สพalif before the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุงุฉ} \emph{fatฤh} \enquote{a young woman}. We will learn how to dualize these nouns later, if Allฤh wills.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Nouns ending with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}}{Nouns ending with ุงุก}}\label{duals-of-extrinsic-alif-mamduda}

If a noun ends with the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} which is extrinsic to the word's root then the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก} shall be replaced with a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} when forming the dual.
Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1429}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4286}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Root
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Singular
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Dual (u-state)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Dual (a- and i-states)
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุตุญุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ} \enquote{a desert} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงููุงูู} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤwฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงูููููู} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤwayni} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญูุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงุก} \emph{แธฅamrฤสพ} \enquote{red\textsubscript{f}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงููุงูู} \emph{แธฅamrฤwฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงูููููู} \emph{แธฅamrฤwayni} \\
\end{longtable}

There are other words where the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก} in the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} ending originates from the word's root.
Example:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฎุจุกยป} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุจูุงุก} (masc.) \enquote{a tent}, pattern: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงู}
\end{itemize}

We will learn how to form duals of these words in later chapters, if Allฤh wills.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Nouns ending with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}}{Nouns ending with ููฐ}}\label{duals-of-extrinsic-alif-maqsura}

If a noun ends with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} which is extrinsic to the word's root then the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} shall be changed to a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} when adding the dual suffixes. Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1429}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4286}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Root
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Singular
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Dual (u-state)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Dual (a- and i-states)
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุบุถุจยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูููฐ} \emph{gอกhaแธbฤ} \enquote{very angry\textsubscript{f}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูููุงูู} \emph{gอกhaแธbayฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจููููููู} \emph{gอกhaแธbayayni} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฐฺฉุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููฐ} \emph{dอกhikrฤ} \enquote{a remembrance} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููุงูู} \emph{dอกhikrayฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑููููููู} \emph{dอกhikrayayni} \\
\end{longtable}

Just like in the case of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, there are some words where \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} is not extrinsic to the word's root.
Example:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุฏูยป} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููููุฏูููฐ} (masc.) \enquote{the guidance}, pattern: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุนูู}
\end{itemize}

We will learn how to form duals of these words in later chapters, if Allฤh wills.

\section{Dual describers and describees in descriptive noun-phrases}\label{dual-describers-and-describees-in-descriptive-noun-phrases}

We learned that when an adjectival noun is a describer in an descriptive noun-phrase, then it matches the describee in definiteness, state, and gender. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฅูููู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabtu สพila -lmadฤซnati -lqadฤซmah.}\\
\enquote{I went to the old city.}

To this we add that the describer shall also match the describee in number. So if the describee is a dual then the adjectival-noun describer shall be dualzed to match it. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃููููุงูู ูฑูุทูููููุจูุชูุงูู ููู ูฑููุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{สพalสพummฤni -แนญแนญayyibatฤni fi -lbayt.}\\
\enquote{The good mothers\textsubscript{2} are in the house.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ฺฉูุชูุงุจููููู ุซูููููููููู ููุฏูููููููู.}\\
\emph{qaraสพa -lgอกhulฤmu kitฤbayni tอกhaqฤซlatayni qadฤซmatayn.}\\
\enquote{The boy read two old heavy books.}

\section{Duals in subject-information sentences}\label{duals-in-subject-information-sentences}

In subject-information sentences, if the subject is a dual, and the information is a adjectival noun, then the information will typically match the subject in being a dual. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃููููุงูู ฺฉูุฑููููุชูุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพalสพummฤni karฤซmatฤn.}\\
\enquote{The mothers\textsubscript{2} are generous.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจูุงูู ูฑููฺฉูุจููุฑูุงูู ุซููููููุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพalkitฤbฤni -lkabฤซrฤni tอกhaqฤซlฤn.}\\
\enquote{The big books\textsubscript{2} are heavy.}

Such is usually also the case even when the information is a common noun, not an adjectival noun. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูููุงูู ููุนููููููุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพarrujulฤni muษallimฤn.}\\
\enquote{The men\textsubscript{2} are teachers\textsubscript{m,2}.}

Sometimes, however, the subject and information may not match in number because of the meaning of the sentence. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุณูุงุฏูุชูุงูู ุณูุฑููุฑู.}\\
\emph{สพalwisฤdatฤni sarฤซr.}\\
\enquote{The two cushions are a bed.}

In the above example, the information does not match the subject in both number, and, as it happens, in gender.

\section{Detached dual pronouns}\label{detached-dual-pronouns}

We have already learned the detached pronouns that are used in place of singular nouns. They are repeated here:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Singular participant & Detached pronoun \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Masc. absentee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{huwa} \enquote{he} \\
Fem. absentee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{hiya} \enquote{she} \\
Masc. addressee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชู} \emph{สพanta} \enquote{you\textsubscript{m,1}} \\
Fem. addressee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชู} \emph{สพanti} \enquote{you\textsubscript{f,1}} \\
Speaker & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง} \emph{สพana} \enquote{I} \\
\end{longtable}

Now we will learn the detached pronouns for the dual participants:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Dual participant & Detached pronoun \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Absentee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง} \emph{humฤ} \enquote{they\textsubscript{2}} \\
Addressee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชูููุง} \emph{สพantumฤ} \enquote{you\textsubscript{2}} \\
Speaker & -- \\
\end{longtable}

Note that the dual detached pronouns are the same for both genders.
Also, there is no detached pronoun for the dual speaker-participant. If the speaker-pariticipant consists of two individuals then we will use the plural pronoun, which we will learn in the next chapter, if Allฤh wills.

Here are some examples of their use:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง ูฑูุฑููุฌูููุงูู.}\\
\emph{huma -rrajulฤn.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{2} are the men\textsubscript{2}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง ููุนููููููุชูุงูู ฺฉูุฑููููุชูุงูู.}\\
\emph{humฤ muษallimatฤni karฤซmatฤni.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{2} are noble teachers\textsubscript{f}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงููุชู ูฑูุฃูููู ููููุฌูุงุฑูููุชููููู ุฃูููุชูููุง ููุฑููุจูุชูุงูู ูููููู.}\\
\emph{qฤlati -lสพummu liljฤriyatayni สพantumฤ qarฤซbatฤni minnฤซ.}\\
\enquote{The mother said to the girls\textsubscript{2}, \enquote*{You\textsubscript{2} are near me.}}

In the last example, the feminine adjectival-noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุจูุชูุงูู} \emph{qarฤซbatฤni} is used because it is referring to the feminine noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุชููููู} \emph{สพaljฤriyatayni} \enquote{the girls\textsubscript{2}}.

\section{Attached dual pronouns}\label{attached-dual-pronouns}

We have also already learned the attached pronouns for the singular participant. They too are repeated here:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Singular participant & Attached pronoun \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Masc. absentee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{-hu} \enquote{him} \\
Fem. absentee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{-hฤ} \enquote{her} \\
Masc. addressee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉู} \emph{-ka} \enquote{you\textsubscript{m,1}} \\
Fem. addressee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉู} \emph{-ki} \enquote{you\textsubscript{f,1}} \\
Speaker & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \enquote{me} \\
\end{longtable}

Now we will learn the attached pronouns for the dual participant:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Dual participant & Attached pronoun \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Absentee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง} \emph{-humฤ} \enquote{them\textsubscript{2}} \\
Addressee & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูููุง} \emph{-kumฤ} \enquote{you\textsubscript{2}} \\
Speaker & -- \\
\end{longtable}

Note the following points about them:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Like the dual detached pronouns, the dual attached pronouns are the same for both genders. Also, there is no attached pronoun for the dual speaker-participant. Again, the plural pronoun will be used in this case.
\item
  The dual absentee-participant detached and attached pronouns (\enquote{they\textsubscript{2}}/\enquote{them\textsubscript{2}}) are the same \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง} \emph{-humฤ}.
\item
  Just like the absentee-participant singular masculine attached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{hu} \enquote{him}, the dual absentee-participant attached pronoun \enquote{them\textsubscript{2}} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง} \emph{-humฤ} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง} \emph{-himฤ} when preceded by the vowels \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} \emph{-i}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูู} \emph{-ฤซ}, or the semi-vowel \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โููู} \emph{-ay}. Examples:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููููุง} \emph{bihimฤ} \enquote{with them\textsubscript{2}}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููููุง} \emph{fฤซhimฤ} \enquote{in them\textsubscript{2}}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููููููุง} \emph{สพilayhimฤ} \enquote{to them\textsubscript{2}}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{li} \enquote{for} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{la} when followed by the dual attached pronouns:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููููุง} \emph{lahumฤ} \enquote{for them\textsubscript{2}}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉูููุง} \emph{lakumฤ} \enquote{for you\textsubscript{2}}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  As expected, the long \emph{ฤ} vowel at the ends of the dual attached pronouns becomes a short \emph{a} vowel when followed by a connecting hamzah \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑ}. Example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ุฅูููููฺฉูููุง ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
    \emph{dอกhahaba สพilaykuma -rrajulu.}\\
    \enquote{The man went toward you\textsubscript{2}.}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Dual doee pronouns}\label{dual-doee-pronouns}

The dual attached pronouns that we have just learned are also used as doee pronouns.
Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููููููุง ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\emph{saสพalahuma -rrajulu.}\\
\enquote{The man asked them\textsubscript{2}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชูฺฉูููุง.}\\
\emph{saสพaltukumฤ}\\
\enquote{I asked you\textsubscript{2}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชูฺฉูููุง.}\\
\emph{saสพalatkumฤ.}\\
\enquote{She asked you\textsubscript{2}.}

\section{Verbs with dual doers}\label{verbs-with-dual-doers}

\subsection{Dual nouns for the doer}\label{dual-nouns-for-the-doer}

We learned that the completed-action verb for a masculine doer is on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู}. And when the doer is feminine, the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} of femininity is attached to the verb thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชู}. We have used these verbs with singular doer nouns. The doer noun always comes after the verb and shall be in the u-state. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -lgอกhulฤmu.}\\
\enquote{The boy went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabat jฤriyatun}\\
\enquote{A girl went.}

These same verbs are used when the doer noun is a dual. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงููุงูู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -lgอกhulฤmฤni.}\\
\enquote{The boys\textsubscript{2} went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฌูุงุฑูููุชูุงูู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabat jฤriyatฤni.}\\
\enquote{Two girls went.}

\subsection{Dual pronouns for the doer}\label{dual-pronouns-for-the-doer}

We have already learned the singular doer pronouns:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2500}}

Note the following regarding the dual doer pronouns:

The dual doer pronouns are the same for both genders.

However, when the absentee-participant doer pronoun (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูุง} \emph{-ฤ}) is used for a feminine doer, it is attached to the verb with an intervening \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} of femininity thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชูุง} \emph{faษalatฤ} \enquote{they\textsubscript{f,2} did}
Here are some examples of the dual doer pronouns:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชูููุงููุง}\\
\emph{saสพaltumฤnฤ}\\
\enquote{You\textsubscript{2} asked us}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชูุงฺฉูููุง}\\
\emph{saสพalatฤkumฤ}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{f,2} asked you\textsubscript{2}}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุงููููุง}\\
\emph{saสพalฤhumฤ}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{m,2} asked them\textsubscript{2}}

\subsection{Sentence word order with dual doers}\label{sentence-word-order-with-dual-doers}

As we've mentioned, the doer, whether a noun or a pronoun, always comes after the verb. Here are a couple of examples of verbal sentences with dual doers:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุง ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabฤ สพilฤ baytin.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{2} went to a house.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌูููุงูู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabฤ -rrujalฤni สพilฤ baytin.}\\
\enquote{The men\textsubscript{2} went to a house.}

The above verbal sentence can be rearranged to be a subject-information sentence. This gives more emphasis to the subject. In this case, the verb shall follow the subject and will need a doer pronoun after it.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูููุงูู ุฐูููุจูุง ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{สพarrujalฤni dอกhahabฤ สพilฤ baytin.}\\
\enquote{The men\textsubscript{2}, they\textsubscript{2} went to a house.}\\
= \enquote{\emph{The men\textsubscript{2}} went to a house.}

If there are multiple verbs associated with the same doer in a verbal sentence, the doer noun will follow the first verb and the rest of the verbs will have doer pronouns. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู ูฑูุฑููุฌูููุงูู ููุดูุฑูุจูุง ููุฐูููุจูุง.}\\
\emph{สพakala -rrajulฤni wasอกharibฤ wadอกhahabฤ.}\\
\enquote{The men\textsubscript{2} ate and they\textsubscript{2} drank and they\textsubscript{2} went.}\\
= \enquote{The men\textsubscript{2} ate and drank and went.}

The above verbal sentence can be rearranged to be a subject-information sentence. In that case, all the verbs shall have doer pronouns. The sentence will have the same translation as above, except for an emphasis on the subject of the sentence.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูููุงูู ุฃูฺฉูููุง ููุดูุฑูุจูุง ููุฐูููุจูุง.}\\
\emph{สพarrajulฤni สพakalฤ wasอกharibฤ wadอกhahabฤ.}\\
\enquote{The men\textsubscript{2}, they\textsubscript{2} ate and they\textsubscript{2} drank and they\textsubscript{2} went.}\\
= \enquote{\emph{The men\textsubscript{2}} ate and drank and went.}

\chapter{Sound plurals}\label{sound-plurals}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-8}

Arabic uses the plural for nouns in number three and higher. The formation and use of plurals in Arabic can be somewhat complicated. One of these complications is that, in using plurals, Arabic distinguishes between intelligent beings and non-intelligent beings. Intelligent beings are those living beings that are endowed with reason like humans, angels, and jinn. Non-intelligent beings include animals, inanimate objects, abstract concepts, etc.

As a further complication, there is sometimes more than one way to use plurals. In this chapter we will explain the most common usages to keep things as simple as possible.

Arabic has two categories of plurals:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  The \emph{sound plural:}
  English regularly forms the plural by adding the plural ending \enquote{s} to the end of a singular noun. For example:

  \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
  \toprule\noalign{}
  Singular & Plural \\
  \midrule\noalign{}
  \endhead
  \bottomrule\noalign{}
  \endlastfoot
  book & books \\
  house & houses \\
  boy & boys \\
  girl & girls \\
  \end{longtable}

  Arabic also forms some plurals by adding plural endings to to the singular noun. This kind of plural is call a \emph{sound} plural because the singular noun is kept more or less sound (intact) when adding the plural ending.

  Arabic has two types of sound plurals:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\roman{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    The \emph{ลซn} sound plural.
  \item
    The \emph{ฤt} sound plural.
  \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

We will describe each of these in this chapter.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{1}
\tightlist
\item
  The \emph{broken plural}: When forming this plural the singular noun is not kept intact. We will learn about this plural in the next chapter, if Allฤh wills.
\end{enumerate}

\section{\texorpdfstring{The \emph{ลซn} sound plural}{The ลซn sound plural}}\label{the-un-sound-plural}

The \emph{ลซn} sound plural is formed by adding the ending \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูููู} \emph{-ลซna} to the singular noun when it is in the u-state, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูููู} \emph{-ฤซna} when the noun is in the a-state or i-state. For convenience, we will call it the \enquote{\emph{ลซn} sound plural} instead of the \enquote{\emph{-ลซna}/\emph{-ฤซna} plural}.

Here is the \emph{ลซn} sound plural of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู} \emph{muษallim} \enquote{a teacher\textsubscript{m}}:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2632}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3684}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3684}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
State
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Indefinite \emph{ลซn} plural \enquote{teachers\textsubscript{m}}
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Definite \emph{ลซn} plural \enquote{the teachers\textsubscript{m}}
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููููู} \emph{muษallimลซna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุนููููููููู} \emph{สพalmuษallimลซna} \\
a- and i-states & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููููู} \emph{muษallimฤซna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุนููููููููู} \emph{สพalmuษallimฤซna} \\
\end{longtable}

Note that, just like for duals, the indefinite \emph{ลซn} sound plural doesn't have \emph{n} marks. The only difference between the definite and indefinite \emph{ลซn} sound plural is the definite article \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \enquote{the}.

The duals of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู} \emph{muษallim} \enquote{a teacher\textsubscript{m}} are included here for comparison:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2632}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3684}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3684}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
State
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Indefinite \emph{ลซn} sound plural \enquote{teachers\textsubscript{m,2}}
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Definite \emph{ลซn} sound plural \enquote{the teachers\textsubscript{m,2}}
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููุงูู} \emph{muษallimฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุนููููููุงูู} \emph{สพalmuษallimฤni} \\
a- and i-states & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููููููู} \emph{muษallimayni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุนูููููููููู} \emph{สพalmuษallimayni} \\
\end{longtable}

Here are some examples of the \emph{ลซn} sound plural in sentences:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  u-state:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููููุนููููููููู ููู ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู.}\\
  \emph{สพalmuษallimลซna fi -lmadrasah}\\
  \enquote{The teachers are in the school.}
\item
  a-state:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ููุนููููููููู ุนููู ุฃูููุฑู.}\\
  \emph{saสพala -lgอกhulฤmu muษallimฤซna fฤซ สพamr.}\\
  \enquote{The boy asked some teachers about a matter.}
\item
  i-state:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ูููู ูฑููููุนููููููููู ุนูููููุง.}\\
  \emph{แนญalaba -lgอกhulฤmu mina -lmuษallimฤซna ษilmฤ.}\\
  \enquote{The boy sought some knowledge from the teachers.}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Applicability of the \emph{ลซn} sound plural}{Applicability of the ลซn sound plural}}\label{applicability-of-the-un-sound-plural}

Except for very few exceptions, the \emph{ลซn} sound plural is used only for male intelligent beings.

The few exceptions of common nouns that denote non-male intelligent beings, yet have an \emph{ลซn} sound plural include:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููู} \emph{ษฤlam} \enquote{a world} forms the \emph{ลซn} plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููููููู} \emph{สพฤlamลซna} \enquote{worlds}.
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฑูุถ} \emph{สพarแธ} (fem.) \enquote{a land}, \enquote{an earth} forms the \emph{ลซn} plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฑูุถูููู} \emph{สพarแธลซna} \enquote{lands}, \enquote{earths}.
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} \emph{สพahl} \enquote{a family} forms the \emph{ลซn} plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููููู} \emph{สพahlลซna} \enquote{families}.
\end{itemize}

\section{\texorpdfstring{The \emph{ฤt} sound plural}{The ฤt sound plural}}\label{the-at-sound-plural}

The \emph{ฤt} sound plural is formed by adding the ending \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุช} \emph{ฤt} to the indefinite singular noun.

Here is the \emph{ฤt} sound plural of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููููุงู} \emph{แธฅayawฤn} \enquote{an animal}:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2632}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3684}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3684}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
State
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Indefinite \emph{ลซn} plural \enquote{animals}
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Definite \emph{ลซn} plural \enquote{the animals}
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููููุงููุงุชู} \emph{แธฅayawฤnฤtun} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูููููุงููุงุชู} \emph{สพalแธฅayawฤnฤtu} \\
a- and i-states & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููููุงููุงุชู} \emph{แธฅayawฤnฤtin} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูููููุงููุงุชู} \emph{สพalแธฅayawฤnฤti} \\
\end{longtable}

Note that:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Unlike the \emph{ลซn} sound plural, the \emph{ฤt} sound plural takes \emph{n} marks. Also, just like for singular nouns, the final vowel on the plural ending \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุช} \emph{ฤt} indicates the state of the plural.
\item
  The \emph{ฤt} sound plural does not take the \emph{a}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} and the \emph{an}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. Instead the \emph{i}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} and the \emph{in}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}-mark are used to indicate both the a-state and the i-state.
\end{itemize}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3500}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3500}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
State
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
the animal
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
the animals
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูููููุงูู} \emph{สพalแธฅayawฤnu} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูููููุงููุงุชู} \emph{สพalแธฅayawฤnฤtu} \\
a-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูููููุงูู} \emph{สพalแธฅayawฤna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูููููุงููุงุชู} \emph{สพalแธฅayawฤnฤti} \\
i-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูููููุงูู} \emph{สพalแธฅayawฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูููููุงููุงุชู} \emph{สพalแธฅayawฤnฤti} \\
\end{longtable}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Nouns ending in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}}{Nouns ending in ุฉ}}\label{nouns-ending-in-ux629}

If a noun ends with a looped tฤสพ \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, then it is removed before appending the \emph{ฤt} sound plural ending.
Here, for example, is the \emph{ฤt} sound plural of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููุฉ} \emph{muษallimah} \enquote{a teacher\textsubscript{f}}:

  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2632}}

Here are some examples of the \emph{ฤt} sound plural in sentences:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  u-state:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู ููุนููููููุงุชู .}\\
  \emph{fi -lmadrasati muษallimฤtun.}\\
  \enquote{In the school are teachers.}
\item
  a-state:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุตูุฑู ูฑููููููฐูู ูฑููููุณูููููููู.}\\
  \emph{naแนฃara -llฤhu -lmuslimฤซna.}\\
  ``Allฤh aided the Muslims.
\item
  i-state:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุธูุฑู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุฅูููู ูฑููุญูููููุงููุงุชู.}\\
  \emph{nadฬฃอกhara -lgอกhulฤmu สพila -lแธฅayawฤnฤti.}\\
  \enquote{The boy looked at the animals.}
\end{itemize}

There are some nouns that end with an สพalif before the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุงุฉ} \emph{fatฤh} \enquote{a young woman}. We will learn how to pluralize these nouns later, if Allฤh wills.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Nouns ending with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}}{Nouns ending with ุงุก}}\label{nouns-ending-with-ux627ux621}

Consistent with what we learned for duals in section~\ref{duals-of-extrinsic-alif-mamduda},
if a noun ends with the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} which is extrinsic to the word's root then the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก} shall be replaced with a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} when forming the \emph{ฤt} sound plural.
Example:

  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1818}}

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead

\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุตุญุฑยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ}\textsuperscript{2} \enquote{a desert} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงููุงุช} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤwฤt} \\

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Nouns ending with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}}{Nouns ending with ููฐ}}\label{nouns-ending-with-ux649}

Consistent with what we learned for duals in section~\ref{duals-of-extrinsic-alif-maqsura},
If a noun ends with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} which is extrinsic to the word's root then the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} shall be changed to a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} when when forming the \emph{ฤt} sound plural. Examples:

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Common nouns of the patterns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ}}{Common nouns of the patterns ููุนูู/ููุนูููุฉ, ููุนูู/ููุนูููุฉ, and ููุนูู/ููุนูููุฉ}}\label{common-nouns-of-the-patterns-ux641ux639ux644ux641ux639ux644ux629-ux641ux639ux644ux641ux639ux644ux629-and-ux641ux639ux644ux641ux639ux644ux629}

Common nouns of the patterns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ} are treated specially when forming their \emph{ฤt} sound plural.

If a common noun is of these patterns and the middle root letter is not \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, and the middle and final root letters are not the same, then the word is modified internally when forming the \emph{ฤt} sound plural.

There are two separate rules to consider:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  If a common noun is of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \emph{faษl} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ} \emph{faษlah}, then the รธ-mark on the middle letter shall be converted to an \emph{a}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} when forming the \emph{ฤt} sound plural. For example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญูููุฉ} \emph{naแธฅlah} \enquote{a bee} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญูููุงุช} \emph{naแธฅalฤt} \enquote{bees}, not \(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญูููุงุช} \emph{naแธฅlฤt}.
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจูุฉ} \emph{แธarbah} \enquote{a strike} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจูุงุช} \emph{แธarabฤt} \enquote{strikes}, not \(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจูุงุช} \emph{แธarbฤt}.
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูููุญูุฉ} \emph{แนฃafแธฅah} \enquote{a page} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูููุญูุงุช} \emph{แนฃafaแธฅฤt} \enquote{pages}, not \(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูููุญูุงุช} \emph{แนฃafแธฅฤt}.
  \end{itemize}

  If the middle root letter is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, or the middle and final root letters are the same then this modification is not done. For example,

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุฒูุฉ} \emph{jawzah} \enquote{a walnut} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุฒูุงุช} \emph{jawzฤt}.
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฌููุฉ} \emph{แธฅajjah} \enquote{a pilgrimage} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฌููุงุช} \emph{แธฅajjฤt}.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  If a common noun is of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \emph{fiษl}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ} \emph{fiษlah}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \emph{fuษl}, or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ} \emph{fuษlah} then the รธ-mark on the middle letter can, optionally, either:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\roman{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    be retained,
  \item
    be converted to an \emph{a} mark, or
  \item
    be converted to the vowel mark on the first letter.
  \end{enumerate}

  For example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธูููููุฉ} \emph{dฬฃอกhulmah} \enquote{a darkness} can become, optionally, either \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธูููููุงุช} \emph{dฬฃอกhulmฤt} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธูููููุงุช} \emph{dฬฃอกhulamฤt}, or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธูููููุงุช} \emph{dฬฃอกhulumฤt} \enquote{darknesses}.
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุณูุฑูุฉ} \emph{kisrah} \enquote{a piece} can become, optionally, either \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุณูุฑูุงุช} \emph{kisrฤt} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุณูุฑูุงุช} \emph{kisarฤt}, or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุณูุฑูุงุช} \emph{kisirฤt} \enquote{pieces}.
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

Note that this rule of changing the vowel mark is only true for common nouns. Adjectival-nouns on these patterns will retain the รธ-mark when forming the \emph{ฤt} sound plural. So \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจ} \emph{แนฃaษb} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจูุฉ} \emph{แนฃaษbah} \enquote{a difficult one} become only \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจูุงุช} \emph{แนฃaษbฤt}, not \(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจูุงุช} \emph{แนฃaษabฤt}.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Applicability of the \emph{ฤt} sound plural}{Applicability of the ฤt sound plural}}\label{applicability-of-the-at-sound-plural}

We had mentioned that the \emph{ลซn} sound plural is used, with very few exceptions, only for male intelligent beings. Conversely, the \emph{ฤt} is used for both female intelligent beings, and for non-intelligent beings (both masculine and feminine) like animals, inanimate objects, and abstract concepts. Rarely, it is also used for male intelligent beings.

\section{Conditions for forming the sound plural}\label{conditions-for-forming-the-sound-plural}

Many times, a noun can form both an \emph{ลซn} sound plural and an \emph{ฤt} sound plural.
However, there are many nouns that can form only one of the two sound plurals.
And many nouns don't form either sound plural; they only form broken plurals. (We will learn about broken plurals in the next chapter, if Allฤh wills.) There are even nouns that can form both sound and broken plurals.

Here we will learn some of the conditions which a noun needs to satisfy in order for it to form the sound plurals.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Conditions for the \emph{ลซn} sound plural}{Conditions for the ลซn sound plural}}\label{conditions-for-the-un-sound-plural}

The \emph{ลซn} sound plural is used, with very few exceptions, only for nouns that denote male intelligent beings. These guidelines will help you determine which nouns form the \emph{ลซn} sound plural.

We will treat common nouns and adjectival nouns separately.

\subsubsection{Common nouns}\label{common-nouns}

With very few exceptions (some of which we saw in
section~\ref{applicability-of-the-un-sound-plural}),
the only common nouns that \emph{may} be allowed to form \emph{ลซn} sound plurals are those that denote male intelligent beings, and whose feminine is formed by adding a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to the masculine noun. So, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูููุงู} \emph{gอกhulฤm} \enquote{a boy} is disqualified from forming a \emph{ลซn} sound plural because its feminine counterpart is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉ} \emph{jฤriyah} \enquote{a girl}, not \(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูููุงููุฉ} \emph{gอกhulฤmah}. In addition, a further restriction is imposed, which we will explain below:

We learned in section~\ref{related-nouns-for-male-and-female-animate-beings} that, in terms of their meaning, nouns that denote animate beings are of two kinds:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\item
  Nouns that have a primitive meaning. That is, their meaning is not derived from a verbal or adjectival meaning. Examples (for male intelligent beings whose feminine is formed by adding \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to the masculine noun):

  \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
  \toprule\noalign{}
  Arabic word & Definition \\
  \midrule\noalign{}
  \endhead
  \bottomrule\noalign{}
  \endlastfoot
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} \emph{สพibn} & a son \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููู} \emph{แนญifl} & a child \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููุณูุงู} \emph{สพinsฤn} & a human being \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฑู} \emph{แธฅurr} & a free man \\
  \end{longtable}

  Such nouns, in general, won't be expected to form \emph{ลซn} sound plurals, unless the \emph{ลซn} sound plural is explicitly allowed in their dictionary definition.
\item
  Nouns that have a meaning that is derived from a verbal or adjectival meaning. Examples (for male intelligent beings whose feminine is formed by adding \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to the masculine noun):

  \begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
  \toprule\noalign{}
  Word & Definition & \emph{ลซn} plural \\
  \midrule\noalign{}
  \endhead
  \bottomrule\noalign{}
  \endlastfoot
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู} & a teacher\textsubscript{m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููููู} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูููู} & a Muslim\textsubscript{m} (one who submits) & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูููููููู} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงููุฑ} & a disbeliever\textsubscript{m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงููุฑูููู} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูุจ} & a player\textsubscript{m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูุจูููู} \\
  \end{longtable}

  Such nouns, in general, can be expected to form \emph{ลซn} sound plurals.
\end{enumerate}

The above condition, as we have explained it, is somewhat imprecise. For example, the word
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฑู} \emph{แธฅurr} (masc.) \enquote{a free man} seems to have a meaning that is derived from the adjective \enquote{free} and it forms its feminine by adding \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to it thus:
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฑููุฉ} \emph{แธฅurrah} (fem.) \enquote{a free woman}.
Yet it is considered a primitve noun, and thus does not form an \emph{ลซn} sound plural.

In later chapters, once we have studied the patterns of the derived nouns, we will try to make this condition more precise, if Allฤh wills.

\subsubsection{Adjectival nouns}\label{adjectival-nouns}

If an adjectival noun forms its feminine by adding the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to the masculine noun, then we may assume that it forms the \emph{ลซn} sound plural.

Most adjectival nouns satisfy this condition. For example, consider the adjectival noun:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑ} \emph{kabฤซr} (masc.) \enquote{a big one}
\end{itemize}

It forms its feminine by adding a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to the masculine noun, thus:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑูุฉ} \emph{kabฤซrah} (fem.) \enquote{a big one}
\end{itemize}

The above condition is satisfied; therefore,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑ} \emph{kabฤซr} (masc.) \enquote{a big one} forms the \emph{ลซn} sound plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑูููู} \emph{kabฤซrลซna} \enquote{big ones}.

By the way, it is only the masculine adjectival noun that will form the \emph{ลซn} sound plural. Nouns with a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} are not allowed to form the \emph{ลซn} sound plural.

We have come across two patterns on adjectival nouns that don't form their feminine by adding \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to masculine noun. These are:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงู} \emph{faษlฤn}\textsuperscript{2}, whose feminine is on the pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููฐ} \emph{faษlฤ}\textsuperscript{2}. Example: \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูุงู} \emph{gอกhaแธbฤn}\textsuperscript{2} (masc.) \enquote{very angry} whose feminine is \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูููฐ} \emph{gอกhaแธbฤ}\textsuperscript{2}.
\item
  \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูู} \emph{สพafษal}\textsuperscript{2}, whose feminine is on the pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงุก} \emph{faษlฤสพ}\textsuperscript{2}. Example: \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูููุฑ} \emph{สพaแธฅmar}\textsuperscript{2} (masc.) \enquote{red}, whose feminine is \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงุก} \emph{แธฅamrฤสพ}\textsuperscript{2}.
\end{enumerate}

Because the above two patterns don't form their feminine by adding \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to the masculine noun, therefore the masculine nouns don't form the \emph{ลซn} sound plural. We will see, if Allฤh wills, that they form broken plurals instead.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Conditions for the \emph{ฤt} sound plural}{Conditions for the ฤt sound plural}}\label{conditions-for-the-at-plural}

Just like the \emph{ลซn} plural, there are conditions that should be fulfilled in order for a noun to form an \emph{ฤt} plural.
We provide the following guidelines to help you determine if a noun can form an \emph{ฤt} plural.

\subsubsection{Nouns that end with a feminine marker}\label{nouns-that-end-with-a-feminine-marker}

Generally, all nouns that end with a feminine marker like
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}
are able to form an \emph{ฤt} plural.
Examples are:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.7273}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Singular
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\emph{ฤt} sound plural
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูููุฉ} \emph{แธฅasanah} \emph{adj.} \enquote{a good one\textsubscript{f}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูููุงุช} \emph{แธฅasanฤt} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูููุฉ} \emph{แธฅasanah} (common noun) \enquote{a good deed} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูููุงุช} \emph{แธฅasanฤt} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุฏููููุฉ} \emph{แนฃadฤซqah} \enquote{a friend\textsubscript{f}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุฏููููุงุช} \emph{แนฃadฤซqฤt} \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ}\textsuperscript{2} \enquote{a desert} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงููุงุช} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤwฤt} \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููฐ} \emph{dอกhikrฤ}\textsuperscript{2} \enquote{a remembrance} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูฺฉูุฑูููุงุช} \emph{dอกhikrayฤt} \\
\end{longtable}

The following are exceptions to this general rule, and don't form \emph{ฤt} sound plurals:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Adjectival nouns of the pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงุก} which is the feminine of the masculine adjectival noun pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูู}. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญูุฑยป} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงุก} \emph{แธฅamrฤสพ} \enquote{red\textsubscript{f}}.
\item
  Adjectival nouns of the pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููฐ} which is the feminine of the masculine adjectival noun pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงู}. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุบุถุจยป} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูููฐ} \emph{gอกhaแธbฤ} \enquote{very angry\textsubscript{f}}.
\item
  The following exceptional nouns:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุฉ} \emph{สพummah} \enquote{a nation}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฉ} \emph{สพamah} \enquote{a female slave}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุฉ} \emph{sอกhafah} \enquote{a lip}
  \end{itemize}

  There are a few more such nouns, some of which we will introduce later.
\end{itemize}

All these exceptional nouns form broken plurals instead of the \emph{ฤt} sound plural.

\subsubsection{Nouns that don't end with a feminine marker}\label{nouns-that-dont-end-with-a-feminine-marker}

\paragraph*{Common nouns}\label{common-nouns-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{paragraph}{Common nouns}

Common nouns that don't end with a feminine marker will form the \emph{ฤt} plural only if they don't have a broken plural listed in the dictionary. Furthermore, it is preferred if the noun have five or more letters.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููููุงู} \emph{แธฅayawฤn} \enquote{an animal} forms the \emph{ฤt} plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููููุงููุงุช} \emph{แธฅayawฤnฤt} \enquote{animals}.
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููุงู} \emph{แธฅammฤm} forms the \emph{ฤt} plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููุงููุงุช} \emph{แธฅammฤmฤt} \enquote{bathrooms}. (The doubled \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} counts as two letters.)
\end{itemize}

\paragraph*{Masculine adjectival nouns}\label{masculine-adjectival-nouns}
\addcontentsline{toc}{paragraph}{Masculine adjectival nouns}

Masculine adjectival nouns are permitted to form an \emph{ฤt} sound plural, but only when they are applied to non-intelligent beings.

For example, if the masculine adjectival noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจ} \emph{แนฃaษb} \enquote{a difficult one} is applied to \enquote{books}, which is the plural of the masculine noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} \emph{kitฤb} \enquote{a book}, then the masculine adjectival noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจ} \emph{แนฃaษb} is permitted to form the \emph{ฤt} plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจูุงุช} \emph{แนฃaษbฤt} \enquote{difficult ones}.

By the way, note that both the masculine adjectival noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจ} \emph{แนฃaษb}, and its feminine \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจูุฉ} \emph{แนฃaษbah} form the same \emph{ฤt} sound plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจูุงุช} \emph{แนฃaษbฤt}.

We have already learned the detached pronouns for singular and dual nouns. They are repeated here:

Absentee sing. fem. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{hiya} \enquote{she} \\

Now we will learn the detached pronouns for the plural participants:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Participant & Detached pronoun \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Absentee pl. masc. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{hum} \enquote{they\textsubscript{3,m}} \\
Absentee pl. fem. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู} \emph{hunna} \enquote{they\textsubscript{3,f}} \\
Addressee pl. masc. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชููู} \emph{สพantum} \enquote{you\textsubscript{3,m}} \\
Addressee pl. fem. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชูููู} \emph{สพantunna} \enquote{you\textsubscript{3,f}} \\
Speaker pl. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญููู} \emph{naแธฅnu} \enquote{we} \\
\end{longtable}

Note that the plural detached pronoun for the speaker participant
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญููู} \emph{naแธฅnu} \enquote{we}
are the same for both genders.

Also, remember that there is no detached pronoun for the dual speaker-participant.
So, if the speaker-pariticipant consists of two individuals then we will use the plural pronoun.

Here are some examples of their use:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ููุณูููููููู.}\\
\emph{hum muslimลซn.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{3,m} are men\textsubscript{3}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู ููุนููููููุงุชู.}\\
\emph{hum muษallimaฤt.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{3,f} are teachers\textsubscript{f}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชููู ููุงุนูุจูููู.}\\
\emph{สพantum lฤษibลซn.}\\
\enquote{You\textsubscript{3,m} are players\textsubscript{3,m}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชูููู ุตูุฏููููุงุชู.}\\
\emph{สพantunna แนฃadฤซqฤt.}\\
\enquote{You\textsubscript{3,f} are friends\textsubscript{3,f}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญููู ุฑูุฌูููุงูู ูููููุฑูุงูู.}\\
\emph{naแธฅnu rajulฤni faqฤซrฤn.}\\
\enquote{We\textsubscript{2,m} are poor men\textsubscript{2}.} (Note the plural pronoun subject with a dual noun in the information.)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญููู ููุณูููููุงุชู.}\\
\emph{naแธฅnu muslimฤt.}\\
\enquote{We\textsubscript{3,f} are Muslims\textsubscript{3,f}.}

\section{Attached plural pronouns}\label{attached-plural-pronouns}

We have also already learned the attached pronouns for the singular and dual participants. They too are repeated here:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Participant & Attached pronoun \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Absentee sing. masc. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{-hu} \enquote{him} \\
Absentee sing. fem. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{-hฤ} \enquote{her} \\
Absentee dual & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง} \emph{-humฤ} \enquote{them\textsubscript{2}} \\
Addressee sing. masc. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉู} \emph{-ka} \enquote{you\textsubscript{m,1}} \\
Addressee sing. fem. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉู} \emph{-ki} \enquote{you\textsubscript{f,1}} \\
Addressee dual & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูููุง} \emph{-kumฤ} \enquote{you\textsubscript{2}} \\
Speaker sing. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \enquote{me} \\
Speaker dual & -- \\
\end{longtable}

Now we will learn the attached pronouns for the plural participant:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Participant & Attached pronoun \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Absentee pl. masc. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{-hum} ``them\textsubscript{3,m} \\
Absentee pl. fem. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู} \emph{-hunna} ``them\textsubscript{3,f} \\
Addressee pl. masc. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} \emph{-kum} \enquote{you\textsubscript{3,m}} \\
Addressee pl. fem. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูููู} \emph{-kunna} \enquote{you\textsubscript{3,f}} \\
Speaker pl & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{-nฤ} \enquote{us} \\
\end{longtable}

Note the following points about them:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The plural absentee-participant detached and attached pronouns (\enquote{they\textsubscript{3,m}}/\enquote{them\textsubscript{3,m}}) are the same:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    masculine: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{-hum}.
  \item
    feminine: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู} \emph{-hunna}.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Just like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{hu} \enquote{him} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง} \emph{-humฤ} \enquote{them\textsubscript{2}}, the plural absentee-participant attached pronouns
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{-hum} \enquote{them\textsubscript{3,m}} and
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู} \emph{-hunna} \enquote{them\textsubscript{3,f}}
  become \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง} \emph{-himฤ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู} \emph{-hinna} respectively, when preceded by the vowels \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} \emph{-i}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูู} \emph{-ฤซ}, or the semi-vowel \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โููู} \emph{-ay}. Examples:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจููููู} \emph{bihimฤ} \enquote{with them\textsubscript{3,m}}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููููู} \emph{fฤซhinna} \enquote{in them\textsubscript{3,f}}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููููููู} \emph{สพilayhim} \enquote{to them\textsubscript{3,m}}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The final รธ-mark on the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} in the masculine plural pronouns (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{hum}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชููู} \emph{สพantum}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} \emph{-kum}) becomes a \emph{u}-mark (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{humu}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชููู} \emph{สพantumu}, and\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} \emph{-kumu} respectively) when followed by a connecting hamzah. Examples:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ูฑููููุนููููููููู.}\\
    \emph{humu -lmuษallimลซn.}\\
    \enquote{They\textsubscript{pl.~masc.} are the (male) teachers.}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ุฅูููููฺฉููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
    \emph{dอกhahaba สพilaykumu -rrajul.}\\
    \enquote{The man went to you\textsubscript{3,m}.}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชููู ูฑููููุณูููููููู.}\\
    \emph{สพantumu -lmuslimลซn.}
    \enquote{You\textsubscript{3,m} are the Muslims\textsubscript{3,m}.}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  When the speaker plural attached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} is attached to a word that ends with a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} with a รธ-mark, there is only one \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} written and it is doubled with a doubling mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} on it. So we get:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} + \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} = \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููุง} \emph{minnฤ}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} + \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููู} = \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููุง} \emph{ษannฤ}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} + \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏููู} = \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏููููุง} \emph{ladunnฤ}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{li} \enquote{for} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{la} when followed by the plural attached pronouns:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููู} \emph{lahum} \enquote{for them\textsubscript{3,m}}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููููู} \emph{lahunna} \enquote{for them\textsubscript{3,f}}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉููู} \emph{lakum} \enquote{for you\textsubscript{3,m}}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉูููู} \emph{lakunna} \enquote{for you\textsubscript{3,f}}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง} \emph{lanฤ} \enquote{for us}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Plural doee pronouns}\label{plural-doee-pronouns}

The plural attached pronouns that we have just learned are also used as doee pronouns.
Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููููููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\emph{saสพalahumu -rrajul.}\\
\enquote{The man asked them\textsubscript{3,m}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชูฺฉููู.}\\
\emph{saสพaltukum}\\
\enquote{I asked you\textsubscript{3,m}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชูฺฉูููู.}\\
\emph{saสพalatkunn.}\\
\enquote{She asked you\textsubscript{3,f}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุงููุง.}\\
\emph{saสพalฤnฤ.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{2,m} asked us.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชูุงูู.}\\
\emph{saสพalatฤh.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{3,m} asked him.}

\section{Verbs with plural doers}\label{verbs-with-plural-doers}

\subsection{Plural nouns for the doer}\label{plural-nouns-for-the-doer}

We learned that the completed-action verb for a masculine doer is on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู}. And when the doer is feminine, the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} of femininity is attached to the verb thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชู}. We have used these verbs with singular and dual doer nouns. The doer noun always comes after the verb and shall be in the u-state. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -lgอกhulฤmu.}\\
\enquote{The boy went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabat jฤriyatun}\\
\enquote{A girl went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงููุงูู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -lgอกhulฤmฤni.}\\
\enquote{The boys\textsubscript{2} went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฌูุงุฑูููุชูุงูู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabat jฤriyatฤni.}\\
\enquote{Two girls went.}

These same verbs are used when the doer noun is a plural. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููููุนููููููููู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -lmuษallimลซn.}\\
\enquote{The teacherm\textsubscript{3,m} went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ููุนููููููุงุชู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabat muษallimฤt.}\\
\enquote{Teachers\textsubscript{3,f} went.}

\subsection{Plural pronouns for the doer}\label{plural-pronouns-for-the-doer}

We have already learned the singular and dual doer pronouns. They are repeated here:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}

Now we will learn the plural doer pronouns:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}

Absentee pl. fem. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{-na} & \enquote{they\textsubscript{3,f}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู} \emph{faษalna} \\
