Speaker pl. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{-nฤ} & \enquote{we} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููุง} \emph{faษalnฤ} \\
\end{longtable}

Note the following regarding the plural doer pronouns:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชู} of femininity does not attach to the absentee plural feminine doer pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{-na} \enquote{they\textsubscript{3,f}} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู}. Example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจููู} \emph{dอกhahabna} \enquote{they\textsubscript{3,f} went}
  \end{itemize}

  This is different from the behavior of the absentee dual doer pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูุง} \emph{-ฤ} \enquote{them\textsubscript{2,f}} which, for a feminine doer, does attach to the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชู} of femininity. Example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชูุง} \emph{dอกhahabatฤ} \enquote{they\textsubscript{2,f} went}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The final รธ-mark on the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} in the masculine plural doer pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชููู} \emph{-tum} becomes a \emph{u}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชููู} \emph{-tumu} when followed by a connecting hamzah. Examples:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉูููุชููู ุฎูุจูุฒูุง.}\\
    \emph{สพakaltum kอกhubzฤ.}\\
    \enquote{You\textsubscript{3,m} ate some bread.}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉูููุชููู ูฑููุฎูุจูุฒู.}\\
    \emph{สพakaltumu -lkอกhubz.}\\
    \enquote{You\textsubscript{3,m} ate the bread.}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The absentee plural masculine verb doer pronoun \enquote{they\textsubscript{3,m}} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{ลซ} is written with a silent สพalif after it which is written only and not pronounced. This สพalif is dropped when a doee pronoun is attached. For example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจููุง ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
    \emph{แธarabu -rrajul.}\\
    ``They\textsubscript{3,m} hit the man.
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจูููู.}\\
    \emph{แธarabลซh.}\\
    \enquote{They\textsubscript{3,m} hit him.}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The plural masculine verb doer pronoun for the addressed person \enquote{you\textsubscript{3,m}} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชููู} \emph{-tum} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููู} \emph{tumลซ} when a doee pronoun is attached. For example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจูุชููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
    \emph{แธarabtumu -rrajul.}\\
    \enquote{You\textsubscript{3,m} hit the man.}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจูุชูููููู.}\\
    \emph{แธarabtumลซh.}\\
    \enquote{You\textsubscript{pl.~masc.} hit him.}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The plural speaking participant doer pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{-nฤ} is the same as the plural speaking participant attached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{-nฤ}. But you can tell them apart because the doer pronoun, when attached to the verb, causes the final letter of the verb to have a รธ-mark. Consider the following two sentences:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููููุง.}\\
  \emph{saสพalnฤ.}\\
  \enquote{We asked.}

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููููุง.}\\
  \emph{saสพalanฤ.}\\
  \enquote{He asked us.}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Sentence word order with plural doers}\label{sentence-word-order-with-plural-doers}

As we've mentioned, the doer, whether a noun or a pronoun, always comes after the verb. Here are a couple of examples of verbal sentences with plural doers:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููููุนููููููููู ุฅูููููฐ ููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -lmuษallimลซna สพilฤ madrasah.}\\
\enquote{The teachers\textsubscript{3,m} went to a school.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจููุง ุฅูููููฐ ููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabฤ สพilฤ madrasah.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{3,m} went to a school.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจูุชู ูฑูุตููุฏููููุงุชู ููู ูฑููุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{laษibati -แนฃแนฃadฤซqฤtu fi -lbayt.}\\
\enquote{The friends\textsubscript{3,f} played in the house.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจููู ููู ูฑููุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{laษibna fi -lbayt.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{3,f} played in the house.}

The above verbal sentences with plural doers can be rearranged to be a subject-information sentences. This gives more emphasis to the subject. In this case, the verb shall follow the subject and will need a doer pronoun after it.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุนููููููููู ุฐูููุจููุง ุฅูููููฐ ููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพalmuษallimลซna dอกhahabลซ สพilฤ madrasah.}\\
\enquote{The teachers\textsubscript{3,m}, they\textsubscript{3,m} went to a school.}
= \enquote{\emph{The teachers\textsubscript{3,m}} went to a school.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุตููุฏููููุงุชู ููุนูุจููู ููู ูฑููุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{สพaแนฃแนฃadฤซqฤtu laษibna fi -lbayt.}\\
\enquote{The friends\textsubscript{3,f}, they\textsubscript{3,f} played in the house.}
= \enquote{\emph{The friends\textsubscript{3,f}} played in the house.}

If there are multiple verbs associated with the same doer in a verbal sentence, the doer noun will follow the first verb and the rest of the verbs will have doer pronouns. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู ูฑููููุงุนูุจูููู ููุดูุฑูุจููุง ููุฐูููุจููุง.}\\
\emph{สพakala -llฤษibลซna wasอกharibลซ wadอกhahabลซ.}\\
\enquote{The players\textsubscript{3,m} ate and they\textsubscript{3,m} drank and they\textsubscript{3,m} went.}\\
= \enquote{The players\textsubscript{3,m} ate and drank and went.}

The above verbal sentence can be rearranged to be a subject-information sentence. In that case, all the verbs shall have doer pronouns. The sentence will have the same translation as above, except for an emphasis on the subject of the sentence.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุงุนูุจูููู ุฃูฺฉููููุง ููุดูุฑูุจููุง ููุฐูููุจููุง.}\\
\emph{สพallฤษibลซna สพakalลซ wasอกharibลซ wadอกhahabลซ.}\\
\enquote{The players\textsubscript{3,m}, they\textsubscript{3,m} ate and they\textsubscript{3,m} drank and they\textsubscript{3,m} went.}\\
= \enquote{\emph{The players\textsubscript{3,m}} ate and drank and went.}

Similarly,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉูููุชู ูฑููููุงุนูุจูุงุชู ููุดูุฑูุจููู ููุฐูููุจููู.}\\
\emph{สพakalati -llฤษibฤtu wasอกharibna wadอกhahabn.}\\
\enquote{The players\textsubscript{3,f} ate and they\textsubscript{3,f} drank and they\textsubscript{3,f} went.}

and

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุงุนูุจูุงุชู ุฃูฺฉููููู ููุดูุฑูุจููู ููุฐูููุจููู.}\\
\emph{สพallฤษibฤtu สพakalna wasอกharibna wadอกhahabn.}\\
\enquote{The players\textsubscript{3,f}, they\textsubscript{3,f} ate and they\textsubscript{3,f} drank and they\textsubscript{3,f} went.}\\
= \enquote{\emph{The players\textsubscript{3,f}} ate and drank and went.}

\subsection{Verbs with multiple doers mentioned individually}\label{verbs-with-multiple-doers-mentioned-individually}

If there are multiple doers of a verb, and each is mentioned individually, then there is often more than one way to handle them. Here we will give the more common usage.

If the verb is followed by multiple doers, only the first is the true doer with respect to modifying the verb according to its gender and number. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ูฑููุฃูููู ูููฑููุบูููุงูู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabati -lสพummu wa-lgอกhulฤmu.}\\
\enquote{The mother and the boy went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ูููฑููุฃูููู .}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -lgอกhulฤmu wa -lสพummu.}\\
\enquote{The boy and the mother went.}

If the doers consist of different persons (speaking person, addressed person, and absent person), then they are placed in order of strength: The speaking person is stronger than the addressed person, who is stronger than the absent person. The verb doer pronoun of the first (true) doer is then used. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฃูููุง ููุฃูููุชู ูููููู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabtu สพana waสพanta wahuwa.}\\
\enquote{I, you, and he went.}

Note how the speaking person detached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง} \emph{สพana} is used in addition to the doer pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชู} \emph{-tu} in order to add \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{wa} \enquote{and} to it.

If the sentence is a subject information sentence, and the verb is in the information, then the doer pronoun corresponding to the number of the subject is used. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููุชู ูููููู ุฐูููุจูุชูููุง.}\\
\emph{สพanta wahuwa dอกhahabtumฤ.}\\
\enquote{You\textsubscript{1,m} and he, you\textsubscript{2} went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง ููููุญููููุฏู ุฐูููุจูููุง.}\\
\emph{สพana wamuแธฅammadun dอกhahabnฤ.}\\
\enquote{I and Muแธฅammad, we went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฃูููู ูููฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ุฐูููุจูุชูุง.}\\
\emph{สพalสพummu wa-ljฤriyatu dอกhahabatฤ.}\\
\enquote{The mother and the girl went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฃูููู ูููฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุชูุงูู ุฐูููุจููู.}\\
\emph{สพalสพummu wa-ljฤriyatฤni dอกhahabna.}\\
\enquote{The mother and the two girls, they\textsubscript{3,f} went.}

If the doers consist of both male and female persons, then the verb will have the masculine doer prenoun corresponding to the number of the doers. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฃูููู ูููฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ูููฑููุบูููุงูู ุฐูููุจููุง.}\\
\emph{สพalสพummu wa-ljฤriyatu wa-lgอกhulฤmu dอกhahabลซ.}\\
\enquote{The mother, the girl, and the boy, they\textsubscript{3,m} went.}

\chapter{Broken plurals}\label{broken-plurals}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-9}

In the previous chapter we introduced sound plurals, which are formed by appending suffixes to the singular noun. The singular noun in these plurals remains, more or less, intact when forming these plurals. The sound plurals correspond to English regular plurals which are formed by appending \enquote{s} to the singular noun. However, English has some plurals that are not formed by adding the plural ending \enquote{s}. Here are some examples,

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Singular & Plural \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
man & men \\
woman & women \\
child & children \\
mouse & mice \\
\end{longtable}

In these plurals, the singular noun is altered to form the plural.

Arabic also forms such plurals. They are called \emph{broken} plurals because the singular noun is not kept intact but its structure is, in most cases, altered, or \enquote{broken-up} when forming the plural.

While English only forms such plurals for a handful of nouns, Arabic forms broken plurals for many nouns.

\section{Review of word patterns and semi-flexible nouns}\label{review-of-word-patterns-and-semi-flexible-nouns}

Before we begin our discussion about broken plurals, we will do a quick review of word patterns and semi-flexible nouns. This will, if Allฤh wills, facilitate the explanation of broken plurals.

Most words in Arabic are formed from three letter roots. We use the paradigm root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุนูยป} to show word patterns. For example, the noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูู} \emph{rajul} \enquote{a man} is formed from the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฑุฌูยป} on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \emph{faษul}.

Most nouns in Arabic are \emph{fully-flexible}. This means that, when indefinite, they take \emph{n}-marks and the i-state is shown by an \emph{in}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} at the end of the noun. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูู} \emph{rajul} \enquote{a man} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุช} \emph{bayt} \enquote{a house} are fully-flexible nouns. So, you can see, below, that they take \emph{n}-marks, and the indefinite i-state is indicated by an \emph{in}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ุฑูุฌููู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba rajulun สพilฤ bayt.}\\
\enquote{A man went to a house.}

Some nouns are \emph{semi-flexible}. This means that they don't take \emph{n} marks, and also, the indefinite i-state is indicated by an \emph{a} mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. Examples of such nouns are:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูููฐ} \emph{gอกhaแธbฤ}\textsuperscript{2} \emph{adj.} (fem.) \enquote{a very angry one\textsubscript{f}} from the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุบุถุจยป}
\item
  \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \emph{แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ}\textsuperscript{2} (fem.) \enquote{a desert\textsubscript{f}} from the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุตุญุฑยป}
\end{itemize}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ุบูุถูุจูููฐ ุฅูููููฐ ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabat jฤriyatun gอกhaแธbฤ สพilฤ แนฃaแธฅrฤสพ.}\\
\enquote{A very angry girl went to a desert.}

When definite, semi-flexible nouns are identical to fully-flexible nouns:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ูฑููุบูุถูุจูููฐ ุฅูููู ูฑูุตููุญูุฑูุงุกู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabati -ljฤriyatu -lgอกhaแธbฤ สพila -แนฃแนฃaแธฅrฤสพ.}\\
\enquote{The very angry girl went to the desert.}

All nouns that have the endings \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}, that are extrinsic to the word's root, are semi-flexible.
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} are also feminine markers for singular nouns, just like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}. (Except that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} does not, in general, make a noun semi-flexible.)

It is important to note that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} are only feminine markers for singular nouns. We will see that they are also endings for broken plural nouns and, in that case, they are not feminine markers. However,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}, when endings for broken plural nouns, will make the broken plural nouns semi-flexible, just as they do for singular nouns.

Nouns that are of the patterns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงููู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงูููู} are also semi-flexible nouns. Here each letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} could be any letter of the alphabet.
These are patterns for broken plurals, as we will see very soon.
We had mentioned this in section~\ref{fafafif-diptote}.

This concludes our short review of word patterns and semi-flexible nouns. We will use these concepts in our discussion of broken plurals.

\section{Patterns of the broken plural}\label{patterns-of-the-broken-plural}

Broken plurals occur in specific patterns, which we will show using the paradigm \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุนูยป} for three-letter roots. Ararbic also has (comparatively fewer) four-letter roots and we will show patterns for broken plurals of four-letter roots using the paradigm root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุนูู}. We will also use the letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, when needed, to indicate any letter of the alphabet.

We now give all but the rarest broken plural patterns below. The singular and plural and given together separated by a colon character \enquote{:}, the singular on the right, and its plural on the left.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \emph{fuษal}}. Examples:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุฉ: ุฃูููู} & a nation & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูฺฉูุจูุฉ: ุฑูฺฉูุจ} & a knee \\
  \end{longtable}

\end{enumerate}

Note the following from the above broken plural patterns and examples:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Both common nouns and adjectival nouns form broken plurals.
\item
  There are comparatively fewer broken plurals for female intelligent beings than for male intelligent beings. We will expand on this in a subsequent section.
\item
  Some patterns of the broken plural are also patterns singular nouns. For example, the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงู} \emph{fiษฤl} has both singular nouns, like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} \emph{kitฤb} \enquote{a book} and broken plurals, like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูุงู} \emph{rijฤl} \enquote{men}
\item
  The broken plural patterns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงู} \emph{fiษlฤn} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงู} \emph{fuษlฤn} are fully-flexible nouns. Although they end with the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงู} ending which is extrinsic to the root, they are not semi-flexible nouns. Only singular adjectival nouns that end with an extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงู} on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงู}, and that also fulfil the other conitions listed in section~\ref{adjectival-noun-an-diptote}, are semi-flexible.
\item
  There is often a correlation between the pattern of a singular noun and the pattern of its plural.

  Sometimes this correlation is very strong:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    All singular nouns of the patterns \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} \emph{สพafษal}\textsuperscript{2} and \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงุก} \emph{faษlฤสพ} that denote colors and physical characteristics, have broken plurals on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \emph{fuษl}. Example:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Singular & Plural \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูููุฑ\textsuperscript{2}ุ ุญูููุฑูุงุก\textsuperscript{2}} \enquote{red} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑ} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูฺฉูู\textsuperscript{2}ุ ุจูฺฉูููุงุก\textsuperscript{2}} \enquote{mute} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูฺฉูู} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \item
    Singular nouns that have four or more consonant letters (excluding \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}) regularly form their broken plurals on the patterns \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงููู} and \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงูููู}. The pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงูููู} is used when there is an intermendiate long vowel between the consonants. Examples:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Singular & Plural \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุตูุจูุน} \enquote{a finger} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุตูุงุจูุน} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชูุงุญ} \enquote{a key} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุชููุญ} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \item
    Singular nouns of the patterns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ} \emph{fiษlah} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ} \emph{fuษlah} regularly form their broken plurals on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \emph{fiษal} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \emph{fuษal} respectively. Examples:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Singular & Plural \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุทูุนูุฉ} \enquote{a piece} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุทูุน} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูฺฉูุจูุฉ} \enquote{a knee} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูฺฉูุจ} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \end{itemize}

  Other times, this correlation is more like a tendency:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Singular nouns on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููุฉ} \emph{faษฤซlah} tend to form broken plurals on the pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงุฆูู} \emph{faษฤสพil}\textsuperscript{2}. Examples:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Singular & Plural \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฏููููุฉ} \enquote{a garden} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฏูุงุฆูู} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููุจูุฉ} \enquote{a bag} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุงุฆูุจ} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \item
    Singular nouns on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูู} \emph{fฤษil}, that denote male intelligent beings, tend to form broken plurals on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{fuษษal}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููุงู} \emph{fuษษฤl}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ} \emph{faษalah}. Examples:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Singular & Plural \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุงุฆูุจ} \enquote{absent} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบููููุจ} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุฑูุฆ} \enquote{a reader\textsubscript{m}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑููุงุก} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูู} \enquote{a killer\textsubscript{m}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูููุฉ} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \item
    Singular nouns on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูู} \emph{fฤษil} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูููุฉ} \emph{fฤษilah}, that don't denote male intelligent beings, tend to form broken plurals on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุนูู} \emph{fawฤษil}. Examples:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Singular & Plural \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุงุญูุจูุฉ} \enquote{a companion\textsubscript{f}} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูููุงุญูุจ} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููู} \enquote{a factor} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุงููู} \\
    \end{longtable}

    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุฑูุณ} \emph{fฤris} \enquote{a horseman} with the plural \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุฑูุณ} is one of a number of exceptions.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Some words have roots that have the same letter repeated in the root. These are called \emph{doubled} roots.

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    For example:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Root & Word & Pattern \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฏุจูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุจู} \enquote{a bear} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญููููุฉ} \enquote{a locality} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููุฉ} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููุงู} \enquote{a leader} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงู} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููู} \enquote{a truth, a right} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุฑูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑู} \enquote{a cat\textsubscript{m}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \end{itemize}

  We will discuss doubled roots in detail in chapter~\ref{doubled-roots}. For now we will mention the following:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    The repeated letter in the word root may get doubled or separated in the word's pattern. Frequently, the repeated letter may be doubled in the singular, and separated in the plural. Examples:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Singular & Plural \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููู} \enquote{a truth, a right} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููู} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุจู} \enquote{a bear} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุจูุจูุฉ} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑู} \enquote{a cat\textsubscript{m}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฑูุฉ} \\
    \end{longtable}

    The reverse also occurs, where the repeated letter may be separated in the singular, and doubled in the plural. Examples:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Singular & Plural \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููุงู} \enquote{a leader\textsubscript{m}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฆููููุฉ} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \item
    The doubled letter may modify the basic word pattern somewhat. For example:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1818}}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Root
    \end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Word pattern
    \end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Expected word
    \end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Actual word
    \end{minipage} \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุดุฏูยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูููุงุก} & \(\times\)~\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุดูุฏูุฏูุงุก} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุดูุฏููุงุก} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญููยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุนูู} & \(\times\)~\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญูุงููู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญูุงูู} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุตููยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูู} & \(\times\)~\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุตูููู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุตููู} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  We have previously learned that the endings \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} that are extrinsic to the word's root are feminine markers for singular nouns. These extrinsic endings also occur for broken plurals but there, they are \emph{not} feminine markers.

  In fact, in a sort of role reversal, the endings \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} in a broken plural tends to indicate that the singular is a masculine noun. And the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} ending is only for broken plurals of male intelligent beings. Examples:

  \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
  \toprule\noalign{}
  Singular & Plural \\
  \midrule\noalign{}
  \endhead
  \bottomrule\noalign{}
  \endlastfoot
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุงู} \enquote{a tongue} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุณูููุฉ} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑู} \enquote{a cat\textsubscript{m}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฑูุฉ} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุฑ} \enquote{a commander\textsubscript{m}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฑูุงุก} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุฏููู} \enquote{a friend\textsubscript{m}} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุตูุฏูููุงุก} \\
  \end{longtable}
\item
  There often exist multiple broken plurals for the same singular noun. Many times, in fact, a singular noun may have a sound plural in addition to one or more broken plurals. Examples:

  \begin{longtable}[]{@{}lr@{}}
  \toprule\noalign{}
  Singular & Plural \\
  \midrule\noalign{}
  \endhead
  \bottomrule\noalign{}
  \endlastfoot
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุฑ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุดูููุฑุ ุดููููุฑ} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนููููุ ุนูููููุ ุฃูุนูููุงู} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงูููููููุ ุนูููุงููู\textsuperscript{2}ุ ุนูููููุฉุ ุนููููุงู} \\
  \end{longtable}

  We will discuss how to manage these multiple plurals in a subsequent section.
\item
  Occasionally, multiple singular nouns will share the same broken plural. Examples:

  \begin{longtable}[]{@{}lr@{}}
  \toprule\noalign{}
  Singular & Plural \\
  \midrule\noalign{}
  \endhead
  \bottomrule\noalign{}
  \endlastfoot
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉูุชูุจ} \enquote{an office} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉูุงุชูุจ} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉูุชูุจูุฉ} \enquote{a library} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉูุงุชูุจ} \\
  \end{longtable}

  Context will then tell us which of two meanings is intended.
\item
  The letters \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} are considered \emph{weak} letters. Words that one or more these weak letters in their roots are called \emph{defective} words. We will discuss defective words more completely in later chapters, if Allฤh wills. For now, we will note the following:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Weak letters often get interchanged with one another when going from a singular to a plural. Examples:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Root & Singular & Plural \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃุฑุฎยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุฃูุฑููุฎ} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุงุฑููุฎ} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงููุฉ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุซูุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูููุฑ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซููุฑูุงู} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \item
    Weak letters can affect surrounding vowels. For example:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}llll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Root & Word pattern & Expected word & Actual word \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุจูุถยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} & \(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุถ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจููุถ} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \item
    The weak letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, when followed by the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} ending, usually modifies (in writing) it to an \emph{ูสพalif} instead. The pronunciation is the same. For example:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1818}}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Root
    \end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Word pattern
    \end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Expected word
    \end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Actual word
    \end{minipage} \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุฏูยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงููููฐ} & \(\times\)~\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏูุงููููฐ} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏูุงููุง} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \item
    A \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} at the end of a word, in some states, gets omitted and replaced by an \emph{in}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} on the preceding letter. This happens even when the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} is extrinsic to the root, and even if the word is semi-flexible (and thus would not normally accept an \emph{n} mark). Examples:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1818}}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
      >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Root
    \end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Word pattern
    \end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Expected word
    \end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
    Actual word
    \end{minipage} \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุถูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูู} & \(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุถูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุถู} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฌุฑูยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุนูู} & \(\times\)~\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุงุฑูู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุงุฑู} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูููยป} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงููู} & \(\times\)~\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงููู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงูู} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \item
    Weak letters can also get omitted in the singular and resurface in the plural. Examples:

    \begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
    \toprule\noalign{}
    Root & Singular & Plural \\
    \midrule\noalign{}
    \endhead
    \bottomrule\noalign{}
    \endlastfoot
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃุฎูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุฎูููุงูุ ุฅูุฎูููุฉ} \\
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฉ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููุงุก} \\
    \end{longtable}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  If there are more than four consonant letters in a word, then only four of them are selected to form the broken plural. For example:

  \begin{longtable}[]{@{}lr@{}}
  \toprule\noalign{}
  Singular & Plural \\
  \midrule\noalign{}
  \endhead
  \bottomrule\noalign{}
  \endlastfoot
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููฺฉูุจููุช} \enquote{a spider} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุงฺฉูุจ} \\
  \end{longtable}
\item
  Some words have individual irrgularities as well and we will discuss them below:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃูุฉ} and its plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุงุก} are both irregular and we will discuss them separately in chapter~\ref{irregular-nouns}.
  \item
    The broken plural \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุดูููุงุก} \emph{สพasอกhyฤสพ} (of the singular noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุก} \emph{sอกhayสพ} \enquote{a thing}) is irregular in that it is semi-flexible. Otherwise its pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูุงู} \emph{สพafษฤl} is regularly fully-flexible.
  \item
    The broken plural of the singular noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฺฉ} \emph{malak} \enquote{an angel} is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุฆูฺฉูุฉ} \emph{malฤสพikah}. It is on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงููููุฉ} \emph{faษฤlilah}. But it is unusual in that the plural has an extra letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก} that is missing in the singular. This is because the singular has a lesser-used variant: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุฃูฺฉ} \emph{malสพak} that is used to form the plural.
  \item
    The broken plural of the singular noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏููููุงุฑ} \enquote{a dฤซnฤr} is \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูููุงูููุฑ}. It is on the pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงูููู}. It is irregular in that there are two \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}'s in the plural whereas the singular only has one.
  \item
    The root of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุฆูุฑ} \emph{biสพr} \enquote{a (water) well} is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุจุฃุฑยป}. The pattern of its broken plural is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูุงู}. Based on its root letters, its plural on this pattern ought regularly to have been \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูุขุฑ} \emph{สพabสพฤr}. And this plural exists but is not very commonly used. Instead, in forming the plural, the root letters \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃ} get swapped irregularly, and the more commonly used plural is actually \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุขุจูุงุฑ} \emph{'สพฤbฤr}.
  \end{itemize}

  There are other words as well with similar irregularities.
\end{itemize}

\section{Co-existence of multiple broken plurals}\label{co-existence-of-multiple-broken-plurals}

We noted that there are often multiple broken plurals for the same singular noun. Many singular nouns even have a sound plural in addition to one or more broken plurals. Here are some examples.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Singular
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Meanings
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedleft
Plural
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุฏูุงุฑ} & a wall & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุฏูุฑุ ุฌูุฏูุฑูุงู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุฑ} & a month & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุดูููุฑุ ุดููููุฑ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุนููู} & a weak one\textsubscript{m} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุนูุงูุ ุถูุนูููุงุก} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฑ} & a matter; a command & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุฑุ ุฃูููุงููุฑ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููู} & an eye; a (water) spring; an eminent person & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนููููุ ุนูููููุ ุฃูุนูููุงู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููู} & a worker; a labourer; a factor & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงูููููููุ ุนูููุงููู\textsuperscript{2}ุ ุนูููููุฉุ ุนููููุงู} \\
\end{longtable}

We will deal with the co-existence of sound and broken plurals in the next section. In this section, we will explain the existence of multiple broken plurals, and when one of them is preferred or required to be used over the other. Basically, there could be a few things going on:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Sometimes it is more or less optional which of the multiple broken plurals to use. For example, the singular noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุฏูุงุฑ} has two broken plurals:
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุฏูุฑุ ุฌูุฏูุฑูุงู}
  Either could be used, more or less, interchangeably.
\item
  Sometimes, the usage of one of the plurals may be restricted. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุนูุงู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุนูููุงุก} are both broken plurals of the masculine adjectival noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุนููู} \enquote{a weak one\textsubscript{m}}. For male intelligent beings, like \enquote{weak men}, either of the two plurals could be used. But remember that broken plurals that end with an extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} ending may only be used for male intelligent beings. So the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุนูููุงุก} may only be used for male intelligent beings like \enquote{men} or \enquote{boys}, and not for masculine nouns that denote non-intelligent beings like \enquote{lions} or \enquote{pens}, etc.

  Interestingly, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุนูุงู} is also shared as the broken plural for the feminine adjectival noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุนููููุฉ} \enquote{a weak one\textsubscript{f}}. So it can be used for plurals of feminine nouns, both for female intelligent beings like \enquote{women} and \enquote{girls}, and for feminie nouns that denote non-intelligent beings like \enquote{trees}.
\item
  Other times, the singular has multiple distinct meanings, and each of these distinct meanings is associated with its own broken plural(s). Here are some examples:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฑ} \emph{สพamr} has two distinct meanings, each with it's own plural:

    \begin{enumerate}
    \def\labelenumii{\roman{enumii}.}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \enquote{a matter}. This has the broken plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุฑ} \emph{สพumลซr}.
    \item
      \enquote{a command}. This has the broken plural \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุงููุฑ} \emph{สพawฤmir}\textsuperscript{2}.
    \end{enumerate}
  \item
    The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููู} \emph{สพayn} has multiple distinct meanings. There are three main meanings, and they share the broken plural with each other in the following way:

    \begin{enumerate}
    \def\labelenumii{\roman{enumii}.}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \enquote{an eye}. This meaning primarily uses the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนูููู} \emph{สพaษyun} but it may also use the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู} \emph{ษuyลซn}, and rarely also the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนูููุงู} \emph{สพaษyฤn}.
    \item
      \enquote{a (water) spring}. This meaning primarily uses the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู} \emph{ษuyลซn} but it may also use the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนูููู} \emph{สพaษyun}, and rarely also the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนูููุงู} \emph{สพaษyฤn}.
    \item
      \enquote{an eminent person}. This meaning only uses the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนูููุงู} \emph{สพaษyฤn}.
    \end{enumerate}
  \item
    The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููู} \emph{ษฤmil} has the following meanings and plurals:

    \begin{enumerate}
    \def\labelenumii{\roman{enumii}.}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \enquote{a worker\textsubscript{m}}. Generally, this has the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููุงู} \emph{ษummฤl}.
    \item
      \enquote{a labourer\textsubscript{m}}. This uses the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููููุฉ} \emph{ษamalah}.
    \item
      \enquote{a factor}. This uses the plural \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุงููู} \emph{ษawฤmil}\textsuperscript{2}.
    \end{enumerate}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Arabic has what are known as \emph{plurals of fewness}. These are specific patterns that may (sometimes, but not always) be used when the persons or things denoted by the plural are only a few (ten or less) and not many. These patterns are:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\roman{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ} \emph{fiษlah}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูู} \emph{สพafษul}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูุงู} \emph{สพafษฤl}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูููุฉ} \emph{สพafษilah}
  \end{enumerate}

  For example:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\roman{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุฑ} \emph{sอกhahr} \enquote{a month}, plurals: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุดูููุฑุ ุดููููุฑ}. The plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุดูููุฑ} could be used when the number of months are only a few (ten or less), and the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดููููุฑ} could be used when the number of months are large.
  \item
    The plurals \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนูููู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู} of the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููู} could also possibly be used similarly in this manner for both meanings: \enquote{an eye} and \enquote{a (water) spring}. (But not for the meaning \enquote{an eminent person} which only uses the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนูููุงู}).
  \end{enumerate}

  Of course, this distinction only applies when the singular noun has additional plurals, not just one from the above four patterns. If a noun has only one of the about four plural patterns then it may be used indiscriminately and will not indicate any limitation in number.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Co-existence of sound and broken plurals}\label{co-existence-of-sound-and-broken-plurals}

Some nouns have both sound and broken plurals for more or less the same meaning. Here are some examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}llll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Singular & Meaning & Sound plural & Broken plural \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูู} & a killer & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูููุฉ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงููุฑ} & a disbeliever & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงููุฑูููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููููุงุฑ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑ} & a big one\textsubscript{m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑูููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจูุงุฑ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑูุฉ} & a big one\textsubscript{f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑูุงุช} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจูุงุฑ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} & a small one\textsubscript{m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑูููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบูุงุฑ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑูุฉ} & a small one\textsubscript{f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑูุงุช} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบูุงุฑ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุงฺฉูุน} & one who bows\textsubscript{m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุงฺฉูุนูููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูฺฉููุน} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุงฺฉูุนูุฉ} & one who bows\textsubscript{f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุงฺฉูุนูุงุช} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูฺฉููุน} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุงุญูุจูุฉ} & a companion\textsubscript{f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุงุญูุจูุงุช} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูููุงุญูุจ} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉ} & a girl & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฑูููุงุช} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุงุฑู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฏููููุฉ} & a garden & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุฏููููุงุช} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฏูุงุฆูู} \\
\end{longtable}

We will treat the \emph{ลซn} and \emph{ฤt} sound plurals separately.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\emph{ลซn} plurals and broken plurals}{ลซn plurals and broken plurals}}\label{un-plurals-and-broken-plurals}

Remember from chapter~\ref{sound-plurals} that \emph{ลซn} plurals are, with very few exceptions, only used for male intelligent beings.

If a singular noun has both an \emph{ลซn} sound plural and one or more broken plurals, then the use of the broken plural is generally preferred. The sound plural is then, generally, reserved for certain verbal usages. (We will study these in later chapters, if Allฤh wills.)

So, for example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูููุฉ} is preferred over \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูููููู} generally for the meaning: \enquote{killers}.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\emph{ฤt} plurals and broken plurals}{ฤt plurals and broken plurals}}\label{at-plurals-and-broken-plurals}

\emph{ฤt} plurals are used for both female intelligent beings and non-intelligent beings. We will discuss each of these separately.

\subsubsection{Female intelligent beings}\label{female-intelligent-beings}

Remember from section~\ref{conditions-for-the-at-plural} that, generally, all nouns that end with feminine markers
(\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ})
can form the \emph{ฤt} sound plural.

There are some nouns that are excepted from this statement. These nouns only have broken plurals and don't form sound plurals. For female intelligent beings, these nouns are:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Adjectival nouns of the pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงุก} which is the feminine of the masculine adjectival noun pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูู}. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญูุฑยป} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงุก} \emph{แธฅawrฤสพ} \enquote{a beautiful eyed one\textsubscript{f}} uses the broken plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููุฑ} \emph{แธฅลซr}
\item
  Adjectival nouns of the pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููฐ} which is the feminine of the masculine adjectival noun pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงู}. For example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุบุถุจยป} \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุจูููฐ} \emph{gอกhaแธbฤ} \enquote{very angry\textsubscript{f}} uses the broken plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุถูุงุจ} \emph{gอกhiแธฤb}.
\item
  The following exceptional nouns:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃูุฉ} \enquote{a woman}, broken plural: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุงุก}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฉ} \enquote{a slave\textsubscript{f}}, broken plural: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููุงุก}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

In the case of these nouns we have no choice but to use the broken plural.

For other nouns that denote female intelligent beings, the use of the \emph{ฤt} sound plural is preferred over any broken plurals that the noun may have.

So, for example,
the use of the \emph{ฤt} sound plural
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑูุงุช}
is preferred over the broken plural
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบูุงุฑ}
for the adjectival noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑูุฉ}
\enquote{a small one\textsubscript{f}}

The following are excepted from this general statement:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุซูููฐ} \enquote{a female}, plural: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููุงุซ}. The \emph{ฤt} sound plural is almost unused for this word.
\item
  Broken plurals of the patterns:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุนูู} \emph{fawฤษil}\textsuperscript{2}
  \item
    \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงุฆูู} \emph{faษฤสพil}\textsuperscript{2}
  \end{itemize}

  These broken plural patterns are, in fact, predominantly used for female intelligent beings and non-intelligent beings, and only rarely for male intelligent beings. So the broken plural \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุงุฑู} \enquote{girls} may be used freely as the plural of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉ} \enquote{a girl} and is not preferred over by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฑูููุงุช}. Similarly, \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูููุงุญูุจ} may freely be used as the plural of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุงุญูุจูุฉ}.

  Only a few nouns denoting male intelligent beings have broken plurals on these patterns, like:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุฑูุณ} \enquote{a horseman}, plural: \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุฑูุณ}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎููููููุฉ} \enquote{a successor}, plural: \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูููุงุฆูู}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

In conclusion, with the general preference of using the \emph{ฤt} sound plural over the broken plural for female intelligent beings, you will find that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุงุก} \emph{nisฤสพ} \enquote{women} is the only widely found broken plural for female intelligent beings in normal usage.

\subsubsection{Non-intelligent beings}\label{non-intelligent-beings}

For non-intelligent beings, the broken plural is preferred for use over \emph{ฤt} sound plurals.

So, for example, \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฏูุงุฆูู} \emph{แธฅadฤสพiq}\textsuperscript{2} is preferred over \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฏููููุงุช} \emph{แธฅadฤซqฤt} as the plural of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฏููููุฉ}, though both are correct.

\section{Usage of plurals of intelligent beings}\label{usage-of-plurals-of-intelligent-beings}

We will now discuss how plurals are used in Arabic. Using plurals is more complicated than using duals.

In order to explain their usage systematically, we will treat plurals of intelligent beings separately from the plurals of non-intelligent beings.

The usage of plurals of intelligent beings is more straightforward and in line with what we have studied for duals. We will discuss descriptive noun-phrases, subject-information sentences, and verbal sentences.

\subsection{Plurals in descriptive noun-phrases}\label{plurals-in-descriptive-noun-phrases}

Consistent with what we have learned so far, when the describee in a noun-phrase is plural, then the describer comes after it, and matches it in state, definiteness, gender, and number.

Either or both of the describer and the describee may be sound plurals or broken plurals.

Here are some examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจู ูฑูุทูููููู ูฑูุตููุบููุฑู ููุนู ูฑููุบูููููุงูู ูฑููฺฉูุจูุงุฑู.}\\
\emph{laษiba -แนญแนญiflu -แนฃแนฃagอกhฤซru maษa -lgอกhilmฤni -lkibฤr.}\\
\enquote{The small child played with the big boys.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐู ูฑูุชูููููููุฐู ูฑููุนููููู ุนููู ูฑููููุนููููููููู ูฑููฺฉูุฑูุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพakอกhadอกha -ttilmฤซdอกhu -lษilma ษani -lmuษallimฤซna -lkirฤm.}\\
\enquote{The pupil took knowledge from the noble teachers.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ุตูููุงุญูุจู ุทููููุจูุงุชู.}\\
\emph{liljฤriyati แนฃawฤhibu แนญayyibฤt.}\\
\enquote{The girl has good companions\textsubscript{f}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู ูฑูุณููููู ุชูุฌููุงุฑู ุตูุงุฏูููููู.}\\
\emph{fi -ssลซqi tujjฤrun แนฃadiqลซn.}\\
\enquote{In the market are honest traders.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุฏููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ูฑูุตููุงููุญู ูฑููุบูููููู ูฑููููููุฑูุงุกู ูฑูุถููุนูุงูู ูููู ูฑููููุชูุงููููฐ ูฑูุตููุบูุงุฑู.}\\
\emph{kอกhadama -rrajulu -แนฃแนฃฤliแธฅu -lgอกhaniyyu -lfuqarฤสพa -แธแธiษฤfa mina -lyatฤmฤ -แนฃแนฃigอกhฤr.}\\
\enquote{The rich righteous man served the weak poor ones from the little orphans.}

\subsection{Plurals in subject-information sentences}\label{plurals-in-subject-information-sentences}

If the subject of a sentence is a plural denoting intelligent beings then the information typically matches it in being a plural. This is especially the case if the information is an adjectival noun. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุบูููููุงูู ุฃูุทูููุงูู ุทููููุจูููู.}\\
\emph{สพalgอกhilmฤnu สพaแนญfฤlun แนญayyibลซn.}\\
\enquote{The boys are good children.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูุงูู ุฃูุบูููููุงุกู.}\\
\emph{สพarrijฤlu สพagอกhniyฤสพ.}\\
\enquote{The men are rich.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุนููููููุงุชู ุนูุงููููุงุชู.}\\
\emph{สพalmuษallimฤtu ษฤlimฤt,}\\
\enquote{The teachers\textsubscript{f} are scholars\textsubscript{f}.}

Sometimes the information may not match the subject in plurality because of the meaning of the sentence. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุณูููููููู ุฃููููุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพalmuslimลซna สพummah.}\\
\enquote{The Muslims are a nation.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌููุฑูุงูู ูฑูุทูููููุจูููู ููุนูููุฉู ูููู ูฑููููููฐูู.}\\
\emph{สพaljฤซrฤnu -แนญแนญayyibลซna niษmatun mina -llฤh.}\\
\enquote{Good neighbors are a blessing from Allah.}

With regards to detached pronouns, the same detached pronouns are used with detached plurals that we learned for in section~\ref{detached-plural-pronouns} for sound plurals. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชูููู ููุณูุงุกู ฺฉูุฑููููุงุชู.}\\
\emph{สพantunna nisฤสพun karฤซmฤt.}\\
\enquote{You\textsubscript{3,f} are generous women.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุชููู ุดูุจููุงูู ุดูุฌูุนูุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพantum sอกhubbฤnun sอกhujษฤnun}\\
\enquote{You\textsubscript{m,3} are courageous young men.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููููุงุทูููู ูููู ูฑููููููุงุนููู.}\\
\emph{สพasอกhsอกhayฤแนญฤซnu humu -lmalฤษฤซn.}\\
\enquote{The devils are the accursed ones.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู ููุณูุงุกู ุบููููููุงุชู.}\\
\emph{hunna nisฤสพun gอกhaniyyฤt.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{3,f} are rich women.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญููู ุบูููููุงูู ุฃูุตูุฏูููุงุกู.}\\
\emph{naแธฅnu gอกhilmฤnun สพaแนฃdiqฤสพ.}\\
\enquote{We are boys who are friends.}

\subsection{Plurals with verbs}\label{plurals-with-verbs}

We have already studied verbs with sound plurals in section~\ref{verbs-with-plural-doers}. The same discussion applies to broken plurals as well. The doer and doee pronouns are the same. Here are a couple of examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃูุชู ูฑููููุณูุงุกู ููฺฉูุชูุจููู.}\\
\emph{qaraสพati -nnisฤสพu wakatabn.}\\
\enquote{The women read and wrote.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุบูููููุงูู ููุนูุจููุง ุจูฺฉูุฑูุฉู ุญูููุฑูุงุกู.}\\
\emph{สพalgอกhilmฤnu laษibลซ bikuratin แธฅamrฤสพ.}\\
\enquote{The boys, they played with a red ball.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูุจูุฎูุชู ูฑููููุณูุงุกู ุทูุนูุงููุง ูููุฑููุฌูุงู ููุฃูฺฉูููููู ููุดูฺฉูุฑููููููู.}\\
\emph{แนญabakอกhati -nnisฤสพu แนญaษฤman lirrijฤli faสพakalลซhu wasอกhakarลซhunn.}\\
``The women prepared some food for the men, so they\textsubscript{3,m} ate it and they\textsubscript{3,m} thanked them\textsubscript{3,f}.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธููููู ูฑููุฌูุจูุงุจูุฑูุฉู ูฑููููุณูุงฺฉูููู ููููุชูููููููู.}\\
\emph{dฬฃอกhalama -ljabฤbiratu -lmasฤkฤซna waqatalลซhum.}\\
\enquote{The tyrants wronged the needy ones\textsubscript{3,m} and killed them\textsubscript{3,m}.}

\section{Usage of plurals of non-intelligent beings}\label{usage-of-plurals-of-non-intelligent-beings}

We now turn our attention to plurals of non-intelligent beings. They treatment of plurals of non-intelligent beings is very different from everything we have learned so far. Regardless of the grammatical or physical gender of the singular noun, plurals of non-intelligent beings are treated, for the purposes of matching adjectival nouns and pronouns, as:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  grammatically feminine singular
\item
  grammatically feminine plural
\end{enumerate}

It is optional which of the above two treatments one uses. However, the former option (feminine singular) is more common and is generally preferred.

For the second option (feminine plural), in addition to the sound feminine plural of adjectival nouns, broken plurals are allowed to be used as well, as long as their meaning allows them to be used for non-intelligent beings.

So, for example, the noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจููุช} \emph{bayt} denotes the inanimate object \enquote{a house}. It's plural is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจููููุช}. This plural is treated as either feminine singular or feminine plural. This is despite the fact that the singular noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุช} \emph{bayt} \enquote{a house} is grammatically masculine. See how the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจููููุช} \emph{buyลซt} is used in the examples below:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจููููุชู ฺฉูุจููุฑูุฉู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจููููุชู ฺฉูุจููุฑูุงุชู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจููููุชู ฺฉูุจูุงุฑู.}\\
\enquote{The houses are big.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูฺฉููููุง ููู ุจููููุชู ุตูุบููุฑูุฉู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูฺฉููููุง ููู ุจููููุชู ุตูุบููุฑูุงุชู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูฺฉููููุง ููู ุจููููุชู ุตูุบูุงุฑู.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{3,m} lived in ssmall houses.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุทูุชู ูฑููุจููููุชู.}\\
\enquote{The houses fell.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจููููุชู ุณูููุทูุชู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจููููุชู ุณูููุทููู.}\\
\enquote{The houses, they fell.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุจููููุชู ููููููููุฑูุงุกู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู ุจููููุชู ููููููููุฑูุงุกู.}\\
\enquote{They are houses for the poor.}

Plurals of inanimate objects and animals (both male and female) are treated the same way. It doesn't matter what the grammatical or physical gender of the singular is or whether it has a sound or broken plural. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุซููุฑูุงูู ููุญูุดูุฉู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุซููุฑูุงูู ููุญููุดู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู ุซููุฑูุงูู ููุญูุดูุงุชู.}\\
\enquote{They are wild bulls.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฑูุฑูุฉู ุดูุฑูุจูุชู ูฑููุญููููุจู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฑูุฑูุฉู ุดูุฑูุจููู ูฑููุญููููุจู.}\\
\enquote{The cats\textsubscript{m}, they drank the milk.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฑูุฑู ุดูุฑูุจูุชู ูฑููุญููููุจู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฑูุฑู ุดูุฑูุจููู ูฑููุญููููุจู.}\\
\enquote{The cats\textsubscript{f}, they drank the milk.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุณูููููู ุทููููููุฉ.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุณูููููู ุทูููุงูู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุณูููููู ุทููููููุงุชู.}\\
\enquote{The ships are tall.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู ูฑูุตููููุฏูููู ุฃูุดูููุงุกู ุนูุฌููุจูุฉู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู ูฑูุตููููุฏูููู ุฃูุดูููุงุกู ุนูุฌููุจูุงุชู.}\\
\enquote{In the box are wonderful things.}\\
(Note how \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุดูููุงุก} is indefinite but has no \emph{n}-mark. This is because it is irregularly semi-flexible.)

By the way, this rule only applies to adjectival nouns in the describee or the information. A common noun in the describer or information will continue match the describee or subject in gender and number.

For example, if you say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃูููุนูุงูู ูฑูุตููุงููุญูุฉู ูููู ูฑููุญูุณูููุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The righteous acts are the good ones.}

then \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูููุฉ} may only be the feminine adjectival noun \enquote{a good one}.

If instead you want to use \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูููุฉ} with its common noun meaning of \enquote{a good deed}, then you have the use the plural:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃูููุนูุงูู ูฑูุตููุงููุญูุฉู ูููู ูฑููุญูุณูููุงุชู.}\\
\enquote{The acts are the good deeds.}

The plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} may continue to be used instead of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู}, although the latter is also valid:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃูููุนูุงูู ูฑูุตููุงููุญูุฉู ููููู ูฑููุญูุณูููุงุชู.}\\
\enquote{The acts are the good deeds.}

Similarly, if an adjectival noun connoting a non-intelligent being is used not as a describer or an information in a sentence, then it should be pluralized to indicate plurality.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูููููุงููุงุชู ุตูุบููุฑูุฉู ููฺฉูุจููุฑูุฉู. ูฑูููฺฉูุจููุฑูุงุชู ููุญูุดูุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The animals are big and small. The big ones are wild.}

In the second sentence above, we could not have said (for the same meaning):

\(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูููููุงููุงุชู ุตูุบููุฑูุฉู ููฺฉูุจููุฑูุฉู. \textbf{ูฑูููููุจููุฑูุฉู} ููุญูุดูุฉู.}

It is important to note that treating non-intelligent beings as grammatically feminine is only for the plural. Singular and dual nouns for non-intelligent beings are treated according to the gender of singular noun, as we have learned in previous chapters. So, for example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุชู ฺฉูุจููุฑู.}\\
\enquote{The house is big.}\\
not\\
\(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุชู ฺฉูุจููุฑูุฉ.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู ูฑููุฃูุณูุฏูุงูู ูฑูุธููุจููู.}\\
\enquote{The lions\textsubscript{2} ate the gazelle.}\\
not\\
\(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉูููุชู ูฑููุฃูุณูุฏูุงูู ูฑูุธููุจููู.}

\subsection{Preferring the feminine plural instead of the feminine singular}\label{preferring-the-feminine-plural-instead-of-the-feminine-singular}

In most cases we will prefer to use the feminine singular over the feminine plural for plurals of non-intelligent beings. So,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฃูุณููุฏู ุฃูฺฉูููุชู ูฑูุธููุจููู.}\\
\enquote{The lions, they ate the gazelle.}

is generally preferred over

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฃูุณููุฏู ุฃูฺฉููููู ูฑูุธููุจููู.}\\
\enquote{The lions, they ate the gazelle.}

However, there may be a couple of reasons to prefer the feminine plural instead of the feminine singular. We will explain them below.

\subsubsection{Using the feminine plural to indicate fewness}\label{using-the-feminine-plural-to-indicate-fewness}

In some circumstances
the feminine plural may be used to indicate fewness
whereas
the feminine singular will be used to indicate a multitude.

So if we say,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฃูุณููุฏู ุฃูฺฉููููู ูฑูุธููุจููู.}\\
\enquote{The lions, they ate the gazelle.}

then this would indicate that there were only a few lions (say ten or less).

And if, instead, we said:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฃูุณููุฏู ุฃูฺฉูููุชู ูฑูุธููุจููู.}\\
\enquote{The lions, they ate the gazelle.}

then this would indicate that there were many lions.

This may seem counter-intuitive at first but you may understand it this way:

If there are many lions then we treat them as \emph{one} group.

And if there are only a few lions, then we treat them \emph{one-by-one}.

\subsubsection{Using the feminine plural to avoid confusion}\label{using-the-feminine-plural-to-avoid-confusion}

Sometimes, if the plural noun is not immediately mentioned, then using the feminine singular may be misinterpreted to only mean one instead of the plural. For example, consider the following example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฑูุจูุชู ูฑููููุฑูุฑู ูฑููุญููููุจู ููููุง ุดูุฑูุจูุชููู ููุฑููุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The cats\textsubscript{f} drank the milk and one cat\textsubscript{f} didn't drink it.}

If we want to follow this sentence with another sentence: \enquote{Then they went.}, if we use the feminine singular:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูููู ุฐูููุจูุชู.}

then this might be misinterpreted to mean that only one cat (the one that didn't drink the milk) went.

So we might prefer to say, instead:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูููู ุฐูููุจููู.}

\chapter{Annexation}\label{annexation}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-10}

Consider the following expression:

\enquote{the boy's book}

This expression establishes a relation of \emph{belonging} between the two nouns: (i)~\enquote{the boy}, and (ii)~\enquote{the book}. It says that the book \emph{belongs} to the boy.

Arabic expresses this meaning using a construction called \emph{annexation}. In this chapter we will learn about this construction.

\section{Forming the annexation}\label{forming-the-annexation}

The word \enquote{annexation} means the addition of a new \emph{annexed} item to an existing \emph{base} item. We use the term \emph{annexation} in Arabic grammar when an \emph{annexe} noun is annexed to a \emph{base} noun by being placed right before it. Here is an example of an annexation:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-11-1.pdf}

The annexation construction consists of two nouns:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  The \emph{annexe} noun: This is the first noun in the annexation.
\item
  The \emph{base} noun: This is the second noun in the annexation.
\end{enumerate}

The annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} is annexed to, and belongs to, the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุบูููุงู}.
You can use the alphabetical order (A,~B) to help you remember that the \textbf{a}nnexe noun comes before the \textbf{b}ase noun.

\section{State of the annexe and base nouns}\label{state-of-the-annexe-and-base-nouns}

The base noun in an annexation is always in the i-state. The annexe noun may be in any state, depending on its function in the sentence. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุซูููููู.}\\
\enquote{The boy's book is heavy.}\\
(The annexe noun is in the u-state.)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐูุชู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู.}\\
\enquote{The girl took the boy's book.}\\
(The annexe noun is in the a-state.)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑููููุนูููููู ููู ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู.}\\
\enquote{The teacher\textsubscript{m} wrote in the boy's book.}\\
(The annexe noun is in the i-state.)

\section{Definiteness of the annexation}\label{definiteness-of-the-annexation}

Consider again the annexation expression we have been using so far:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู}\\
\enquote{the boy's book}

The base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุบูููุงู} is definite because it is prefixed by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \enquote{the}.
Therefore we have translated it as \enquote{the boy}.
The annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} is not made definite by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}.
Nor is it made indefinite by an \emph{n}-mark.
Rather, its definiteness is determined by the base noun.
Because the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุบูููุงู} is definite, therefore the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} is also definite.
The entire annexation is definite.

Consider now the case when the base noun is indefinite.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ุบูููุงูู}\\
\enquote{a boy's book}

In the above example, the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูููุงูู} is indefinite because it has the \emph{n}-mark~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} and because it does not prefixed by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}. Therefore we have translated it as \enquote{a boy}.
The annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} has neither an \emph{n}-mark, nor the prefix \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}.
Its definiteness is, again, determined by the base noun.
Because the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูููุงูู} is indefinite, therefore the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} is also indefinite.
The entire annexation is indefinite.

We will see soon, if Allฤh wills, why the definiteness of the annexe noun is important.

Here are some examples of definite and indefinite annexations.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูุณู ูฑูุทูููููู ูููููุตู ุฑูุฌููู.}\\
\enquote{The child wore \emph{a} man's shirt.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐู ุฃููููุฑู ูฑููุฌูููุดู ุฑูุงููุฉู ูฑููููููฺฉู ููุฑูููุนูููุง.}\\
\enquote{\emph{The} army's commander took \emph{the} king's flag and raised it.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุณู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููู ุธูููู ุดูุฌูุฑูุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The man sat in \emph{a} tree's shade.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Translating the annexation using \enquote{of}}{Translating the annexation using ``of''}}\label{translating-the-annexation-using-of}

So far we have been using the English \enquote{'s} to translate the Arabic annexation. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชู ุฑูุฌููู}\\
\enquote{a man's house}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\enquote{the man's house}

Instead of using \enquote{'s} we may use \enquote{of} as well. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชู ุฑูุฌููู}\\
\enquote{a/the house of a man}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\enquote{a/the house of the man}

Note that the annexe noun \enquote{house} may be prefixed with either \enquote{a} or \enquote{the}. This will depend on what is more natural in English. Often time both will fit. Here are some examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูุณู ูฑูุทูููููู ูููููุตู ุฑูุฌููู.}\\
\enquote{The child wore a/the shirt of a man.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐู ุฃููููุฑู ูฑููุฌูููุดู ุฑูุงููุฉู ูฑููููููฺฉู ููุฑูููุนูููุง.}\\
\enquote{\emph{The} commander of the army took \emph{the} flag of the king and raised it.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุณู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููู ุธูููู ุดูุฌูุฑูุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The man sat in \emph{the} shade of a tree.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุญู ูฑูููุตูู ุดูุจููุงฺฉู ูฑููุจูููุชู ููุฏูุฎููู ูฑููุจูููุชู.}\\
\enquote{The thief opened \emph{a/the} window of the house and entered the house.}

It is important to understand that translating the annexe noun into English with \enquote{a} or \enquote{the} is purely for the reason of obtaining a natural translation. This does not affect whether or not the annexe noun is grammatically considered definite in Arabic.

As we mentioned earlier, the definiteness of the annexe noun in Arabic depends only on the definiteness of the base noun.
If the base noun is definite then the annexe noun shall be considered definite as well.
And if the base noun is indefinite then the annexe noun shall be considered indefinite as well.

The need to maintain this distinction will become apparent in the next section.

If the base noun is definite, and it is desired to make the annexe noun grammatically indefinite, then it is necassary to break the annexation, and use a prepositional phrase instead, usually with the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, which, here, will mean \enquote{of}. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ูููุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\enquote{The boy went to a house of the man.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุญู ูฑูููุตูู ุดูุจููุงฺฉููุง ูููู ูฑููุจูููุชู ููุฏูุฎููู ูฑููุจูููุชู.}\\
\enquote{The thief opened a window of the house and entered the house.}

\section{\texorpdfstring{Broken plurals and \emph{ฤt} sound plurals in annexations}{Broken plurals and ฤt sound plurals in annexations}}\label{broken-plurals-and-at-sound-plurals-in-annexations}

There is no special rules for broken plurals and \emph{ฤt} sound plurals in annexations. They behave just like singular nouns. Remember only that \emph{ฤt} plurals end with \emph{โู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} in the a-state. Here are some examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููููุงููุงุชู ูฑููุบูุงุจูุฉู ููุญูุดูุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The animals of the forest are wild.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃูุชู ุทูุงููุจูุงุชู ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู ุตูููุญูุงุชู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุจู}\\
\enquote{The school's students\textsubscript{f} read the pages of the books.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู ูฑููุฎูุฒูุงููุฉู ุฃูููููุงูู ููุนููููููุงุชู.}\\
``In the cupboard are teachers'\textsubscript{f} pens.

Contrary to broken plurals and \emph{ฤt} plurals, duals and \emph{ลซn} sound plurals behave differently in annexations. We will deal with them in section~\ref{duals-and-sound-un-plurals-in-annexations}

\section{Describers in an annexation}\label{describers-in-an-annexation}

\subsection{Describing the base noun}\label{describing-the-base-noun}

Consider the following expression:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู}\\
\enquote{the girl's book}

Now say that we want to form an descriptive noun-phrase \enquote{the small girl's book}.
Basically, we want to describe the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉ} \enquote{the girl} with the adjectival noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \enquote{a small one}.
Here is how we will express this in Arabic:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ูฑูุตููุบููุฑูุฉู}\\
\enquote{the small girl's book}

In the manner we are already familiar with, we place the describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \enquote{a small one} after the describee
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉ} \enquote{the girl}
and match the describer with the describee in definiteness, state, gender and number (singular, dual, or plural).

Similarly, if we had an indefinite annexation, we would get:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉูู ุตูุบููุฑูุฉู}\\
\enquote{a small girl's book}

Here are some more examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจูุชู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ููู ุญูุฏููููุฉู ูฑููุจูููุชู ูฑููฺฉูุจููุฑู.}\\
\enquote{The girl played in the garden of the big house.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุณููุฑูุฉู ูฑููููุฑูุขูู ูฑููฺฉูุฑูููู.}\\
\enquote{The boy read the sลซrah of the Noble Qurสพฤn.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุณู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููู ุธูููู ุดูุฌูุฑูุฉู ุนูุฑููุถูุฉู ููุณููุนูุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The man sat in the shade of a wide broad tree.}

\subsection{Describing the annexe noun}\label{describing-the-annexe-noun}

Consider, again, the same annexation:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู}\\
\enquote{the girl's book}

Say, now, that we want to describe the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} \enquote{book} with the adjectival noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑ} \enquote{a small one}. Normally, nothing can come between the annexe noun and the base noun in an annexation. So, the describer needs to be placed, again, after the base noun.
However, this time it will match the annexe noun, not the base noun, in state, definiteness, gender, and number. So we get:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ูฑูุตููุบููุฑู}\\
\enquote{the girl's small book}

Note how the describer
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุบููุฑู} matches the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู} in state and gender.
