Note also how the describer is definite with an \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}. This is because it is matching the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู} in definiteness.
The annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} is definite, not with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}, but rather because of the definite base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู} \enquote{the girl}.
We've already learned this rule in
section~\ref{definiteness-of-the-annexation}
above.

Similarly, if we describe the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} in an indefinite annexation, we get:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ุตูุบููุฑู}\\
\enquote{a girl's small book}

This time
the describer
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑู}
is indefinite with an \emph{un}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.
This is because
the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู} is indefinite. It is indefinite because base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู} \enquote{a girl} is indefinite.

Now, you might be foreseeing a problem. What if the annexe noun and the base noun have the same gender, and the annexe too is in the i-state? For example, in the sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ูฑููฺฉูุจููุฑู.}\\
\enquote{The boy went to the big/old man's house.}\\
or\\
\enquote{The boy went to the man's big house.}

How do we know whether the describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑ} is meant to describe the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชู} or the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู}?
The annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชู} and the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู} are both masculine, singular, definite, and in the i-state.

The answer is that in such cases, context will have to be clear to tell us which of the two meanings is intended.
If the context makes it clear then there is no harm in using such a sentence for either of the two meanings.

Also, sometimes, the meaning of the describer is such that it will likely apply to only one of the two nouns. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ูฑููฺฉูุฑูููู.}\\
\enquote{The boy went to a noble/generous man's house.}

In the sentence above the describer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฑููู} \enquote{noble/generous} is likely to apply to a man, and not to a house.

If, however, the context is not clear, and the meaning of the describer can apply to both the annexe noun and the base noun, then the describer is likely to apply to the base noun and not to the annexe noun. So then, this interpretation is more likely:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ูฑููฺฉูุจููุฑู.}\\
\enquote{The boy went to the big/old man's house.}

In order to apply a describer to the annexe noun in such a case, it is better to break the annexation and form a prepositional phrase instead, usually with the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, which, here, will mean \enquote{of}. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุฅูููููฐ ูฑููุจูููุชู ูฑููฺฉูุจููุฑู ูููุฑููุฌููู .}\\
\enquote{The boy went to the big house of the man.}

Here are some more examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจูุชู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ุจูฺฉูุฑูุฉู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ูฑููุญููุฑูุงุกู.}\\
\enquote{The girl played with the boy's red ball.}\\
(Note that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฑูุงุก} feminine to match \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฑูุฉ}.)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุทูุชู ููุฑูููุฉู ูฑูุดููุฌูุฑูุฉู ูฑููุฎูุถูุฑูุงุกู ุนูููููฐ ููุงุกู ูฑููููุนูุฑู ูฑููุนูุฑููุถู.}\\
\enquote{The green leaf of the tree fell on the water of the broad river.}\\
(Note that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุถูุฑูุงุก} is in the u-state to match \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููุฉ})

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุญููููุจูุฉู ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู ูฑูุซูููููููุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The boy carried the heavy school-bag.}\\
(literally: the heavy bag of the school).

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ุนูููููฐ ุตูููุญูุฉู ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ุจูููุถูุงุกู.}\\
\enquote{The man wrote on the white page of a book.}\\
(Note that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุถูุงุกู} is feminine to match \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูููุญูุฉ}. However, also note that it has an a-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} in the i-state because it is semi-flexible.)

\section{Semi-flexible nouns in an annexation}\label{semi-flexible-nouns-in-an-annexation}

Remember that semi-flexible nouns don't take \emph{n}-marks and that when indefinite, the i-state is indicated by an \emph{a}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.
But when definite with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} then they behave just like fully-flexible nouns.
Example of the semi-flexible noun
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \enquote{a desert}:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
State & Indefinite & Definite \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุญูุฑูุงุกู} \\
a-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุญูุฑูุงุกู} \\
i-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตููุญูุฑูุงุกู} \\
\end{longtable}

We will now see how semi-flexible nouns behave in an annexation.

\subsection{A semi-flexible noun as the base noun}\label{a-semi-flexible-noun-as-the-base-noun}

Here are examples of the semi-flexible noun \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \enquote{a desert} as the base noun in an annexation:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฑูููุฉู ููู ููุณูุทู ูฑูุตููุญูุฑูุงุกู.}\\
\enquote{The village is in the middle of the desert.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฑูุจู ูฑููุฃูุนูุฑูุงุจูููู ููุงุกู ูููู ุจูุฆูุฑู ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู.}\\
\enquote{The bedouin drank some water from a desert's well.}

As you can see, when
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก}
is definite, then its i-state is indicate by an \emph{i}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, just like fully-flexible nouns.
However, when it is indefinite, then
its i-state is indicate by an \emph{a}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.

This is consistent with the general behavior of semi-flexible nouns that we are familiar with.

\subsection{A semi-flexible noun as the annexe noun}\label{a-semi-flexible-noun-as-the-annexe-noun}

Contrary from expected behavior, a semi-flexible annexe noun, even when indefinite, takes an \emph{i}-mark~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} in the i-state instead of an \emph{a}-mark~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. Example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏููู ูฑููุฃูุนูุฑูุงุจูููู ูููู ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู ุฃูุฑูุถู ุจูุนููุฏูุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The bedouin came from the desert of a far land.}

In the above example,
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุญูุฑูุงุก} \enquote{a desert} is indefinite because it is the annexe noun to an indefinite base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฑูุถ} \enquote{a land}.
It is in the i-state because it is preceded by the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \enquote{from}.
Nevertheless, it takes an \emph{i}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู ุฃูุฑูุถู}, not an \emph{a}-mark, which would be incorrect: \(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู ุฃูุฑูุถู}.

\section{Annexations with more than two nouns}\label{annexations-with-more-than-two-nouns}

So far we have seen annexations with two nouns. Annexations may be arbitrarily long. Here is an example of a noun-chain with more than two nouns:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-12-1.pdf}

The above annexation consists of three nouns. It may be divided into two sub-annexations:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชูุงุญู ุจูุงุจู} \enquote{door's key}. Its annexe noun \(a_1\)~is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชูุงุญ} and its base noun~\(b_1\) is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุงุจู}.
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุงุจู ูฑููุจูููุชู} \enquote{the house's door}. Its annexe noun \(a_2\)~is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุงุจู} and its base noun~\(b_2\) is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุจูููุชู}.
\end{enumerate}

The noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุงุจ} \enquote{door} is common to both sub-annexations. It is the base noun of the first sub-annexation
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชูุงุญู ุจูุงุจู} \enquote{door's key}.
At the same time, it is also the annexe noun of the second sub-annexation
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุงุจู ูฑููุจูููุชู} \enquote{the house's door}.

Only the final base noun may have \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} or an \emph{n} mark. If the final base noun has \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} (as above) then all the nouns in the annexation are definite.

And if the final base noun is indefinite, as in the example below, then all the nouns in the annexation are indefinite.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชูุงุญู ุจูุงุจู ุจูููุชู}\\
\enquote{a house's door's key}

All the nouns except the first annexe noun must be in the i-state. Consistent with
section~\ref{a-semi-flexible-noun-as-the-annexe-noun}
if a semi-flexible noun is any of the annexe nouns and is in the i-state, then its i-state is indicated by an \emph{a}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุจูุฆูุฑู ุตูุญูุฑูุงุกู ุฃูุฑูุถู}\\
\enquote{from the well of the desert of a land}

\section{Pronouns as base nouns}\label{pronouns-as-base-nouns}

Consider the expression:

\enquote{his book}

This expression is very similar to the annexation:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู}\\
\enquote{the boy's book}

The difference is that we would like to replace the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุบูููุงู} \enquote{the boy} with the pronoun \enquote{his}. For this we use the attached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}. When we place this pronoun as the base noun, we get:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู}\\
\enquote{his book}

This annexation follows the same rules as the other annexations we have been studying so far:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The annexe noun may be in any state, depending on its function in the sentence.
\item
  The base noun is in the i-state. But because the base noun is a pronoun, and pronouns are rigid nouns (see section~\ref{rigidity-of-pronouns} that don't change their ending based on their state, therefore it's i-state will not be apparent.
\end{itemize}

Here are some examples of this annexation used in sentences:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู ุซูููููู.}\\
\enquote{His book is heavy}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู.}\\
\enquote{The man read his book.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑููููุนูููููู ููู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูู.}\\
\enquote{The teacher\textsubscript{m} wrote in his book.}

If the annexe noun ends with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} then it is converted to a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} when annexing it to an attached pronoun. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจููุง ุฅูููููฐ ููุฏูุฑูุณูุชููููู.}\\
\enquote{They went to their school.}

Here are some more examples of annexing to the different attached pronouns:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุฎูููุชู ุจูููุชูฺฉู.}\\
\enquote{You\textsubscript{1,m} entered your\textsubscript{1,m} house.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉูููุชูุง ุทูุนูุงููููููุง.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{2,f} ate their\textsubscript{2} food.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏูููุชู ุฅูููููฐ ููุฏููููุชูฺฉููู}\\
\enquote{I have arrived to your\textsubscript{3,m} city.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุฅูููุงูู ููุณูุฌูุฏูููุง.}\\
\enquote{He is the สพImฤm of our mosque.}

If the annexe noun is semi-flexible then it gets a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} in the i-state, as we've already learned. Example with the semi-flexible broken plural \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฏูุงุฆูู} \enquote{gardens}.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจููู ููู ุญูุฏูุงุฆูููููููู.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{3,f} played in their\textsubscript{3,f} gardens.}

If an annexe noun ends with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} then it gets converted to an สพalif when annexing it to an attached pronoun. Example with \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุงููููฐ} \enquote{legal opinions}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ุชูููุงููุฐูุฉู ูฑูุดููููุฎู ููุชูุงููุงูู ููู ฺฉูุชูุจููููู.}\\
\enquote{The pupils of the religious scholar wrote down his legal opinions in their books.}

For the singular speaker-participant there are two variants for the attached pronoun:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{-ฤซ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{-ya}
\end{enumerate}

The first (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{-ฤซ}) is more commonly used. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃูุชู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูู}\\
\enquote{I read my book.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููุงููู ููุตููุฑูุฉ.}\\
\enquote{My pens are short.}

If, however, the annexe noun ends in a long vowel or a semi-vowel then
(\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{-ฤซ}) is disallowed and only
(\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{-ya}) shall be used.
Example with the semi-flexible broken plural \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏูุงููุง} \enquote{gifts}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนูุฌูุจูุชููููู ููุฏูุงููุงูู.}\\
\enquote{My gifts pleased them.}

\subsection{Describers with annexations to pronouns}\label{describers-with-annexations-to-pronouns}

Consider the annexation:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู}\\
\enquote{his book}

The annexe noun is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} and the base noun is the pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}.
We would like add a describer to this expression.
Remember from section~\ref{definiteness-of-pronouns} that pronouns are definite nouns. That makes the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} also definite.
Therefore, any describer for this annexation will need to be definite too.

Here is a new rule: Pronouns may not be describees. That is: they are not allowed to have describers.
Even in English you may say:

\enquote{The good boy went.}

but you can't say:

\(\times\)~\enquote{The good \emph{he} went.}

So, any describers for the annexation must necessarily only describe the annexe noun, not the base pronoun. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู ุงูุฃูุญูููุฑู}\\
\enquote{his red book}

Here are some more examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจูุชู ุจููููููููู ูฑููุฃูุณูููุฏู}\\
\enquote{I wrote with my black pen.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููู ุบูููููุงูู ูฑููููุฑูููุฉู ุญูููุงุฆุจููููู ูฑูุซูููููููุฉ ุฅูููููฐ ููุฏูุฑูุณูุชููููู ูฑููุจูุนููุฏูุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The village boys carried their heavy bags to their distant school.}\\
(literally: the village's boys.)

\section{\texorpdfstring{Duals and \emph{ลซn} sound plurals in annexations}{Duals and ลซn sound plurals in annexations}}\label{duals-and-sound-un-plurals-in-annexations}

We have already dealt with broken plurals and \emph{ฤt} sound plurals in annexations in
section~\ref{broken-plurals-and-at-sound-plurals-in-annexations}.

In this section we will deal with
duals and \emph{ลซn} sound plurals in annexations.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Duals and \emph{ลซn} sound plurals as base nouns}{Duals and ลซn sound plurals as base nouns}}\label{duals-and-un-sound-plurals-as-base-nouns}

As base noun, duals and \emph{ลซn} sound plurals behave no differently than other nouns. Being base nouns they will be in the i-state and this shall be indicated by:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โููููู} \emph{-ayni} for duals
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูููู} \emph{-ฤซna} for \emph{ลซn} sound plurals
\end{enumerate}

Here are some examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฌูุฆู ูฑููููุธูููููููู ูฑูุถููุนูููุงุกู ููู ุจูููุงุฏู ูฑููููุณูููููููู ูฑููุขููููุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The weak wronged ones took refuge in the secure lands of the Muslims.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุชู ูฑููุบูููุงูููููู ูฑูุทููููููููููู ุตูุบููุฑูุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The tall boys'\textsubscript{2} sister is little.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุทูุงููุจูุฉู ููุนููููููุชููููู ฺฉูุฑููููุชููููู.}\\
\enquote{She is the student\textsubscript{f} of noble teachers\textsubscript{2,f}.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Duals and \emph{ลซn} sound plurals as annexe nouns}{Duals and ลซn sound plurals as annexe nouns}}\label{duals-and-un-sound-plurals-as-annexe-nouns}

When duals and \emph{ลซn} sound plurals are annexe nouns, then their final \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} is treated as an \emph{n}-mark and is, therefore, deleted before annexing them to a base noun. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชูุง ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\enquote{the man's houses\textsubscript{2}}\\
not\\
\(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชูุงูู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}

Note, also, that because the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู} begins with a connecting hamzah \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑ}, therefore the long vowel \emph{ฤ} at the end of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชูุง} is pronounced as a short vowel \emph{a}, thus:

\emph{bayta -rrajuli}\\
not\\
\(\times\)~\emph{baytฤ -rrajuli}

If the dual annexe noun were in the i-state then the final \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} gets an \emph{i}-mark \emph{โู} if there is following connecting hamzah. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃูุชู ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\emph{qaraสพtu kitฤbayi -rrajul}\\
\enquote{I read the man's books\textsubscript{2}.}

Here are some more examples including \emph{ลซn} sound plurals:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ฺฉูุฑูุงูู.}\\
\emph{muษallimu -lgอกhulฤmi kirฤm.}\\
\enquote{The boy's teachers\textsubscript{3} are noble.}\\
(Note that there is no silent สพalif after \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููู} as there is after a verb with a plural absentee-participant doer pronoun, e.g.~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจููุง} \enquote{they\textsubscript{3,m} played})

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจู ูฑุจูููุง ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููุนู ููุงุนูุจูู ููุฏููููุชููููู.}\\
\emph{laษiba -bna -rrajuli maษa lฤษibฤซ madฤซnatihim.}\\
\enquote{The man's sons\textsubscript{2} played with the players of their city.}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Annexing duals and \emph{ลซn} sound plurals to pronouns}{Annexing duals and ลซn sound plurals to pronouns}}\label{annexing-duals-and-un-sound-plurals-to-pronouns}

Duals and \emph{ลซn} sound plurals can be annexed to attached pronouns, and in this case too, they will lose their final \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููููููุง ุทููููุจูููู.}\\
\enquote{Our teachers\textsubscript{3,m} are good.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจูุชู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ููุนู ุตูุฏููููุชูููููุง}\\
\enquote{The girl played with her friends\textsubscript{2,f}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชูุงูู ฺฉูุจููุฑูุงูู.}\\
\enquote{My houses\textsubscript{2} are big.}\\
(Note that only the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} variant is allowed to be used because of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชูุง} ending with a long vowel.)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃูุชู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูููู}\\
(Note how \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู + ูู} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจูููู}.)

There are also two special cases in this category and we will examine them below:

\paragraph*{\texorpdfstring{Annexing an \emph{ลซn} sound plural to the singular speaker participant pronoun}{Annexing an ลซn sound plural to the singular speaker participant pronoun}}\label{annexing-an-un-sound-plural-to-the-singular-speaker-participant-pronoun}
\addcontentsline{toc}{paragraph}{Annexing an \emph{ลซn} sound plural to the singular speaker participant pronoun}

When an \emph{ลซn} sound plural is annexed to the singular speaker participant pronoun, then again, only the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} variant can be used. However, in addition, the expression will appear the same regardless of the state of the annexe noun. So for all states (u-state, a-state, and i-state), we will get:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููููู}

We don't say \(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููููู} for the u-state. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููููููู ฺฉูุฑูุงูู.}\\
\enquote{My teachers\textsubscript{3,m} are noble.}\\
(u-state)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชู ููุนููููููููู}\\
\enquote{I asked my teachers\textsubscript{3,m}.}\\
(a-state)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐูุชู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง ูููู ููุนููููููููู}\\
\enquote{I took a book from my teachers\textsubscript{3,m}.}\\
(i-state)

\paragraph*{Annexing an dual noun to a dual pronoun}\label{annexing-an-dual-noun-to-a-dual-pronoun}
\addcontentsline{toc}{paragraph}{Annexing an dual noun to a dual pronoun}

When a dual noun is to be annexed to a dual pronoun, then the dual annexe noun is often converted to a plural. For example, instead of saying

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุธูุฑูุชู ุฅูููููฐ ุฑูุฃูุณูููููููุง}
\enquote{I looked at their\textsubscript{2} heads\textsubscript{2}.}

it is in fact, more common, to say

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุธูุฑูุชู ุฅูููููฐ ุฑูุคููุณูููููุง}
\enquote{I looked at their\textsubscript{2} heads\textsubscript{3}.}

Although the former is also correct. This is because the annexation of a dual to a dual is considered burdensome upon the tongue to utter, and so the plural is prefered.

\section{\texorpdfstring{Annexations with \enquote{and}}{Annexations with ``and''}}\label{annexations-with-and}

\subsection{Multiple annexe nouns and one base noun}\label{multiple-annexe-nouns-and-one-base-noun}

In English we can have an expression like \enquote{the pen and the book of the boy} = \enquote{the boy's pen and book}. In this sentence there are two annexe nouns and one base noun.

In order to express this in Arabic, we will say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ููฺฉูุชูุงุจููู}\\
\emph{qalamu -lgอกhulฤmi wa kitฤbuhu}\\
\enquote{the boy's pen and his book} = \enquote{the boy's pen and book}

Note that the annexation is not broken by the insertion of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{wa} \enquote{and}. Rather a second annexation is used and the two are separated by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{wa} \enquote{and}. This is the preferred way of expressing such expressions.

There is another, less preferred way of expressing this. And this is by breaking the first annexation and inserting \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{wa} \enquote{and}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููู ููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู}\\
\emph{qalamu wa kitฤbu -lgอกhulฤmi}\\
\enquote{the boy's pen and book}

This second method is not considered as eloquent. Some even consider it incorrect. So we advise you to use the first method whenever possible.

\subsubsection{With pronouns}\label{with-pronouns}

If the base noun in the first annexation is replaced with a pronoun then only the first method is allowed. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููููู ููฺฉูุชูุงุจููู}\\
\emph{qalamuhu wakitฤbuhu}\\
\enquote{his pen and his book}

\subsection{One annexe noun and multiple base nouns}\label{one-annexe-noun-and-multiple-base-nouns}

We can also have expressions like \enquote{the house of the boy and the girl}. In this sentence there is one annexe noun and two base nouns.

To express this in Arabic we will say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ูููฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู}\\
\emph{baytu -lgอกhulฤmi wa-ljฤriyati}\\
\enquote{the house of the boy and the girl}

Note that both
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุบูููุงูู} \emph{สพalgอกhulฤmi} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู} \emph{สพaljฤriyati} are in the i-state because they are both base nouns in the annexation.

\subsubsection{With pronouns}\label{with-pronouns-1}

If one or both of the base nouns in the annexation is replaced with a pronoun then the first noun must be repeated. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ููุจูููุชูููุง}\\
\enquote{the boy's house and her house}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชููู ููุจูููุชูููุง}\\
\emph{baytuhu wabaytuhฤ}\\
\enquote{his house and her house}

\section{Usage of the annexation}\label{usage-of-the-annexation}

\subsection{Primarily belonging}\label{primarily-belonging}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุญูุ ูุซูุ ุดุจู}}{ูุญูุ ูุซูุ ุดุจู}}\label{ux646ux62dux648-ux645ux62bux644-ux634ux628ux647}

Don't become definite when annexed to pronoun

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณ} \enquote{self}}{ููุณ ``self''}}\label{ux646ux641ux633-self}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจุง ุฃููุณููุง}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุงูุช ููู ูููุณู}

\subsection{annexation of material}\label{annexation-of-material}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎุงุชูู ุฐูููุจู}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎุงุชูู ุฐูููุจู}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎุงุชูู ููู ุฐูููุจู}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏูููุฉู ุฏููุดู}}{ููุฏูููุฉู ุฏููุดู}}\label{ux645ux62fux64aux646ux629-ux62fux645ux634ux642}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุฌุฑุฏ ุชุฑููู}}{ูุฌุฑุฏ ุชุฑููู}}\label{ux645ux62cux631ux62f-ux62aux631ux641ux64aux647}

\chapter{Irregular nouns}\label{irregular-nouns}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-11}

There are some nouns in Arabic which are \emph{irregular} and behave a little differently than other \emph{regular} nouns. In this chapter we will study these irregular nouns.

\section{The five nouns}\label{the-five-nouns}

There are five nouns in Arabic which are irregular in the same basic way. Collectively, they are called \enquote{the five nouns}.
They behave a little differently from regular nouns in how they display their state.

We have learned that regular nouns have three states: the u-state, a-state, and i-state. For singular nouns, the u-state is marked by the \emph{u}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, the a-state is marked by the \emph{a}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, and the i-state is marked by the \emph{i}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. The \emph{n}-marks \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} are only but extensions of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} respectively.

We now present the five irregular nouns that behave differently.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจู} \emph{สพab}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎู} \emph{สพakอกh}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููู} \emph{แธฅam}}{ุฃูุจู สพab, ุฃูุฎู สพakอกh, and ุญููู แธฅam}}\label{ux623ux628-eab-ux623ux62e-eax-and-ux62dux645-ham}

The first three nouns that we will talk about are:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจ} \emph{สพab} \enquote{a father} (root: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃุจูยป})
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎ} \emph{สพakอกh} \enquote{a brother} (root: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃุฎูยป})
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูู} \emph{แธฅam} \enquote{a father-in-law} (root: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญููยป})
\end{enumerate}

The final root letter of all three of these nouns is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}. However, irregularly, it is omitted in most formations of the word. It does resurface in some cases as we will describe below.

Without the final root letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, these nouns display their state like regular nouns. Here are some examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ุฃูุจู ฺฉูุจููุฑู ููุฃูุฎู ุตูุบููุฑู.}\\
\emph{liljฤriyati สพabun kabฤซrun waสพakอกhun แนฃagอกhฤซr}\\
\enquote{The girl has an old father and a young brother.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุฃูุฎูุง ูููู.}\\
\emph{แธaraba -lgอกhulฤmu สพakอกhan lahu.}\\
\enquote{The boy beat a brother of his.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญููู ูููฑููุฃูุจู ููู ุจูููุชู ูฑููุฃูุฎู.}\\
\emph{สพalแธฅamu walสพabu fฤซ bayti -lสพakอกh.}\\
\enquote{The father-in-law and the father are in the brother's house.}

Where the nouns behave irregularly is when they are an annexe noun in an annexation. Then instead of displaying their state with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, they display their state using the long vowels \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{ลซ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} \emph{ฤ}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{ฤซ} instead. Here are some examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุฃูุฎูู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู.}\\
\emph{huwa สพakอกhu -ljฤriyah}\\
\enquote{He is the girl's brother.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชู ุฃูุจูุง ุตูุฏููููู ุนููู ุฃูููุฑู.}\\
\emph{saสพaltu สพabฤ แนฃadฤซqฤซ ษan สพamr.}\\
\enquote{I asked my friend's father about a matter.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ุญูููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabtu สพilฤ bayti แธฅami -rrajul.}\\
\enquote{I went to the man's father-in-law's house.}

When these nouns are annexed to attached pronouns, then in most cases they will behave as above. So, for example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูููู}\\
\emph{สพabลซhu}\\
\enquote{his father} (u-state).

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุงููุง}\\
\emph{สพakอกhฤnฤ}\\
\enquote{our brother} (a-state).

However, if the attached pronoun is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} (for the singular speaker participant), then in that case, the attached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} attaches to the annexe noun directly, without any intervening long vowel:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูู}\\
\emph{สพakอกhฤซ}\\
\enquote{my brother} (u-state, a-state, and i-state).

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูู}\\
\emph{สพabฤซ}\\
\enquote{my father} (u-state, a-state, and i-state).

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููู}\\
\emph{แธฅamฤซ}\\
\enquote{my father-in-law} (u-state, a-state, and i-state).

Here are some more examples in sentences:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูููู ุทูููููู ููุฃูุฎููููุง ููุตููุฑู ููุฃูุฎูู ฺฉูุจููุฑู.}\\
\emph{สพakอกhลซhu แนญawฤซlun waสพakอกhลซhฤ qaแนฃฤซrun waสพakอกhฤซ kabฤซr.}\\
\enquote{His brother is tall and her brother is short and my brother is big.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ุฃูุฎูุงูููู ููุฃูุฎูุงููุง.}\\
\emph{saสพaltu สพakอกhฤhum waสพakอกhฤnฤ.}\\
\enquote{I asked their\textsubscript{m,3+} brother and our brother.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูฺฉูุฑู ุฃูุฎูู ุฃูุจูู.}\\
\emph{sอกhakara สพakอกhฤซ สพabฤซ.}\\
\enquote{My brother thanked my father.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ุฃูุฎููููููู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabtu สพilฤ bayti สพakอกhฤซhinn.}\\
\enquote{I went to their\textsubscript{f,3+} brother's house.}

The above irregular behavior of these three nouns is only when they are annexe nouns. When they happen to be base nouns in annexations, then they again they behave like regular nouns and their state is displayed by the short vowel marks \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, when definite, and by the \emph{n}-marks
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู},
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, when indefinite.
. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุชู ูฑููุฃูุฎู ฺฉูุจููุฑู.}\\
\emph{baytu -lสพakอกhi kabฤซr.}\\
\enquote{The brother's house is big.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ุฃูุฎู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabtu สพilฤ bayti สพakอกh.}\\
\enquote{I went to a brother's house.}

When these nouns form their duals and plurals, then the final root letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} is resurfaces.
In forming the broken plural, the final root letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, being a weak letter, sometimes converts to a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก}.
The following table shows their duals and plurals.

\end{longtable}

One special note regarding the dual \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูููุงูู}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจููููููู}: in addition to meaning \enquote{two fathers}, they can also mean \enquote{both parents}, i.e., \enquote{a father and a mother}. Here are examples of these words in sentences:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุฃูุฎูููุงูู ุฅูููู ูฑููููุณูุฌูุฏู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba -lสพakอกhawฤni fi -lmasjidi.}\\
\enquote{The brothers\textsubscript{2} went to the mosque.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชู ุฃูุฎูููููู ุนููู ุฃูููุฑู}\\
\emph{saสพaltu สพakอกhawayya ษan สพamrin.}\\
\enquote{I asked my brothers\textsubscript{2} about a matter.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูฺฉูุฑูุชู ููุฃูุจููููููู}\\
\emph{sอกhakartu liสพabawayhi.}\\
\enquote{I thanked his parents.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู} \emph{dอกhลซ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุงุช} \emph{dอกhฤt}}{ุฐูู dอกhลซ and ุฐูุงุช dอกhฤt}}\label{zu}

The fourth irregular noun from \enquote{the five nouns} is the masculine noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู} \emph{dอกhลซ} and its feminine counterpart \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุงุช} and \emph{dอกhฤt}. The words \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู} \emph{dอกhลซ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุงุช} \emph{dอกhฤt} mean \enquote{owner of} or \enquote{possessor of}.

So, for example, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู ูฑููููุงูู} \emph{dอกhu -lmฤli} means \enquote{possessor\textsubscript{m} of wealth} or \enquote{wealthy person\textsubscript{m}}. The singular, dual, and plural of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู} \emph{dอกhลซ} in all three states is shown in the table below:

a-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุง} \emph{dอกhฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐููููู} \emph{dอกhaway} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููู} \emph{dอกhawฤซ} \\
i-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู} \emph{dอกhฤซ} & same as a-state & same as a-state \\

The noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู} \emph{dอกhลซ} and its duals and plurals are only ever used as annexe nouns in annexations. Furthermore, they may not be annexed to pronouns. Here are some examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู ุฐูู ูฑููููุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพarrujulu dอกhu -lmฤl.}\\
\enquote{The man is the possessor of wealth.} = \enquote{This man is wealthy.}

The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุงุช} is the feminine of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู}. When used as an annexe noun, its states, duals, and plurals are as in the table below:

a-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุงุชู} \emph{dอกhฤta} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุงุชููู} \emph{dอกhawฤtay} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุงุชู} \emph{dอกhawฤti} \\
i-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุงุชู} \emph{dอกhฤti} & same as a-state & same as a-state \\
\end{longtable}

Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐููู ูฑูุดููุฌูุฑูุฉู ุฐูุงุชู ุซูููุฑู ฺฉูุซููุฑู.}\\
\emph{hฤdอกhihi -sอกhsอกhajaratu dอกhฤtu tอกhamarin katอกhฤซrin.}\\
\enquote{This tree is the possessor of much fruit.} = \enquote{This tree is very fruitful.}

As opposed to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู} which is only an annexe noun, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุงุช} may be used a noun in its own right. In this case it means \enquote{personality} or \enquote{essence}. This usage is often found in theological or philosophical works. And, as such, unlike \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู} which can't be annexed to attached pronouns, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุงุช} can be annexed to attached pronouns. Examples:

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{fam}}{ููู fam}}\label{ux641ux645-fam}

The fifth of \enquote{the five nouns} is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{fam} \enquote{a mouth}. It is the most irregular of \enquote{the five nouns}.

In some ways, the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{fam} is regular. It is only irregular when it is a singular annexe noun. Let's first see its regular bahavior.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููู ูฑููููุฌููู ูููู ููููู ูฑูููููู ููุณูุงูู.}\\
\emph{ษala -lwajhi famun wafi -lfami lisฤn}\\
\enquote{On the face is a mouth, and in the mounth is a tongue.}

It is a base noun in an annexation regularly:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุทููู ููุณูุงูู ูฑูููููู.}\\
\emph{naแนญaqa lisฤnu -lfam.}\\
\enquote{The mouth's tongue articulated {[}speech{]}.}

It forms duals regularly, which are used in annexations regularly

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง ูฑููููููุฑููููู ฺฉูุจููุฑูุงูู.}\\
\emph{fama -nnahrayni kabฤซrฤni.}\\
\enquote{The mouths\textsubscript{2} of the rivers\textsubscript{2} are big.}

Let's now see its irregular behavior.

When \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} is a singular annexe noun, then it is usual for it to follow the example of the rest of the five nouns.

Here is how it will appear as a singular annexe noun in the three states:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
u-state
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
a-state
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
i-state
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{fลซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{fฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{fฤซ} \\
\end{longtable}

Examples of usage:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู ูฑููููููุฑู ฺฉูุจููุฑู.}\\
\emph{fu -nnahri kabฤซr.}\\
\enquote{The mouth of the river is big.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููุง ุฌูููููู.}\\
\emph{fลซhฤ jamฤซl.}\\
\enquote{Her mouth is beautiful.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุญู ููุงูู.}\\
\emph{fataแธฅa fฤh.}\\
\enquote{He opened his mouth.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุนูููุชู ูฑููุฃูููู ููููููุฉู ุทูุนูุงูู ููู ููู ูฑุจูููุชูููุง.}\\
\emph{jaษalati -lสพummu luqmata แนญaษฤmin fฤซ fi -bnatihฤ.}\\
\enquote{The mother put a morsel of food in her daughter's mouth.}

When the attached pronoun for the speaking person \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} is attached to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{fลซ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{fฤ}, or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{fฤซ} the combination is always \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู} \emph{fiyya} in all three states. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู ููููุชููุญู.}\\
\emph{fiyya maftลซh.}\\
\enquote{My mouth is open.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุญูุชู ููููู.}\\
\emph{fataแธฅtu fiyy.}\\
\enquote{I opened my mouth.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉูููุชู ุจูููููู.}\\
\emph{สพakaltu bifiyy.}\\
\enquote{I ate with my mouth.}

In addition to the above irregular behavior, it is permissible, but less common, to treat \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} regularly as an annexe noun in an annexation. So it is permissible to also say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ูฑููููููุฑู ฺฉูุจููุฑู.}\\
\emph{famu -nnahri kabฤซr.}\\
\enquote{The river's mouth is big.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู ููููุชููุญู.}\\
\emph{famฤซ maftลซhun.}\\
\enquote{My mouth is open.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููููุง ุฌูููููู.}\\
\emph{famuhฤ jamฤซlun.}\\
\enquote{Her mouth is beautiful.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุญู ูููููู.}\\
\emph{fataแธฅa famahu.}\\
\enquote{He opened his mouth.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุนูููุชู ูฑููุฃูููู ููููููุฉู ุทูุนูุงูู ููู ูููู ูฑุจูููุชูููุง.}\\
\emph{jaษalati -lสพummu luqmata แนญaษฤmin fฤซ fami -bnatihฤ.}\\
\enquote{The mother put a morsel of food in her daughter's mouth.}

The other irregularity of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{fam} \enquote{a mouth} is that its broken plural is
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุงู} \emph{สพafwฤh}.

Note that the letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} has not been used to form the broken plural, and instead a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, and a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} are used to form it.

\section{Other irregular nouns}\label{other-irregular-nouns}

There are more nouns that have irregularity in their own ways. We will discuss them below.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู} \emph{สพulลซ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุงุช} \emph{สพulฤt}}{ุฃููููู สพulลซ and ุฃููููุงุช สพulฤt}}\label{ux623ux648ux644ux648-eulu-and-ux623ux648ux644ux627ux62a-eulat}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู} \emph{สพulลซ}
(first syllable has a short vowel with a silent \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู})
means \enquote{people\textsubscript{m} of}. It is only used as a masculine plural annexe noun, similar in meaning to
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููู} \emph{dอกhawลซ} which we discussed in section~\ref{zu} above.
There is no singular or dual of this noun.

Here is its form in the different states:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
u-state & a-and i-state \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู} \emph{สพulลซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู} \emph{สพulฤซ} \\
\end{longtable}

Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃููููู ูฑููุฃูุฑูุญูุงูู ุญูููููู.}\\
\emph{liสพuli -lสพarแธฅฤmi แธฅuqลซq.}\\
\enquote{The people of the wombs (i.e.~blood relatives) have rights.}

The feminine counterpart of
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู} \emph{สพulลซ}
is
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุงุช} \emph{สพulฤt} \enquote{women of}.
The first syllable again has a short vowel with a silent \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
u-state & a-and i-state \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุงุชู} \emph{สพulฤtu} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุงุชู} \emph{สพulฤti} \\
\end{longtable}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃููููุงุชู ูฑููุญููููู ุญูููููู ุนูููููฐ ุจูุนููููุชูููููู.}\\
\emph{liสพulฤti -lแธฅamli แธฅuqลซq ษalฤ buษลซlatihinn.}\\
\enquote{The women of pregnancy (i.e.~pregnant women) have rights upon their husbands.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} \emph{สพumm}}{ุฃููู สพumm}}\label{ux623ux645-eumm}

The noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} \emph{สพumm} \enquote{a mother}
forms two \emph{ฤt} sound plural variants:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููุงุช} \emph{สพummahฤt}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุงุช} \emph{สพummฤt}
\end{enumerate}

The first variant
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููุงุช} \emph{สพummahฤt}
is more commonly used.
Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุงููุงุชู ูฑููุบูููููุงูู ุทููููุจูุงุชู.}\\
\emph{สพummahฤtu -lgอกhilmฤni แนญayyibฤt.}\\
\enquote{The boys' mothers are good.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุฉ} \emph{sanah}}{ุณูููุฉ sanah}}\label{ux633ux646ux629-sanah}

The noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุฉ} \emph{sanah} \enquote{a year}
forms both an \emph{ฤt} sound plural
and an \emph{ลซn} sound plural.
(Remember from
section~\ref{applicability-of-the-un-sound-plural}
that a few nouns that don't denote male intelligent beings have \emph{ลซn} sound plurals.)

In both plurals, the singular noun is modified irregularly.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2667}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2667}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2667}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Singular
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\emph{ฤt} sound plural
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\emph{ลซn} sound plural (u-state)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\emph{ลซn} sound plural (a- and i-states)
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุฉ} \emph{sanah} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููููุงุช} \emph{sanawฤt} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููููู} \emph{sinลซna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููููู} \emph{sinฤซna} \\
\end{longtable}

Either of the two plurals may be used interchangeably.
Here are some examples:

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุก} \emph{mฤสพ}}{ููุงุก mฤสพ}}\label{ux645ux627ux621-mae}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุก} \emph{mฤสพ} \enquote{a water} forms its broken plural irregularly: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงู} \emph{miyฤh} \enquote{waters}.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุฉ} \emph{sอกhafah}}{ุดูููุฉ sอกhafah}}\label{ux634ux641ux629-cafah}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุฉ} \emph{sอกhafah} \enquote{a lip}
forms its broken plural irregularly: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุงู} \emph{sอกhifฤh} \enquote{lips}.

Also, despite ending in the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, it does not form an \emph{ฤt} sound plural.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} \emph{สพibn}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูููุฉ} \emph{สพibnah}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุช} \emph{bint}}{ูฑูุจูู สพibn, ูฑูุจูููุฉ สพibnah, and ุจูููุช bint}}\label{ux671ux628ux646-eibn-ux671ux628ux646ux629-eibnah-and-ux628ux646ux62a-bint}

The noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} \emph{สพibn} \enquote{a son} is from the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุจููยป}.
It has two feminine counterparts:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูููุฉ} \emph{สพibnah}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุช} \emph{bint}
\end{enumerate}

which mean \enquote{a daughter}.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} \emph{สพibn} \enquote{a son}
forms both a broken plural
and an \emph{ลซn} sound plural.

Its broken plural is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูููุงุก} \emph{สพabnฤสพ} \enquote{sons}.

In forming the
\emph{ลซn} sound plural,
the singular noun is modified irregularly:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3636}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3636}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Singular
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\emph{ลซn} sound plural (u-state)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\emph{ลซn} sound plural (a- and i-states)
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู} \emph{สพibn} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููููู} \emph{banลซna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููููู} \emph{banฤซna} \\
\end{longtable}

The feminine
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูููุฉ}
and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุช}
\enquote{a daughter}
form the irregular \emph{ฤt} sound plural
\emph{ุจูููุงุช} \emph{banฤt} \enquote{daughters}.
Note that
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุงุช} \emph{banฤt}
is not a broken plural from the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุจูุชยป}. Therefore, it obeys the rules of \emph{ฤt} sound plurals and does not end with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} in the a-state.

Here are some examples using these nouns:

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุณ} \emph{nฤs}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุงุณ} \emph{สพunฤs}}{ููุงุณ nฤs, and ุฃูููุงุณ สพunฤs}}\label{ux646ux627ux633-nas-and-ux623ux646ux627ux633-eunas}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุณ} \emph{nฤs}
and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุงุณ} \emph{สพunฤs}
are from the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃูุณยป}.
They both mean \enquote{a people}.

When indefinite, only
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุงุณ} \emph{สพunฤs}
tends to be used, and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุณ} \emph{nฤs}
tends to be unused.

When definite, only
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุงุณ} \emph{สพannฤs}
tends to be used, and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃูููุงุณ} \emph{สพalสพunฤs}
is unused.

Here are some examples using these nouns:

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The nouns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃูุฉ}}{The nouns ูฑูููุฑูุฃ and ูฑูููุฑูุฃูุฉ}}\label{the-nouns-ux671ux645ux631ux623-and-ux671ux645ux631ux623ux629}

The nouns
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃ} \emph{สพimraสพ} (masc.) \enquote{a man, a person} and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃูุฉ} \emph{สพimraสพah} (fem.) \enquote{a woman}
are quite irregular.

Firstly,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃูุฉ} \emph{สพimraสพah} \enquote{a woman}
is, from the perspective, of its meaning, the feminine counterpart of
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูู} \emph{rajul} \enquote{a man (male human being)}.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃ} \emph{สพimraสพ}, on the other hand, only means \enquote{a man} in a general sense. For example, in the sentence \enquote{A man is only as good as his word.} It can also be translated as \enquote{a person}.

Secondly,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃ} \emph{สพimraสพ} \enquote{a man, a person} has no plural.
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุณ}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุงุณ} \enquote{a people} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู} \enquote{a population} may be used when a plural is required.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃูุฉ} \emph{สพimraสพah} \enquote{a woman} irregularly forms the broken plurals
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุงุก} \emph{nisฤสพ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูููุฉ} \emph{niswah} \enquote{women}. The former
(\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุงุก} \emph{nisฤสพ}) is more commonly used.

Like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุฉ} \emph{sอกhafah}
it also, despite ending in the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, does not form an \emph{ฤt} sound plural.

Thirdly, both nouns are very irregular in how they become definite nouns with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}.
When \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} is prefixed to these nouns to make them definite, they lose the initial connecting hamzah and change their internal vowels. This table shows what we mean:

  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4028}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
u-state & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฑูุกู} \emph{สพalmarสพu} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฑูุฃูุฉู} \emph{สพalmarสพatu} \\

\end{longtable}

The masculine noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฑูุฃ} \emph{สพimraสพ}
has an additional irregularity.
When it is indefinite, it irregularly displays its state, not only on its final letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก}, but also on the letter before it \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑ}.

It is also permissible for it to behave regularly by displaying its state on its final letter only, but this is not as commonly used.

This table shows what we mean:

Note the following points from the list abobe:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Although some names begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}, most don't.
\item
  Many names are semi-flexible (indicated by \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โ}).
\item
  Some names consist of more than a single word, like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏ ูฑููููููฐู} ฦabd Allฤh
\end{itemize}

We will explain these and more details regarding proper nouns in this chapter.

\section{Definiteness of proper nouns}\label{definiteness-of-proper-nouns}

Proper nouns differ from common nouns and adjectival nouns in a couple of important ways:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  All proper nouns, even if they don't begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}, are definite.
\item
  A proper noun which does not begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}, and which is fully-flexible, shall have an \emph{n} mark, despite being definite.
\end{itemize}

The above points are exemplified in the following sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ููุญููููุฏู ูฑููฺฉูุฑูููู ููุฒูููููุจู ูฑูุทูููููุจูุฉู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabtu สพilฤ bayti muแธฅammadini -lkarฤซmi wazaynaba -แนญแนญayyibah.}\\
\enquote{I went to the house of the noble Muแธฅammad and the good Zaynab.}

Note the above from the above example:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญููููุฏู} is fully-flexible so it has an \emph{in}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} in the i-state.
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููููุจู} is semi-flexible so it does not have an \emph{n}-mark, and instead has an \emph{a} mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} in the i-state.
\item
  The proper nouns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญููููุฏ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููููุจ} are describees in descriptive noun phrases.
\item
  Their describers (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููฺฉูุฑูููู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุทูููููุจูุฉู.}, respectively) have \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} to match the definiteness of the definite proper noun describees. Furthermore, they both end with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} because they match the i-state of their describees.
\end{itemize}

\section{Meanings of names}\label{meanings-of-names}

Many names are re-used from common nouns and adjectival nouns with positive meanings. Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญููููุฏ} Muแธฅammad \enquote{a highly praised one\textsubscript{m}}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุนููุฏ} Saษฤซd \enquote{a happy (fortunate) one\textsubscript{m}}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูุณูู} al-แธคasan \enquote{the good one\textsubscript{m}}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููุญูุฉ} แนฌalแธฅah \enquote{an acacia (tree)}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููููููุฉ} Jamฤซlah \enquote{a beautiful one\textsubscript{f}}
\end{itemize}

It is possible for these names to sometimes (technically) cause a sentence to have an ambiguous meaning. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุณู ูฑููุญูุณููู ููุนู ุณูุนููุฏู.}\\
\emph{jalsa -lhasanu maษa saษฤซd}\\
\enquote{al-แธคasan sat with Saษฤซd.}\\
or\\
\enquote{The good one\textsubscript{m} sat with a happy (fortunate) one\textsubscript{m}.}

Context would tell us whether the proper noun or the common/adjectival noun meaning is intended.

Note however the following sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฌููููููุฉู ุฅูููููฐ ูฑููุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabat jamฤซlatu สพila -lbayt.}

This sentence can only be understood to use \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููููููุฉ} with its proper noun meaning:

\enquote{Jamฤซlah went to the house.}

This is because \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููููููุฉ} is semi-flexible as a proper noun and fully-flexible as an adjectival/common noun. If \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููููููุฉ} were intended to be used with its adjectival/common noun meaning then it would have an \emph{un}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} and the sentence would be:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฌููููููุฉู ุฅูููููฐ ูฑููุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabat jamฤซlatun สพila -lbayt.}\\
\enquote{A beautiful one\textsubscript{f} went to the house.}

We will learn why \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููููููุฉ} is semi-flexible as a proper noun in section~\ref{proper-nouns-ending-with-looped-ta} below.

\section{Flexibility of proper nouns}\label{flexibility-of-proper-nouns}

In this section we will discuss the flexibility of proper nouns.
For now, we will deal only with proper nouns that do not begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}.
In terms of their flexibility, proper nouns consist of two types:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Fully-flexible proper nouns.
\item
  Semi-flexible proper nouns.
\end{enumerate}

We will treat each of them below.

\subsection{Fully-flexible proper nouns}\label{fully-flexible-proper-nouns}

For names that don't begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}, the default assumption is that they are fully-flexible, unless they fall into one of the categories of semi-flexible nouns (which we will study soon).

Examples of fully-flexible names are:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญููููุฏ} & Muแธฅammad & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงุฐ} & Muษฤdอกh \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููุญ} & Nลซh & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุนูุฏ} & Saษd \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุนูููุจ} & Sอกhuษayb & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููุงุฑ} & ฦammฤr \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู} & ฦalฤซ & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณููุงู} & แธคassฤn \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏ} & Zayd & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุนููุฏ} & Saษฤซd \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุณ} & Anas & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูุฏ} & Uแธฅud (a mountain) \\
\end{longtable}

These are all masculine names.

Examples of sentences with fully-flexible proper nouns:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู ุบูููุงูู ุทููููุจูู.}\\
\emph{zaydun gอกhulฤmun แนญayyib}\\
\enquote{Zayd is a good boy.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูฺฉูุฑู ุฃูููุณู ุนููููููุง.}\\
\emph{sอกhakara สพanasun ษaliyyฤ.}\\
\enquote{Anas thanked ฦalฤซ.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูุณู ุณูุนููุฏู ูููููุตู ูููุญู ูฑูุฃูุฎูุถูุฑู.}\\
\emph{labisa saษฤซdun qamฤซแนฃa nลซแธฅini -lสพakอกhแธar.}\\
\enquote{Saษฤซd wore Nลซแธฅ's green shirt.}

\subsection{Semi-flexible proper nouns}\label{semi-flexible-proper-nouns}

The rules for the semi-flexibility of proper nouns are a little different from the rules for the semi-flexibility of common nouns and adjectival nouns that we learned in chapter~\ref{semi-flexible-nouns}.
Proper nouns shall be semi-flexible if they fall under one of the categories below. Note that the categories are not mutually exclusive. That is: some semi-flexible proper nouns will fall into more than one category.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Names ending with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}}{Names ending with ุฉ}}\label{names-ending-with-ux629}

All names ending with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} shall be semi-flexible. This rule is specific to proper nouns. We have already seen that common nouns and adjectival nouns that end ith \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} are fully-flexible.

Most such proper nouns are feminine names. Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุฏููุฌูุฉ} & Kอกhadฤซjah & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููููููุฉ} & Maymลซnah \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุทูููุฉ} & Faแนญimah & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตููููููุฉ} & แนขafiyyah \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงุฆูุดูุฉ} & ฦฤสพisอกhah & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูููููุฉ} & Kอกhawlah \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููููุฉ} & Sumayyah & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููููููุฉ} & Jamฤซlah \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุตูุฉ} & แธคafแนฃah & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุขุณูููุฉ} & ฤsiyah \\
\end{longtable}

However, some masculine names may end with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} too:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฒูุฉ} & แธคamzah & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงููููุฉ} & Muษฤwiyah \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูุงููุฉ} & Usฤmah & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูฺฉูุฑูููุฉ} & ฦikrimah \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููุญูุฉ} & แนฌalแธฅah & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุงุฏูุฉ} & ฦubฤdah \\
\end{longtable}

Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููุญูุฉู ูฑููุทููููููู ุจูุนููู ุฌููููููุฉู ูฑููฺฉูุฑููููุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The tall แนฌalแธฅah is the husband of the generous Jamฤซlah.}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Names ending with an extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}}{Names ending with an extrinsic ุงุก or ููฐ}}\label{names-ending-with-an-extrinsic-ux627ux621-or-ux649}

Similar to common nouns and adjectival nouns, all names ending with an extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} shall be semi-flexible. These are usually feminine names. Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูููุงุก} & Asmฤสพ & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููููููฐ} & Laylฤ \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุฑูุฏูุงุก} & Dardฤสพ & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููููููฐ} & Salmฤ \\
\end{longtable}

Examples in sentences:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุณูููููููฐ ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ุฃูุณูููุงุกู.}\\
\enquote{Salmฤ went tp Asmฤสพ's house.}

Sentence word order is usually pretty flexible. For stylistic reasons, it is permissible for a doee to precede the doer.
For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชู ุฏูุฑูุฏูุงุกู ุฃูุณูููุงุกู.}\\
\enquote{Asmฤสพ asked Dardฤสพ}

But because words that end with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ} never display any state, then for these words the sentence word order becomes more rigid. So the following sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชู ููููููููฐ ุณูููููููฐ.}\\
would usually only mean
\enquote{Laylฤ asked Salmฤ.}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Names ending with an extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงู}}{Names ending with an extrinsic ุงู}}\label{names-ending-with-an-extrinsic-ux627ux646}

All names ending with an extrinsic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงู} will be semi-flexible.

This is somewhat different from the rule we learnt for common noun and adjectival nouns in section~\ref{adjectival-noun-an-diptote}. There only adjectival nouns of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุงู} and whose feminine was not formed by adding \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} to it were considered semi-flexible nouns.

Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุซูููุงู} & ฦutอกhmฤn & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูููุถูุงู} & Ramaแธฤn \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููููุงู} & Sufyฤn & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุนูุจูุงู} & Sอกhaษbฤn \\
\end{longtable}

Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุณ ุนูุซูููุงูู ููุนู ุณูููููุงูู ููู ุฑูููุถูุงูู.}\\
\enquote{ฦutอกhmฤn sat with Sufyฤn in Ramaแธฤn.}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Names on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูู}}{Names on the pattern ุฃูููุนูู}}\label{names-on-the-pattern-ux623ux641ux639ux644}

All names on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูู} shall be semi-flexible. Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูููุฏ} & Aแธฅmad & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูุนูุฏ} & Asษad \\
\end{longtable}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Names of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู}}{Names of the pattern ููุนูู}}\label{names-of-the-pattern-ux641ux639ux644}

Names of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} shall be semi-flexible. Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}

\end{longtable}

Interestingly, the fully-flexible name ฦamr is written with a silent \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} at its end: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุฑู} when in the u- and i-states in order to distinguish it from the more common name ฦumar. Otherwise, both names would appear identical when written without vowel marks, thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุฑ}.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}

ฦumar & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุฑู} \emph{ษumaru} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุฑู} \emph{ษumara} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุฑู} \emph{ษumara} \\
\end{longtable}

\subsubsection{Names that are originally verbs}\label{names-that-are-originally-verbs}

Names that are originally verbs are semi-flexible. Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฒููุฏ} Yazฤซd \enquote{He increases}
\item
  \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููุด} Yaษฤซsอกh \enquote{He lives}
\end{itemize}

Their origin as verbs will be apparent when we study incomplete-action verbs.

\subsubsection{Names of foreign origin}\label{names-of-foreign-origin}

Names of foreign origin are generally semi-flexible. These include the names of angels, many of the previous prophets and messengers, and other persons. Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุจูุฑููู} & Jibrฤซl & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูฺฉูุฑููููุง} & Zakariyyฤ \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุจูุฑูุงูููู} & Ibrฤhฤซm & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญูููููฐ} & Yaแธฅyฤ \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุณูููุงุนููู} & Ismฤษฤซl & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุฌูุฑ} & Hฤjar \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุณูุญูุงู} & Isยทแธฅฤq & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููู} & Maryam \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููุจ} & Yaษqลซb & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃูุฌููุฌ} & Yaสพjลซj \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููุณูู} & Yลซsuf & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃูุฌููุฌ} & Maสพjลซj \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููุณ} & Yลซnus & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุจููููุณ} & Iblฤซs \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุฏูุฑููุณ} & Idrฤซs & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุนููู} & Pharoah \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููุจ} & Ayyลซb & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููู} & Heraclius \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููุณูููฐ} & Mลซsฤ & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุณูุฑูููฐ} & Chosroes \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููุณูููฐ} & ฦฤซsฤ & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุตูุฑ} & Caesar \\
\end{longtable}

Note that
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุนููู} \enquote{Pharoah}
as \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุตูุฑ} \enquote{Caesar},
despite being titles,
are treated as proper names.

The only exception to this rule is a masculine name of foreign origin that comprises of only three letters, and whose middle letter has an รธ-mark. Such a name will be fully-flexible. Example:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููุญ} Nลซแธฅ
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Feminine names}\label{feminine-names}

All feminine names, regardless of their origin, or their ending, shall be semi-flexible. We have already given examples of semi-flexible feminine names that end with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงุก}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}, so we will provide other examples here:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููููุจ} & Zaynab & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููู} & Maryam \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุนูุงุฏ} & Suษฤd & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุฌูุฑ} & Hฤjar \\
\end{longtable}

The only exception to this rule is a feminine name of native Arabic origin, that comprises of only three letters, and whose middle letter has an รธ-mark. Such a name is permitted to be optionally fully-flexible or semi-flexible. Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุฏ} Hind
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุนูุฏ} Daษd
\end{itemize}

Example of usage:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ููููุฏู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ุฏูุนูุฏู.}\\
or\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ููููุฏู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ุฏูุนูุฏู.}\\
``Hind went to Daษd's house.

\section{\texorpdfstring{The name \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงู}}{The name ููููุงู}}\label{the-name-ux641ux644ux627ux646}

The fully-flexible name \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงู} is used as a place-holder name in casual conversations. It may be translated into English as \enquote{so-and-so}. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธููููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููููุงููุง ููุบูุฏูุฑู ุจูููููุงูู.}
\enquote{The man wronged so-and-so and he acted treacherously with so-and-so.}

For females, the name \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงููุฉ} is used.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุฏูููุชู ููููุงููุฉู.}\\
\enquote{So-and-so\textsubscript{f} told the truth.}

\section{The Replacement}\label{the-replacement}

Before we proceed with our discussion on proper nouns, we will take a short digression to discuss a grammatical concept called the \emph{replacement}. We will only give a short preview here and will treat it fully in chapter~\ref{the-replacement-chapter}.

A \emph{replacement} is a word that follows another word, the \emph{replacee}, and replaces it from the perspective of the grammar of the sentence. The replacement is put in the same state as the replacee.
Here is an example of a sentence with a replacement and a replacee:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-13-1.pdf}

In the above sentence, the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง} \enquote{a book} is the replacement of
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดููููููุง} \enquote{something}. Therefore, it is put in the same a-state.

The replacement is frequently used with proper nouns. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ุนูููููู ุนูููููู.}\\
\enquote{The boy went to his uncle ฦalฤซ's house.}

In this sentence, the name \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู} ฦalฤซ is the replacement of the replacee \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููู} \enquote{uncle}. Note, again, that the replacement comes after the replacee and matches it in state. However, the replacement does not need to come directly after the replacee. We can see that there is the pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \enquote{his} between them.

Here is another example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ูฑูุทููุงููุจู ููุนูุงุฐู ูฑููููุนูููููู ุณูุนูุฏูุง.}\\
\enquote{The student Muษฤdอกh asked the teacher Saษd.}

\section{Annexed names}\label{annexed-names}

So far we have only dealt with proper nouns that are single words. There are some proper nouns that may be formed from two words that are in an annexation. These belong to different categories:

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\enquote{Slave of} names}{``Slave of'' names}}\label{slave-of-names}

Some names are formed by annexing the noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏ} \emph{ษabd} \enquote{a slave} to one of the names of Allฤh. The most common of these names are:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏ ูฑููููููฐู} ฦabd Allฤh \enquote{the Slave of Allฤh}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏ ูฑูุฑููุญูููููฐู} ฦabd al-Raแธฅmฤn \enquote{the Slave of the Most Merciful}
\end{itemize}

As usual, the base noun shall always be in the i-state. And the state of the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏ} is variable, depending on it's function in the sentence. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏู ูฑููููููฐูู ูููู ุฃูุฎูู ุนูุจูุฏู ูฑูุฑููุญูููููฐูู.}
\enquote{ฦabd Allฤh is the brother of ฦabd al-Raแธฅmฤn.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\enquote{Parent of} names}{``Parent of'' names}}\label{parent-of-names}

It is common to call a man, not by his own given name, but rather by calling him the father of one of his children, usually his first born son. For example, if a man named \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูููุฏ} \enquote{Aแธฅmad} had a son named \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏ} \enquote{Zayd}, he may be called \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูู ุฒูููุฏ} Abลซ Zayd \enquote{Zayd's father}. Example of usage in a sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ุฃูุจูู ุฒูููุฏู.}\\
\enquote{I went to Abลซ Zayd's house.}\\
(Note how \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู} has an \emph{in}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} in the i-state because it is fully-flexible.)

While using the name of first-born son is more common, a daughter's name could be used as well. Example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชู ุฃูุจูุง ุฑููููููุฉู ุณูุคุงููุง.}\\
\enquote{I asked Abลซ Ruqayyah a question.}\\
(Note how \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑููููููุฉู} has an \emph{a}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} in the i-state because it is semi-flexible.)

Women, too, are similarly called as the mother of one of their children. For example,
the wife of the Prophet
(may Allฤh grant peace and confer blessing upon him)
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู ุญูุจููุจูุฉ} Umm แธคabฤซbah
was called thus because she had a daughter named
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุจููุจูุฉ}
from a previous marriage.

By the way, a person need not literally be a father or a mother to be called in such a way. These names may be applied as nicknames.

For example, the Companion of the Prophet
(may Allฤh grant peace and confer blessing upon him)
was called
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูู ููุฑููุฑูุฉ}
Abลซ Hurayrah
because it is reported that he used to have a pet kitten (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููุฑูุฉ}). Here is an example of this name in a sentence.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูู ููุฑูููุฑูุฉู ุตูุญูุงุจูููู ุฌูููููู.}\\
\enquote{Abลซ Hurayrah is a great Companion.}\\
