(Note how \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููุฑูุฉู} is now considered a semi-flexible proper noun even though it may originally have been derived from the common noun \enquote{a kitten}.)

Similarly, the Companion \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูู ุจูฺฉูุฑู} Abลซ Bakr is not known to have a son named \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูฺฉูุฑ}.

It is often the case that a \enquote{parent of} name overtakes the actual given name of person in popularity, and becomes the person's name for all intents and purposes. Such is indeed the case for the Companions
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูู ุจูฺฉูุฑู} Abลซ Bakr
and
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูู ููุฑููุฑูุฉ}
Abลซ Hurayrah.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\enquote{Son of} names}{``Son of'' names}}\label{son-of-names}

In a manner similar to \enquote{parent of} names, a person may be referred to as the son of his parent. For example, the Companion \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุฑ} ฦumar had a son named
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏ ูฑููููููฐู} ฦabd Allฤh. He is commonly known as \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู ุนูููุฑ} Ibn ฦumar \enquote{ฦumar's son}.

Attributing a son to his father is most common. But attributing him to a mother or other ancestor is also possible.

Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  the Companion \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููุงุฑ} was affectionately called \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู ุณููููููุฉ} Ibn Sumayyah \enquote{Sumayyah's son} by the Prophet
  (may Allฤh grant peace and confer blessing upon him). His mother Sumayyah was an early martyr in Islฤm.
\item
  the famous scholar \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู ฺฉูุซููุฑ} Ibn Katอกhฤซr is referred to by his grandfather's name \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุซููุฑ} Katอกhฤซr.
\item
  a human being is called \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู ุขุฏูู} based on his being a descendent of the first man, the Prophet Adam.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Full names}\label{full-names}

The full name of a person is formed by putting his given name first, and then his \enquote{son of} name after it as a replacement. Here is an example of a full name:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู ุจููู ุนูููููู}\\
Zayd the son of ฦalฤซ

Note some peculiarities of the full name:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The name \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏ} \enquote{Zayd} has lost its \emph{n} mark.
\item
  The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูู} \enquote{son} is not written with its initial connecting hamzah \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑ}.
\end{itemize}

These peculiarities are only when forming a full name in this manner. Consider for example the following sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู ูฑุจููู ุนูููููู.}\\
\enquote{Zayd is the son of ฦalฤซ.}

In the above example, the name \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู} has its \emph{n}-mark and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑุจูู} is written with its connecting hamzah \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑ}. Therefore this is not an expression of the full name in a replacee-replacement format. Rather, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑุจููู ุฃูุญูููุฏู} here is the information of the sentence.

For women, the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุช} is used instead of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูู}.

Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃูุชู ูฑููููุนููููููุฉู ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุทููุงููุจูุฉู ุฒูููููุจู ุจูููุชู ุฃูุญูููุฏู.}\\
\enquote{The teacher read the book of the student Zaynab the daughter of Aแธฅmad.}

The names of multiple forefathers may be strung together in this way separated by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูู}. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุณููู ููุจููููููุง ููุญููููุฏู ุจููู ุนูุจูุฏู ูฑููููููฐูู ุจููู ุนูุจูุฏู ูฑููููุทููููุจู.}\\
\enquote{Our prophet's name is Muแธฅammad the son of ฦabd Allฤh the son of ฦabd al-Muแนญแนญalib.}\\
(Note that the second \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจููู} is in the i-state to match the state of the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏู} in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏู ูฑูููููฐู}.)

We will deal with complete full names in section~\ref{complete-full-names} below.

\subsection{Other annexed names}\label{other-annexed-names}

Other words besides
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏ},
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจ},
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู},
and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู}
may be used in annexed names too. Here are some examples:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูู ูฑููููุฑูููููู} Dอกhu l-Qarnayn \enquote{He of the two horns}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏููููุฉ ูฑููููุจูู} \emph{madinatu -nnabiyyi} \enquote{The City of the Prophet}, frequently reduced to simply \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฏููููุฉ} \enquote{Medina}.

  Context is used to infer whether by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฏููููุฉ} is meant \enquote{Medina} or \enquote{the city}.
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑูุคู ูฑููููููุณ} Imruสพ al-Qays \enquote{The man of al-Qays}, a pre-Islฤmic poet.
\end{itemize}

\section{\texorpdfstring{Names beginning with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}}{Names beginning with ูฑููู}}\label{names-beginning-with-ux671ux644}

Most names do not begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}. Some, however, do begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}. Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูุณูู} & al-แธคasan & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฒููุจูููุฑ} & al-Zubayr \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูุณูููู} & al-แธคusayn & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุนูููุงู} & al-Nuษmฤn \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุนูุจููุงุณ} & al-ฦabbฤs & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูุงุฑูุซ} & al-แธคฤritอกh \\
\end{longtable}

If a proper noun begins with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} then the question of its flexibility is mostly irrelevant.
This is because noun beginning with with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} display their state fully, regardless of whether or not they are semi-flexible without the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูุณููู ุญููููุฏู ุฑูุณูููู ูฑููููููฐูู ุตูู ุงูููู ุนููู ูุณูู.}\\
\enquote{al-แธคasan is the grandson of the messenger of Allฤh (may Allฤh grant peace and confer blessing upon him).}\\
(u-state displayed with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ูฑููููุนูููุงูู ุนููู ุฃูููุฑู.}\\
\enquote{The man asked al-Nuษmฤn about a matter.}\\
(a-state displayed with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฅูููู ุจูููุชู ูฑููููุนูููุงูู.}\\
\enquote{I went to al-Nuษmฤn's house.}\\
(i-state displayed with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.)

Names that begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} can sometimes lose their initial \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}. Sometimes, this is systematic, as we will lear in section~\ref{calling-names-with-al}.
Other times, it's hard to tell why.

Conversely, names that don't begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} can sometimes gain it.

Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The name of the daughter of the Companion \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูู ูฑูุฏููุฑูุฏูุงุก} Abu l-Dardฤสพ is actually \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุฑูุฏูุงุก} Dardฤสพ, not \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฏููุฑูุฏูุงุก}.
\item
  The son of the uncle of the Prophet
  (may Allฤh grant peace and confer blessing upon him)
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุนูุจููุงุณ} al-ฦabbฤs
  is called
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู ุนูุจููุงุณ} Ibn ษabbฤs, not \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู ูฑููุนูุจููุงุณ}.
\end{itemize}

However, the son of
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฒููุจูููุฑ} al-Zubayr\textbar{}
is called
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู ูฑููุฒููุจูููุฑ} Ibn al-Zubayr with the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}.

\section{Place names}\label{place-names}

Place names are generally feminine. Because of their feminine gender, those not beginning with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} will be semi-flexible according to section~\ref{feminine-names} above.

Examples of place names are:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉููุฉ} & Mecca & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฏููููุฉ} & Medina \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูููุดูู} & Damascus & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุงููุฑูุฉ} & Cairo \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุบูุฏูุงุฏ} & Bagอกhdฤd & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููููุฏ} & India \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุตูุฑ} & Egypt & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุตูููู} & China \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุฑูุณ} & Persia & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑูููู} & Rome \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุจููฺฉ} & Tabลซk & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูุตูุฑูุฉ} & Baแนฃrah \\
\end{longtable}

Example of use:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ุฅูููููฐ ููฺฉููุฉู ูฑููููฺฉูุฑููููุฉู ูููฑููููุฏููููุฉู ูฑูููููููููุฑูุฉู.}\\
\enquote{The man went to the ennobled Mecca and the illuminated Medina.}

While most place names are feminine, a few are masculine. Among these are:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}rlrl@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููููู} & Yemen & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุงู} & the Levant \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุนูุฑูุงู} & Iraq & & \\
\end{longtable}

\section{Names of tribes}\label{names-of-tribes}

Here are examples of names of tribes:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑููุด} & Quraysอกh & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃูููุณ} & al-Aws \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููู ุชููููู} & Banลซ Tamฤซm & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฎูุฒูุฑูุฌ} & al-Kอกhazraj \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุฒูู} & Hawฤzin & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููู ุฅูุณูุฑูุงุฆููู} & Banลซ Isrฤสพฤซl \\
\end{longtable}

Tribes are usually called by the name of their progenitor. For example, \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุณูุฑูุงุฆููู} Isrฤสพฤซl is a name of the Prophet \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููุจ} Yaษqลซb.
The \emph{ลซn} sound plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููููู} \enquote{sons/children} is annexed to the name
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุณูุฑูุงุฆููู} Isrฤสพฤซl
to get the name of the tribe
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููู ุฅูุณูุฑูุงุฆููู} Banลซ Isrฤสพฤซl \enquote{the children of Isrฤสพฤซl}. In the a- and i-states, this becomes
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููู ุฅูุณูุฑูุงุฆููู} Banฤซ Isrฤสพฤซl.

Not all tribe names have \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููููู} \enquote{sons} annexed to them, but many do. And often it is optional to keep or drop the annexed \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููููู}. Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููุด} Quraysอกh usually does not have \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููููู} annexed to it.
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููู ุชููููู} Banลซ Tamฤซm may optionally drop the annexed \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููููู} and be called simply \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชููููู} Tamฤซm.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Flexibility of tribe names}\label{flexibility-of-tribe-names}

The flexibility of tribe names depends on the name. Here are some examples:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุณูุฑูุงุฆููู} Isrฤสพฤซl is a name of foreign origin and is therefore semi-flexible. Example:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุนูุซู ูฑููููููฐูู ูููุณูููฐ ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููู ุฅูุณูุฑูุงุฆูููู.}\\
  \enquote{Allฤh sent Mลซsฤ to the children of Isrฤสพฤซl.}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููุด} Quraysอกh and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชููููู} Tamฤซm are native Arabic masculine names and are therefore fully-flexible. Example:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑููุดู ููุจูููู ุชูููููู ููุจููููุชูุงูู.}\\
  \enquote{Quraysอกh and Banลซ Tamฤซm are tribes\textsubscript{2}.}
\item
  \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุฒูู} Hawฤzin is on the semi-flexible noun pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงููู} and is therefore semi-flexible.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Gender of tribe names}\label{gender-of-tribe-names}

Tribe names are unusual in that they are treated as both singular feminine and plural masculine.
If the tribe name is the doer of a verb then it is usually treated as singular feminine.
Otherwise, for example, if it comes before the verb, then the plural masculine pronouns are used for it.

Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูฺฉูููุชู ููุฑูููุดู ููฺฉููุฉู ููุนูุจูุฏููุง ูฑููุฃูุตูููุงูู.}\\
\enquote{Quraysอกh dwelled in Mecca and they worshipped idols.}

\section{Titles}\label{titles}

Titles are common nouns that denote a rank or position of a person. Titles in English include: Doctor, Mister, and King. For example:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  King David
\item
  Mr.~Smith
\item
  Dr.~Adams
\end{itemize}

Here are some examples of titles in Arabic:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}rlrl@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุจููู} & Prophet & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฅูููุงู} & Imฤm \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููููฺฉ} & King & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููููุฎ} & Sอกhaykอกh \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃููููุฑ} & Commander & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูุงููุธ} & แธคฤfidฬฃอกh \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุงุถูู} & Judge & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃูุณูุชูุงุฐ} & Professor \\
\end{longtable}

Some Arabic titles are left untranslated in English like

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฅูููุงู} Imฤm (a leader)
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููููุฎ} Sอกhaykอกh (a venerable man)
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูุงููุธ} แธคฤfidฬฃอกh (one who has memorized, and preserved religious texts)
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Titles as replacees}\label{titles-as-replacees}

Titles are usually placed in front a proper noun and made definite with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} to match the proper noun. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ุฑูุฌููู ูฑููุฅูููุงูู ููุงููฺฉูุง ุนููู ุฃูููุฑู.}\\
\enquote{A man asked Imฤm Mฤlik about a matter.}

In the above sentence, the title \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฅูููุงูู} Imฤm is a replacee and the name \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงููฺฉูุง} Mฤlik is the replacement.

Some titles are formed from annexations. Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎููููููุฉู ุฑูุณูููู ูฑููููููฐูู} & the Successor of the Messenger of Allฤh & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู ูฑููููููฐูู} & the Sword of Allฤh \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุฑู ูฑููููุคูููููููู} & the Commander of the Believers & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุงุฏู ูฑูุฏููููู} & the Pillar of the Faith \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู ูฑููููุคูููููููู} & the Mother of the Believers & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูููุงุญู ูฑูุฏููููู} & the Righteousness of the Faith \\
\end{longtable}

Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู ูฑููููุคูููููููู ุนูุงุฆูุดูุฉู ูููู ูฑูุจูููุฉู ุฎููููููุฉู ุฑูุณูููู ูฑููููููฐูู ุฃูุจูู ุจูฺฉูุฑู.}\\
\enquote{The Mother of the Believers ฦฤสพisอกhah is the daughter of the Successor of the Messenger of Allฤh Abลซ Bakr.}

\subsection{Titles in annexations}\label{titles-in-annexations}

Some prominent inanimate objects, like mountains, rivers, and cities, may have titles. For example:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Mount Everest
\item
  the river Nile
\item
  the city of Damascus
\end{itemize}

In Arabic, the titles for these objects usually don't occur as replacees as they do for persons. Rather, the title is annexed to the proper noun in an annexation. Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุจููู ุฃูุญูุฏู} & Mount Uแธฅud & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏููููุฉู ุฏูููุดููู} & the city of Damascus \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุฑู ูฑููููููู} & the river Nile & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุฑู ุฑูููุถูุงูู} & the month of Ramaแธฤn \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููู ูฑููุฌูููุนูุฉู} & the day of Friday & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููุฑูุฉู ูฑููููุงุชูุญูุฉู} & the Sลซrah of al-Fฤtiแธฅah \\
\end{longtable}

Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃูุชู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ุณููุฑูุฉู ูฑููููุงุชูุญูุฉู ููู ุดูููุฑู ุฑูููุถูุงูู.}\\
\enquote{The girl read the Sลซrah of al-Fฤtiแธฅah in the month of Ramaแธฤn.}

\section{Nicknames}\label{nicknames}

Nicknames are often given to people. They are usually descriptive of some physical quality or character trait of the person. For example, the Companion Abลซ Bakr was given the nickname \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุตููุฏูููู} \enquote{the steadfast affirmer of the truth}.

Nicknames usually come after a person's name as a replacement.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูู ุจูฺฉูุฑู ูฑูุตููุฏููููู ูููู ุฎููููููุฉู ุฑูุณูููู ูฑููููููฐูู.}\\
\enquote{Abลซ Bakr the steadfast affirmer of the truth is the successor of the messenger of Allฤh.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃู ุณูููููููุงูู ูฑูุฃูุนูููุดู ูฑููููุฑูุขูู.}\\
\enquote{Sulaymฤn the weak-sighted read the Qurสพฤn.}

\section{The affiliate adjectival noun}\label{the-affiliate-adjectival-noun}

The affiliate adjectival noun is a kind of adjectival noun that indicates an affiliation.

Here are some examples of affiliate adjectival nouns:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2857}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุฑูุงูููู} & an Iraqi & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุดููู} & a Quraysอกhite \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉููู} & a Meccan & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููููููู} & a Tamฤซmian \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูููุดููููู} & a Damascan & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุณูุฑูุงุฆูููููู} & an Isrฤสพฤซlite \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุงููุนููู} & a Sอกhฤfiษite & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููููู} & a แธคanafฤซ \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงููฺฉููู} & a Mฤlikฤซ & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุจููููู} & a แธคanbalฤซ \\
\end{longtable}

Note the following about affiliate adjectival nouns:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Generally, the ending \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โููู} \emph{-iyy} is suffixed to a noun to create an affiliate adjectival noun.
\item
  The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} ending is removed before adding the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โููู} \emph{-iyy} suffix.
\item
  Sometimes there are other internal changes to the word before this suffix is added. For example,

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููุด} becomes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุดููู}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The affiliate adjectival noun may be formed from any of the names of a person. (Usually, one of the more distinctive names is chosen.) For example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    A follower of the school of thought of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฅูููุงู ุฃูุจูู ุญููููููุฉ} Imฤm Abลซ แธคanฤซfah is called \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููููู} \enquote{a แธคanafฤซ}.
  \item
    A follower of the school of thought of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฅูููุงู ุฃูุญูููุฏ ุจูู ุญูููุจูู} Imฤm Aแธฅmad ibn แธคanbal is called \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุจููููู} \enquote{a แธคanbalฤซ}.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

We will treat adjectival nouns more fully in chapter~\ref{the-affiliate-adjective-chapter}.

Afflilate adjectival nouns frequently occur with proper nouns. They come after the proper noun as a replacement, and are made definite by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} to match the proper noun in definiteness. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุจูู ฺฉูุซููุฑู ูฑูุฏููููุดูููููู ููููุณููุฑู ููููุคูุฑููุฎู.}\\
\enquote{Ibn Katอกhฤซr the Damascan is an exegete and a historian.}

\section{Complete full names}\label{complete-full-names}

We have already studied how a basic full name is formed in section~\ref{full-names}. Here, we will expand on that topic.

The complete full name of a person is formed by placing some or all of his different names in a particular order. Each name in the order is a replacement of one of the names before it. Generally, the order is:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Titles
\item
  \enquote{Father of} name
\item
  Given name
\item
  \enquote{Son of} names
\item
  Affiliate names
\end{enumerate}

The nickname's position is variable.

Here are some examples of full names in varying degrees of completeness:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงุฆูุดูุฉู ูููู ูฑุจูููุฉู ุฎููููููุฉู ุฑูุณูููู ูฑููููููฐูู ุฃูุจูู ุจูฺฉูุฑู ูฑูุตููุฏููููู.}\\
\enquote{ฦฤสพisอกhah is the daughter of the Successor of the Messenger of Allฤh, Abลซ Bakr, the steadfast affirmer of the truth.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชููู ุฃูุจูู ููุคูููุคูุฉู ูฑููููุฌููุณูููู ุฃููููุฑู ูฑููููุคูููููููู ุฃูุจูุง ุญูููุตู ุนูููุฑู ุจููู ูฑููุฎูุทููุงุจู.}\\
\enquote{Abลซ Luสพluสพah, the Magian killed the Commander of the Believers, Abลซ แธคafแนฃ, ฦumar the son of al-Kอกhaแนญแนญฤb.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑููุญูุงููุธู ูฑููููุคูุฑููุฎู ูฑููููููุณููุฑู ุนูููุงุฏู ูฑูุฏููููู ุฃูุจูู ูฑููููุฏูุงุกู ุฅูุณูููุงุนูููู ุจููู ุนูููุฑู ุจููู ฺฉูุซููุฑู ูฑููููุฑูุดูููู ูฑูุฏููููุดูููููู ูฑูุดููุงููุนูููู ุชูููุณููุฑูุง.}\\
\enquote{The แธคฤfidฬฃอกh, the historian, the exegete, the Pillar of the Faith, the father of al-Fidฤสพ, Ismฤสพฤซl the son of ฦumar the son of Katอกhฤซr, the Quraysอกhite, the Damascan, the Sอกhฤfiษite wrote an exegesis.}\\
(Note how the second \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจููู} is in the i-state because it is a replacement of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุฑู} which is in the i-state because it is a base noun of the first \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจููู}.)

\chapter{Calling out}\label{calling-out}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-13}

When calling out to someone in Arabic, the particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{yฤ} is usually prefixed to the person's name. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุณููููุงูู ุนูููููฺฉููู ููุง ุฒูููููุจู.}\\
\emph{สพassalฤmu ษalaykum yฤ zaynabu.}\\
\enquote{Peace be upon you, O Zaynab.}

There are different rules regarding the state markings of the noun following \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{yฤ} and we will describe them in the following sections.

\section{Calling out to specific persons}\label{calling-out-to-specific-persons}

\subsection{Using single word personal names}\label{using-single-word-personal-names}

When a specific person is called out to, and the name used to call him consists of a single word, then that word shall be in the u-state. The sentence above is an example of this rule where the name \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒููููุจู} \emph{zaynabu} \enquote{Zaynab} is in the u-state.

If the word would have an \emph{n}-mark, then the \emph{n}-mark is dropped. So, for example, the name \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู} \emph{zaydun} \enquote{Zayd} usually has an \emph{n}-mark. But when used for being called out to, the \emph{n}-mark is dropped and it becomes:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุณููููุงูู ุนูููููฺฉููู ููุง ุฒูููุฏู.}\\
\emph{สพassalฤmu ษalaykum yฤ zaydu.}\\
\enquote{Peace be upon you, O Zayd.}

\subsection{Using single word indefinite common nouns}\label{using-single-word-indefinite-common-nouns}

The examples above show the person being called out to using a personal name. Instead of a personal name, a common noun can also be used with the same rule. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุณููููุงูู ุนูููููฺฉููู ููุง ุบูููุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพassalฤmu ษalaykum yฤ gอกhulฤmu.}\\
\enquote{Peace be upon you, O you boy.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุณููููุงูู ุนูููููฺฉููู ููุง ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพassalฤmu ษalaykum yฤ jฤriyatu.}\\
\enquote{Peace be upon you, O you girl.}

In English, we have shown that a specific person is being called using the word \enquote{you}, e.g., \enquote{O you boy}. Duals and plurals are also allowed, again with the same rule:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฑูุฌูุงููุ ููุฏู ุญูุฏูุซู ุฃูููุฑู.}\\
\emph{yฤ rijฤlu qad แธฅadatอกha สพamrun.}\\
\enquote{O you men, a matter has occurred.}

Note how the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุฌูุงูู} \emph{rijฤlu} \enquote{men} does not have an \emph{n}-mark because the word is used to call out to the specific persons.

Similarly,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ููุงุนูุจูุงูู ุจูุฏูุฃูุชูููุง ูฑููููุนูุจู ููููุง ููุนูููุชูููุง ูฑููุนููููู.}\\
\emph{yฤ lฤษibฤni badaสพtuma -llaษiba wamฤ faษaltuma -lษamal.}\\
\enquote{O you players\textsubscript{2}, you have started playing and you have not done the work.}

\subsection{Using single word definite common nouns}\label{using-single-word-definite-common-nouns}

When using a common noun to call out to a person, especially if the common noun is a title, it is often desired to make the common noun definite with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}. In this case, the particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{yฤ} is modified to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููุง} \emph{สพayyuhฤ}, or sometimes \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฃููููููุง} \emph{yฤ สพayyuhฤ}. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฃููููููุง ูฑููุฃูุณูุชูุงุฐูุ ููุฏู ููุนูููุชู ูฑููููุงุฌูุจู.}\\
\emph{yฤ สพayyuha -lสพustฤdอกhu, qad faษaltu -lwฤjiba}\\
\enquote{O you the Professor, I have done the obligatory {[}work{]}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง ุณูููููู ุฃููููููุง ูฑูุทููุจููุจู.}\\
\emph{สพana saqฤซmun, สพayyuha -แนญแนญabฤซbu.}\\
\enquote{I am ill, O you the Doctor.}

If the person being called out to is feminine, then \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููุง} \emph{สพayyuhฤ} is modified to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุชูููุง} \emph{สพayyatuhฤ}. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุชูููุง ูฑููููุนููููููุฉูุ ููููฐุฐูุง ฺฉูุชูุงุจูู.}\\
\emph{สพayyatuha -lmuษallimatu, hฤdอกhฤ kitฤbi.}\\
\enquote{O you the teacher\textsubscript{f}, this is my book.}

\subsection{Using multiple words}\label{using-multiple-words}

The above discussion pertains to calling out to the addressed person with a single word. Often times a person's name may consist of multiple words. For example:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุจูุฏู ูฑููููููฐูู}\\
  \emph{ษabdu -llฤhi}\\
  \enquote{ฦabd Allฤh}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูู ุจูฺฉูุฑู}\\
  \emph{สพabลซ bakrin}\\
  \enquote{Abลซ Bakr}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูููุงุญู ูฑูุฏููููู}\\
  \emph{แนฃalฤแธฅu -ddฤซni}\\
  \enquote{Salฤแธฅ ad-Dฤซn}
\end{itemize}

In this case, then instead of the u-state, the word is put into the a-state. Furthermore, the \emph{n}-mark, if any, is preserved. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุนูุฑูููุชู ุฐู ูฐููฺฉู ูฑูุฑููุฌูููุ ููุง ุนูุจูุฏู ูฑููููููฐูู.}\\
\emph{mฤ ษaraftu dอกhฤlika -rrajula, yฤ ษabda -llฤhi.}\\
\enquote{I have not recognized that man, O ฦabd Allฤh.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฃูุจูุง ุจูฺฉูุฑูุ ุฃูููุชู ุฑูุฌููู ฺฉูุฑูููู.}\\
\emph{yฤ สพabฤ bakrin, สพanta rajulun karฤซmun}\\
\enquote{O Abลซ Bakr, You are a noble man.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุตูููุงุญู ูฑูุฏูููููุ ุตูุจูุฑูุชู ููููุตูุฑูฺฉู ูฑููููููฐูู.}\\
\emph{yฤ แนฃalฤแธฅa -ddฤซni, แนฃabarta fanaแนฃaraka -llฤhu.}\\
\enquote{O Salฤแธฅ ad-Dฤซn, you were patient so Allฤh gave you victory.}

If, instead of a personal name, a noun phrase consisting of multiple words is used to call out to a person, then in this case as well, the first noun shall be in the a-state. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฃููููุฑู ูฑููููุคูููููููุ ููุฏู ุญูุถูุฑู ูฑูููููููู.}\\
\emph{yฤ สพamฤซra -lmuสพminฤซna, qad แธฅaแธara -lqawmu.}\\
\enquote{O Commander of the Believers, the people are present.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ูฑุจููู ุฃูุฎููุ ููุฏู ุณูููุทู ููููููฺฉู ุนูููู ูฑููุฃูุฑูุถู.}\\
\emph{ya -bna สพakอกhฤซ, qad saqaแนญa qalamuka.}\\
\enquote{O my nephew, your pen has fallen on the ground.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุชูููุงูููุฐู ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉูุ ูฑููุนููููู ุฃูููุงููุฉู.}\\
\emph{yฤ talฤmฤซdอกha -lmadrasati, -lษilmu สพamฤnatun.}\\
\enquote{O pupils of the school, knowledge is a trust.}

When multiple words are used to call out to a person, the second word in the noun-chain may be a pronoun. Here too, the first noun shall be in the a-state. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฃูุจุงูุง}\\
\emph{yฤ สพabฤnฤ}\\
\enquote{O our father}

\section{Calling out to unspecified persons}\label{calling-out-to-unspecified-persons}

All the discussion so far has pertained to calling out to specific persons. So for example, when you say,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ููุณูููููุ ููุตูุฑู ูฑููููููฐูู ููุฑููุจู.}\\
\emph{yฤ muslimu, naแนฃru -llฤhi qarฤซbun.}\\
\enquote{O you Muslim, the victory of Allฤh is near.}

then you are addressing a specific Muslim, who is perhaps in front of you.

If an unspecified person or persons are being called out, then the word used to call out is put into the a-state. Furthermore, the \emph{n}-mark, if any, is preserved. So if you want to address any unspecific Muslim, you will say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ููุณูููููุงุ ููุตูุฑู ูฑููููููฐูู ููุฑููุจู.}\\
\emph{yฤ musliman, naแนฃru -llฤhi qarฤซbun.}\\
\enquote{O {[}any{]} Muslim, the victory of Allฤh is near.}

If multiple words are used, whether or not the person called out to is specific or unspecified, then too the first noun is put in the a-state.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ููุงุนูุจูู ููุนูุจูุ ูฑููููููุชู ุซูููููู.}\\
\emph{yฤ lฤษibฤซ luษabini, -lwaqtu tอกhamฤซnun.}\\
\enquote{O {[}any{]} players of games, time is precious.}

\section{\texorpdfstring{Omitting \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{yฤ}}{Omitting ููุง yฤ}}\label{omitting-ux64aux627-ya}

When calling out to someone, it is permissible to omit the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{yฤ}, especially when the person being called is very near. So, instead of saying,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฒูููุฏูุ ุณูุคุงููฺฉู ุฌููููุฏู.}\\
\emph{yฤ zaydu, suสพฤluka jayyidun.}\\
\enquote{O Zayd, your question is excellent.}

it is permissible to say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏูุ ุณูุคุงููฺฉู ุฌููููุฏู.}\\
\emph{zaydu, suสพฤluka jayyidun.}\\
\enquote{Zayd, your question is excellent.}

Note that even when \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{yฤ} is ommitted the name \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู} \emph{zaydu} \enquote{Zayd} is in the u-state without any \emph{n}-mark.

This usage is especially common when supplicating to Allฤh with the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุจูู} \emph{rabbun} \enquote{lord}, to emphasize the closeness of Allฤh to the supplicator. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุจูููุง ููฺฉู ูฑููุญูููุฏู.}\\
\emph{rabbanฤ laka -lแธฅamdu.}\\
\enquote{Our Lord, for you is {[}all{]} praise.}

\section{\texorpdfstring{Shortening the attached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{ฤซ} \enquote{my}}{Shortening the attached pronoun ู ฤซ ``my''}}\label{shortening-the-attached-pronoun-ux64a-i-my}

When calling someone with the pronoun \enquote{my}, for example \enquote{O my people}, it is common to shorten the attached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{ฤซ} \enquote{my} to an \emph{i}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. So while the following is permissible,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ููููููู}\\
\emph{yฤ qawmฤซ}\\
\enquote{O my people}

it is more common to say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ูููููู}\\
\emph{yฤ qawmi}\\
\enquote{O my people}

This usage is especially common when supplicating to Allฤh with the phrase \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุจูู} \emph{rabbi} \enquote{my Lord}.

\section{Calling out to Allฤh by name}\label{calling-out-to-allah-by-name}

When calling out to Allฤh by name, it is permissible to prefix the name Allฤh with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง}. So we can say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฃูููููููฐูู}\\
\emph{yฤ สพallฤhu}\\
\enquote{O Allฤh}

Note that the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููููฐูู} \emph{สพallฤhu} now has a regular hamzah \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃ} instead of a connecting hamzah \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑ}.

However, instead of saying \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฃูููููููฐูู} \emph{yฤ สพallฤhu} for \enquote{O Allฤh}, it is in fact more common to use a special word:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููููฐููููู}\\
\emph{สพallฤhumma}\\
\enquote{O Allฤh}

Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููููฐููููู ุฃูููุชู ูฑูุณููููุงูู ููููููฺฉู ูฑูุณููููุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพallฤhumma สพanta -ssalฤmu waminka -ssalฤmu.}\\
\enquote{O Allฤh, You are Peace and from You is peace.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููููฐููููู ุฃูููุชู ูฑูุตููุงุญูุจู ููู ูฑูุณููููุฑู.}\\
\emph{สพallฤhumma สพanta -แนฃแนฃฤแธฅibu fi -ssafari.}\\
\enquote{O Allฤh, You are the companion in the journey.}

\chapter{Pointing nouns}\label{pointing-nouns}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-14}

Consider the following expression:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-14-1.pdf}

The word \enquote{this} is what we will call a \emph{pointing noun}. We call it this because we can imagine standing next to a book and pointing to it and saying \enquote{this book}.

The word \enquote{book} here is similarly called the \emph{pointed-to} noun. It refers to the object being pointed to.

\section{The pointing nouns in Arabic}\label{the-pointing-nouns-in-arabic}

There are two types of pointing nouns:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Near pointing nouns: \enquote{this-one} (singular) and \enquote{these-ones} (dual and plural).
\item
  Far pointing nouns: \enquote{that-one} (singular) and \enquote{those-ones} (dual and plural).
\end{enumerate}

The following are the pointing nouns in Arabic:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2174}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.0870}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1304}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2174}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1304}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2174}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Participant
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
State
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedleft
Near pointing noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedleft
Far pointing noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
sing. masc. & all & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง} & this one\textsubscript{m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐู~ูฐููฺฉู} & that one\textsubscript{m} \\
sing. fem. & all & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐููู} & this one\textsubscript{f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููฺฉู} & that one\textsubscript{f} \\
dual masc. & u & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุงูู} & these ones\textsubscript{2,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐู~ูฐููฺฉู} & those ones\textsubscript{2,m} \\
dual masc. & a,i & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐููููู} & these ones\textsubscript{2,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููููฺฉู} & those ones\textsubscript{2,m} \\
dual fem. & u & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูุงูู} & these ones\textsubscript{2,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุงููฺฉู} & those ones\textsubscript{2,f} \\
dual fem. & a,i & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชููููู} & these ones\textsubscript{2,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููููฺฉู} & those ones\textsubscript{2,f} \\
plural & all & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุคูููุงุกู} & these ones\textsubscript{3} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููฐุฆูฺฉู} & those ones\textsubscript{3} \\
\end{longtable}

Note the following:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Many of the pointing nouns contain small สพalif \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โูฐ}. For most of them, this is how they must be written. It would be incorrect to write \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง} \emph{hฤdอกhฤ} as \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุฐูุง}.
\item
  All the near pointing nouns begin with a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}. And all the far pointing nouns end with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉ}.
\item
  The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููฐุฆูฺฉู} \emph{สพulฤสพika} is silent and not pronounced. That is, the first syllable has a short vowel \emph{u}, not the long vowel \emph{ลซ}.
\item
  Most of the pointing nouns are rigid nouns. That is: their endings are not modified for their state.

  The dual pointing nouns, however, are flexible nouns, for example: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุงูู} (u-state) / \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐููููู} \emph{hฤdอกhayni} (a- and i-states).
\item
  The pointing nouns for the plural are the same for both masculine and feminine genders.
\end{itemize}

\section{Definiteness of pointing nouns}\label{definiteness-of-pointing-nouns}

The pointing nouns share some similarities with pronouns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู}, etc. Just like pronouns, pointing nouns, too, are definite nouns even though they don't have \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}.

Remember, however, from section~\ref{describers-with-annexations-to-pronouns}, that pronouns may not be describees.
Pointing nouns are different from pronouns in this regard. It is allowed to describe a pointing noun with a describer in a noun phrase.

Both these facts will prove useful in the next section.

\section{Pointing noun for plurals of non-intelligent beings}\label{pointing-noun-for-plurals-of-non-intelligent-beings}

Consistent with how we have been dealing with the so far,
, we can choose between the following pointing nouns for
the plurals of non-intelligent beings:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2174}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.0870}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1304}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2174}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1304}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2174}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Near pointing noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedleft
Far pointing noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedleft
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
sing. fem. & all & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐููู} & this one\textsubscript{f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููฺฉู} & that one\textsubscript{f} \\
plural & all & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุคูููุงุกู} & these ones\textsubscript{3} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููฐุฆูฺฉู} & those ones\textsubscript{3} \\
\end{longtable}

The singular feminine pointing noun is usually preferred, unless the plural plural pointing noun is needed to indicate that there is more than one.
We will be giving examples throughout this chapter.

\section{The pointing noun phrase}\label{the-pointing-noun-phrase}

Remember from chapter~\ref{adjectival-nouns-and-descriptive-noun-phrases} that a descriptive noun-phrase consists of a describer and a describee. The describer follows the describer and matches it in definiteness, state, gender, and number.

Here is an example of a descriptive noun-phrase in a sentence.

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-15-1.pdf}

We will now see how this same descriptive noun-phrase can be used with pointing nouns.

\subsection{Pointing to a single noun}\label{pointing-to-a-single-noun}

We will first deal with nouns that are single words, like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจููููู} above.
In section~\ref{pointing-to-an-annexation}
below, we will deal with nouns that are part of an annexation, like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{The pointed-to noun is definite with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}}{The pointed-to noun is definite with ูฑููู}}\label{phrase-single-pointed-to-noun-with-al}

Just like an adjectival noun, a pointing noun can be a describer in a noun-phrase. But remember from
section~\ref{definiteness-of-pointing-nouns}
above that pointing nouns are definite.
So, if a pointing noun is a describer in a noun-phrase, the describee has to be definite too.
Example:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-16-1.pdf}

In the above example, the pointed-to noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจููููู} is the describee in a descriptive noun-phrase. It is definite, in the a-state, masculine, and dual.

The pointing noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐููููู} is its describer. It follows the describee and matches it being dual, in the a-state, masculine, and dual.

As a special case, when the pointed-to noun has \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} (as in this case: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจููููู}), then the order of the pointing noun and the pointed to noun is permitted to be reversed.

The pointing noun is then a replacee (see section~\ref{the-replacement}), and the pointed-to noun is its replacement.

Example:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-17-1.pdf}

In the above example, the
pointing noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐููููู}
is a replacee. It is definite, in the a-state, masculine, and dual.

The
pointed-to noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจููููู}
is its replacement. It follows the replacee and matches it being dual, in the a-state, masculine, and dual.

As a matter of fact, even though both orders are permitted, this reverse order of placing the pointing noun first and following it with the pointed-to noun is more common.

Here are some more examples of pointing noun phrases when the pointed-to noun is definite with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููุฑููุฌููู ูฑููฺฉูุฑูููู ุฅูููุงูู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู ูฑููฺฉูุฑูููู ููููฐุฐูุง ุฅูููุงูู.}\\
\enquote{This noble man is an imฤm.}

\subsubsection{The pointed-to noun is a proper noun}\label{the-pointed-to-noun-is-a-proper-noun}

Remember that proper noun are definite nouns, even though they usually don't begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}. For example:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}rlrl@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏ} & Zayd & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุญูุงุฑูุซ} & al-แธคฤritอกh \\
\textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููููุจ} & Zaynab & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููุด} & Quraysอกh \\
\end{longtable}

Such names may also be part of a pointing noun phrase.
If they don't begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} then only the {[}pointed-to noun first, then pointing noun{]} order is permitted.
Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู ููููฐุฐูุง ุฃูุฎูู ุฒูููููุจู ุชูููฺฉู.}\\
\enquote{This Zayd is that Zaynab's brother.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูููุดู ููููฐุคูููุงุกู ุณูฺฉููููุง ุจูููฺฉููุฉู.}\\
\enquote{These Quraysอกh dwelled in Mecca.}

If the name begins with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} then both orders are permitted.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููุญูุงุฑูุซ}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุญูุงุฑูุซ ููููฐุฐูุง}\\
\enquote{this al-แธคฤritอกh}

\subsection{Pointing to an annexation}\label{pointing-to-an-annexation}

Consider the following expression:

\enquote{the man's book}

We can apply the pointing noun \enquote{this} to either \enquote{the book} or to \enquote{the man} in a pointing noun phrase. So we have two options:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \enquote{the book of this man}
\item
  \enquote{this book of the man}
\end{enumerate}

Similarly, consider the following expression:

\enquote{Zayd's book}

We can, again, apply the pointing noun \enquote{this} to either \enquote{the book} or to \enquote{Zayd}:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \enquote{the book of this Zayd}
\item
  \enquote{this book of Zayd}
\end{enumerate}

In this section we will learn how to construct these pointing noun phrases in Arabic.
Arabic uses annexations to express the above meanings. So we will discuss annexations like:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\enquote{the book of the man}

and

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ุฒูููุฏู}\\
\enquote{the book of Zayd}

Note that both the above annexations are definite because their base nouns are definite.

Indefinite annexations like
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ ุฑูุฌููู} \enquote{a man's book}
cannot be used in pointing noun phrases.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{The definite base noun begins with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}}{The definite base noun begins with ูฑููู}}\label{the-definite-base-noun-begins-with-ux671ux644}

We will first consider annexations where the definite base noun begins with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}, like:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\enquote{the book of the man}

\paragraph{Pointing to the base noun}\label{pointing-to-the-base-noun}

We would like to express the phrase:

\enquote{the book of this man}

In order to point to the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู} \enquote{the man} with the pointing noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง} \enquote{this-one\textsubscript{m}}, we can put the pointing noun either before or after the base noun, thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููููฐุฐูุง}\\
\enquote{the book of this man}

Both these pointing noun phrases give the same meaning: \enquote{the book of this man}.
However, the first phrase
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}
is preferred, consistent with what we learned in
section~\ref{phrase-single-pointed-to-noun-with-al}, above.

The second phrase
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููููฐุฐูุง},
although correct, would only rarely be used with this meaning.
(In fact, it has another meaning: \enquote{this book of the man} which we will learn in
section~\ref{pointing-to-the-annexe-noun}, below.)

Here is how these phrases could be used in complete sentences:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ุฌูุฏููุฏู.}\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููููฐุฐูุง ุฌูุฏููุฏู.}\\
\enquote{The book of this man is new.}

Before we give more examples, let's analyze these phrases in detail.

Consider the first pointing noun phrase:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-18-1.pdf}

As you can see the pointing noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง} has taken the place of
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู} as the base noun in the annexation.
In addition to being the base noun,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง} is also a replacee, whose replacement is
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู}.
The literal, word-for-word, translation of this phrase is:

\enquote{the book of this-one: the man}

The more natural translation is:

\enquote{the book of this man}

Consider, now, the second pointing noun phrase:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-19-1.pdf}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู}, here, keeps its place as the base noun in the annexation.
In addition to being the base noun,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู}
is also a describee, whose describer is the pointing noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง}.
The literal, word-for-word, translation of this phrase is:

\enquote{the book of the this-one man}

The more natural translation is:

\enquote{the book of this man}

\paragraph{Pointing to the annexe noun}\label{pointing-to-the-annexe-noun}

Consider, again, the annexation:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\enquote{the book of the man}

We have already discussed how to point to the base noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูู}
in a pointing noun phrase.
Now, we would like to point to the annexe noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ}
in a pointing noun phrase.

In other words, we would like to express the meaning:

\enquote{this book of the man}

The way to express this in Arabic is

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููููฐุฐูุง}\\
\enquote{this book of the man}

But wait! Didn't we see in section~\ref{pointing-to-the-base-noun} above that this expression has the meaning
\enquote{the book of this man}?

It turns out that this expression supports both meanings.

But it will generally only be used for the meaning: \enquote{this book of the man}

In order to express
\enquote{the book of this man}
we will typically use the expression \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}.

Let's analyze the expression
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููููฐุฐูุง}
\enquote{this book of the man}
in detail:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-20-1.pdf}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ}, here, is both and annexe noun and a describee.
Its describer is the pointing noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง}.
The literal, word-for-word, translation of this phrase is:

\enquote{the this-one book of the man}

The more natural translation is:

\enquote{this book of the man}

Here is this pointing noun phrase in a complete sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููููฐุฐูุง ุฃูุฎูุถูุฑ.}\\
\enquote{This book of the man is green.}

\subparagraph*{Ambiguity of this phrase}\label{ambiguity-of-this-phrase}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subparagraph}{Ambiguity of this phrase}

A quick note about the ambiguity of this expression:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููููฐุฐูุง}\\
\enquote{this book of the man} (usual)\\
\enquote{the book of this man} (rare)

The ambiguity of whether the pointing noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง} points to the annexe noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู}
or the base noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}
only exists because the annexe noun and the base noun match each other in gender and number: singular masculine.
If the annexe noun and the base noun were different in gender and number, then there would be no ambiguity. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููููฐุฐูุงูู}\\
\enquote{these books\textsubscript{2} of the man}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููููููู ููููฐุฐูุง}\\
\enquote{this book of the men\textsubscript{2}}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููููุฑูุฃูุฉู ููููฐุฐูุง}\\
\enquote{this book of the woman}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑููููุฑูุฃูุฉู ููููฐุฐููู}\\
\enquote{the book of this woman}

Here are some more examples of pointing to annexe nouns:

\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{The base noun is a proper noun beginning with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}}{The base noun is a proper noun beginning with ูฑููู}}\label{the-base-noun-is-a-proper-noun-beginning-with-ux671ux644}

Consider the annexation:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฒููุจูููุฑู}\\
\enquote{the book of al-Zubayr}

We can apply the preceding discussion of pointing to the annexe noun and base noun to this annexation as well. So we get:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑูุฒููุจูููุฑู}\\
\enquote{the book of this al-Zubayr}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ูฑูุฒููุจูููุฑู ููููฐุฐูุง}\\
\enquote{this book of al-Zubayr} (usual)\\
\enquote{the book of this al-Zubayr} (rare)

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{The definite base noun does not begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}}{The definite base noun does not begin with ูฑููู}}\label{the-definite-base-noun-does-not-begin-with-ux671ux644}

Consider, now, that the base noun is definite but does not begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}. There are two such types of nouns that we will discuss:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Proper nouns not beginning with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}
\item
  Pronouns
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{The base noun is a proper noun not beginning with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}}{The base noun is a proper noun not beginning with ูฑููู}}\label{the-base-noun-is-a-proper-noun-not-beginning-with-ux671ux644}

We will first deal with proper nouns that don't begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}.
Consider the annexation:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ุฒูููุฏู}\\
\enquote{the book of Zayd}

Because the base noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏ} does not begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}, any pointing nouns can come only after the entire annexation, thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ุฒูููุฏู ููููฐุฐูุง}

In theory, this supports two meanings:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \enquote{this book of Zayd}
\item
  \enquote{the book of this Zayd}
\end{enumerate}

In practice, however, the first meaning
(\enquote{this book of Zayd})
is much more likely.
Pointing to a proper noun in a pointing noun phrase
(\enquote{the book of this Zayd})
is uncommon, generally.

\paragraph{The base noun is a pronoun}\label{the-base-noun-is-a-pronoun}

We have learned, in
section~\ref{definiteness-of-pronouns},
that pronouns are always definite, despite not beginning with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}.

We have also learned, in
section~\ref{pronouns-as-base-nouns},
that a pronoun may be a base noun in an annexation.
Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู}\\
\enquote{his book}

Neither the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ}, nor the attached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู}.
So if we want to add the pointing noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง} to this annexation to form a pointing noun phrase,
then we have to place it at the end, after the annexation, thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู ููููฐุฐูุง}

The pointing noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง}, here, is a describee. But what is its describer?

We have also learned, in
section~\ref{describers-with-annexations-to-pronouns}
that pronouns may not be describees in a descriptive noun phrase.

So, we are left with only one option: the annexe noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจ} is the desceibee. And the meaning of the phrase is:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุงุจููู ููููฐุฐูุง}\\
\enquote{this book of his}

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-21-1.pdf}

Here are some more examples:

\section{Pointing nouns as subjects}\label{pointing-nouns-as-subjects}

Besides their use in pointing noun phrases, pointing nouns are very often used as the subject of a sentence. For example:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-22-1.pdf}

The pointing noun is (usually) made to match the information in number and gender. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูุงูู ุฌูุงุฑูููุชูุงูู.}\\
\enquote{These are girls\textsubscript{2}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููฐุฆูฺฉู ููุนููููููููู.}\\
\enquote{Those are teachers.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุคูููุงุกู ุฃูููููุงูู.}\\
\enquote{These are pens.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููฺฉู ุจููููุชู.}\\
\enquote{Those are houses.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุงูู ุตูุบููุฑูุงูู.}\\
\enquote{These are small ones\textsubscript{2}.}

The information may be a single word (as above) or more complex (as below):

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐู~ูฐููฺฉู ุฃููููุฑู ูฑููููุคููููููููู.}\\
\enquote{That is the commander of the believers.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููฐุฆูฺฉู ุฃูฺฉููููู ูฑูุทููุนูุงูู..}\\
\enquote{Those-ones ate\textsubscript{3,f} the food.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ุซูููุจู ุฑูุฌููู.}\\
\enquote{This is a man's garment.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐููู ฺฉูุชูุจููู.}\\
\enquote{These are his books.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุงูู ุจูููุชูุงูู ฺฉูุจููุฑูุงูู..}\\
\enquote{These are big houses\textsubscript{2}.}

If the information is a noun that begins with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} then it may be placed after the pointing noun subject in the same manner:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-23-1.pdf}

While the this is permitted and correct, it may be sometimes confused with for the pointing noun phrase \enquote{this man}. So, in the same way that we learned in
section~\ref{chap-smp-sent-sec-def-info},
we insert a detached pronoun between the subject and the information, thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ูููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\enquote{This is the man.}

Here are some more examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูุงูู ููููุง ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุชูุงูู.}\\
\enquote{These are the girls\textsubscript{2}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููฐุฆูฺฉู ูููู ูฑููููุนููููููููู.}\\
\enquote{Those are the teachers.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุคูููุงุกู ููููู ูฑููุฃูููููุงูู.}\\
\enquote{These are the pens.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููฺฉู ูููู ูฑููุจููููุชู.}\\
\enquote{Those are the houses.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุงูู ูููู ูฑูุตููุบููุฑูุงูู.}\\
\enquote{These are the small ones\textsubscript{2}.}

\subsection{Mismatched pointing noun subject}\label{mismatched-pointing-noun-subject}

When the pointing noun is a subject we usually match its number and gender with the number and gender of the information, as we have been doing so far. However, when the pointing noun subject refers to a noun in a previous sentence, then we may prefer to match to the previous noun than to the the following information. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุบูููุง ุฎูุจูุฑู ูฑููููุทูุฑู ุนูููู ูฑููุฌูุจูุงูู. ุฐู~ูฐููฺฉู ุจูุดูุฑูููฐ ูููุฒููุฑููุงุนู.}\\
\enquote{The news of the rain on the mountains has reached us. That is a good tiding for the sowers.}

Note that the second sentence's subject and information mismatch:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐู~ูฐููฺฉู ุจูุดูุฑูููฐ}\\
\enquote{That is a good tiding.}

The information
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุดูุฑูููฐ} \enquote{a good tiding} is a feminine noun but the subject
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐู~ูฐููฺฉู} is masculine.
This is because
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐู~ูฐููฺฉู}
is actually referring to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุจูุฑ} in the previous sentence which is a masculine noun.

\section{Pointing nouns as other parts of speech}\label{pointing-nouns-as-other-parts-of-speech}

Besides their use in pointing noun phrases and as subjects, pointing nouns may be used as other parts of speech as well, typically where one would expect pronouns. Here are some examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐูุชู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจููููู ูููู ูฑููููฺฉูุชูุจูุฉู. ููุฑูุฃูุชู ููููฐุฐุง ููููุง ููุฑูุฃูุชู ุฐู~ูฐููฺฉู.}\\
\enquote{I took the books\textsubscript{2} from the library. I read this one and I didn't read that one.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุบูููููู ูฑููุนููููู ูฑูุตููุนูุจู ููููุง ููุฑูุบูุชู ูููู ุฐู~ูฐููฺฉู.}\\
\enquote{The difficult work occupied me and I did not get done with that.}

\chapter{u-state incomplete-action verbs}\label{u-state-incomplete-action-verbs}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-15}

We had mentioned that there are approximately 10 commonly used verb forms. And we have already studied the completed-action verb for form 1. In this chapter we will study incomplete-action form 1 verbs. Incomplete-action verbs are used when the action of a verb is on-going at present or will occur in the future.

\section{Pattern for form 1}\label{pattern-for-form-1}

Using the root paradigm \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุนูยป}, we have already seen that completed-action verbs for form 1 occur in the patterns \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษala}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษila}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษula}. The patterns for form 1 incomplete-action verbs are \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \emph{yafษalu}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \emph{yafษilu}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \emph{yafษulu}.

Note that the incomplete-action verb forms add an extraneous \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{ya-} to the beginning of the verb. This extra letter can change, as we will see soon, to the letters \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูู} \emph{ta-}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{na}, or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃู} \emph{สพa-} depending on the doer.

\section{Vowel-mark on the middle root letter}\label{vowel-mark-on-the-middle-root-letter}

We have seen that vowel on the middle root letter in a completed-action verb can vary depending on the verb. So we can have,

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู} \emph{kataba} \enquote{he wrote}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู} \emph{ษamila} \enquote{he worked}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจูุฑู} \emph{kabura} \enquote{he became big}
\end{itemize}

Similarly, the vowel on the middle letter in an incomplete-action verb can also vary depending on the verb. Generally, this will need to be looked up in a dictionary and memorized. But there are the following rules which limit the variation:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  If the completed-action verb has an \emph{a}-mark on the middle letter, the incomplete-action verb's middle letter can have either an \emph{a}-mark, \emph{i}-mark, or an \emph{u}-mark, depending on the verb. For example,

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ููฺฉูุชูุจู} \emph{kataba yaktubu} \enquote{he wrote, he writes}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ููุฐูููุจู} \emph{dอกhahaba yadอกhยทhabu} \enquote{he went, he goes}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุดููู ููฺฉูุดููู} \emph{kasอกhafa yaksอกhifu} \enquote{he uncovered, he uncovers}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  If the completed-action verb has an \emph{i}-mark on the middle letter, the incomplete-action verb's middle letter will usually have an \emph{a}-mark. Rarely, for a few verbs, it may be an \emph{i}-mark instead. For example,

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู ููุนููููู} \emph{ษamila yaษmalu} \enquote{he worked, he works}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูุจู ููุญูุณูุจู} \emph{แธฅasiba yaแธฅsibu} \enquote{he deemed, he deems}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  If the completed-action verb has an \emph{u}-mark on the middle letter, the incomplete-action verb's middle letter shall have a \emph{u}-mark. For example,

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจูุฑู ููฺฉูุจูุฑู} \emph{kabura yakburu} \enquote{he grew big, he grows big}
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

It is possible for some incomplete-action verbs to have more than option for the vowel mark on the middle letter. Both variants give the same meaning for the verb. For example, the completed-action verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูุจู} \emph{แธฅasiba} \enquote{he deemed} has as its incomplete-verb both \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญูุณูุจู} \emph{yaแธฅsibu} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุญูุณูุจู} \emph{yaแธฅsabu}.

\section{Verb state}\label{verb-state}

As you know, nouns in Arabic have a state that is determined by the function of the noun in the sentence. For example, consider the following sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ุนููู ุดูููุกู.}\\
\emph{saสพala -lgอกhulฤmu -rrajula สพan sอกhayสพin.}\\
\enquote{The boy asked the man about something.}

In the above sentence, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุบูููุงูู} \emph{สพalgอกhulฤmu} is the doer of the verb so it is in the u-state and this is indicated by the \emph{u}-mark on its final letter.
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู} \emph{สพarrujala} is the direct doee of the verb so it is in the a-state and this is indicated by the \emph{a}-mark on its final letter.
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุกู} \emph{sอกhayสพin} is directly preceded by a preposition so it is in the i-state and this is indicated by the \emph{in}-mark on its final letter.
The ending of the completed-action verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู} is not determined based on the function of the verb in the sentence, and therefore, it does not have any state. (Its ending can change depending on whether a pronoun is attached to it but this is not related to the function of the verb in the sentence and does not represent any state.)

As opposed to completed-action verbs, which don't have any state, incomplete-action verbs do have a state which is determinined by the function of the verb in a sentence. Similar to nouns, the state of an incomplete-action verb is indicated by the vowel mark or suffix at the end of the verb.

Incomplete action verbs have three states, just like nouns. These states are called:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  The u-state
\item
  The a-state
\item
  The รธ-state
\end{enumerate}

Two of the states have their names in common with nouns: the u-state and the a-state. The the รธ-state (null-state) is named differently.

The \emph{u}-mark on the final letter of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \emph{yafษalu} indicates that it is in the u-state. We will study only the u-state of incomplete-action verbs in this chapter. And we will study the a-state and รธ-state in later chapters if Allฤh wills.

\section{With doer nouns}\label{with-doer-nouns}

As with completed-action verbs, doer nouns are placed after the verb in sentence word order. However, the gender of the doer noun affects the beginning of the incomplete-action verb. If the doer noun is masculine, then the incomplete-action verb shall begin with used is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู} \emph{ya-}. And if the doer noun is feminine, then the incomplete-action verb shall begin with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูู} \emph{ta-}. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ููู ฺฉูุชุงุจููู.}\\
\emph{yaktubu -lgอกhulฤmu fฤซ kitฤbihi}\\
\enquote{The boy writes in his book.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู ูฑูุฑููุฌูููุงูู ููู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู.}\\
\emph{yaษmalu -rrajulฤni fi -lmadฤซnati.}\\
\enquote{The men\textsubscript{dual.} work in the city.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ููู ฺฉูุชุงุจูููุง.}\\
\emph{yaktubu -ljฤriyatu fฤซ kitฤbihฤ.}\\
\enquote{The girl writes in her book.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุนููููู ูฑููููุณูุงุกู ููู ุจููููุชูููููู.}\\
\emph{taษmalu -nnisฤสพu fฤซ buyลซtihinna.}\\
\enquote{The women work in their houses.}

\section{With doee nouns and pronouns}\label{with-doee-nouns-and-pronouns}

Doee nouns and pronouns with incomplete-action verbs work exactly as with completed-action verbs.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุฃููู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ุณูุคูุงููุง.}\\
\emph{yasสพalu -lgอกhulฤmu -rrajula suสพฤlan.}\\
\enquote{The boy asks the man a question.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุฃูููููุง ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุณูุคูุงููุง.}\\
\emph{yasสพaluha -lgอกhulฤmu suสพฤlan.}\\
\enquote{The boy asks her a question.}

\section{With doer pronouns}\label{with-doer-pronouns}

When we studied completed-action verbs, we saw that doer pronouns are either visible or invisible. Visible doer pronouns are added to the end of the verb, modifying the end of the verb in the process.

The doer pronouns for incomplete-action verbs are different from the doer pronouns for completed-action verbs.
Incomplete-action verbs' doer pronouns are also added to the end of the verb, but in addition to modifying the end of the verb, they modify the beginning of the verb as well. Futhermore, additional letters may be added after the doer pronoun to indicate the state of the verb.

We'll show what all this means in the table below of verbs with doer pronouns. Completed-action verbs are included as well so that you can contrast them with their incomplete-action counterparts.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1500}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2500}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1500}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2500}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Person
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Completed-action doer pronoun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Completed-action verb with doer pronoun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Incomplete-action verb doer pronoun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Incomplete-action verb with doer pronoun in the u-state
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
he & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษala} & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \emph{yafษalu} \\
she & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชู} \emph{faษalat} & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููู} \emph{tafษalu} \\
you\textsubscript{1,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชู} \emph{-ta} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชู} \emph{faษalta} & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููู} \emph{tafษalu} \\
you\textsubscript{1,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชู} \emph{-ti} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชู} \emph{faษalti} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{-ฤซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููููู} \emph{tafษalฤซna} \\
I & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชู} \emph{tu} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชู} \emph{faษaltu} & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} \emph{สพafษalu} \\
they\textsubscript{2,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} \emph{-ฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุง} \emph{faษalฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} \emph{-ฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููุงูู} \emph{yafษalฤni} \\
they\textsubscript{2,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} \emph{-ฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชูุง} \emph{faษalatฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} \emph{-ฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููุงูู} \emph{tafษalฤni} \\
you\textsubscript{2} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุง} \emph{-tumฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชูููุง} \emph{faษaltumฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} \emph{-ฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููุงูู} \emph{tafษalฤni} \\
they\textsubscript{3+,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{-ลซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููุง} \emph{faษalลซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{-ลซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููููู} \emph{yafษalลซna} \\
they\textsubscript{3+,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{-na} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู} \emph{faษalna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{-na} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููู} \emph{yafษalna} \\
you\textsubscript{3+,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชููู} \emph{-tumฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชููู} \emph{faษaltum} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} \emph{-ลซ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููููู} \emph{tafษalลซna} \\
you\textsubscript{3+,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููู} \emph{-tunna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุชูููู} \emph{faษaltunna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{na} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููู} \emph{tafษalna} \\
we & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{nฤ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููุง} \emph{faษalnฤ} & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \emph{nafษalu} \\
\end{longtable}

Note the following:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููู} is used both for \enquote{she} and \enquote{you\textsubscript{2m}} doers. Only context will be able to help us differentiate between the two.
\item
  In incomplete action verbs which have invisible doer pronouns, the u-state of the verb is indicated by the \emph{u}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} on the final letter of the verb.
\item
  For incomplete-action verbs that have \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} as the doer pronoun, the u-state is indicated by an extraneous \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} added to the end of the verb.
\item
  And for the remaining incomplete action verbs whose doer pronoun is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, there is no indication of the state of the verb.
\end{itemize}

Here are some examples of the usage of the doer pronouns:

Remember that in Arabic, each verb must have it's own doer, so when there are multiple verbs associated with the same doer, the first verb can be used with the doer noun and the rest with doer pronouns. This is the same behavior as with completed-action verbs. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฌูููุณู ูฑูุฑููุฌูุงูู ููููุฃูฺฉูููููู ููููุดูุฑูุจูููู.}\\
\emph{yajlisu -rrijฤlu wa yaสพkulลซna wa yasอกhrabลซna.}\\
\enquote{The men sit and (they) eat and (they) drink.}

\section{Future}\label{future}
