The incomplete-action verb is used to express both the present (habitual and progressive) and future tenses. Sometimes all meanings are meant in the same expression. And if only one of the meanings is intended, context can be sufficient to determine which is intended. So, for example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\emph{yadอกhยทhabu -rrajulu.}

can mean, either one, or even all, of:

\enquote{The man goes.} or\\
\enquote{The man is going.} or\\
\enquote{The man will go.}

Arabic does provide a mechanism for specifying that the use of an incomplete-action verb is solely to intend a future action. This is by means of the particles \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูู} \emph{sa-} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู} \emph{sawfa} that can be placed before the verb. They provide a meaning of \enquote{will} or \enquote{will soon}. \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูู} \emph{sa-}, being a single letter particle, is attached to the verb.

For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\emph{sayadอกhยทhabu -rrajulu.}\\
and\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู ููุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\emph{sawfa yadอกhยทhabu -rrajulu.}\\
\enquote{The man will go.} or\\
\enquote{Soon the man will go.}

The difference in usage of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูู} \emph{sa-} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู} \emph{sawfa} can be thought of as one of emphasis. \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู} \emph{sawfa} is more emphatic than \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูู} \emph{sa-}. This emphasis can translate to more definiteness in the action or even that the action is farther in the future.

\section{Negation}\label{negation}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Negation using \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{mฤ}}{Negation using ููุง mฤ}}\label{negation-using-ux645ux627-ma}

As with completed-action verbs, incomplete-action verbs too can be negated by placing the particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} before them. This negates the meaning of the verb usually for the present tense. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ููุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\emph{mฤ yadอกhยทhabu -rrajulu.}\\
\enquote{The man does not go.} or,\\
\enquote{The man is not going.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Negation using \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{lฤ}}{Negation using ููุง lฤ}}\label{u-state-verb-negation-la}

In addition to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{mฤ}, incomplete-action verbs can be negated using \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{lฤ} in the same manner. In addition to negating the meaning of the verb for the present tense, it can also negate the meaning for the future tense.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ููุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\emph{lฤ yadอกhยทhabu -rrajulu.}\\
\enquote{The man does not go.} or,\\
\enquote{The man is not going.} or,\\
\enquote{The man will not go.}

The particles \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูู} \emph{sa-} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู} \emph{sawfa} may not be combined with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{mฤ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{lฤ} when negating verbs.

\chapter{The verbal-noun of doing}\label{the-verbal-noun-of-doing}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-16}

Every verb has a set of \emph{verbal-nouns} derived from it that, despite being nouns, have a verbal meaning to them. One of these verbal-nouns is the \enquote{doing} verbal-noun, that we shall study in this chapter.

Consider the following form~1 verb:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1579}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2632}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3158}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2632}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Root
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Completed-action verb
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Incomplete-action verb (u-state)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Doing verbal-noun
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฐูุจยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู} \enquote{he went} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฐูููุจู} \enquote{he goes} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุงุจ} \enquote{going} \\
\end{longtable}

The doing verbal-noun associated with this verb is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุงุจ} \emph{dอกhahฤb}. It denotes \enquote{the action of going}, or simply \enquote{going}. In this section we shall learn how this and other verbal-nouns are used.

Before we proceed, we present a new method to present a verb and its meaning in this book.
We will often give a new verb in the format:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ููุฐูููุจู ุฐูููุงุจูุง} \enquote{to go}

The completed-action verb for the singular masculine absentee participant \enquote{he}, the corresponding incomplete-action verb, and their doing verbal-noun are given together, in sequence. The doing verbal-noun is given in the a-state, because of a usage that we shall learn in a later chapter, if Allฤh wills. This is how verb definitions are traditionally found in Arabic dictionaries. And the English meaning is given using the dictionary definition, in this case, the phrase: \enquote{to go}.

\section{Patterns of the doing verbal-noun for form~1 verbs}\label{patterns-of-the-doing-verbal-noun-for-form-1-verbs}

The patterns of the doing verbal-noun for form~1 verbs are very variable. It is best to learn the doing verbal-noun when you learn a new verb. Having said that, there are some general trends which may be useful to keep in mind:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  If the verb takes a direct doee, then the completed-action verb must necessarily be of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษala} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษila} (because completed-action verbs of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษula} never take a direct doee). In this case:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\alph{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    The doing verbal-noun for many verbs, in general, tends to be \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \emph{faษl}. Examples:

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุญู ููููุชูุญู ููุชูุญูุง} \enquote{to open (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)}
    \item
      \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐู ููุฃูุฎูุฐู ุฃูุฎูุฐูุง} \enquote{to take (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)}
    \item
      \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุฏู ููุญูููุฏู ุญูููุฏูุง} \enquote{to praise (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} s.o.)}
    \end{itemize}
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  If the verb does not take a direct doee, then:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\alph{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    If the completed-action verb is of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษila}, then:

    \begin{enumerate}
    \def\labelenumiii{\roman{enumiii}.}
    \tightlist
    \item
      If the meaning of the verb does not fall under the cases ii., iii., and iv. (below), then the doing verbal-noun tends to be, in general, of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูู} \emph{faษal}. Examples:

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุนูุจู ููุชูุนูุจู ุชูุนูุจูุง} \enquote{to become tired}
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุฒูุนู ููุฌูุฒูุนู ุฌูุฒูุนูุง} \enquote{to be impatient}
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณููู ููุฃูุณููู ุฃูุณูููุง} \enquote{to be sorrowful}
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      If, instead, the meaning of the verb denotes being a color, then the doing verbal-noun is usually of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููุฉ} \emph{fuษlah}. Examples:

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุถูุฑู ููุฎูุถูุฑู ุฎูุถูุฑูุฉู} \enquote{to be green}
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุฑู ููุณูููุฑู ุณูููุฑูุฉู} \enquote{to be brown}
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      If, instead, the meaning of the verb denotes some work or effort, then the doing verbal-noun tends to be of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{fuษลซl}. Example:

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏููู ููููุฏููู ููุฏููููุง} \enquote{to arrive}
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      If, instead, the meaning of the verb denotes some static quality, then the doing verbal-noun tends to be of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููุฉ} \emph{fuษลซlah}. Example:

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูุณู ููููุจูุณู ููุจููุณูุฉ} \enquote{to be dry}
      \end{itemize}
    \end{enumerate}
  \item
    If the completed-action verb is of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษala}, then:

    \begin{enumerate}
    \def\labelenumiii{\roman{enumiii}.}
    \tightlist
    \item
      If the meaning of the verb does not fall under the cases ii., iii., and iv. (below), then the doing verbal-noun tends to be, in general, of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{fuษลซl}. Examples:

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุฏู ููููุนูุฏู ููุนููุฏูุง} \enquote{to sit, stay back}
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฌูุฏู ููุณูุฌูุฏู ุณูุฌููุฏูุง} \enquote{to prostrate down}
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุถูุนู ููุฎูุถูุนู ุฎูุถููุนูุง} \enquote{to be humble}
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      If, instead, the meaning of the verb denotes an ailment, then the doing verbal-noun is usually of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงู} \emph{fuษฤl}. Examples:

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุนููู ููุณูุนููู ุณูุนูุงูู} \enquote{to cough}
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      If, instead, the meaning of the verb denotes travelling, then the doing verbal-noun is usually of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษฤซl}. Examples:

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุญููู ููุฑูุญููู ุฑูุญููููุง} \enquote{to depart}
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      If, instead, the meaning of the verb denotes a sound, then the doing verbal-noun is usually of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษฤซl} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงู} \emph{fuษฤl}, or both. Examples:

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุฑูุฎู ููุตูุฑูุฎู ุตูุฑููุฎูุง ููุตูุฑูุงุฎูุง} \enquote{to scream}
      \end{itemize}
    \end{enumerate}
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  If the verb denotes a craft or a profession or a rank, then the doing verbal-noun is often of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงููุฉ} \emph{fiษฤlah}. Examples:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุฌูุฑู ููุชูุฌูุฑู ุชูุฌูุงุฑูุฉู} \enquote{to trade}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฑู ููุฃูููุฑู ุฅูููุงุฑูุฉู} \enquote{to be a commander}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  If the completed-action verb is of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษula}, then the doing verbal noun tends to be of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููุฉ} \emph{fuษลซlah} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงููุฉ} \emph{faษฤlah}. Examples:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจู ููุตูุนูุจู ุตูุนููุจูุฉู} \enquote{to be difficult}
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฌูุนู ููุดูุฌูุนู ุดูุฌูุงุนูุฉู} \enquote{to be brave}
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

As mentioned earlier, these are only general trends and there are many verbs that have doing verbal-nouns which don't fall under the above rules.

\section{Usage of the doing verbal-noun}\label{usage-of-the-doing-verbal-noun}

\subsection{State and definiteness}\label{state-and-definiteness}

The doing verbal noun has properties of a noun, like state and definiteness. But it gives the meaning of a verb. For example, consider the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู ููุฃูฺฉููู ุฃูฺฉูููุง} \enquote{to eat}. We can use its doing verbal noun in a sentence like this:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุบู ุฒูููุฏู ูููู ูฑููุฃูฺฉููู.}\\
\emph{faragอกha zaydun mina -lสพakli.}\\
\enquote{Zayd got done with eating.}

Note how the doing verbal noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฃูฺฉููู} \emph{สพalสพakli} gives the meaning of the action of the verb \enquote{eating}. But since it is a noun, it obeys the rules for nouns, like being in the i-state when preceded by the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{min}.

Another point worth noting is that we have made it definite by saying \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฃูฺฉููู} \emph{สพalสพakli} instead of saying \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู} \emph{สพaklin} for the meaning of \enquote{eating}. This is because, as we explained in section
\ref{usage-of-definite-and-indefinite-nouns},
the definite noun is usually used in Arabic to give a general meaning, where in English we would not use \enquote{the}. This may be a good time to re-read that section.

Having said that, the indefnite doing verbal-noun may be used too, and this will give the meaning of \enquote{a certain}, or \enquote{a specific}. For example, with the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู ููุนููููู ุนูููููุง} \enquote{to work}, we can say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุบู ูููู ุนููููู ุตูุนูุจู.}\\
\emph{faragอกha min ษamalin แนฃaษbin.}\\
\enquote{He got done with a {[}certain{]} difficult work.}

\subsection{With a doer}\label{with-a-doer}

A doer may be used with the doing verbal-noun to show who is doing the action. In this case, the doing verbal-noun and the doer are usually placed in an annexation. The doing verbal-noun shall be the annexe noun and the doer shall be in the i-state as the base noun in the annexation. For example, consider the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃู ููููุฑูุฃู ููุฑูุงุกูุฉู} \enquote{to read}. We can say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุนูุชู ููุฑูุงุกูุฉู ุฒูููุฏู.}\\
\emph{samiษtu qirฤสพata zaydin.}\\
\enquote{I heard Zayd's reading.}

The doer may similarly be a pronoun, in which case, as usual, attached pronouns are used. So we can say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุนูุชู ููุฑูุงุกูุชููู.}\\
\emph{samiษtu qirฤสพatahu.}\\
\enquote{I heard his reading.}

\subsection{With an indirect doee}\label{with-an-indirect-doee}

If a verb uses a particular preposition with indirect doees, and the doing verbal-noun of that verb is to be used with an indirect doee, then that same preposition is used with the doing verbal-noun.

For example the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ููุฐูููุจู ุฐูููุงุจูุง} \enquote{to go} is used with the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููฐ} \emph{สพilฤ} \enquote{to} with an indirect doee to give the place to which the doer is going. This same preposition is then used with the doing verbal noun, thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุนูุจูุชู ูููู ูฑูุฐููููุงุจู ุฅูููููฐ ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู ูฑููุจูุนููุฏูุฉู.}\\
\emph{taษibtu mina -dอกhdอกhahฤbi สพila -lmadฤซnati -lbaษฤซdati.}\\
\enquote{I became tired from going to the far city.}

If a doer is used along with the indirect doee, then the doer shall be placed in a noun chain with the doer verbal-noun, as explained in the previous section. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฒูููุชู ูููู ุฐูููุงุจู ุฒูููุฏู ุฅูููููฐ ููุฏููููุฉู ุจูุนููุฏูุฉู.}\\
\emph{แธฅazintu min dอกhahฤbi zaydin สพilฤ madฤซnatin baษฤซdatin.}\\
\enquote{I became sad from Zayd's going to a far city.}

\subsection{With a direct doee}\label{with-a-direct-doee}

If a verb takes a direct doee, and we wish to use the direct doee with the verb's doing verbal noun, then we may deal with it in one of three ways:

\subsubsection{The direct doee in the i-state in an annexation with the doing verbal noun}\label{the-direct-doee-in-the-i-state-in-an-annexation-with-the-doing-verbal-noun}

In the first method, the direct doee is in the i-state as the base noun in an annexation with the doing verbal-noun. This method is used when the doer of the verbal noun is not mentioned with the doing verbal-noun, or when there is no other phrase between the doing verbal-noun and the direct doee. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุบู ุฒูููุฏู ูููู ููุฑูุงุกูุฉู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู.}\\
\emph{faragอกha zaydun min qirฤสพati -lkitฤbi.}\\
\enquote{Zayd got done with reading the book.}

In this sentence, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{สพalkitฤbi} \enquote{the book} is the direct doee of the doing verbal-noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุงุกูุฉู} \emph{qirฤสพati} \enquote{reading}. The doer \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู} \emph{zayd} \enquote{Zayd} is only mentioned in the beginning of the sentence but not again with the doing verbal-noun. Therefore, the direct doee \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{สพalkitฤbi} \enquote{the book} is allowed to be put in an annexation with the doing verbal noun thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุงุกูุฉู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{qirฤสพati -lkitฤbi} \enquote{reading the book}.

Instead of a noun, the direct doee may be a pronoun instead. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃ ุฒูููุฏู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููููุฑูุบู ูููู ููุฑูุงุกูุชููู.}\\
\emph{qaraสพa zayduni -lkitฤba fafaragอกha min qirฤสพatihi}\\
\enquote{Zayd read the book, and then he got done with reading it.}

Remember from the previous section, that a doer is handled in the same way with a doing verbal-noun by placing it in an annexation with the doing verbal-noun. So how do we know whether the base noun in an annexation with a doing verbal-noun is a doer or a doee? Well, for many verbs the meaning of the verbal-noun and the noun is sufficient. For example, in the phrase \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุงุกูุฉู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{qirฤสพati -lkitฤbi} \enquote{reading the book}, the meaning of \enquote{reading} makes it clear that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุงุจู} \emph{สพalkitฤbi} can only be a doee, because a book can't be the one doing the reading.

But there are some verbs, however, where the meaning of the verbal-noun itself is not sufficient to tell us whether the noun following it in an annexation is a doer or a doee. Consider the verb
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจู ููุถูุฑูุจู ุถูุฑูุจูุง} \enquote{to beat (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} s.o.)}. If we form an annexation using its doing verbal-noun, thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจู ุฒูููุฏู} \emph{แธarbu zaydin}, we cannot know whether Zayd is the doer (the one doing the beating), or the doee (the one getting beaten). In this case, we will need more context to help us determine whether Zayd is the doer or the doee. Here are a few sentences that may help illustrate this point:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจู ุฒูููุฏู ุนูููุฑูุง. ุณูููุนู ูฑููุฃูุจู \textbf{ุถูุฑูุจู ุฒูููุฏู} ููุบูุถูุจู ุนููููููู. ููููุฏููู ุฒูููุฏู ูููู \textbf{ุถูุฑูุจู ุนูููุฑูู}.}\\
\emph{แธaraba zaydun ษamran. samiษa -lสพabu แธarba zaydin fagอกhaแธiba ษalayhi. fa nadima zaydun min แธarbi ษamrin.}\\
\enquote{Zayd beat ฦamr. The father heard Zayd's beating so he became angry with him. So, Zayd became remorseful of beating ฦamr.}

We can see that the meaning of the sentences help us determine that in the phrase
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจู ุฒูููุฏู}
\emph{แธarba zaydin}, Zayd is the doer, and in
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจู ุนูููุฑูู}
\emph{แธarbi ษamrin}, ฦamr is the doee.

\subsubsection{The direct doee in a-state following the doing verbal-noun}\label{the-direct-doee-in-a-state-following-the-doing-verbal-noun}

The second way to deal with a direct doee and a doing-verbal noun is to put it in the a-state after the doing verbal-noun. This is usually done when the doer is mentioned with the doing verbal-noun in an annexation with it. The direct doee is then placed after the doer in the a-state. For example, we can re-word the previous example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจู ุฒูููุฏู ุนูููุฑูุง. ุณูููุนู ูฑููุฃูุจู \textbf{ุถูุฑูุจู ุฒูููุฏู ุนูููุฑูุง} ููุบูุถูุจู ุนููููููู. ููููุฏููู ุฒูููุฏู ูููู \textbf{ุถูุฑูุจููู ุนูููุฑูุง}.}\\
\emph{แธaraba zaydun ษamran. samiษa -lสพabu แธarba zaydin ษamran fagอกhaแธiba ษalayhi. fa nadima zaydun min แธarbihi ษamran.}\\
\enquote{Zayd beat ฦamr. The father heard Zayd's beating ฦamr so he became angry with him. So, Zayd became remorseful of his beating ฦamr.}

Notice that in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุฑูุจููู ุนูููุฑูุง} \emph{แธarbihi ษamran} \enquote{his beating ฦamr}, the doer is a pronoun instead of a noun. This is permissible, and is in line with other usages we have learned so far.

The doee noun in the a-state, too, may be replaced with a pronoun, but just like when the attached doee pronoun is separated from its verb it has to instead be attached to the prefix \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุง} \emph{สพiyyฤ}, here too this prefix is used. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู ุนูููุฑูู ูููู ุถูุฑูุจู ุฒูููุฏู ุฅููููุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพalima ษamrun min แธarbi zaydin สพiyyฤhu.}\\
\enquote{ฦamr was in pain from Zayd's beating him.}

This usage of putting the direct doee in the a-state after the doing verbal noun is not only done when the doer is mentioned with the doing verbal-noun. But it is also done when the direct doee is separated from the doing verbal-noun by some other words, like a prepositional phrase. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุบูุชู ูููู ูฑููููุฑูุงุกูุฉู ููู ูฑููููฺฉูุชูุจูุฉู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง.}\\
\emph{faragอกhtu mina -lqirฤสพati fi -lmaktabati kitฤban.}\\
\enquote{I got done with reading, in the library, a book.}

The prepositional phrase \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู ูฑููููฺฉูุชูุจูุฉู} \emph{fi -lmaktabati} in the above example is placed between the doing verbal-noun and the doee for effect. It could, of course, also have been placed after the doee, in a more normal fashion. In this case, it would be preferred for the doing verbal-noun and the doee to be placed in an annexation, in the manner we have already learned.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุบูุชู ูููู ููุฑูุงุกูุฉู ฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููู ูฑููููฺฉูุชูุจูุฉู .}\\
\emph{faragอกhtu min qirฤสพati kitฤbin fi -lmaktabati.}\\
\enquote{I got done with reading a book in the library.}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{The direct doee in i-state preceded by the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{li}}{The direct doee in i-state preceded by the preposition ูู li}}\label{the-direct-doee-in-i-state-preceded-by-the-preposition-ux644-li}

The third way to deal with a direct doee and a doing-verbal noun is to put it in the i-state preceded by the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{li}. This is usually done in one of the following scenarios:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  When the doing verbal-noun is indefinite and immediately precedes the direct doee. Example:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุบูุชู ูููู ููุฑูุงุกูุฉู ููููฺฉูุชูุจู.}\\
  \emph{faragอกhtu min qirฤสพatin lilkutubi.}\\
  \enquote{I got done with a reading of the books.}

  This sentence can be used to indicate one particular instance of reading the books. As opposed to saying \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุงุกูุฉู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุจู} \emph{qirฤสพati -lkutubi} which would indicate that the reading was general or complete.
\item
  When the doer comes between the doing verbal-noun and the doee. Example,

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู ุนูููุฑูู ูููู ุถูุฑูุจู ุฒูููุฏู ูููู.}\\
  \emph{สพalima ษamrun min แธarbi zaydin lahu.}\\
  \enquote{ฦamr was in pain from Zayd's beating him.}

  This is as an optional alternative to putting the doee in the a-state, in the manner we have already learned in the previous section:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู ุนูููุฑูู ูููู ุถูุฑูุจู ุฒูููุฏู ุฅููููุงูู.}\\
  \emph{สพalima ษamrun min แธarbi zaydin สพiyyฤhu.}\\
  \enquote{ฦamr was in pain from Zayd's beating him.}
\end{enumerate}

\section{Multiple doing verbal-nouns for the same verb}\label{multiple-doing-verbal-nouns-for-the-same-verb}

It is possible, and fairly common, for verbs to have more than one doing verbal-noun. Usually, each of the doing verbal-nouns has its own meaning, distinct from each other.

For example, the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููู ููุญููููู ุญูููููุง} means \enquote{to carry (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} Here is an example of its doing verbal noun in a sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุนูุจู ุฒูููุฏู ูููู ุญููููููู ููููฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑูุซูููููููุฉู.}\\
\emph{taษiba zaydun min แธฅamlihi lilkutubi -tอกhtอกhaqฤซlati.}\\
\enquote{Zayd became tired from his carrying the heavy books.}

There exists another meaning for this verb with its own doing verbal-noun: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููู ููุญููููู ุญูููููุฉู} which means \enquote{to launch an attack (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููููฐ} on s.o.)} Here is an example of its doing verbal noun in a sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูููุดู ูฑูููููููู ูููู ุญูููููุฉู ูฑููุนูุฏูููู ุนููููููููู.}\\
\emph{dahisอกha -lqawmu min แธฅamlati -lษaduwwi ษalayhim.}\\
\enquote{The people were astonished at the attack launched by the enemy on them.}

Sometimes the meaning between the multiple doing verbal-nouns is only slight. Consider, for example, the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููููู ููุฌููููู} \enquote{to not know, or to be ignorant (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} of s.th.)}

It has two doing verbal-nouns: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููููู} \emph{jahl} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุงููุฉ} \emph{jahฤlah} which have meanings that are close to each other.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌููููู} \emph{jahl} is the more simple doing verbal-noun used for not knowing something. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ููุนููู ุฒูููุฏู ูฑููููุงุฌูุจู ููุฌููููููู ุฅููููุงูู.}\\
\emph{mฤ faษala zayduni -lwฤjiba lijahlihi สพiyyฤhu.}\\
\enquote{Zayd did not do the obligatory {[}work{]} because of his not knowing it.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุงููุฉ} \emph{jahฤlah} has the more abstract meaning of \enquote{ignorance}. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุฑู ูฑููููุณููููู ูููู ุฌูููุงููุฉู ูฑููููุดูุฑูฺฉูููู.}\\
\emph{nafara -lmuslimu min jahฤlati -lmusอกhrikฤซna.}\\
\enquote{The Muslim was repulsed by the ignorance of the pagans.}

As a general rule of thumb, the fewer letters in a doing verbal-noun, the simpler its meaning. And doing verbal-nouns of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุงููุฉ} \emph{faษฤlah} tend to have an abstract meaning.

\section{Doing verbal-nouns re-used as common nouns}\label{doing-verbal-nouns-re-used-as-common-nouns}

There are many doing verbal-nouns, that in addition to their verbal meaning, are also re-used as common nouns. Their common noun meaning is typically associated, in some manner, with their verbal meaning.

For example, the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ููุณูุฃููู ุณูุคูุงููุง} means \enquote{to question or ask (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู ุนู} s.o. about s.th.)}. The doing verbal-noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุคูุงูู} \emph{suสพฤlun} can be used with its verbal meaning: \enquote{questioning}. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฆููู ูฑููุฃูุจู ูููู ฺฉูุซูุฑูุฉู ุณูุคูุงูู ูฑุจููููู ุฅููููุงูู.}\\
\emph{saสพima -lสพabu min katอกhrati suสพฤli -bnihi สพiyyฤhu.}\\
\enquote{The father became weary from the excessiveness of his son's questioning him.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุคูุงูู} \emph{suสพฤlun}, in addition to being a doing verbal-noun \enquote{questioning} is re-used as a common noun with the meaning \enquote{a question} and the broken plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูุฆูููุฉ} \emph{สพasสพilah} \enquote{questions}. So, for example, we can say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑููุฃูุณูุชูุงุฐู ุณูุคูุงููุง ุนูููู ูฑูุณููุจูููุฑูุฉู.}\\
\emph{kataba -lสพustฤdอกhu suสพฤlan ษala -ssabbลซrati.}\\
\enquote{The professor wrote a question on the board.}

\section{Common nouns re-used as doing verbal-nouns}\label{common-nouns-re-used-as-doing-verbal-nouns}

Just as some doing verbal-nouns are re-used as common nouns, there are some common nouns that may be re-used as doing verbal-nouns. For example, the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู ููููุนููู} \enquote{to do (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} an action)} has the doing verbal-noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษlun}.

There is an associated common noun from this root: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{fiษlun} \enquote{an act}. This common noun is frequently used in place of the doing verbal-noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{faษlun}. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููุจู ูฑููุฃูุณูุชูุงุฐู ูููู ูฑูุชููููุงูููุฐู ููุนููู ูฑููููุงุฌูุจู.}\\
\emph{แนญalaba -lสพustฤdอกhu mina -ttalฤmฤซdอกha fiษla -lwฤjibi.}\\
\enquote{The professor wanted from his students the doing of the obligatory {[}work{]}.}
