\chapter{The verbal-nouns of the doer and the doee}\label{the-verbal-nouns-of-the-doer-and-the-doee}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-17}

In the previous chapter we studied the verbal-noun of doing. In this chapter we shall study two more kinds of verbal-nouns. These are the doer verbal-noun and the doee-verbal noun. These, too, are nouns that can give the meaning of the verb they are derived from. In places, they may even replace the verb, thereby adding some nuances in meaning.

The doer verbal-noun gives the meaning of the doer, that is the person doing the action of the verb. For example, for the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃู ููููุฑูุฃู ููุฑูุงุกูุฉู} \enquote{to read}, the doer verbal-noun is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุฑูุฆ} \emph{qฤriสพ} \enquote{a reader}.

\section{Pattern of the doer verbal-noun}\label{pattern-of-the-doer-verbal-noun}

We saw in the previous chapter that the pattern for the doing verbal-noun for form~1 verbs was very variable. In contrast, the pattern for the doer verbal-noun for form~1 verbs is fixed. It is always on the pasttern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูู} \emph{fฤษil}. Also, the doer verbal-noun is modified for gender and number. Its forms its feminine by appending \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ} thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูููุฉ}. It takes sound plurals: the \emph{-ลซn} for the masculine, and the \emph{-ฤt} plural for the feminine. In many case, it may also have broken plurals. Here is a table showing these modifications for the u-state. You should be able to extend them for the a-state and i-state.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Number
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Masculine
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Feminine
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
singular & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนููู} \emph{fฤษilun} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูููุฉู} \emph{fฤษilatun} \\
dual & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูููุงูู} \emph{fฤษilฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูููุชูุงูู} \emph{fฤษilatฤni} \\
plural & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูููููู} \emph{fฤษilลซna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูููุงุชู} \emph{fฤษilฤtun} \\
\end{longtable}

\section{The doer verbal-noun as a noun}\label{the-doer-verbal-noun-as-a-noun}

Like the doing verbal-noun, the doer verbal noun occupies a place that is between a noun and a verb. The basic, most essential, meaning of the doer verbal noun is that of a noun which denotes the doer of the verb.

So, for example, consider the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ููุณูุฃููู ุณูุคูุงููุง} \enquote{to question}. Its doer verbal-noun is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงุฆูู}. Since it refers to the doer of this verb, we can translate it as \enquote{a questioner\textsubscript{m.}}.

By itself, the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงุฆูู} \enquote{a questioner} just denotes a noun. It does not indicate when the doer does the action of the verb: has the questioner already asked the question, is he asking it at present, or will he ask it in the future? So, for example, we can say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููููุฏููู ุณูุงุฆููู ููุณูููุณูุฃููู ุณูุคูุงููุง.}\\
\emph{sayaqdamu sฤสพilun wasayaqdamu suสพฤlan.}\\
\enquote{A questioner\textsubscript{m.} will arrive and he will ask a question.}

In the above sentence, the doer verbal-noun is being described as performing the action of the verb in the future.

Here is another example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุชู ูฑููููููููู ุณูุงุฆูููุฉู ุนููู ุฃูููุฑู.}\\
\emph{saสพalati -lfaqฤซha sฤสพilatun ษan สพamrin.}\\
\enquote{A questioner\textsubscript{f.} asked the jurist about a matter.}

In the above sentence, the doer verbal-noun is being described as having performed the action of the verb in the past.

Doer verbal-nouns of form~1 verbs, when used with this nounal meaning, often have broken plurals, in addition to their sound plurals. Generally, either could be used in most cases, but the usage of the broken plurals is preferred.

For example, consider the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชููู ููููุชููู ููุชูููุง} \enquote{to kill (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} s.o.)}. Its doer verbal-noun is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูู} \enquote{a killer\textsubscript{m.}}. Its sound plural is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูููููู} \emph{qฤtilลซna} and its broken plurals are \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชููุงู} \emph{quttฤl} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูููุฉ} \emph{qatalah}. Any of these could be used but the broken plural is often preferred.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุจู ููุชูููุฉู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ุฅูููููฐ ููุฎูุจูุฆููููู.}\\
\emph{haraba qatalatu -rrajuli สพilฤ makอกhbaสพihim.}\\
\enquote{The killers of the man fled to their hideout.}

\section{The doer verbal-noun as a verb}\label{the-doer-verbal-noun-as-a-verb}

We have learned that the essential meaning of the doer verbal-noun is the doer of the action of the verb from which it is derived. In addition to this essential meaning, the doer verbal-noun can also be used in place of the verb from which it is derived. This is only done when the verb to be replaced is the incomplete-action verb. The doer verbal-noun does not replace the completed-action verb. We will now explain this usage.

\subsection{Usage of the doer verbal-noun as a present tense verb}\label{usage-of-the-doer-verbal-noun-as-a-present-tense-verb}

Consider the following sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฐูููุจู ุฒูููุฏู ุฅูููู ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู.}\\
\emph{yadอกhยทhabu zaydun สพila -lmadrasati.}\\
\enquote{Zayd goes to school.}

The above sentence does not explicitly specify whether Zayd is actually going to school at present, or that he goes to school habitually and not necessarily right now.

If we wish to indicate that Zayd is actually going to school at present we can replace the incomplete-action verb with the indefinite doer verbal-noun. So we get:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู ุฐูุงููุจู ุฅูููู ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู.}\\
\emph{yadอกhยทhabu zaydun สพila -lmadrasati.}\\
\enquote{Zayd is going to school.}

Note that the same preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููฐ} \emph{สพilฤ} \enquote{to} is used with the doer verbal-noun as is used with the verb.
Also note that this is now a subject-information sentence instead of a verbal sentence. \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู} \emph{zaydun} \enquote{Zayd} is the subject, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูุงููุจู} \emph{dอกhฤhibun} is part of the information.

This usage of the doer verbal-noun to indicate that the action of the verb is ocurring at present is mostly done for what we call \emph{verbs of posture} and \emph{verbs of motion}.

Verbs of posture denote a static position or activity of the doer's body and include verbs like sitting, standing, lying down, sleeping, etc.

Verbs of motion denote a moving action of the doer's body and include verbs like
going, coming, running, etc.

So, if, for example, we say,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููููุจู ุฌูุงููุณูุฉู ุนูููู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููฺฉูุฑูุณูููู.}\\
\emph{zaynabu jฤlisatun ษala hฤdอกha -lkursiyyi.}\\
\enquote{Zaynab is sitting on this chair.}

this indicates that Zaynab is sitting on this chair at present. And if we say,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูุฌูููุณู ุฒูููููุจู ุนูููู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููฺฉูุฑูุณูููู.}\\
\emph{tajlisu zaynabu ษala hฤdอกha -lkursiyyi.}\\
\enquote{Zaynab sits on this chair.}

this indicates that Zaynab usually sits on this chair.

If this usage of the doer verbal-noun to indicate a present action is mostly only for verbs of posture and motion, how then do we indicate this distinction for other verbs? We have answered this in section {[}TODO: add section to incomplete-action verb{]} where we said that in order to give the meaning that the action of the verb is happening right now, a verbal sentence can be converted to a subject-information sentence.

\subsection{Usage of the doer verbal-noun as a future tense verb}\label{doer-verbal-noun-for-intended-future-action}

The doer verbal-noun may be used in place of the verb it is derived from to indicate an intent on the part of the doer, or to indicate that the action will occur in the future.

This usage of the doer verbal-noun is not just for verbs of posture and motion like the present tense usage. Rather, it is for all verbs in general.

And since intention is something that is mostly expressed by the speaker for himself, rather than for someone else, we will often find this usage with the subject \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง} \emph{สพana} \enquote{I}.

\subsubsection{With an indirect doee}\label{with-an-indirect-doee-1}

Here is an example of the usage of the doer verbal-noun as a future tense verb with an indirect doee:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง ุฐูุงููุจู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ุตูุฏููููู ููู ูฑูุตููุจูุงุญู.}\\
\emph{สพana dอกhฤhibun สพilฤ bayti แนฃadฤซqฤซ fi -แนฃแนฃabฤแธฅi.}\\
\enquote{I'm going to go to my friend's house in the morning.}

In the above sentence it is possible for the phrase
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู ูฑูุตููุจูุงุญู}
\emph{fi -แนฃแนฃabฤแธฅi}
\enquote{in the morning}
to be ommitted for the same meaning. In that case, surrounding context could tell us that the person is intending to go in the future, and is not actually in the process of going there at present.

Here is another example (by a female speaker):

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููุฏูู ฺฉูุฑูุฉู ููู ูฑููุจูููุชู ููุฃูููุง ุฑูุงุฌูุนูุฉู ุฅูููู ูฑููุจูููุชู ููููุงุนูุจูุฉู ุจูููุง.}\\
\emph{ษindฤซ kuratun fi -lbayti faสพana rฤjiษatun สพila -lbayti walฤษibatun bihฤ.}\\
\enquote{I have a ball at home, so I'm going to go home and play with it.}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Difference with the particles \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูู} \emph{sa-} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู} \emph{sawfa}}{Difference with the particles ุณูู sa- and ุณููููู sawfa}}\label{difference-with-the-particles-ux633ux640-sa--and-ux633ux648ux641-sawfa}

We have already learned a method to express a future action using the particles \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูู} \emph{sa-} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู} \emph{sawfa} with the incomplete-action verb. So we could also have said:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูุฐูููุจู ุฅูููููฐ ุจูููุชู ุตูุฏููููู.}\\
\emph{saสพadอกhยทhabu สพilฤ bayti แนฃadฤซqฤซ.}\\
\enquote{I will to go to my friend's house.}

The difference between using the particles \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูู} \emph{sa-} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู} \emph{sawfa} and using the doer verbal-noun is that using the doer verbal-noun signifies more emphasis, or, as a possible consequence of the emphasis, that the action is more imminent. That is:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง ุฐูุงููุจู \ldots{}}\\
\emph{สพana dอกhฤhibun \ldots{}}\\
\enquote{I will {[}definitely{]} go \ldots{}}\\
or\\
\enquote{I'm going to go \ldots{}}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูุฐูููุจู \ldots{}}\\
\emph{saสพadอกhยทhabu \ldots{}}\\
\enquote{{[}Soon{]} I will go \ldots{}}

\subsubsection{With a direct doee}\label{with-a-direct-doee-1}

If a verb takes a direct doee, and we wish to use the direct doee with the verb's doer verbal-noun when the doer verbal-noun is acting as a verb, then we may deal with it in one of three ways:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  The direct doee in a-state following the doer verbal-noun

  The most basic method of dealing with a direct doee of a doer verbal noun is by placing it in the a-state right after the doer verbal-noun. Here is an example,

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏู ุฏูุฎููู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู ุฑูุฌููู ุดูุฑููุฑู. ูููู \textbf{ููุงุชููู ุณููููุงููููุง}.}\\
  \emph{qad dakอกhala -lmadฤซnata rajulun sอกharฤซrun. hua qฤtilun sukkฤnahฤ.}\\
  \enquote{An evil man has entered the city. He is going to kill its residents.}
\item
  The direct doee in i-state annexed to the doer verbal-noun

  The combination of the doer verbal-noun and following direct doee in the a-state is often replaced with an annexation of the doer verbal-noun to the i-state direct doee. So, for example, instead of the above example, we can say:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏู ุฏูุฎููู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู ุฑูุฌููู ุดูุฑููุฑู. ูููู \textbf{ููุงุชููู ุณููููุงููููุง}.}\\
  \emph{qad dakอกhala -lmadฤซnata rajulun sอกharฤซrun. hua qฤtilu sukkฤnihฤ.}\\
  \enquote{An evil man has entered the city. He is going to kill its residents.}

  Note that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชููู ุณูฺฉููุงููููุง} \emph{qฤtilu sukkฤnihฤ.} can also support the non-verbal meaning of the doer verbal-noun: \enquote{killer of its residents}, i.e., he has already killed its residents in the past. So, when an annexation is used with a doer verbal-noun, we will often need surrounding context to tell us whether the verbal (incomplete-action) meaning is intended, or the noun meaning.

  This usage of annexing the doer verbal-noun to the i-state direct doee instead of employing the more basic usage of the doer verbal-noun and a following a-state direct doee is optional, but fairly common. In fact, when the doer-verbal noun ends with an \emph{n}-mark, and the direct doee begins with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููู} \emph{สพal} \enquote{the}, then the annexation usage becomes predominant over the basic a-state usage. So we will be more likely to see:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง ููุงุนููููู.}\\
  \emph{สพana fฤษiluhu.}

  instead of:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง ููุงุนููู ุฅููููุงูู.}\\
  \emph{สพana fฤษilun สพiyyฤhu.}

  for the meaning: \enquote{I will do it.} Note again, that the latter sentence could also support the nounal meaning of the doer-verbal noun: \enquote{I am its doer.}, i.e., \enquote{the one who did it.}

  Similarly, it will be more common to find:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ููุงุชููู ูฑููููุงุณู.}\\
  \emph{huwa qฤtilu -nnฤsi.}

  instead of:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ููุงุชููู ูฑููููุงุณู.}\\
  \emph{huwa qฤtiluni -nnฤsa.}

  for the meaning: \enquote{He is going to kill the people.} Note, once again, that the former sentence also supports the meaning: \enquote{He is the people's killer.}, i.e., \enquote{the one who killed them}, and that context would be needed to tell us which of the two meanings is intended.

  The annexation of a doer verbal-noun to its direct doee in the i-state is not the kind of \enquote{proper} annexation that we have learned so far. In fact, it is called an \emph{improper annexation} and we shall study it in more detail in chapter \hyperref[todo]{TODO}, if Allฤh wills.
\item
  Quite similar to what we learned in section~@ref(the-direct-doee-in-i-state-preceded-by-the-preposition-\%D9\%84-li) for doing verbal-nouns, the direct doee can follow the doer verbal-noun in the i-state preceded by the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{li}.

  This is often optional, as an alternative to the above two methods. For example,

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ููุงุชููู ูููููู.}\\
  \emph{huwa qฤtilun lahum.}\\
  \enquote{He will kill them.}

  Using \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{li} in this manner is also a technique to move the direct doee before the doer verbal-noun for effect, if desired. For example,

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ูููููู ููุงุชููู.}\\
  \emph{huwa lahum qฤtilun.}\\
  \enquote{He will kill them.}
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{The definite doer verbal-noun as a verb}\label{the-definite-doer-verbal-noun-as-a-verb}

So far we have seen only an indefinite doer verbal-noun being used with the meaning of an incomplete-action verb. However, the definite doer verbal-noun, too, can give this meaning. The meaning is often in the present tense. Here are some examples:

With an indirect doee:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏููู ุฒูููุฏู ูฑูุฐููุงููุจู ุฅูููู ูฑููุฌูุงููุนูุฉู.}\\
\emph{qadima zayduni -dอกhdอกhฤhibu สพila -ljฤmiษati.}\\
\enquote{Zayd, the one who goes to the university, has arrived.}

With a direct doee in the a-state:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุจูุชู ูููู ูฑููุฃูุณูุฏู ูฑููุขฺฉููู ูฑููุฅูููุณูุงูู.}\\
\emph{harabtu mina -lสพasadi -lสพฤkilu -lสพinsฤna.}\\
\enquote{I fled from the lion, the one that eats man.}

With a direct doee in the i-state preceded by the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{li}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููููุฌูุญู ูฑูุทููุงููุจู ูฑูุชููุงุฑูฺฉู ููููููููู.}\\
\emph{sayanjaแธฅu -แนญแนญฤlibu -ttฤriku lillahwi.}\\
\enquote{The student, the one who leaves idle amusement, will succeed.}

\subsection{Plurals of the doer verbal-noun when used as a verb}\label{plurals-of-the-doer-verbal-noun-when-used-as-a-verb}

We mentioned in section~\ref{the-doer-verbal-noun-as-a-noun} that doer-verbal nouns when used with their nounal meaning often have broken plurals along with their sound plural.
We gave the example of the doer verbal-noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูู} \emph{qฤtil} \enquote{a killer\textsubscript{m.}} with the sound plural is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูููููู} \emph{qฤtilลซna} and the broken plurals \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชููุงู} \emph{quttฤl} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูููุฉ} \emph{qatalah}.

When the doer verbal-noun is used as a verb, only the sound plural is permitted to be used, and the broken plurals, if any are not used. So we can only say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ููุงุชูููููู ูฑููููุงุณู.}\\
\emph{hum qฤtilลซna -nnฤsa.}\\
and\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ููุงุชูููู ูฑููููุงุณู.}\\
\emph{hum qฤtilu -nnฤsi.}\\
for\\
\enquote{They will kill the people.}\\
not, for example\\
\(\times\)~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ููุชููุงูู ูฑููููุงุณู.}

(In the second sentence, the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุชูููููู} is ommitted because it is an annexe noun).

\section{The doee verbal-noun}\label{the-doee-verbal-noun}

The doee verbal-noun for form~1 verbs is on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \emph{mafษลซl}. It carries the meaning of the person or thing to whom the action of the verb has been done. For example, the doee verbal-noun for the verb
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชููู ููููุชููู ููุชูููุง} \enquote{to kill (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} s.o.)}
is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชููู} \emph{maqtลซl} and means \enquote{a killed person}.

\subsection{The plural of the doee verbal noun}\label{the-plural-of-the-doee-verbal-noun}

The doee verbal-noun almost always takes the sound plurals \emph{-ลซn} for masculine intelligent beings, and \emph{-ฤt} otherwise. Therefore the plural of the doee verbal-noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชููู} \emph{maqtลซl} \enquote{a killed person\textsubscript{m.}} is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชููููููู} \emph{maqtลซlลซna} \enquote{killed persons\textsubscript{m.}}.
and the plural of the doee verbal-noun
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชููููุฉ} \emph{maqtลซlah} \enquote{a killed person\textsubscript{f.}} is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชููููุงุช} \emph{maqtลซlฤt} \enquote{killed persons\textsubscript{f.}}.

There are a only a few doee verbal-nouns that, as an exception, have broken plurals. The broken plural for these exceptions is than always on the pattern \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุนููู} \emph{mafฤษฤซl}\textsuperscript{2}. For example, the doee verbal-noun for the verb
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู ููููุนููู ููุนูููุง} \enquote{to curse (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} s.o.)} is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \emph{malษลซn} \enquote{accursed} and its plural is \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุงุนููู} \emph{malฤษฤซn}\textsuperscript{2}.

\subsection{Usage of the doee verbal-noun}\label{usage-of-the-doee-verbal-noun}

Much of what has been said regarding the doer verbal-noun applies to the doee verbal-noun as well: The doee verbal-noun may be used with a verbal meaning for the incomplete-action verb only. So if we say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ููููุชูููู.}\\
\emph{huwa maqtลซl}

with a verbal meaning, then it means \enquote{He will be killed.} And if we say it using its nounal meaning, then it means \enquote{He is the person killed.}

Unlike the doer verbal-noun which can take doees, since the doee verbal-noun is itself the doee, there is no question of it taking other doees. So this does simplify matters.

\subsection{The doee verbal-nouns of indirect doee verbs}\label{the-doee-verbal-nouns-of-indirect-doee-verbs}

Consider the verb
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ููุณูุฃููู ุณูุคูุงููุง} \enquote{to question (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู ุนู} s.o. about s.th.)}.

Here it is used in a sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ุฒูููุฏู ุฒูููููุจู ุนููู ุญูุงุฏูุซูุฉู.}\\
\emph{saสพala zaydun zaynaba ษan แธฅฤditอกhah.}\\
\enquote{Zayd questioned Zaynab about an accident.}

In this sentence, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู} \emph{zaydun} \enquote{Zayd} is the doer. The corresponding doer verbal-noun that refers to him is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงุฆูู} \emph{sฤสพil} \enquote{a questioner\textsubscript{m.}}.
Next, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููููุจู} \emph{zaynaba} \enquote{Zaynab} is the direct doee. The corresponding doee verbal-noun that refers to her is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุคููููุฉ} \emph{masสพลซlah} \enquote{a questioned person\textsubscript{f.}}.
But how, now, do we refer to the indirect doee: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุงุฏูุซูุฉู} \emph{แธฅaditอกhatin} \enquote{an accident}? The answer is that the doee verbal-noun referring to this indirect doee is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุคููู ุนูููููุง} \emph{masสพลซl ษanhฤ} \enquote{a thing\textsubscript{f.} questioned about}.

Let's analyze this term \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุคููู ุนูููููุง} \emph{masสพลซl ษanhฤ} \enquote{a thing questioned about} carefully. The first word is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูุคููู} \emph{masสพลซl} which shall always be singular masculine, regardless of the gender and number of the indirect doee. The second word is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููููุง} \emph{ษanhฤ} \enquote{about it}. Here \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููู} \emph{ษan} is the same preposition that has been used with the verb. And \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \emph{hฤ} is the pronoun that refers to the indirect doee \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุงุฏูุซูุฉู} \emph{แธฅaditอกhatin} \enquote{an accident}. If the number or gender of the indirect doee were to change then this would be reflected in this pronoun.

So, for example, if we say,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุธูุฑู ุฒูููุฏู ุฅูููู ูฑูุฑููุฌูุงูู.}\\
\emph{nadฬฃอกhara zaydun สพila -rrijฤli.}\\
\enquote{Zayd looked at the men.}

then, the doee verbal-noun that refers to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุฑููุฌูุงูู} \emph{สพarrijฤli} \enquote{the men} is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุธููุฑ ุฅููููููููู} \emph{mandฬฃอกhลซr สพilayhim} \enquote{persons\textsubscript{m.} looked at}.

If doee verbal-nouns of indirect doees are used in sentences then it is the first word (in this case \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุธููุฑ} \emph{mandฬฃอกhลซrun}) that changes for definiteness and state (but not for gender or number, as already discussed). Here are some examples:

From the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจู ููููุนูุจู ููุนูุจูุง} \enquote{to play (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐููู ูฑููฺฉูุฑููฐ ูููู ูฑููููููุนููุจู ุจูููุง.}\\
\emph{hฤdอกhi -lkurฤ hiya -lmalษลซbu bihฤ.}\\
\enquote{These balls are the ones played with.}

From the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฑู ููุฃูููุฑู ุฃูููุฑูุง} \enquote{to order (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} s.o. \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจ} to do s.th.)}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ูฑูููุฃูููุฑู ุจูููููู.}\\
\emph{faษala -lgอกhulฤmu -lmaสพmลซra bihinna.}\\
\enquote{The boy did the {[}things{]} ordered to do.}

(Remember that the feminine plural pronouns may be used to refer to plural non-intelligent beings, regardless of their grammatical gender, in order to indicate plurality.)

Having said all this, in practice, you may find that indirect doees are sometimes treated as direct doees when forming their doee verbal-noun. This is especially common when forming plurals for terms that are very common. So instead of referring to \enquote{{[}things{]} ordered to do} in the above example as
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููููุฃูููุฑู ุจูููููู}
\emph{สพalmaสพmลซra bihinna}, you may find the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฃููููุฑูุงุชู} \emph{สพalmaสพmลซrฤti} used instead.

TODO: The doee verbal noun for indirect doees may have some ambiguity with the doee verbal for direct doees. \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุณุคูู ุนูู} can also be \enquote{the person who is asked about it} where the pronoun has been substituted for a noun, for example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุณุคูู ุนู ุงูุฃูุฑ} . In this case it is the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุณุคูู} which will be feminized and pluralized. \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุงููุณุคูููู ุนูู} \enquote{the persons asked about it.}

For that matter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณุงุกู ุนูู} is also valid as \enquote{the questioner about it}.

\section{Doer and doee verbal-nouns re-used as adjectival-nouns}\label{doer-and-doee-verbal-nouns-re-used-as-adjectival-nouns}

Doer and doee verbal-nouns are often re-used as adjectival-nouns with meanings that are directly formed from their doer and doee meaning respectively. Here are some examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5833}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1667}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2500}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Verb
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Doer/doee verbal-noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Adjectival-noun meaning
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู ููููุนููู ููุนููููุฉู} \enquote{to be soft} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูู} & \enquote{soft} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุจูุณู ููููุจูุณู ููุจููุณูุฉู} \enquote{to be dried up} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุจูุณ} & \enquote{dried up} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุถูุฑู ููุญูุถูุฑู ุญูุถููุฑูุง} \enquote{to be present} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุงุถูุฑ} & \enquote{present (attending)} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุนู ููุฌูููุนู ุฌูููุนูุง} \enquote{to gather (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงููุน} & \enquote{comprehensive} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนู ููููููุนู ููููุนูุง ููููููุนูุงููุง} \enquote{to be shiny} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงููุน} & \enquote{shiny} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุญู ููููุชูุญู ููุชูุญูุง} \enquote{to open (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชููุญ} & \enquote{open} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุฑู ููุดูููุฑู ุดูููุฑูุง} \enquote{to make famous (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูุ ูู} s.o., s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุดููููุฑ} & \enquote{famous} \\
\end{longtable}

\subsection{Genderizability of doer and doee verbal-nouns when re-used as adjectival-nouns}\label{genderizability-of-doer-and-doee-verbal-nouns-when-re-used-as-adjectival-nouns}

When a doer or doee verbal-noun is re-used as an adjectival-noun, then it generally retains its genderizability. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุงุจู ููููุชููุญู}\\
\emph{bฤbun maftลซแธฅun}\\
\enquote{an open door}

and

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงููุฐูุฉู ููููุชููุญูุฉู}\\
\emph{nฤfidอกhatun maftลซแธฅatun}\\
\enquote{an open window}

If, however, the adjectival-noun is only applicable to females, then, only a female adjectival-noun is formed but, peculiarly, without the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}. The most common example is from the verb:
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญููููู ููุญููููู ุญูููููุง} \enquote{to carry (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)}. The doer verbal-noun is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุงููู} \emph{แธฅฤmil} \enquote{a carrier}. The adjectival-noun formed from the doer verbal-noun is \enquote{pregnant}, but because it is only applicable to females, it does not get the feminine marker \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฉ}. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฑูุฃูุฉู ุญูุงูููู.}\\
\emph{สพalmarสพatu แธฅฤmil.}\\
\enquote{The woman is pregnant.}

This does not affect the doer verbal-noun when it is not used with this adjectival-noun meaning. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููููุฑูุฃูุฉู ุญูุงููููุฉู ูฑููููุงุกู.}\\
\emph{สพalmarสพatu แธฅฤmilatu -lmฤสพ.}\\
\enquote{The woman will carry the water.}\\
or\\
\enquote{The woman is the water-carrier.}

\subsection{Corresponding with English adjectives}\label{corresponding-with-english-adjectives}

Sometimes both the doer verbal-noun and the doee verbal-noun are used in Arabic with distinct meanings where we would use the same word in English. For example, the verb
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู ููุนููููู ุนูููููุง} \emph{ษaqala yaษqilu ษaqlan} means \enquote{to make sense (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} of s.th.)}.
Its doer verbal-noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููู} \emph{ษฤqil} means \enquote{one who makes sense (of something)} and may be re-used as an adjectival noun meaning \enquote{sensible} when it refers to a person who makes sense of something. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู ุบูููุงูู ุนูุงูููู.}\\
\emph{zaydun gอกhulฤmun ษฤqil.}\\
\enquote{Zayd is a sensible boy.}

Its doee verbal-noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููููู} \emph{maษqลซl} means \enquote{something which makes sense} and may be re-used as an adjectival noun meaning \enquote{sensible} when it refers to a something which makes sense. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ููููููุฌู ููุนูููููู.}\\
\emph{hฤdอกhฤ manhajun maษqลซl.}\\
\enquote{This is a sensible approach.}

\section{Doer and doee verbal-nouns re-used as common nouns}\label{doer-and-doee-verbal-nouns-re-used-as-common-nouns}

The doer verbal-noun is often re-used as a common noun with a meaning that is either directly, or indirectly related to the meaning of the verb. For example, the doer verbal-noun of the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ููุณูุฃููู ุณูุคูุงููุง} \emph{saสพala yasสพalu suสพฤlan} is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงุฆูู} \enquote{a questioner} with the sound plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงุฆูููููู} \emph{sฤสพilลซna} and the broken plurals \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุคููุงู} \emph{suสพสพฤl} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃูููุฉ} \emph{saสพalah}.

The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงุฆูู} \emph{sฤสพil} \enquote{a questioner} is re-used with the meaning \enquote{a beggar}. The association in meaning is that a beggar continually asks people for money.

The re-use of a doer verbal-noun or doee verbal-noun as a common noun does not prevent it from being used with its doer/doee or verbal meaning any more.
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงุฆูู} \emph{sฤสพil} may be used to mean both \enquote{a questioner} and \enquote{a beggar}, and context will help us determine which of the meanings is intended.

When a doer verbal-noun is re-used as a common noun then only the broken plural, if it exists, may be used. The sound plural is only permitted to be used if no broken plurals exist. Here are some more examples of doer verbal-nouns re-used as common nouns:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1429}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1429}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2143}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Verb
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Doer/doee verbal-noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Plural
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Common noun meaning
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู ููุนููููู ุนูููููุง} \enquote{to know (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููููุงุก} & \enquote{a scholar} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููุจู ููุทูููุจู ุทูููุจูุง} \enquote{to seek (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูุงููุจ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทููููุงุจุ ุทูููุจูุฉ} & \enquote{a student} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูุจู ููููุนูุจู ููุนูุจูุง} \enquote{to play (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูุจ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุนูุจูููู} & \enquote{a player} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุนู ููุฌูููุนู ุฌูููุนูุง} \enquote{to gather (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงููุนูุฉ} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงููุนูุงุช} & \enquote{a university} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุนู ููุฌูููุนู ุฌูููุนูุง} \enquote{to gather (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงููุน} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุงููุน} & \enquote{a mosque (in which the Friday prayers are performed)} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุฏูุซู ููุญูุฏูุซู ุญูุฏููุซูุง} \enquote{to happen} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุงุฏูุซูุฉู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูููุงุฏูุซ} & \enquote{an accident} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฑูุจู ููุดูุฑูุจู ุดูุฑูุจูุง} \enquote{to drink (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุงุฑูุจ} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูููุงุฑูุจ} & \enquote{a moustache} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุญููู ููุณูุญููู ุณูุญูููุง} \enquote{to abrade (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุงุญููู} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูููุงุญูู} & \enquote{a seashore} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถููููู ููุถููููู ุถูููุงููุง} \enquote{to guarantee (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} s.th.)} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุถููููxk} & \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุถูุงูููู} & \enquote{a content (of a letter, etc.)} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุฎููู ููุฏูุฎููู ุฏูุฎููููุง} \enquote{to enter} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุงุฎูู} & none & \enquote{inside} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุฑูุฌู ููุฎูุฑูุฌู ุฎูุฑููุฌูุง} \enquote{to exit} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุงุฑูุฌ} & none & \enquote{outside} \\
\end{longtable}

The last two \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฏูุงุฎููู} \enquote{inside} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุงุฑูุฌู} \enquote{outside} are notable. Here, for example, is how they can be used:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุบูุณููู ูฑููฺฉููุจู ูููู ุฏูุงุฎููู.}\\
\emph{gอกhasala -lkลซba min dฤkอกhilin.}\\
\enquote{He washed the tumbler from inside.}

\subsection{Genderizability of doer and doee verbal-nouns when re-used as common nouns}\label{genderizability-of-doer-and-doee-verbal-nouns-when-re-used-as-common-nouns}

When a doer or doee verbal-noun is re-used as a common noun, then it loses its genderizability. For example, if we wish to say \enquote{The building is a university.} we will say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุงุกู ุฌูุงููุนูุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพalbinฤสพu jฤmiษah.}\\
\enquote{The building is a university.}

We cannot masculinize \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงููุนูุฉ} \emph{jฤmiษah} \enquote{a university} to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงููุน} \emph{jฤmiษ} in order to make it match the gender of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุงุก} \emph{binฤสพ} (masc.) \enquote{a building}. Were we to do so, then
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงููุน} \emph{jฤmiษ} would get interpreted with either:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Its doer verbal-noun meaning \enquote{a gatherer}:

  \enquote{The building is a gatherer.}

  which doesn't make sense as a sentence.
\item
  Or, with the common noun meaning of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงููุน} \emph{jฤmiษ}, if one happens to exist. There is such a meaning in this case: \enquote{a mosque (in which the Friday prayers are performed)}. So then we would get:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุงุกู ุฌูุงููุนู.}\\
  \emph{สพalbinฤสพu jฤmiษun.}\\
  \enquote{The building is a mosque (in which the Friday prayers are performed).}
\item
  Or, with the adjectival noun meaning of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงููุน} \emph{jฤmiษ}, if one happens to exist. There is such a meaning in this case: \enquote{comprehensive}. So then we would get:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุจูููุงุกู ุฌูุงููุนู.}\\
  \emph{สพalbinฤสพu jฤmiษun.}\\
  \enquote{The building is comprehensive.}
\end{enumerate}

None of these give the original meaning we intended: \enquote{The building is a university.} So, in summary,
once a doer or doee verbal-noun is re-used as a common noun, it loses its genderizability.

Having said this, when a doer verbal-noun is re-used as a common noun that applies to humans, both the masculine and feminine common-noun typically exist together. So for example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููู} \emph{ษฤlim} is re-used as the common-noun for \enquote{a (male) scholar} with the plural \textsuperscript{2}\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูููููุงุก} \emph{ษulamฤสพ}.
And\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููููุฉ} \emph{ษฤlimah} is re-used as the common-noun for \enquote{a (female) scholar} with the plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุงููููุงุช} \emph{ษฤlimฤt}.

In such cases, i.e., when applicable to humans, the dictionary will generally only list, and supply the definition for the masculine common-noun. The reader is expected to know that its feminine exists and how to form it.

There are exceptions, however. The verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุฑูููฐ ููุฌูุฑูู ุฌูุฑูููุง} \emph{jarฤ yajrฤซ jaryan} \enquote{to run} is formed from the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฌุฑูยป}. This is a weak root because of the letter \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} in it, and we will study it in more detail later in chapter~\ref{roots-with-weak-final-letter}. In any case, its feminine doer verbal-noun is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุฑูููุฉ} \emph{jฤriyah} and is re-used for the common noun meaning \enquote{a girl}. The masculine doer verbal noun is not re-used as a common noun for the meaning \enquote{a boy}.

\chapter{a-state incomplete-action verbs}\label{a-state-incomplete-action-verbs}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-18}

In
chapter~\ref{u-state-incomplete-action-verbs}
we mentioned that incomplete action verbs have three states (like nouns).
These states are called:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  The u-state
\item
  The a-state
\item
  The รธ-state
\end{enumerate}

We introduced the u-state incomplete-action verb in
chapter~\ref{u-state-incomplete-action-verbs}.
In this chapter we will study the
a-state
incomplete-action verb.

The u-state incomplete-action verb makes a plain statement.
The a-state incomplete-action verb implies a wish or purpose.
The a-state incomplete-action verb is used after the following articles:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} \emph{สพan}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{lan}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{li}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} \emph{kay}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุชููููฐ} \emph{แธฅattฤ}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุฐููู} \emph{สพidอกhan}
\end{itemize}

We will go over these cases in this chapter.

\section{Forming the a-state incomplete-action verb}\label{forming-the-a-state-incomplete-action-verb}

Here is the u-state incomplete action verb for the singular masculine absentee participant doer \enquote{he}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู}\\
\emph{yafษalu}\\
\enquote{he does}

Note that, because it is in the u-state, the its final letter ends with a \emph{u}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.
In order to form the
a-state
incomplete-action verb,
we change the \emph{u}-mark into a
a-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู}\\
\emph{yafษala}

This is done for all participants whose doer pronoun is invisible and u-state verb ends with a \emph{u}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.

For participants whose doer pronoun is followed by an extra \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} in the u-state verb, this final \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} is dropped in order to form the
a-state
incomplete-action verb.
So, for example, the u-state
incomplete-action verb:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููุงูู}\\
\emph{yafษalฤni}\\
\enquote{they\textsubscript{2,m} do}

becomes, for the
a-state:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููุง}\\
\emph{yafษalฤ}

Here is the complete table of the
a-state
incomplete-action verb
for all doer participants.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2667}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2667}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2667}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Participant
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Incomplete-action verb doer pronoun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
u-state incomplete-action verb
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
a-state incomplete-action verb
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
he & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \\
she & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููู} \\
you\textsubscript{1m} & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููู} \\
you\textsubscript{1f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููู} \\
I & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} \\
they\textsubscript{2m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููุงูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููุง} \\
they\textsubscript{2f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููุงูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููุง} \\
you\textsubscript{2} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููุงูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููุง} \\
they\textsubscript{3m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููุง} \\
they\textsubscript{3f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููู} (same) \\
you\textsubscript{3m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููุง} \\
you\textsubscript{3f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููู} (same) \\
we & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \\
\end{longtable}

Take note the following:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The u-state and a-state verbs are the same for the feminine plural absentee and addressee participants:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููู} (they\textsubscript{3f})
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููู} (you\textsubscript{3f})
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The a-state verbs for the masculine plural absentee and addressee participants have a final silent สพalif:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููุง} (they\textsubscript{3m})
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููุง} (you\textsubscript{3m})
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\section{\texorpdfstring{After \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} \emph{สพan}}{After ุฃููู สพan}}\label{after-ux623ux646-ean}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} \emph{สพan} \enquote{that} is the main article which causes the following
incomplete-action verb to be in the a-state.
The other articles that we listed in the introduction are all either derived from
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}
or include its meaning implicitly without expressing it.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Basic usage of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} \emph{สพan} with the a-state incomplete-action verb}{Basic usage of ุฃููู สพan with the a-state incomplete-action verb}}\label{basic-usage-of-ux623ux646-ean-with-the-a-state-incomplete-action-verb}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} often follows verbs that have a meaning of wishing or hoping. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู ูฑูุทููุงููุจู ุฃููู ููููุฌูุญู.}\\
\emph{สพamala -แนญแนญฤlibu สพan yanjaแธฅ.}\\
\enquote{The student hoped that he succeed.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} can be used to negate the following a-state incomplete-action verb.
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} combines with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} and assimilates with it to form \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุง} \emph{สพallฤ} \enquote{that not}.
For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฑู ูฑููุฃูุจู ูฑูููฑุจููู ุฃููููุง ููฺฉูุณููู.}\\
\emph{สพamara -lสพabu li-bna สพallฤ yaksal.}\\
\enquote{The father ordered the son that he not be lazy.}

Other than this \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง},
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} must directly precede the following a-state incomplete-action verb and must not be separated from it.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Grammatical equivalence of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} clause with a doing verbal noun}{Grammatical equivalence of ุฃููู clause with a doing verbal noun}}\label{grammatical-equivalence-of-ux623ux646-clause-with-a-doing-verbal-noun}

In grammatical theory,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}
and the following verb form a clause that is equivalent in meaning to the doing verbal-noun of the verb. So in the example,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู ูฑูุทููุงููุจู ุฃููู ููููุฌูุญู.}, the
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} clause
(\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู ููููุฌูุญู})
is equivalent to the doing verbal noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููููุฌูุงุญ}.
So the sentence is grammatically equivalent to

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู ูฑูุทููุงููุจู ูฑููููุฌูุงุญู.}\\
\emph{สพamala -แนญแนญฤlibu -nnajฤแธฅ.}\\
\enquote{The student hoped {[}for{]} success.}

This grammatical equivalence of the
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} clause
with a noun aloows the
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} clause to take the place of a noun in various positions in a sentence.
So, in the above example, the
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} clause
is in place of the direct doee of the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู}:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-24-1.pdf}

We show other examples below where the
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} clause
occurs in place of other noun positions.

As the subject:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-25-1.pdf}

As the information:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-26-1.pdf}

As a doer noun:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-27-1.pdf}

In the i-state as the base noun in an annexation:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-28-1.pdf}

In the i-state after a preposition:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-29-1.pdf}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Option to drop the preposition before \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}}{Option to drop the preposition before ุฃููู}}\label{option-to-drop-the-preposition-before-ux623ux646}

In the above example the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุบูุจู ููุฑูุบูุจู} takes an indirect doee after the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู}.
In such cases, where the
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} clause
occurs after a preposition, it is common to drop the preposition as long as there is not resulting confusion in meaning.
So, we can also say (without the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููู}) for the same meaning:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฑูุบูุจู ูฑููุบูููุงูู ุขููู ููุฃูฺฉููู.}\\
\enquote{The boy desired that he eat.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Uncommon usages of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}}{Uncommon usages of ุฃููู}}\label{uncommon-usages-of-ux623ux646}

Ocassionally, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} is used with the meaning \enquote{lest}. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูููุชู ูฑูุซููุนูุจูุงูู ุฃููู ููููุชูููููู.}\\
\enquote{I killed the serpent lest it kill me.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} may also occur before a completed-action verb. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูููุบูููู ุฃููู ุฑูุฌูุนูุชู.}\\
\enquote{That you have returned has reached me.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Other types of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}}{Other types of ุฃููู}}\label{other-types-of-ux623ux646}

There are other types of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} in the Arabic language. They all have the basic meaning \enquote{that}. But they are used in different grammatical ways.

The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} we have learned here is called the \emph{verbal noun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}} because of the equivalence of its clause with a doing verbal noun.

There is also another type of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} called the \emph{lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}} that we will learn in section~\ref{lightened-an}.

There is also the \emph{explanatory \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}} and the \emph{extra \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}} that we will cover in chapter~\ref{types-of-an}.

\section{\texorpdfstring{After \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{li}}{After ูู li}}\label{after-ux644-li}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} of purpose}{The ูู of purpose}}\label{the-ux644-of-purpose}

The article \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} may be attached to the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \emph{li} thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃููู} \emph{liสพan} to give the purpose of the following verb. This \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} may be translated as \enquote{so that}. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู ููุฃููู ููุดูุจูุนู.}\\
\enquote{He ate so that he be sated.}

When \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} is thus used, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} is optionally allowed to be dropped while its meaning is retained.
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} is then attached to the verb.
So we can say, for the same meaning:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู ููููุดูุจูุนู.}\\
\enquote{He ate so that he be sated.}

But when using \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} to negate the verb, then \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} must be expressed, and the combination of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} is written as \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฆููููุง} \emph{liสพallฤ}. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฑูุจู ูฑููููุงุกู ููุฆููููุง ููุนูุทูุดู.}\\
\enquote{He drank the water so that he not be thirsty.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} of denial}{The ูู of denial}}\label{the-ux644-of-denial}

There is a specific \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, called the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} of denial, which is used with a-state incomplete-action verbs and the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงูู} that we will discuss in section (TODO in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงูู} chapter).

\section{\texorpdfstring{After \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} \emph{kay}}{After ฺฉููู kay}}\label{after-ux643ux64a-kay}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} \emph{kay}
is a preposition similar to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} in meaning. It may be translated as \enquote{in order that}, or also as \enquote{so that}.
It is also used before the a-state incomplete-action verb.
The difference from \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} is that, when \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} is used with the a-state incomplete-action verb, expressing or dropping the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} was optional.
But with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู}, dropping the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} is mandatory, while its meaning is retained. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู ฺฉููู ููุดูุจูุนู.}\\
\enquote{He ate in order that he be sated.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} is used, as usual, to negate the verb and is attached to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูููููุง} \emph{kaylฤ}. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฑูุจู ูฑููููุงุกู ฺฉูููููุง ููุนูุทูุดู.}\\
\enquote{He drank the water in order that he not be thirsty.}

The preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} may be combined with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} thus: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉููู} \emph{likay}, for more or less the same meaning. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู ููฺฉููู ููุดูุจูุนู.}\\
\enquote{He ate in order that he be sated.}

With \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} the whole combination is written as \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉูููููุง} \emph{likaylฤ}. \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} must again be not be expressed.

Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฑูุจู ูฑููููุงุกู ููฺฉูููููุง ููุนูุทูุดู.}\\
\enquote{He drank the water in order that he not be thirsty.}

\section{\texorpdfstring{After \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุชููููฐ} \emph{แธฅattฤ}}{After ุญูุชููููฐ แธฅattฤ}}\label{after-ux62dux62aux649-hatta}

The preposition
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุชููููฐ} \emph{แธฅattฤ}
is unusual in that the incomplete-action verb following it may either be in the a-state or the u-state, with slightly different meanings.
When the incomplete-action verb following it is in the u-state then
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุชููููฐ} gives the meaning \enquote{until}.
And when the incomplete-action verb following it is in the a-state then
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุชููููฐ} gives the meaning \enquote{for the result that}.
A mandatorily unexpressed \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} is assumed with
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุชููููฐ}
when the verb following it is in the a-state.
Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃูฺฉููู ุญูุชููููฐ ููุดูุจูุนู.} (a-state, unexpressed \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} assumed)\\
\enquote{He eats for the result that he be sated.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃูฺฉููู ุญูุชููููฐ ููุดูุจูุนู.} (u-state, no \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู})\\
\enquote{He eats until he is sated.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุชููููฐ}
is also used with completed-action verbs. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู ุญูุชููููฐ ุดูุจูุนู.} (completed-action, no \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู})\\
\enquote{He ate until he was sated.}

\section{\texorpdfstring{After \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{lan}}{After ูููู lan}}\label{after-ux644ux646-lan}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} are combined to form \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{lan} with the meaning \enquote{shall not}. \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} is used with the a-state incomplete-action verb to emphatically negate the future.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุชูุฐูููุจู.}\\
\enquote{You\textsubscript{1m} shall not go.}

\section{\texorpdfstring{After \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูุฐููู} \emph{สพidอกhan}}{After ุฅูุฐููู สพidอกhan}}\label{after-ux625ux630ux646-eipan}

\section{\texorpdfstring{After \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูููู}}{After ูู, ูู, ุฃููู, and ุซูููู}}\label{after-ux648-ux641-ux623ux648-and-ux62bux645}

\subsection{As connectors}\label{as-connectors}

If the connectors
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูููู}
occur after an a-state incomplete-action verb, then
a second a-state incomplete-action verb
(that doesn't have its own \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}, etc.)
may be either in the a-state or the u-state.
For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฑูุบูุจู ุฃููู ุฃูุญูุถูุฑู ูฑููููุฌูููุณู ููุฃูุณูููุนู.} (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูููุนู} in a-state)\\
\enquote{I desire that I attend the session and {[}that{]} I listen.}

or

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฑูุบูุจู ุฃููู ุฃูุญูุถูุฑู ูฑููููุฌูููุณู ููุฃูุณูููุนู.} (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูููุนู} in u-state)\\
\enquote{I desire that I attend the session and I will listen.}

\subsection{With special meanings}\label{with-special-meanings}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูููู} also cause the following incomplete-action verb to be in the a-state in their own right, not simply as connectors. This is discussed in more detail in chapter TODO.

\chapter{รธ-state incomplete-action verbs}\label{state-incomplete-action-verbs}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-19}

In
chapter~\ref{u-state-incomplete-action-verbs}
we mentioned that incomplete action verbs have three states (like nouns).
These states are called:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  The u-state
\item
  The a-state
\item
  The รธ-state
\end{enumerate}

We have already studied the u-state of incomplete-action verbs in
chapter~\ref{u-state-incomplete-action-verbs}.
And we will defer the study of
a-state of incomplete-action verbs to
chapter~\ref{a-state-incomplete-action-verbs}.
In this chapter we will study the
รธ-state
incomplete-action verb.

We will also study the \emph{verb of command} which is very similar to the
รธ-state
incomplete-action verb.

\section{Forming the รธ-state incomplete-action verb}\label{forming-the-0-state-incomplete-action-verb}

Here is the u-state incomplete action verb for the singular masculine absentee participant doer \enquote{he}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู}\\
\emph{yafษalu}\\
\enquote{he does}

Note that, because it is in the u-state, the its final letter ends with a \emph{u}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.
In order to form the
รธ-state
incomplete-action verb,
we change the \emph{u}-mark into a
รธ-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู}\\
\emph{yafษal}

This is done for all participants whose doer pronoun is invisible and u-state verb ends with a \emph{u}-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}.

For participants whose doer pronoun is followed by an extra \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} in the u-state verb, this final \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} is dropped in order to form the
รธ-state
incomplete-action verb.
So, for example, the u-state
incomplete-action verb:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููุงูู}\\
\emph{yafษalฤni}\\
\enquote{they\textsubscript{2,m} do}

becomes, for the
รธ-state:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููุง}\\
\emph{yafษalฤ}

Here is the complete table of the
รธ-state
incomplete-action verb
for all doer participants.

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} \\
