you\textsubscript{1,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููู} \\
I & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} \\
they\textsubscript{2,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููุงูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููุง} \\
they\textsubscript{2,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููุงูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููุง} \\
you\textsubscript{2} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููุงูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููุง} \\
they\textsubscript{3+,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนูููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููุง} \\
they\textsubscript{3+,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููู} (same) \\
you\textsubscript{3+,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนูููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููุง} \\
you\textsubscript{3+,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููู} (same) \\
we & \emph{invisible} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู} \\
\end{longtable}

Take note the following:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The u-state and รธ-state verbs are the same for the feminine plural absentee and addressee participants:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููู} (they\textsubscript{3+,f})
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููู} (you\textsubscript{3+,f})
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The u-state and รธ-state verbs for the masculine plural absentee and addressee participants have a final silent สพalif:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููููุง} (they\textsubscript{3+,m})
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุชูููุนููููุง} (you\textsubscript{3+,m})
  \end{itemize}
\item
  When the
  รธ-state
  incomplete-action verb
  ends with a
  รธ-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, and the next word begins with a connecting hamzah \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑ} then the
  รธ-mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} is converted to an \emph{i} mark \foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. For example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุนููู + ูฑูุฑููุฌููู = ููููุนููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\section{\texorpdfstring{With \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} for indirect commands}{With ูู for indirect commands}}\label{indirect-commands}

The particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} when connected to the front of a
incomplete-action verb
causes it to be in the
รธ-state
and gives it the meaning of an indirect command. In English this can be translated using \enquote{should} or \enquote{let}:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุฐูููุจู ูฑููุฑููุฌููู}\\
\enquote{The man should go!}\\
or\\
\enquote{Let the man go!}\\
(\enquote{Let} is being used here as a command for the man, not for the addressee of this speech.)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุฐูููุจู!}\\
\enquote{Let's go!}

The indirect command is only rarely used for the addressee participant. Instead, the verb of command is used which we will study in section~\ref{verb-of-command} later in this chapter.

The particles \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{so} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{and} are frequently used before this \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}.
The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} then loses its \emph{i}-mark and gets a
รธ-mark. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููููุฃูฺฉููู ุทูุนูุงููููุง ููููููุดูุฑูุจู ุดูุฑูุงุจูููุง.}\\
\enquote{So let us eat our food and drink our drink!}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชูุฌูููุณููุง ุนูููู ูฑููุฃูุฑูุถู.}\\
\enquote{You should sit on the ground!}

\section{\texorpdfstring{With \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} for prohibitions}{With ููุง for prohibitions}}\label{la-of-prohibition}

The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} when in front of a
รธ-state
incomplete-action verb
gives the meaning of a prohibition.
In English this can be translated using \enquote{Don't}.

For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุชูฺฉูุชูุจููุง}\\
\enquote{Don't write\textsubscript{3,m}!}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฒูููุฏูุ ููุง ุชูุฏูุฎููู ูฑููุจูููุชู!}\\
\enquote{Don't\textsubscript{1,m} enter the house!}

The particles \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{so} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{and} may be used before this \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง}.
Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง ุชูุฃูฺฉููู ููููุง ุชูุดูุฑูุจู!}\\
\enquote{So don't eat\textsubscript{1,m} and don't drink\textsubscript{1,m}!}

Such prohibitions are generally for the addressee participant.
However, rarely, they may be issued for the absentee participant as well. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงููููููุนู ุฒูููุฏูุง ูฑูุฏููุฎูููู.}\\
\enquote{Let him not prevent Zayd from entering!}

By the way, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} does not force a verb to be in the u-state
รธ-state. We have already seen in
section~\ref{u-state-verb-negation-la}
that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} can be used to negate a u-state incomplete-action verb for the present and future tense.
Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ููุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู}\\
\emph{lฤ yadอกhยทhabu -rrajulu.}\\
\enquote{The man does not go.} or,\\
\enquote{The man is not going.} or,\\
\enquote{The man will not go.}

\section{\texorpdfstring{With \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} for \enquote{did not}}{With ูููู for ``did not''}}\label{with-ux644ux645-for-did-not}

The particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} when in front of an
incomplete-action verb
causes it to be in the
รธ-state
and gives it the meaning of
negating the past tense
In English this can be translated using \enquote{did not}.
For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ููุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\enquote{The man did not go.}

We have already learned in
section~\ref{negating-completed-action-verbs} that the completed-action verb is negated using the particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง}. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฐูููุจู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู.}\\
\emph{mฤ dอกhahaba -rrajulu.}\\
\enquote{The man did not go.}\\
or,\\
\enquote{The man has not gone.}

Both \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} are used commonly to negate the past tense.
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} has a more emphatic meaning than \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู}.

Here are some more examples:

\section{\texorpdfstring{With \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููุง} for \enquote{did not yet}}{With ูููููุง for ``did not yet''}}\label{with-ux644ux645ux627-for-did-not-yet}

The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููุง} when in front of a
รธ-state
incomplete-action verb
gives the meaning \enquote{did not yet}.
For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููููุง ููุฐูููุจู ุฒูููุฏู.}\\
\enquote{Zayd did not go yet.}

\section{Other uses of the รธ-state incomplete-action verb}\label{other-uses-of-the-0-state-incomplete-action-verb}

The รธ-state incomplete-action verb is also used for \emph{consequential actions} and in \emph{conditional statements}. We will deal with these in
chapters~\ref{the-consequential-action}
and~\ref{conditional-statements}
respectively

\section{The verb of command}\label{verb-of-command}

In order to give a direct command to an addressee, Arabic uses the verb of command. The verb of command is very similar to the
รธ-state
incomplete-action verb.
The verb of command is only available for the addressee participant.

\subsection{Forming the verb of command}\label{forming-the-verb-of-command}

Here is the verb of command for the addressee participants:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Participant & Verb of command \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
you\textsubscript{1,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุนููู} \\
you\textsubscript{1,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุนูููู} \\
you\textsubscript{2} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุนูููุง} \\
you\textsubscript{3+,m} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุนููููุง} \\
you\textsubscript{3+,f} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุนููููู} \\
\end{longtable}

In order to form the verb of command, we remove the initial \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุช} from the addressee particpant verb. The verb then begins with an รธ-mark so we place a connecting hamzah in front of it.

When the verb of command occurs in the beginning of a sentence, then the vowel mark for the connecting hamzah is selected according to the following criteria:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\item
  When the middle root letter of the verb of command has an \emph{u}-mark~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, then the connecting hamzah gets an \emph{u}-mark~too. Examples:

  \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
  \toprule\noalign{}
  Verb & Verb of command for \enquote{he} \\
  \midrule\noalign{}
  \endhead
  \bottomrule\noalign{}
  \endlastfoot
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุธูุฑู ููููุธูุฑู ููุธูุฑูุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุธูุฑู} \enquote{Look!} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุชููู ููููุชููู ููุชูููุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุชููู} \enquote{Kill!} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฺฉูุซู ููููฺฉูุซู ููฺฉููุซูุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุซู} \enquote{Stay!} \\
  \end{longtable}
\item
  Otherwise, when the middle root letter of the verb of command has an \emph{a}-mark~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู} or an \emph{i}-mark~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}, then the connecting hamzah gets an \emph{i}-mark~\foreignlanguage{arabic}{โู}. Examples:

  \begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
  \toprule\noalign{}
  Verb & Verb of command for \enquote{he} \\
  \midrule\noalign{}
  \endhead
  \bottomrule\noalign{}
  \endlastfoot
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนููููู ููุนููููู ุนูููููุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุนููููู} \enquote{Work!} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ููุฐูููุจู ุฐูููุงุจูุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุฐูููุจู} \enquote{Go!} \\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูููุณู ููุฌูููุณู ุฌููููุณูุง} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุฌูููุณู} \enquote{Sit!} \\
  \end{longtable}
\end{enumerate}

Here are some examples of using the verb of command:

The verb of command is not used to issue negative commands, like \enquote{Don't go!}.
Instead, the
รธ-state verb is used with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง}
as described in
section~\ref{la-of-prohibition}
above.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุชูุฐูููุจู}\\
\enquote{Don't go!}

\subsection{The verb of command for roots begin with hamzah}\label{the-verb-of-command-for-roots-begin-with-hamzah}

Appendix~\ref{hamzarules} details the rules for speeling words that contain hamzah generally.
In addition to those rules, the verb of command for roots that begin with hamzah warrant additional discussion.

Consider the following form~1 verbs and their verbs of command
for the singular masculine addressee doer \enquote{he}:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1818}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4545}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3636}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Root
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Verb
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Verb of command
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููู ููุฃููููู ุฃูููููุง} \enquote{to hope} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑุคููููู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃุฐูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฐููู ููุฃุฐููู ุฃูุฐูููุง} \enquote{to permit} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑุฆูุฐููู} \\
\end{longtable}

Here are examples of these verbs of commands in the middle of a sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฃููููู ูฑุฆูุฐูููู ููู ูฑููููุนูุจู!}\\
\emph{yฤ สพummi -สพdอกhanฤซ li -llaษib!}\\
\enquote{O my mother, permit me to play!}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุฒูููุฏู ูฑุคููููู ูฑููุฎูููุฑู!}\\
\emph{yฤ zaydu -สพmuli -lkอกhayr!}\\
\enquote{O Zayd, hope for good!}

When these verbs of command occur in the beginning of the sentence, then there would be two hamzahs occuring next to each other which is not permitted. So the second hamzah is pronounced as a long vowel, though it may still be written as a hamzah. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุคูููู ูฑููุฎูููุฑู ููุง ุฒูููุฏู!}\\
\emph{สพลซmul}\\
not\\
\(\times\)~\emph{สพuสพmul}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุฆุฐูููู ููู ูฑููููุนูุจู ููุง ุฃููููู!}\\
\emph{สพฤซdอกhanฤซ}\\
not\\
\(\times\)~\emph{สพiสพdอกhanฤซ}

As a further complication, when the verb of command is preceded by
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{and}
or
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{so}
then the connecting \emph{hamza} is not written
and the hamzah of the first root letter is written seated on an สพalif.
Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃููููู}\\
\emph{waสพmul}\\
\enquote{And hope!}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃูุฐููู}\\
\emph{faสพdอกhan}\\
\enquote{So permit!}

\subsection{Irregular verbs of command}\label{irregular-verbs-of-command}

In addition to the rules states above there are four verbs of command (all containing hamzah) that are irregular. We will discuss them below:

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{The verbs \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู}~, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐู}~, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฑู}}{The verbs ุฃูฺฉููู~, ุฃูุฎูุฐู~, and ุฃูููุฑู}}\label{the-verbs-ux623ux643ux644-ux623ux62eux630-and-ux623ux645ux631}

The verbs of command for the following three verbs are irregular:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Root & Verb & Verb of command \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃฺฉูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉููู ููุฃูฺฉููู ุฃูฺฉูููุง} \enquote{to eat} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃุฎุฐยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฐู ููุฃูุฎูุฐู ุฃูุฎูุฐูุง} \enquote{to take} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุฐู} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฃูุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฑู ููุฃูููุฑู ุฃูููุฑูุง} \enquote{to order} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑู} \\
\end{longtable}

As you can see, the initial hamzah has been completely deleted for the verbs of command.
However, of these verbs, the verb of command for
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฑู ููุฃูููุฑู ุฃูููุฑูุง}
is permitted to retain its initial hamzah when preceded by
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{and}
or
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{so}. Then, it becomes

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃูููุฑู} \emph{waสพmur}\\
and\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃูููุฑู} \emph{faสพmur}

This retaining of the initial hamzah is not done for the other two verbs.

Here are some examples of these verbs of command:

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{The verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู}}{The verb ุณูุฃููู}}\label{the-verb-ux633ux623ux644}

The verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฃููู ููุณูุฃููู ุณูุคูุงููุง} \enquote{to question} forms its verb of command both regularly, and irregularly:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Regular: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑุณูุฃููู} \emph{สพisสพal}
\item
  Irregular: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููู} \emph{sal}
\end{enumerate}

If the verb of command is preceded by
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{and}
or
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{so}, then the regular verb of command
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑุณูุฃููู} \emph{สพisสพal} is often preferred.

Otherwise, the irregular verb of command
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููู} \emph{sal} is often preferred.

Examples of usage:

\chapter{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} and its sisters}{ุฅูููู and its sisters}}\label{ux625ux646-and-its-sisters}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-20}

In the basic subject-information sentence, both the subject and the information are in the u-state. For example:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-30-1.pdf}

\enquote{This man is a teacher.}

In the above sentence both the subject \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู} \enquote{the man}, and the information \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููู} \enquote{a teacher} are in the u-state. In this chapter we will study a family of particles, called
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} and its sisters,
that modify the subject-information sentence by placing the subject in the a-state instead of the u-state. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ููุนูููููู.}\\
\emph{สพinna -rrajula muษallimun.}\\
\enquote{Indeed the man is a teacher.}

Note how, in the above example, the subject \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู} \enquote{the man} is now in the a-state. The information \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููู} \enquote{a teacher} remains in the u-state.

The particles constituting the family of
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} and its sisters
are:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} \emph{สพanna} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃูููู} \emph{kaสพanna} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐฺฉูููู} \emph{lฤkinna} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชู} \emph{layta} \vphantom{\huge J}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููู} \emph{laษalla} \vphantom{\huge J}
\end{enumerate}

We shall now study each of these particles.

\section{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna}}{ุฅูููู สพinna}}\label{ux625ux646-einna}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna} is used to begin independent sentences. It has an emphatic meaning, as if the speaker is asserting the information about the subject. It is often translated into English as \enquote{indeed} or \enquote{verily}, but it is also often left untranslated.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna} is only used to begin subject-information sentences. Verbal sentences cannot be introduced by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna} directly. (Later, in section~\ref{damiir-al-shan}, we shall see how to overcome this restriction.). For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ูฑูุฏููููู ุนูููุฏู ูฑููููููฐูู ูฑููุฅูุณูููุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพinna -ddฤซna ษinda -llฤhi -lสพislฤmu.}\\
\enquote{Indeed, the religion in the sight of Allฤh is Islฤm.} (Qurสพฤn~3:19, trans. Saheeh International)

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna}
may be preceded by other particles like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{and}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{so}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูููู} \enquote{then}. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุทูููุจู ูฑููุนููููู ูฑููููุงููุนู. ููุฅูููู ุทูููุจู ูฑููุนููููู ูฑููููุงููุนู ุนููููู ุตูุงููุญู. ููุฅูููู ุชูุฑูฺฉููู ุบูููููุฉู.}
\enquote{Seek the useful knowledge. For indeed the seeking of the useful knowledge is a good deed. And indeed leaving it is a negligence.}

The subject of
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna}
may be a noun phrase, in which case, any describers or replacements of the subject are also in the a-state. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ููููฐุคูููุงุกู ูฑูุฑููุฌูุงูู ูฑููฺฉูุฑูุงูู ุฃูุตูุฏูููุงุฆูู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed these noble men are my friends.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna} may have multiple subjects, each in the a-state, separated by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}. Example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ูฑููููุณูููููููู ูููฑููููุณูููููุงุชู ููุนูุจูุฏูููู ูฑููููููฐูู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed the Muslim men and Muslim women worship Allฤh.}

If the information of the first subject has been mentioned before the second subject, then the second subject may optionally be in the a-state or the u-state. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ุฒูููุฏูุง ุฌูุงููุณู ููุนูููุฑูุง.}\\
or\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ุฒูููุฏูุง ุฌูุงููุณู ููุนูููุฑูู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed Zayd is sitting and ฦamr {[}as well{]}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููู ููุฐู ูฐููฺฉู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููฺฉู.}\\
or\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููู ููุฐู ูฐููฺฉู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงฺฉู ููฺฉู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed this book is for me and that book is for you.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna} may be used to begin sentences with an indefinite subject. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ููููฺฉูุง ูููู ูฑููููููุฏู ฺฉูุชูุจู ุฅูููููฐ ุฃูุญูุฏู ููุฒูุฑูุงุฆููู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed a king from India wrote to one of his ministers.}

Note that in all the above examples that
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna} is only used to begin subject-information sentences. Verbal sentences cannot be introduced by \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna} directly. (Later, in section~\ref{damiir-al-shan}, we shall see how to overcome this restriction.).
By default, the subject of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna} must directly follow it with no intervening words or particles. The only exception is when the information consists of a prepositional or adverbial phrase, it is then allowed to precede the subject. The subject, in any case, shall be in the a-state. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ููู ูฑููุจูููุชู ุฑูุฌูููุง.}\\
\enquote{Indeed, in the house, is a man.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ุชูุญูุชู ูฑูุดููุฌูุฑูุฉู ฺฉูููุฒูุง ุซููููููุง.}\\
\enquote{Indeed, under the tree, is a precious treasure.}

This reverse order is permitted even when the subject is definite. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ููุนูฺฉู ุตูุงุญูุจูฺฉู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed, with you, is \emph{your companion}.}

This puts the logical accent on the subject \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุงุญูุจูฺฉู} \enquote{your companion}. If the subject is placed first then this puts the logical accent on the information:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ุตูุงุญูุจูฺฉู ููุนูฺฉู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed your companion is \emph{with} you.}

If the subject contains a pronoun that refers to a noun in the information then the information must precede the subject. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ููู ูฑููููุตูููุนู ุนููููุงูููู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed, in the factory, are its workers.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ุฃูููุงูู ูฑูุฏููุงุฑู ุญูุงุฑูุณูููุง.}\\
\enquote{Indeed, in front of the door, is its guard.}

\subsection{Pronoun subjects}\label{pronoun-subjects}

The subject of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} may be a pronoun instead of a noun. For this the attached pronouns are used. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง ุชูููุทูุนู ุชูููฺฉู ูฑูุดููุฌูุฑูุฉู ููุฅููููููุง ุธููููููุฉู.}\\
\enquote{Don't cut that tree, for it is shady.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููฺฉูููุง ุตูุฏููููุงูู.}\\
\enquote{You\textsubscript{2} are my friends.}

The speaker pronouns, both singular and plural, may optionally keep or drop their \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}.
So for the singular speaker pronoun both \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููููู} \emph{สพinnanฤซ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููู} \emph{สพinnฤซ} may be used.
And for the plural speaker pronoun both \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููููุง} \emph{สพinnanฤ} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุง} \emph{สพinnฤ} may be used.
Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููู ููุณููููู.}\\
or\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููููู ููุณููููู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed I am a Muslim.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููููุง ฺฉูุงุชูุจูู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู.}\\
or\\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููุง ฺฉูุงุชูุจูู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed we are the writers of this book.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} with the speaker pronouns are often used with doer verbal nouns to signify that the speaker intends to to the action of the verb. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููููู ุฐูุงููุจู ุฅูููููฐ ูฑููููุณูุฌูุฏู.}\\
\enquote{I'm going to the mosque.}

We also mentioned this point in section~\ref{doer-verbal-noun-for-intended-future-action}.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} \emph{สพinna} with a strengthening \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}}{ุฅูููู สพinna with a strengthening ูู}}\label{inna-strengthening-la}

The strengthening particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} adds extra emphasis and may optionally be used between the subject of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} and its information.
If the subject occurs first (as is the default) then \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} is connected to and placed directly before the information. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ุฒูููุฏูุง ููููุงุฆููู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed Zayd is definitely standing.}

If the information precedes the subject, then then \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} is connected to and placed directly before the subject. For example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ููู ูฑููุจูููุชู ููุฑูุฌูููุง.}\\
\enquote{Indeed, in the house, is definitely a man.}

The strengthening particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} is only used with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} and not for any of its other sisters
(\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู},
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู},
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃูููู},
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐฺฉูููู},
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุชู}, and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููู}).

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Commonality of rules for \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} and its sisters}{Commonality of rules for ุฅูููู and its sisters}}\label{commonality-of-rules-for-ux625ux646-and-its-sisters}

Unless otherwise noted, the rules we have presented above for
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู},
for example, the subject being in the a-state, the order of the subject and the predicate, the use of attached pronouns for the subject, etc.,
apply also to its other sisters.

The strengthening particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}, as mentioned above, is only used with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} and not for any of its other sisters.

\section{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} \emph{สพanna}}{ุฃูููู สพanna}}\label{ux623ux646-eanna}

The particle
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} \emph{สพanna}
can be translated as \enquote{that}.
It is similar to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} in that it is asserts the information about the subject.
But
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} is different from \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} in that \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู}, its subject, and its information together constitute a complete sentence.
Whereas
the
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู}
clause
(\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู}
, its subject, and its information together) does not constitute a complete sentence. For example, consider the expression:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฒูููุฏู ุตูุงุฏููู.}\\
\enquote{Zayd is truthful.}

This is a complete sentence. But if we add
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} \enquote{that} to its beginning, it no longer remains a complete sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู ุฒูููุฏูุง ุตูุงุฏููู}\\
\enquote{that Zayd is truthful}

We need to additional words, external to the
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู}
clause
to complete the sentence. We will see examples of this below.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause in place of the direct doee}{The ุฃูููู clause in place of the direct doee}}\label{the-ux623ux646-clause-in-place-of-the-direct-doee}

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-31-1.pdf}

\enquote{I know that Zayd is truthful.}

Note how, in the example above the
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู}
clause
(\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู ุฒูููุฏูุง ุตูุงุฏููู})
has occupied the place of the direct doee of the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนููููู}.

In a similar manner,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู}
clauses can be placed where one would expect other noun positions, such as: a subject, an information, a doer, and more. Here are some examples:

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause in place of the doer}{The ุฃูููู clause in place of the doer}}\label{the-ux623ux646-clause-in-place-of-the-doer}

Example:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-32-1.pdf}

\enquote{That you are sick has reached me.} (\enquote{It has reached me that you are sick.})

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause in place of the subject}{The ุฃูููู clause in place of the subject}}\label{the-ux623ux646-clause-in-place-of-the-subject}

Example (with information before subject in sentence word order):

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-33-1.pdf}

\enquote{From his characteristeics is that he is noble.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause in place of the information}{The ุฃูููู clause in place of the information}}\label{the-ux623ux646-clause-in-place-of-the-information}

Example:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-34-1.pdf}

\enquote{The truth is that he went.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงูู}}{ุฃูููู with ฺฉูุงูู}}\label{ux623ux646-with-ux643ux627ux646}

As you know, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงูู}'s doer is also its subject, and its doee is also its information.
The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause can occur in either the subject or the information of \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉููู}.
For example, the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause as the information:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-35-1.pdf}

\enquote{The matter was that he didn't do his obligation.}

Now, the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause as the subject:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-36-1.pdf}

\enquote{That he didn't do his obligation was the matter.}

Note that in the latter case, the information precedes the subject.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause in place of an i-state noun}{The ุฃูููู clause in place of an i-state noun}}\label{the-ux623ux646-clause-in-place-of-an-i-state-noun}

The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause can occur in place of an i-state base noun in an annexation. Example:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-37-1.pdf}

\enquote{The highway robbers (literally: the cutters of the way) have increased to the degree that the journey is dangerous.}

The \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause can occur in place of an i-state noun directly following a preposition. Example:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-38-1.pdf}

\enquote{I wondered at that Zayd is asleep.}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Optionally deleting the preposition directlt before an \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause}{Optionally deleting the preposition directlt before an ุฃูููู clause}}\label{optionally-deleting-the-preposition-directlt-before-an-ux623ux646-clause}

If an
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause
directly follows a preposition, it is permissible to optionally delete the preposition as long as the meaning remains clear.
So the previous example can be expressed without the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} with the same meaning:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุฌูุจูุชู ุฃูููู ุฒูููุฏูุง ููุงุฆููู.}\\
\enquote{I wondered at that Zayd is asleep.}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฃูููู} \enquote{because}}{ููุฃูููู ``because''}}\label{ux644ux623ux646-because}

The combination of the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} \enquote{for} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} is used to mean \enquote{because}. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉูููุชู ูฑูุทููุนูุงูู ููุฃููููููู ฺฉูููุชู ุฌูุงุฆูุนูุง.}\\
\enquote{I ate the food because I was hungry.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Equivalence of the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause with a verbal noun of doing}{Equivalence of the ุฃูููู clause with a verbal noun of doing}}\label{equivalence-of-the-ux623ux646-clause-with-a-verbal-noun-of-doing}

As a matter of grammatical theory, the
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} clause, i.e.~(\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} itself, its subject, and its information) is considered equivalent to a verbal noun of doing (typically in an annexation, and possibly with a doee as well). It is this equivalalence that allows it to thake the place of a doer, direct doee, and the other categories we have given above.
For instance, consider the example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุฌูุจูุชู ูููู ุฃูููู ุฒูููุฏูุง ุฐูููุจ.}\\
\enquote{I wondered at that Zayd went.}

Here, the clause
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู ุฒูููุฏูุง ุฐูููุจ}
is equivalent to the verbal noun phrase \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุงุจู ุฒูููุฏู} \enquote{Zayd's going}. So the grammatically equivalent sentence with this verbal noun phrase is:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุนูุฌูุจูุชู ูููู ุฐูููุงุจู ุฒูููุฏู.}\\
\enquote{I wondered at Zayd's going.}

Similarly, in the example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุตูููุงุชููู ุฃูููููู ฺฉูุฑูููู.}\\
\enquote{From his characteristics is that he is generous.}

the clause
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููู ฺฉูุฑูููู}
is equivalent to the verbal noun phrase \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฑูุงููุชููู} \enquote{his generosity}. So the grammatically equivalent sentence with this verbal noun phrase is:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฑูุงููุชููู ูููู ุตูููุงุชููู.}\\
\enquote{His generosity is from his characteristics.}

This grammatical equivalence is more a matter of theory than of practical usefulness to us.
And you have seen this grammatical equivalence before with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} and a-state incomplete action verbs in chanpter~\ref{a-state-incomplete-action-verbs-verbal-noun}.

\section{\texorpdfstring{\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃูููู} \emph{kaสพanna}}{ฺฉูุฃูููู kaสพanna}}\label{ux643ux623ux646-kaeanna}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃูููู} \emph{kaสพanna}
may be translated as \enquote{{[}It is{]} as if}.
It is actually simply the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉู} \enquote{like} attached to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู}. But it is treated separately because, unlike \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู},
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃูููู} \emph{kaสพanna}, its subject, and its information constitute a complete sentence. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃูููู ูฑููุฃูููู ููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู.}\\
\enquote{{[}It is{]} as if the mother is a school.}
