\section{Topic-comment sentences and the pronoun of the fact}\label{topic-comment-sentences-and-the-pronoun-of-the-fact}

\subsection{Topic-comment sentences}\label{topic-comment-sentences}

There is a sub-type of subject-information sentence called a topic-comment sentence. Here is an example:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-39-1.pdf}

\enquote{The tree: its branches are long.}

In these kinds of sentences, the subject introduces a topic, and the information is itself a sentence which comments on the topic/subject.
We have, in fact, already seen sentences like this in section~\ref{past-verbs-order-of-words}, when we take a verbal sentence and convert it to a subject-information sentence. This is the example we discussed there:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู ฺฉูุชูุจู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง.}\\
\enquote{The man: he wrote a book.}

\subsubsection{The linker pronoun}\label{the-linker-pronoun}

A topic-comment sentence typically requires a pronoun in the comment that links back to the comment.
In the example
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุฌูุฑูุฉูููุฑููุนูููุง ุทููููููุฉู.}, the attached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} \enquote{it} in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑููุนูููุง} \enquote{its tree} is the linker pronoun that links back to the topic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุฌูุฑูุฉู} \enquote{the tree}.

Similarly, in the example
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู ฺฉูุชูุจู ฺฉูุชูุงุจูุง.}
the linker pronoun is the invisible doer pronoun \enquote{he} of the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุชูุจู} \enquote{he wrote} that links back to the topic \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌููู} \enquote{the man}.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Topic-comment sentences with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} and its sisters}{Topic-comment sentences with ุฅูููู and its sisters}}\label{topic-comment-sentences-with-ux625ux646-and-its-sisters}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} and its sisters are very often used in topic-comment sentences. (With \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} it is, as usual, an incomplete sentence.) Here are some examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู ุฒูููุฏูุง ูููู ุฃูุฎู ููุฃูุฎูุชู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed Zayd: he has a brother and sister.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูุนููููู ุฃูููู ูฑููุนููููู ุญูุตูููููู ููุชูุทููููุจู ุฌูููุฏูุง.}\\
\enquote{Know that knowledge: its obtaining requires effort.}

\subsubsection{Topic-comment sentences with a pronoun topic}\label{topic-comment-sentences-with-a-pronoun-topic}

The topic, in a topic-comment sentence, is frequently a pronoun. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง ูฑุณูููู ุฒูููุฏู.}\\
\enquote{I: my name is Zayd.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉูููุชู ูฑูุทููุนูุงูู ููููฐฺฉููููฺฉู ูููู ุชูุฃูฺฉููู.}\\
\enquote{I ate the food but you: you didn't eat.}

\subsection{The pronoun of the fact}\label{the-pronoun-of-the-fact}

Mostly, pronouns are used in place of nouns when it is already known to whom the noun refers to. So if you say:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง ูฑุณูููู ุฒูููุฏู.}\\
\enquote{I: my name is Zayd.}

the pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุง} \enquote{I} refers to the speaker, who is known.

There is a special pronoun, called the \emph{pronoun of the fact} that begins topic-comment sentences. This pronoun does not refer to any previously known entity, but rather refers to the comment that follows it. It is sometimes translated as \enquote{the fact is} but is often left untranslated. Here is an example:

\includegraphics{Learn-Standard-Arabic_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-40-1.pdf}

\enquote{The fact is: the cold is intense.}

This pronoun is usually the singular masculine pronoun (as above) but it is also sometimes the singular feminine pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู}.
It is typically used with statements of import, to which the speaker wishes to draw attention.
The comment does not contain a linker pronoun because the whole comment refers back to the topic.
The pronoun of the fact is frequently used with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} and its sisters.
Here are some examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููู ููุง ููููููุญู ูฑููฺฉูุงููุฑูููู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed, the disbelievers will not succeed.}\\
(Qurสพฤn 23:117, trans. Saheeh International)

Sometimes, one can choose between using the pronoun of the fact and a pronoun matching the participant resulting in different emphasis. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููู ูููู ูฑููููุงุนูููููู}\\
\enquote{Indeed, the fact is: they are the doers.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููููู ูููู ูฑููููุงุนูููููู}\\
\enquote{Indeed, \emph{they} are the doers.}

\section{\texorpdfstring{The lightened versions \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃููู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐฺฉููู}}{The lightened versions ุฅููู, ุฃููู, ฺฉูุฃููู, and ููููฐฺฉููู}}\label{the-lightened-versions-ux625ux646-ux623ux646-ux643ux623ux646-and-ux644ux640ux643ux646}

The particles \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃูููู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐฺฉูููู}, because of the doubled \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} are considered \emph{heavy}.
There exist \emph{lightened} versions of these particles that are:
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃููู}, and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐฺฉููู}.
These lightened versions have similar meanings to their heavy counterparts but they have somewhat different rules. We will discuss them below.
In terms of their usage
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู} and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃููู} are not very commonly used except in the Qurสพฤn, poetry, and other rhetorical texts.
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐฺฉููู}
are relatively more common.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู}}{The lightened ุฅููู}}\label{the-lightened-ux625ux646}

The lightened
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู}
is used in two different ways.
In the more common way, the subject is not put in the a-state but is rather in the u-state.
However, the strengthening \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} (see section~\ref{inna-strengthening-la} above), that was optional with the heavy \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู}, is now mandatory with the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู}. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู ุฒูููุฏู ููููุณููููู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed Zayd is a Muslim.}

The other notable difference between
the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู}
and
the heavy \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู}
is that
the heavy \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู} is only used to introduce subject-information sentences.
The lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู},
however, can be used to introduce verbal sentences, but only those that begin with the verbs:
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงูู} and its sisters,
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุงุฏู} and its sisters, and
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธูููู} and its sisters.
For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃูุชู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููุฅููู ฺฉูุงูู ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููุฌููููุฏูุง.}\\
\enquote{I read the book and indeed the book was good.}

The second, less common way, of using
the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู}
is following the same rules as the
the heavy \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููู}.
Where the subject is in the a-state and the use of the strengthening \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู} is optional. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅููู ุฒูููุฏูุง ููุณููููู.}\\
\enquote{Indeed Zayd is a Muslim.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}}{The lightened ุฃููู}}\label{lightened-an}

As we know, the heavy \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} is an emphatic particle and is frequently used with the pronoun of the fact, thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนููููู ุฃูููููู ูฑููุจูุฑูุฏู ุดูุฏููุฏู.}\\
\enquote{I know that the fact is: the cold is intense.}

When we wish not to use much emphasis, we may replace
the heavy \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} along with its following pronoun of the fact (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููู}/\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููููุง}) with
a lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}, thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนููููู ุฃููู ูฑููุจูุฑูุฏู ุดูุฏููุฏู.}\\
\enquote{I know that the cold is intense.}

Note that
the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} replaces
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููู},
which is the combination of
heavy \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} \emph{and} the pronoun of the fact \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}.
So the pronoun of the fact (\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}) does not appear with
the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}.

In the above example,
the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}
introduces a comment which is a subject-predicate sentence.
But the more common use of
the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}
is to introduce comments that are verbal sentences.

When the comment of the
lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}
is a verbal sentence, then it is preferred to separate the verb from \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} with one of the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏู}. Example:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุธูููู ุฃููู ููุฏู ุบูุฑูุจูุชู ูฑูุดููููุณู.}\\
  \enquote{I think that the sun has set.}
\item
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณู} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู}. Example:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนููููู ุฃููู ุณูููุฐูููุจู.}\\
  \enquote{I know that he will go.}
\item
  A negative particle like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง}, \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู}, or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู}.

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนููููู ุฃููู ููุง ููุฐูููุจู.}\\
  \enquote{I know that he does/will not go.}

  Note that, in writing, we have not combined the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุง} to form \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุง}, as is done for the a-state-verbal \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} (for example: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููููุง ููุฐูููุจู} \enquote{that he not go}) in chapter~\ref{chapter-a-state-incomplete-action-verbs}. This distinction in spelling is not obligatory, but some authorities recommend it. In any case, they are both pronounced the same: \emph{สพallฤ}.

  More examples:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนููููู ุฃููู ูููู ููุฐูููุจู.}\\
  \enquote{I know that he shall not go.}

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนููููู ุฃููู ูููู ููุฐูููุจู.}\\
  \enquote{I know that he did not go.}

  Note that the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู}, even when after the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}, change the state of the following incomplete-action verb to the a-state and รธ-state respectively.
\item
  The conditional particle \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู}. We will study conditional sentences in chapter~\ref{conditional-sentences}. TODO: add example.
\end{enumerate}

Rigid verbs like \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุณู} and verbs expressing supplications are exempted from needing to be separated from the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธูููููุชู ุฃููู ููููุณู ูฑููุจูุฑูุฏู ุดูุฏููุฏูุง.}\\
\enquote{I thought that the cold is not intense.}

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Distinguishing between the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} and the a-state-verbal \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}}{Distinguishing between the lightened ุฃููู and the a-state-verbal ุฃููู}}\label{distinguishing-between-the-lightened-ux623ux646-and-the-a-state-verbal-ux623ux646}

Although they are similar in meaning,
care must be taken to distinguish between this lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} and the a-state-verbal \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}
(that we learned in chapter~\ref{chapter-a-state-incomplete-action-verbs}),
The a-state-verbal \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} puts the following incomplete action verb in the a-state.
Whereas the incomplete action verb directly after the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} remains in the u-state.
The following guidelines can help to distinguish between these two \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}s:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  If the verb before \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} signifies certainty then only \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููู} and its lightened version \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} is used. For example,

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุนููููู ุฃููู ููุฏู ุฐูููุจู ููุฃููู ุณูููุฑูุฌูุนู.}\\
  \enquote{I know that he has gone and that he will return.}
\item
  If the verb before \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} signifies wanting, hoping, or expecting, then the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} puts the following verb in the a-state. For example,

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุทูููุนู ุฃููููุง ููุฐูููุจู.}\\
  \enquote{I hope that he not go.}

  Note that the verb \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฐูููุจู} is in the a-state.
\item
  If the verb before \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} reflects a view of something going to occur, and signifies neither certainty nor expectation, but rather doubt or neutrality, then either of the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}s may be used, depending on the intended meaning. Such verbs include \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธูููู ููุธูููู} \enquote{to think} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณูุจู ููุญูุณูุจู} \enquote{to deem}. For example,

  a-state-verbal \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}:\\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธูููููุชู ุฃููู ููุฑูุฌูุนู.}\\
  \enquote{I thought that he should return.}

  lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}:\\
  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุธูููููุชู ุฃููู ููุฑูุฌูุนู.}\\
  \enquote{I thought that he will return.}
\item
  If the verb before \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} does not reflect a view of something going to occur then the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} is typically the a-state-verbal \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}. For example,

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฑููููู ุฃููู ุชูููุฌูุญู}\\
  \enquote{That you succeed {[}will have{]} gladdened me.}

  Remember from chapter~\ref{chapter-a-state-incomplete-action-verbs}), that the a-state-verbal \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} can occur with completed-action verbs as well. Example:

  \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฑููููู ุฃููู ููุฌูุญูุชู}\\
  \enquote{That you have succeeded {[}has{]} gladdened me.}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃููู}}{The lightened ฺฉูุฃููู}}\label{the-lightened-ux643ux623ux646}

The lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃููู} is similar to the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู} in that it introduces a topic-comment sentence and the topic is usually a deleted pronoun of the fact. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃููู ูฑููุจูุฑูุฏู ุฐูููุจู.}\\
\enquote{{[}It is{]} as if the cold has gone.}

Also similar to the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃููู}, the lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃููู} may introduce a verbal sentence but it must be separated from \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุฃููู} by either \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏู} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู}. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ฺฉูุฃููู ูููู ููุณูููุนู.}\\
\enquote{He went as if he did not hear.}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐฺฉููู}}{The lightened ููููฐฺฉููู}}\label{the-lightened-ux644ux640ux643ux646}

The lightened \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐฺฉููู} has the same meaning as the heavy \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐฺฉูููู} but it has no grammatical effect on the word or sentence after it. It may introduce either subject-information or verbal sentences. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฌูุญู ุฒูููุฏู ููููฐฺฉููู ุตูุฏูููููู ูููู ููููุฌูุญู.}\\
\enquote{Zayd succeeded but his friend did not succeed.}

\chapter{Nouns of superiority}\label{nouns-of-superiority}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-21}

Consider the sentence:

\enquote{The book is heavier than the pen.}

In this sentence a relationship of superiority is established between the two nouns: \enquote{the book} and \enquote{the pencil}. The book is being described as being superior in heaviness.

By the way, we are using the \enquote{superiority} in a technical sense. For example, we can say \enquote{The donkey is weaker than the horse.} Here the donkey is being described as superior in weakness.

In order to express a superiority relationship between nouns, for example, , Arabic uses qualitative nouns with a distinct form. Here is a table of some common qualitative nouns and their corresponding nouns of superiority.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Root
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Qualitative noun
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Noun of superiority
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซฺฉุจุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุจููุฑู} \emph{kabฤซrun} \enquote{big} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉูุจูุฑู} \emph{สพakbaru} \enquote{biger} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุตุบุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุบููุฑู} \emph{แนฃagอกhฤซrun} \enquote{small} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุตูุบูุฑู} \emph{สพaแนฃgอกharu} \enquote{smaller} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุญุณูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณููู} \emph{แธฅasanun} \enquote{good} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูุณููู} \emph{สพaแธฅsanu} \enquote{better} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุณูุกยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููุฆู} \emph{sayyiสพun} \enquote{bad} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูููุฃู} \emph{สพaswaสพu} \enquote{worse} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุฏูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฏูููู} \emph{qadฤซmun} \enquote{old} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฏููู} \emph{สพaqdamu} \enquote{older} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฌุฏยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุฏููุฏู} \emph{jadฤซdun} \enquote{new} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฌูุฏูู} \emph{สพajaddu} \enquote{newer} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุณููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููู} \emph{sahlun} \enquote{easy} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณููููู} \emph{สพasยทhalu} \enquote{easier} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุตุนุจยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุตูุนูุจู} \emph{แนฃaษbun} \enquote{difficult} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุตูุนูุจู} \emph{สพaแนฃษabu} \enquote{more difficult} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุทููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุทูููููู} \emph{แนญawฤซlun} \enquote{long} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุทููููู} \emph{สพaแนญwalu} \enquote{longer} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุตุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุตููุฑู} \emph{qaแนฃฤซrun} \enquote{short} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุตูุฑู} \emph{สพaqแนฃaru} \enquote{shorter} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุซููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุซูููููู} \emph{tอกhaqฤซlun} \enquote{heavy} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุซููููู} \emph{สพatอกhqalu} \enquote{heavier} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฎูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูููููู} \emph{kอกhafฤซfun} \enquote{light} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูููู} \emph{สพakอกhaffu} \enquote{lighter} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุณุนยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุงุณูุนู} \emph{wฤsiษun} \enquote{wide} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุณูุนู} \emph{สพaswaษu} \enquote{wider} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุถููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูููููู} \emph{แธayyiqun} \enquote{narrow} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุถููููู} \emph{สพaแธyaqu} \enquote{narrower} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุณุฑุนยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณูุฑููุนู} \emph{sarฤซษun} \enquote{fast} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูุฑูุนู} \emph{สพasraษu} \enquote{faster} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุจุทุกยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุจูุทููุกู} \emph{baแนญฤซสพun} \enquote{slow} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุจูุทูุฃู} \emph{สพabtaสพu} \enquote{slower} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููููู} \emph{qawiyyun} \enquote{strong} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููููฐ} \emph{สพaqwฤ} \enquote{stronger} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุถุนูยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุถูุนูููู} \emph{แธaษฤซfun} \enquote{weak} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุถูุนููู} \emph{สพaแธษafu} \enquote{weaker} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซฺฉุซุฑยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ฺฉูุซููุฑู} \emph{katอกhฤซrun} \enquote{many} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูฺฉูุซูุฑู} \emph{สพaktอกharu} \enquote{more} \\
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซููยป} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููููู} \emph{qalฤซlun} \enquote{few/less} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููู} \emph{สพaqallu} \enquote{fewer/lesser} \\
\end{longtable}

Note the following points regarding the form of the noun of superiority:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Nouns of superiority are regularly of the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} \emph{สพafษalu} using the template root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูุนูยป}.
\item
  Nouns of superiority are non-fully changing nouns, so they won't have \emph{n} marks and the indefinite noun in the i-state will have an \emph{a}-mark on the last letter.
\item
  If a root's last two letters are the same, it is shown as a two-letter root and the noun of superiority is formed by doubling the last letter. Example: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุฌุฏยป}: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฌูุฏูู} \emph{สพajaddu} \enquote{newer}.
\item
  If a root's last letter is \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู} or \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}, then the noun of superiority's last letter will be \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููฐ}. Example: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซูููยป}: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููููููฐ} \emph{สพaqwฤ} \enquote{stronger}.
\end{itemize}

You may remember that the pattern of the identical is identical to the pattern of colors and physical characteristics. For example \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูููุฑู} \emph{สพaแธฅmaru} \enquote{red}. However, this similarity is largely superficial. We will see that nouns of superiority are feminized differently and sometimes not at all.

\section{Comparing two nouns}\label{comparing-two-nouns}

Nouns of superiority can be used to compare a qualitative quality between two nouns. Here is an example sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุบูููุงูู ุฃูุทููููู ูููู ูฑููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู.}\\
\emph{สพalgอกhulฤmu สพaแนญwalu mina -ljฤriyati.}\\
\enquote{The boy is taller than the girl.}

Here you can see that the preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{min} is used to mean \enquote{than}.

If we wish to say: \enquote{The girl is taller than the boy.}, we will use the same \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุทูููู} \emph{สพaแนญwalu} even though the subject \enquote{the girl} is now feminine:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููุฌูุงุฑูููุฉู ุฃูุทููููู ูููู ูฑููุบูููุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพaljฤriyati สพaแนญwalu mina -lgอกhulฤmu.}\\
\enquote{The girl is taller than the boy.}

Similarly, if the subject noun to be compared is a plural, whether masculine or feminine, rational or non-rational, the same noun of superiority is used. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุฑููุฌูุงูู ุฃูุทููููู ูููู ูฑููููุณูุงุกู ููููููู ุฃูููุตูุฑู ูููููููู.}\\
\emph{สพarrijฤlu สพatwalu mina -nnisฤสพi wa hunna สพaqแนฃaru minhum.}\\
\enquote{The men are taller than the women and they\textsubscript{fem.} are shorter than them\textsubscript{masc.}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููฺฉูุชูุจู ุฃูุซููููู ูููู ูฑููุฃูููููุงูู.}\\
\emph{สพalkutubu สพatอกhqalu mina -lสพaqlฤmi.}\\
\enquote{The books are heavier than the pens.}

\subsection{Nouns of superiority without a second noun}\label{nouns-of-superiority-without-a-second-noun}

The above example compared one noun to another. Often, the second noun need not be mentioned. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููฺฉูุชูุจู ุฃูุซููููู.}\\
\emph{สพalkutubu สพatอกhqalu.}\\
\enquote{The books are heavier.}

\section{Conveying the meaning of the highest degree}\label{conveying-the-meaning-of-the-highest-degree}

The same nouns of superiority are also used in Arabic to convey the meaning of the highest degree of a quality, like \enquote{the biggest house}, \enquote{the weakest link}, \enquote{the best book}, etc. This can be done in a number of ways.

\subsection{With indefinite noun-chains}\label{with-indefinite-noun-chains}

The most common way to express this in Arabic is using a noun-chain with the noun of superiority and an indefinite noun. Here is an example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุฃูุณูุฑูุนู ุบูููุงูู ููู ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู.}\\
\emph{huwa สพasraษu gอกhulฤmin fi -lmadrasati.}\\
\enquote{He is the fastest boy in the school.}

An important point to note is that while in English we used the definite in the translation: \enquote{the fastest boy}, in Arabic the noun-phrase \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูุฑูุนู ุบูููุงูู} \emph{สพasraษu gอกhulฤmin} is technically indefinite. It is just hard to find a suitable translation in English where the noun-phrase could be indefinite.

The same noun of superiority is used with feminine and dual/plural nouns. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุฃูุทููููู ูฑููุฑูุฃูุฉู.}\\
\emph{hiya สพaแนญwalu -mraสพatin.}\\
\enquote{She is the tallest woman.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง ุฃูุทููููู ุฑูุฌููููููู.}\\
\emph{humฤ สพaแนญwalu rajulayni.}\\
\enquote{They are tallest (two) men.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู ุฃูุทููููู ููุณูุงุกู.}\\
\emph{hunna สพaแนญwalu nisฤสพin.}\\
\enquote{They are the tallest women.}

\subsection{With definite noun-chains}\label{with-definite-noun-chains}

The noun of superiority can also be used in definite noun-chains with a slightly different meaning. However, the second noun of the noun-chain will need to be in the plural. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุฃูุทููููู ูฑูุฑููุฌูุงูู.}\\
\emph{huwa สพaแนญwalu -rrijฤli.}\\
\enquote{He is the tallest of the men.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง ุฃูุทููููู ูฑููููุณูุงุกู}\\
\emph{humฤ สพaแนญwalu -nnisฤสพi.}\\
\enquote{They (two) are the tallest of the women.}

\section{Feminine, dual, and plural forms}\label{feminine-dual-and-plural-forms}

So far we have used only one form of the noun of superiority: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} \emph{สพafษalu}. Technically, this is the masculine singular form, although it can be used for feminine, dual, and plural nouns as we have seen above.

However, when the meaning of the highest degree is to be conveyed for definite nouns without using noun-chains, then we will use new feminine, dual, and plurals forms for the noun of superiority. We will give these forms below:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Number
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Masc.
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Fem.
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
sing. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} \emph{สพafษalu} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููฐ} \emph{fuษlฤ} \\
dual & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูููุงูู} \emph{สพafษalฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููุงูู} \emph{fuษlayฤni} \\
sound plur. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนูููููู} \emph{สพafษalลซna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนูููููุงุชู} \emph{fuษlayฤtun} \\
broken plur. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุงุนููู} \emph{สพafฤษilu} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{fuษalun} \\
\end{longtable}

These forms are to be used when the noun of superiority is usually definite and either:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\roman{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  by itself, or
\item
  a describer.
\end{enumerate}

We will give some examples below:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ูฑูุฑููุฌููู ูฑููุฃูุทููููู.}\\
\emph{huwa -rrajulu -lสพaแนญwalu.}\\
\enquote{He is the tallest man.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ูฑููููุฑูุฃูุฉู ูฑูุทูููููููฐ.}\\
\emph{hiya -lmarสพatu -แนญแนญลซlฤ.}\\
\enquote{She is the tallest woman.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง ูฑูุฑููุฌูููุงูู ูฑููุฃูุทูููููุงูู.}\\
\emph{huma -rrajulฤni -lสพaแนญwalฤni.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{masc.~dual} are the two tallest men.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง ูฑููููุฑูุฃูุชูุงูู ูฑูุทูููููููุงูู.}\\
\emph{huma -lmarสพatฤni -แนญแนญลซlayฤni.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{fem.~dual} are the two tallest women.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุคูููุงุกู ูููู ูฑูุฑููุฌูุงูู ูฑููุฃูุทูููููููู ููุฃููููููฐุฆูฺฉู ูููู ูฑูุฃูููุงุตูุฑู.}\\
\emph{hฤสพulฤสพi humu -rrijฤlu -lสพaแนญwalลซna waสพulฤสพika humu -lสพaqฤแนฃiru.}\\
\enquote{These are the tallest men and those are the shortest {[}men{]}.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุคูููุงุกู ููููู ูฑููููุณูุงุกู ูฑูุทูููููููุงุชู ููุฃููููููฐุฆูฺฉู ููููู ูฑูููุตูุฑู.}\\
\emph{hฤสพulฤสพi hunna -nnisฤสพu -แนญแนญลซlayฤtu waสพulฤสพika hunna -lquแนฃaru.}\\
\enquote{These are the tallest women and those are the shortest {[}women{]}.}

\subsection{Plural forms with non-rational beings}\label{plural-forms-with-non-rational-beings}

If a noun of superiority is to be used with a definite plural noun for (masculine or feminine) non-rational beings, either by itself or as a describer, then it will usually be the feminine singular form. This is consistent with what we have learned so far regarding the use of feminine singular qualitative nouns and pronouns for non-rational beings. Here is an example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑูููฺฉูุชูุจู ูฑููฺฉูุจููุฑูุฉู ูููู ูฑูุซููููููููฐ.}\\
\emph{สพalkutubu -lkabฤซratu hiya -tอกhtอกhuqlฤ.}\\
\enquote{The big books are the heaviest.}

Sometimes, however, if the plural noun is not mentioned in a sentence we can use the broken plural of the feminine noun of superiority to convey the meaning of plurality. For example,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุณูููุชู ูฑููุฃูููููุงูู. ููููฐุคูููุงุกู ููููู ูฑูุทูููููู ููุฃููููููฐุฆูฺฉู ููููู ูฑูููุตูุฑู.}\\
\emph{qasamtu -lสพaqlฤma. hฤสพulฤสพi hunna -แนญแนญuwalu waสพulฤสพika hunna -lquแนฃaru.}\\
\enquote{I divided the pens. These are the tallest and those are the shortest.}

\subsection{Dual and plural forms in definite noun-chains}\label{dual-and-plural-forms-in-definite-noun-chains}

In section X above we learned that that definite noun-chains use the form \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} \emph{สพafษalu}. We gave the following examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุฃูุทููููู ูฑูุฑููุฌูุงูู.}\\
\emph{huwa สพaแนญwalu -rrijฤli.}\\
\enquote{He is the tallest of the men.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง ุฃูุทููููู ูฑููููุณูุงุกู}\\
\emph{humฤ สพaแนญwalu -nnisฤสพi.}\\
\enquote{They (two) are the tallest of the women.}

We now modify this rule to state that dual and plural forms of the noun of superiority can be used as well, especially when no other indication of number is present.

For example, in the sentence,

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุฃูุทููููู ูฑูุฑููุฌูุงูู.}\\
\emph{hum สพaแนญwalu -rrijฤli.}\\
\enquote{They\textsubscript{masc.~plur.} are the tallest of the men.}

the pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} tells us that we are talking about multiple persons who are the tallest of the men. But if we have a sentence like:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ุฃูุทููููู ูฑูุฑููุฌูุงูู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba สพaแนญwalu -rrijฤli.}\\
\enquote{The tallest of the men went.}

Here we cannot say that one man had gone or more than one. To remove this ambiguity we can use the plural form \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุทูุงูููู} \emph{สพaแนญฤwilu} thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ุฃูุทูุงูููู ูฑูุฑููุฌูุงูู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba สพaแนญฤwilu -rrijฤli.}\\
\enquote{The tallest\textsubscript{plur.} of the men went.}

\section{Comparing a noun with itself}\label{comparing-a-noun-with-itself}

A noun can be compared with itself in a different respect. For example, we can say:

\enquote{The tree is closer to Zayd than it is to Muแธฅammad.}

Here the tree is being compared with itself with respect to its position near Zayd and its position near Muแธฅammad. We will use the appropriate attached pronoun for the object being compared and attach it tp the preposition of comparison \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{min} \enquote{than}. So the above sentence can be expressed as:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุฌูุฑูุฉู ุฃูููุฑูุจู ุฅูููููฐ ุฒูููุฏู ููููููุง ุฅูููููฐ ููุญููููุฏู.}\\
\emph{สพasอกhsอกhajaratu สพaqrabu สพilฤ zaydin minhฤ สพilฤ muแธฅammadin.}

The attached pronoun \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููุง} \emph{-hฤ} refers to \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููุดููุฌูุฑูุฉู} \emph{สพasอกhsอกhajaratu} \enquote{the tree}.
The preposition \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฅูููููฐ} \emph{สพilฤ} is used with the noun of superiority \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุฑูุจู} \emph{สพaqrabu} to express \enquote{nearer to}.

\section{Attention to the definiteness and plurality of noun-chains}\label{attention-to-the-definiteness-and-plurality-of-noun-chains}

We have seen that if a noun of superiority is used in an indefinite noun-chain, it conveys the idea of the highest degree, and the singularity or plurality of second noun in the noun-chain conveys the number of object whose superiority is being expressed. The examples we gave were:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู ุฃูุทููููู ูฑููุฑูุฃูุฉู.}\\
\emph{hiya สพaแนญwalu -mraสพatin.}\\
\enquote{She is the tallest woman.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููุง ุฃูุทููููู ุฑูุฌููููููู.}\\
\emph{humฤ สพaแนญwalu rajulayni.}\\
\enquote{They are tallest (two) men.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููู ุฃูุทููููู ููุณูุงุกู.}\\
\emph{hunna สพaแนญwalu nisฤสพin.}\\
\enquote{They are the tallest women.}

Here we would like to stress that second-noun of the noun chain must be indefinite. So, for example, we can have a sentence:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ุฃูฺฉูุจูุฑู ุจูููุชู.}\\
\emph{hฤdอกhฤ สพakbaru baytin.}\\
\enquote{This is the biggest house.}

If we would like to express \enquote{This is the biggest house of the city} then we cannot simply extend the noun-chain by adding \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู} \emph{-lmadฤซnati} \enquote{of the city} to it thus:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ุฃูฺฉูุจูุฑู ุจูููุชู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู.}\\
\emph{hฤdอกhฤ สพakbaru bayti -lmadฤซnati.}

This is because the noun-chain is now definite. This sentence can now only mean \enquote{This is the biggest {[}part{]} of the house of the city.}

In order to express the desired meaning, we have a few options with similar meanings:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ุจูููุชู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู ูฑููุฃูฺฉูุจูุฑู.}\\
\emph{hฤdอกhฤ baytu -lmadฤซnati -lสพakbaru.}
\enquote{This is the biggest house of the city.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ุฃูฺฉูุจูุฑู ุจูููุชู ููู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู.}\\
\emph{hฤdอกhฤ สพakbaru baytin fi -lmadฤซnati.}\\
\enquote{This is the biggest house in the city.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ุฃูฺฉูุจูุฑู ุจููููุชู ูฑููููุฏููููุฉู.}\\
\emph{hฤdอกhฤ สพakbaru buyลซti -lmadฤซnati.}\\
\enquote{This is the biggest of the houses of the city.}

\section{\texorpdfstring{Expressing \enquote{better than} and \enquote{worse than}}{Expressing ``better than'' and ``worse than''}}\label{expressing-better-than-and-worse-than}

To express the meaning \enquote{better} Arabic can use \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุญูุณููู} \emph{สพaแธฅsanu} from \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุญูุณููู} \emph{แธฅasanun}. There is also the word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุถููู} \emph{สพafแธalu} is very commonly used. Technically it means \enquote{more preferred} but it is often used where in English we would say \enquote{better}.

Similarly, to express worse we can use \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุณูููุฃู} \emph{สพaswaสพu} from \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุณููููุฆู} \emph{sayyiสพun}.

In addition, there are two words: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูููุฑู} \emph{kอกhayrun} and \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุดูุฑูู} \emph{sอกharrun}, which are really designative nouns meaning \enquote{goodness} and \enquote{evil} respectively.

These same words, although they not in the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} \emph{สพafษalu}, are used with \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูููู} \emph{min} \enquote{than} to express \enquote{better} and \enquote{worse} respectively. Here are some examples:

\section{\texorpdfstring{The word \enquote{other}}{The word ``other''}}\label{the-word-other}

The word \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุขุฎูุฑู} \emph{สพฤkอกharu} is a qualitative noun meaning \enquote{other}. It is actually on the pattern of the noun of superiority \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุนููู} \emph{สพafษalu} with the root \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ยซุกุฎุฑยป} but is somewhat of an anomaly because it does not have a meaning of superiority and is not used for comparison. That is to say: we cannot say that something is more \enquote{other} than something else. It shares some of the qualities of the noun of superiority in the formation of its feminine and plurals. We will describe these and their usages below.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Number
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Masc.
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Fem.
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
sing. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุขุฎูุฑ} \emph{สพฤkอกharu} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฑูููฐ} \emph{สพukอกhrฤ} \\
dual & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุขุฎูุฑูุงูู} \emph{สพฤkอกharฤni} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฑูููุงูู} \emph{สพukอกhrayฤni} \\
sound plur. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุขุฎูุฑูููู} \emph{สพฤkอกharลซna} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฑูููุงุชู} \emph{สพukอกhrayฤtun} \\
broken plur. & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุงุฎูุฑู} \emph{สพawฤkอกhiru} & \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฑู} \emph{สพukอกharu} \\
\end{longtable}

Note that the masculine broken plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุงุฎูุฑู} \emph{สพawฤkอกhiru} (on the pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูููุงุนููู} \emph{สพafฤษilu}) has replaced the \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก} in the root with a \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ู}. This is a regular replacement in order to avoid two \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุก}s next to one another in \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุกูุงุฎูุฑู} \emph{สพaสพฤkอกhiru}. This broken plural is given here for completeness but it is actually very rarely used. The sound \emph{ลซn} plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุขุฎูุฑูููู} \emph{สพฤkอกharลซna} is used instead.

Also note that the feminine broken plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฑู} \emph{สพukอกharu} is non-fully changing. This is irregular because the broken plural pattern \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุนููู} \emph{fuษalun} is usually fully-changing.

We use \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุขุฎูุฑู} \emph{สพฤkอกharu} just like any other qualitative noun and we will give some examples below.

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฌูุงุกู ุฒูููุฏู ููุฑูุฌููู ุขุฎูุฑู.}\\
\emph{jฤสพa zaydun warajulun สพฤkอกharu.}\\
\enquote{Zayd and another man came.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฒูููููุจู ุฅูููู ูฑููููุฏูุฑูุณูุฉู ูฑููุฃูุฎูุฑูููฐ.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabat zaynabu สพila -lmadrasati -lสพukอกhrฤ.}\\
\enquote{Zaynab went to the other school.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃูุชู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููฺฉูุชูุงุจููููู ุขุฎูุฑููููู.}\\
\emph{qaraสพtu hฤdอกha -lkitฤba wakitฤbayni สพฤkอกharayni.}\\
\enquote{I read this book and two other books.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจู ุฑูุฌูุงูู ุขุฎูุฑูููู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahaba rijฤlun สพฤkอกharลซna}\\
\enquote{Other men went.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฐูููุจูุชู ุฒูููููุจู ููุนู ูฑููููุณูุงุกู ูฑููุฃูุฎูุฑูููุงุชู.}\\
\emph{dอกhahabat zaynabu maษa -nnisฤสพi -lสพukอกhrayฤti.}\\
\enquote{Zaynab went with the other women.}

With non-rational nouns, just like other qualitative nouns, the feminine singular is usually used. Example:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃูุชู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููฺฉูุชูุจูุง ุฃูุฎูุฑูููฐ.}\\
\emph{qaraสพtu hฤdอกha -lkitฤba wakutuban สพukอกhrฤ.}\\
\enquote{I read this book and other books.}

However, the feminine broken plural \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฃูุฎูุฑู} \emph{สพukอกharu} can also be used, especially if there is no other indication of plurality. Examples:

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ุฎูููููู ูููฑููุฃูุฎูุฑู ุซููููููุฉู.}\\
\emph{hฤdอกha -lkitฤbu kอกhafฤซfun wa-lสพukอกharu tอกhaqฤซlatun.}\\
\enquote{This book is light and the others are heavy.}

\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ููุฑูุฃู ููููฐุฐูุง ูฑููฺฉูุชูุงุจู ููููุฑูุฃู ุฃูุฎูุฑู.}\\
\emph{qaraสพa -lkitฤba waqaraสพa สพukอกhara.}\\
\enquote{He read this book and and he read others.}

\appendix


\chapter{Rules for writing hamzah}\label{hamzarules}

\section{Seats of hamzah}\label{seats-of-hamzah}

Hamzah is written in four different ways:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Seated on an สพalif: {\tradarab{ุฃ}} or {\tradarab{ุฅ}}
\item
  Seated on an wฤw: {\tradarab{ุค}}
\item
  Seated on an yฤสพ: {\tradarab{ุฆ}}
\item
  Unseated: {\tradarab{ุก}}
\end{enumerate}

Here are some of notes about writing hamzah in the above four methods:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  When unseated hamzah comes between two letters that are joined, then it is written above the line that joins them, for example: {\tradarab{ุฎูุทููุกูุฉ}} \emph{kอกhaแนญฤซสพah}. In this word, the yฤสพ {\tradarab{ู}} joins to the \emph{tฤสพ marbลซแนญah} {\tradarab{ุฉ}}.

  As a special case, when unseated hamza comes between joined lฤm and สพalif ({\tradarab{ูุง}}), then it is positioned between them thus: {\tradarab{ูุกุง}}. (In most cases, this is replaced with {\tradarab{ูุข}} as we will explain in the next point below.) And this is different from hamzah on the สพalif following the lฤm: {\tradarab{ูุฃ}}.
\item
  When unseated hamzah is followed by an สพalif: {\tradarab{ุกุง}}, the combination of hamzah and สพalif is usually written as {\tradarab{ุข}} as a convention. Examples: {\tradarab{ุขูููู}} \emph{สพฤmana}, {\tradarab{ุธูููุขู}} \emph{dฬฃอกhamสพฤn}, {\tradarab{ุดูููุขู}} \emph{sอกhanaสพฤn}. However, when the สพalif is a suffix or part of a suffix, or the hamzah is doubled, or there is an สพalif before the hamzah then we will write {\tradarab{ุกุง}}, not {\tradarab{ุข}}. Examples: {\tradarab{ุดูููุกูุงูู}} \emph{sอกhayสพฤni}, {\tradarab{ุณูุกููุงู}} \emph{saสพสพฤl}, {\tradarab{ููุฑูุงุกูุงุช}} \emph{qirฤสพฤt}.
\item
  When hamzah is seated on สพalif, if it has an \emph{i}-mark, it is written below the สพalif: {\tradarab{ุฅู}}. Otherwise, it is written above the สพalif: {\tradarab{ุฃู}}, {\tradarab{ุฃู}}, {\tradarab{ุฃู}}.
\item
  When hamzah is seated on yฤสพ {\tradarab{ุฆ}} the dots of the yฤสพ are no longer written. Here's how it will appear in different positions:

  \begin{longtable}[]{@{}llll@{}}
  \toprule\noalign{}
  Isolated & End & Middle & Beginnning \\
  \midrule\noalign{}
  \endhead
  \bottomrule\noalign{}
  \endlastfoot
  {\tradarab{ุฆ}} & {\tradarab{ูุฆ}} & {\tradarab{ูุฆู}} & {\tradarab{ุฆู}} \\
  \end{longtable}

  Note that hamzah is seated on yฤสพ in the middle position {\tradarab{ูุฆู}} is different from unseated hamzah between two joining letters {\tradarab{ูุกู}}.
\end{itemize}

So how do we know when to write hamzah unseated and when seated? And how do we choose between its three different seats? There are a set of rules that we need to follow in order to correctly write hamzah.

\section{Rules for determining the seat of hamzah}\label{rules-for-determining-the-seat-of-hamzah}

\subsection{Without prefixes and suffixes}\label{without-prefixes-and-suffixes}

We will first learn how to determine the seat of hamzah for a word
without any prefix or suffix.

Hamzah can occur in three positions in a word:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  At the beginning of the word
\item
  In the middle of the word
\item
  At the end of the word
\end{enumerate}

We will treat each of these positions below.

\subsubsection{At the beginning of the word}\label{at-the-beginning-of-the-word}

When hamzah occurs in the beginning of a word, then:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\alph{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  If the hamzah carries a long-\emph{ฤ} vowel, it is written unseated followed by an สพalif and written as {\tradarab{ุข}}, for example {\tradarab{ุขูููู}} \emph{สพฤmana}.
\item
  If the hamzah carries any other vowel, it is written seated on an สพalif, and is marked with the appropriated vowel mark, for example {\tradarab{ุฃูุณููููู}} \emph{สพaslama}, {\tradarab{ุฃูุฑููุฏู}} \emph{สพurฤซdu}, {\tradarab{ุฅูุณูููุงู}} \emph{สพislฤm}, {\tradarab{ุฅููููุงู}} \emph{สพฤซmฤn}, {\tradarab{ุฃููุฎูุฐู}} \emph{สพลซkอกhidอกha}.
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection{In the middle of the word}\label{in-the-middle-of-the-word}

The most general case is when hamzah is in the middle of a word.

Arabic has three short vowels, three long vowels, two semi-vowels, and a zero-vowel indicated by a รธ-mark {\tradarab{โู}}. Each of these has an order of precedence and a hamzah seat, that we have shown in the table below:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Precedence & Vowel & Seated hamzah \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
1. & \emph{ฤซ}/\emph{ay} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
2. & \emph{i} & {\tradarab{ุฆ}} \\
3. & \emph{ลซ}/\emph{aw} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
4. & \emph{u} & {\tradarab{ุค}} \\
5. & \emph{ฤ} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
6. & \emph{a} & {\tradarab{ุฃ}} \\
7. & {\tradarab{โู}} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
\end{longtable}

\textbf{Main rule:} Disregard any doubling mark {\tradarab{โู}}. Consider the vowel on the consonant before the hamzah and the \emph{shortened} vowel on the hamzah itself. Determine which of the two vowels wins by being higher in precedence in the above table. The winning vowel's seat will be the seat of the hamzah.

\textbf{Sub-rule:} If the main rule determines that hamzah is to be seated on สพalif, and there is a long \emph{ฤ} vowel on the hamzah using an สพalif, then hamzah shall be unseated. And the combination of {\tradarab{ุกูุง}} will usually be written as {\tradarab{ุข}}.

Examples:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3158}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1053}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1053}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1053}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3684}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Word
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Vowel on consonant before hamzah
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Shortened vowel on hamzah
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Winning vowel
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Seated hamzah
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููููุกูุฉ}} \emph{hayสพah} & \emph{ay} & \emph{a} & \emph{ay} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุฎูุทููุกูุฉ}} \emph{kอกhaแนญฤซสพah} & \emph{ฤซ} & \emph{a} & \emph{ฤซ} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุงุณูุชููุกูุงุณ}} \emph{สพistฤซสพฤs} & \emph{ฤซ} & \emph{a} & \emph{ฤซ} & {\tradarab{ุก}} (Exception: {\tradarab{ุกูุง}} is not written as {\tradarab{ุข}} when the preceding vowel is \emph{ฤซ}.) \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุชูููุกูู}} \emph{tawสพam} & \emph{aw} & \emph{a} & \emph{aw} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุณูุงุฆูู}} \emph{sฤสพil} & \emph{ฤ} & \emph{i} & \emph{i} & {\tradarab{ุฆ}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุชูุณูุงุคูู}} \emph{tasฤสพul} & \emph{ฤ} & \emph{u} & \emph{u} & {\tradarab{ุค}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุชูุณูุงุกููู}} \emph{tasฤสพala} & \emph{ฤ} & \emph{a} & \emph{ฤ} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููุฑูุงุกูุงุช}} \emph{qirฤสพฤt} & \emph{ฤ} & \emph{a} & \emph{ฤ} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ูููุขูู}} \emph{nลซสพฤnun} & \emph{ลซ} & \emph{a} & \emph{ลซ} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููุณูุคููู}} \emph{masสพลซl} & {\tradarab{โู}} & \emph{u} & \emph{u} & {\tradarab{ุค}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุชูุฑูุฆููุณ}} \emph{tarสพฤซs} & {\tradarab{โู}} & \emph{i} & \emph{i} & {\tradarab{ุฆ}} \\

\subsubsection{At the end of the word}\label{at-the-end-of-the-word}

When hamzah occurs at the end of a word, disregard the vowel on hamzah itself, and consider only the vowel on preceding consonant.
Plug it into the precedence table as above to determine the seat of hamzah.

\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุจูุทูุคู}} \emph{baแนญuสพa} & \emph{u} & {\tradarab{ุค}} \\

\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููุจูุชูุฏูุฅู}} \emph{mubtadaสพi} & \emph{a} & {\tradarab{ุฅ}} \\
\end{longtable}

The exception to this rule is when the previous letter is a doubled wฤw with an \emph{u}-mark.
In this case the hamzah will again be unseated. Example {\tradarab{ุชูุจููููุกู}} \emph{tabawwuสพu}.

Note also that {\tradarab{ููุจูุชูุฏูุฅู}} \emph{mubtadaสพi} can be written with the hamzah below the สพalif because of the \emph{i}-mark on the hamzah.
But it is also common to write it as {\tradarab{ููุจูุชูุฏูุฃ}} \emph{mubtadaสพ}, especially when the hamzah is unvoweled.

\subsection{With prefixes and suffixes}\label{with-prefixes-and-suffixes}

\subsubsection{Prefixes}\label{prefixes}

If hamzah is in the beginning of a word, adding a prefix to the word will not alter the writing of the hamzah.
Hamzah will continue to be seated on an สพalif.
Here are some examples of words with beginning hamzahs and prefixes.

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} \\

\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุฃูุณูุชูุงุฐู}} & {\tradarab{ูู}} & {\tradarab{ููุฃูุณูุชูุงุฐู}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุขุฎูุฑูุฉ}} & {\tradarab{ุงูู}} & {\tradarab{ุงููุขุฎูุฑูุฉ}} \\

\subsubsection{Suffixes}\label{suffixes}

If hamzah is at the end of a word, adding a suffix to the word can, in general, alter the writing of the hamzah.
Hamzah is now, generally, treated as if it is in the middle of the word, and the rules for hamzah in the middle of a word apply.
Examples:

\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุณููุกูุงูู}} \emph{sลซสพฤni} & \emph{ลซ} & \emph{a} & \emph{ลซ} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุถูููุกููู}} \emph{แธawสพahu} & \emph{aw} & \emph{a} & \emph{aw} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุถูููุกูุงูู}} \emph{แธawสพฤni} & \emph{aw} & \emph{a} & \emph{aw} & {\tradarab{ุก}} \\
\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููุชููฺฉูุฆูููู}} \emph{muttakiสพฤซna} & \emph{i} & \emph{i} & \emph{i} & {\tradarab{ุฆ}} \\

\vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููุจูุฑููุคูููู}} \emph{yubarraสพลซna} & \emph{a} & \emph{u} & \emph{u} & {\tradarab{ุค}} \\
\end{longtable}

There are some exceptions:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  If the letter before the hamzah has a รธ-mark and is not wฤw or yฤสพ,
  then the hamzah will be written unseated. Examples:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุฌูุฒูุกูุงูู}} \emph{juzสพฤni}\\
  \item
    \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุนูุจูุกูุงูู}} \emph{ษibสพฤni}\\
  \item
    \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุนูุจูุกููููู}} \emph{ษibสพayni}\\
  \item
    \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุจูุทูุกููู}} \emph{buแนญสพahu}\\
  \item
    \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุจูุทูุกููู}} \emph{buแนญสพuhu}\\
  \item
    \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุจูุทูุกููู}} \emph{buแนญสพihi}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  If the hamzah is after a long-\emph{ลซ} vowel or an \emph{aw} semi-vowel, and the hamzah does not have an \emph{i}-mark, then it is written unseated. Example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููุณููุกูููู}} \emph{yasลซสพลซna}
  \end{itemize}

  This is actually an acceptable variant in the general case, as will discuss below in section~\ref{hamza-variants}
\end{itemize}

({\tradarab{ุงูู}}, {\tradarab{ูููู}}, {\tradarab{ูู}}, and {\tradarab{ูู}} are suffixes.)
Note that the combination \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุกุง} is not written as \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุข} when the สพalif is part of the suffix.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\emph{n}-marks on final hamzah}{n-marks on final hamzah}}\label{n-marks-on-final-hamzah}

\emph{n}-marks on a final hamzah do not affect the writing of the hamzah except in the case of \emph{an}-mark. When writing \emph{an}-mark on a hamzah at the end of a word:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  If there is an สพalif before a unseated hamzah {\tradarab{ุงุก}}, then we don't add a silent สพalif when writing \emph{an}-mark. For example {\tradarab{ุฏูุงุก}} becomes {\tradarab{ุฏูุงุกู}} \emph{dฤสพan}, not {\tradarab{ุฏูุงุกูุง}}.
\item
  Otherwise, we add the silent สพalif after the hamzah so that the hamzah is now in the middle of the word with a suffix สพalif after it. We now pretend that the hamzah has an \emph{a}-mark and that the สพalif after it is a long-\emph{ฤ} vowel. Then we go through the rules for writing hamzah in the middle of a word (given above) to determine how hamzah will be written. We then write the \emph{an}-mark on the hamzah. Examples:
\end{enumerate}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููุจูุชูุฏูุฃ}} becomes {\tradarab{ููุจูุชูุฏูุฃูุ ููุจูุชูุฏูุกูุงุ ููุจูุชูุฏูุฅู}}
\item
  \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููููุฌูุฃ}} becomes {\tradarab{ููููุฌูุฃูุ ููููุฌูุกูุงุ ููููุฌูุฅู}}
\item
  \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุฌูุฒูุก}} becomes {\tradarab{ุฌูุฒูุกูุ ุฌูุฒูุกูุงุ ุฌูุฒูุกู}}
\item
  \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุดูููุก}} becomes {\tradarab{ุดูููุกูุ ุดูููุกูุงุ ุดูููุกู}}
\item
  \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุณููููุฆ}} becomes {\tradarab{ุณููููุฆู, ุณููููุฆูุง, ุณููููุฆู}}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Variants}\label{hamza-variants}

There are some historical and regional variants to the above rules. The main one is when the letter before hamzah has a รธ-mark, the hamzah is generally written unseated. So with this variant, we write:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููุณูุกููู}} instead of {\tradarab{ููุณูุคููู}}
\item
  \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุฃูุณูุกูููุฉ}} instead of {\tradarab{ุฃูุณูุฆูููุฉ}}
\item
  \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ููุณูุกูููุฉ}} instead of {\tradarab{ููุณูุฃูููุฉ}}
\end{itemize}

However, this rule appears to be not consistently followed. For example, \emph{nasอกhสพah} is generally always written {\tradarab{ููุดูุฃูุฉ}} never {\tradarab{ููุดูุกูุฉ}}.

A second variant is to avoid the repetition of vowel letters like {\tradarab{ู}} and {\tradarab{ู}}. So they write:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุฑูุกููุณ}} instead of {\tradarab{ุฑูุคููุณ}}.
\item
  \vphantom{\huge J} {\tradarab{ุฑูุกููุณ}} instead of {\tradarab{ุฑูุฆููุณ}}.
\end{itemize}

\section{Why so complicated?}\label{why-so-complicated}

Hamzah was originally not pronounced everywhere in some Classical Arabic dialects.
So, for many words, speakers of these dialects would typically only pronounce hamzah in the beginning of a word.
When hamzah would occur in the middle of a word, they would replace it with an \emph{a}, \emph{u}, or \emph{i} vowel.
So they would adjust their pronunciation as follows:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Proununciation with hamzah
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Proununciation without hamzah
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\vphantom{\huge J}{\tradarab{ููููุกูุฉ}} \emph{hayสพah} & {\tradarab{ูููููุฉ}} \emph{hayyah} \\
\vphantom{\huge J}{\tradarab{ุฎูุทููุกูุฉ}} \emph{kอกhaแนญฤซสพah} & {\tradarab{ุฎูุทููููุฉ}} \emph{kอกhaแนญiyyah} \\
\vphantom{\huge J}{\tradarab{ุชูููุกูู}} \emph{tawสพam} & {\tradarab{ุชููููู}} \emph{tawwam} \\
\vphantom{\huge J}{\tradarab{ุชูุณูุงุคูู}} \emph{tasฤสพul} & {\tradarab{ุชูุณูุงููู}} \emph{tasฤwul} \\
\vphantom{\huge J}{\tradarab{ุจูุฆูุณู}} \emph{biสพsa} & {\tradarab{ุจููุณู}} \emph{bฤซsa} \\
\vphantom{\huge J}{\tradarab{ุณูุคูู}} \emph{suสพl} & {\tradarab{ุณููู}} \emph{sลซl} \\
\vphantom{\huge J}{\tradarab{ฺฉูุฃูุณ}} \emph{kaสพs} & {\tradarab{ฺฉูุงุณ}} \emph{kฤs} \\
\end{longtable}

When the Classical Standard Arabic variety emerged,
then, for reasons that are beyond the scope of this text,
the pronunciation \emph{with} hamzah
and the consonantal spelling \emph{without} hamzah became standardized.
So {\tradarab{ุก}} is now added as a pronunciation mark on top of the various \emph{seats} that would instead have been dialectally pronounced without hamzah.

\section{Typographical limitations}\label{typographical-limitations}

Unfortunately, most digital fonts do not currently allow for correctly typing an unseated hamzah between two joined letters
({\tradarab{ูุกู}}),
as in
{\tradarab{ุฎูุทููุกูุฉ}} \emph{kอกhaแนญฤซสพah}.
In most fonts, the hamzah character (Unicode {\texttt{u+0621}}) will break the joining between the two letters surrounding it, and the output will be rendered incorrectly:
\foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุทููุกูุฉ} \emph{kอกhaแนญฤซสพah}.

Two typefaces which allow for the correct typesetting are

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Amiri from Alif Type (\href{https://www.amirifont.org/}{amirifont.org})
\item
  Naskhโข from DecoType (\href{https://www.decotype.com/}{decotype.com})
\end{itemize}

We have used the Amiri font for typesetting this appendix chapter.

For most other fonts,
an unseated hamzah between two joined letters
would have to be approximated
in one of two ways:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Hamzah superscript on a taแนญwฤซl character: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ูู}. Example: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุทูููููุฉ}. The Unicode input sequence is:

  {\texttt{u+0640}} \textsc{arabic tatweel}\\
  {\texttt{u+0654}} \textsc{arabic hamza above}

  This is a more accurate approximation, but some fonts may not position the hamzah correctly on the taแนญwฤซl, or position vowel marks on the superscript hamzah correctly.
\item
  Hamzah seated on yฤสพ: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฆ}. Example: \foreignlanguage{arabic}{ุฎูุทููุฆูุฉ}. This is a reprehensible, yet more prevalent, and better supported, approximation.
\end{enumerate}

Beware, though, that neither of these approximations would allow for the correct rendering of a complex (but thankfully rare) word like
{\tradarab{ููุกููุงู}} \emph{laสพสพฤl} \enquote{pearl seller}, where the hamzah is not allowed to disturb the lฤm-สพalif ligature {\tradarab{ูุง}}.
